First Page

॥ Shri Hari॥

Sure Steps to God Realization

 

tvameva mātā ca pitā tvameva

tvameva bandhuśca sakhā tvameva

tvameva vidyā draviṇaṁ tvameva

tvameva sarvaṁ mama devadeva

 

Honourable Shri Jayadayal Goyandka

Gita Seva Trust

 

॥ Shri Hari॥

Introduction

Shri Jayadayalji Goyandka, the writer of the spiritual essays, stands in little need of any introduction to our readers.

A true light on the path of God-realization can be shed only by a person, who has himself realized God, who has known Him, who lives in Him and through whom God manifests His will. Shri Jayadayalji was indeed a realized soul, obviously his ideas on the technique of realizing God must be most dependable, most illuminating and real gems of Godly wisdom.

We are full of joy to present this small volume in the hands of those who are keen to know God and are true aspirants and seekers of Truth as an unfailing guide, a comforting companion and an affectionate friend on their journey towards Godhead.

We are sure that devotees would derive definite aid and illumination from this volume.

 

The Secret of the Atmā and the Paramātmā

Q.—Are Atmā and Paramātmā one and the same, or is there any difference between the two ?

A.—This question covers the highest truth and the greatest secret. Truly speaking, it is extremely difficult to say anything categorial on this matter. Nevertheless, all the Teachers of religion have expressed their views on it, following the teachings of their respective sects. According to the schools that uphold Bhakti or Devotion, the Jīva is different from the Paramātmā, that is, the Paramātmā can never be the Jīva, and the Jīva can never be the Paramātmā. Though one in kind, they are different in form. According to the teachings of Adwaita, the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā are identical. Like space within a pot and space outside it, though appearing different on account of the Jīva’s identification with the body, the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā are, in reality, one and the same. The Lord described the Jīvātmā in the Gītā as a portion of Him—

ममैवांशो जीवलोके जीवभूत: सनातन:।

मन:षष्ठानीन्द्रियाणि प्रकृतिस्थानि कर्षति॥

(XV. 7)

“The Jīvātmā (soul) in the body is an eternal portion of Myself; it draws with it the mind and the five senses that rest in Matter.”

Goswāmī śrī Tulasīdāsajī also says—

“God’s part, the Jīva (Jīvātmā) is imperishable sentient, stainless and by nature delight itself.”*

* ईस्वर अंस जीव अबिनासी।

चेतन अमल सहज सुखरासी॥

Just as the various cāryas hold their own individual views in respect of the Jīvātmā, even so their views on God also differ from one another. But the actual truth about the matter can never be understood unless the Sādhaka has directly realized God. The views of all these Teachers are both reasonable and approved by the scriptures. The Vedas and Upaniṣads also uphold these contradictory views. Some of their statements stand for Dualism1, and the others for Identity.2 Similarly the Gītā also describes the Jīva and ºśwara sometimes as Dual (Cf. verses XV. 7 and XVIII.61) and sometimes Identical (Cf. verses XIII. 30 and XVIII.20). All these are included in the two paths of discipline enunciated by the Lord in the Gītā. The Lord says—

लोकेऽस्मिन् द्विविधा निष्ठा पुरा प्रोक्ता मयानघ।

ज्ञानयोगेन सांख्यानां कर्मयोगेन योगिनाम्॥

(III. 3)

“O sinless Arjuna! In this world two courses of spiritual discipline have been enunciated by Me in the past. In the case of the Sāṅkhyayogī, the Sādhanā proceeds along the path of knowledge, whereas in the case of the Karmayogī, it proceeds along the path of Action.”

1. The Muṇḍakopaniṣad says—

द्वा सुपर्णा सयुजा सखाया समानं वृक्षं परिषस्वजाते।

तयोरन्य: पिप्पलं स्वाद्वत्त्यनश्नन्नन्यो अभिचाकशीति॥

(III. i. 1)

“Two birds (Jīvātmā and Paramātmā), living together and friendly to each other, dwell upon one and the same tree (the body). One of them eats and enjoys the fruits of that tree in the form of Karma, and the other does not eat but merely looks on as witness.”

The śwetāśwataropaniṣad says—

मायां तु प्रकृतिं विद्यान्मायिनं तु महेश्वरम्।

तस्यावयवभूतैस्तु व्याप्तं सर्वमिदं जगत्॥

(IV. 10)

“Māyā should be known as the Prakr̥ti, and the Maheśwara should be known as the Lord of Māyā. This whole world is, in reality, pervaded by Prakr̥ti, which represents the Energy of the Lord.”

2. The ºśāvāsyopaniṣad says—

यस्तु सर्वाणि भूतान्यात्मन्येवानुपश्यति।

सर्वभूतेषु चात्मानं ततो न विजुगुप्सते॥

यस्मिन् सर्वाणि भूतान्यात्मैवाभूद्विजानत:।

तत्र को मोह: क: शोक एकत्वमनुपश्यत:॥

(6-7)

“Whoever beholds all beings in the soul alone, and the soul in all beings, that is, regards all beings as his own soul—does not look down on any creature. How can he really look down on others regarding them as his own soul?”

“Thus when to the saint, the knower of the Self, all beings appear as the soul; in other words, when he beholds the unity of the soul, then there is no delusion, no grief for him. That is, modifications like grief, delusion, etc., wholly cease in the case of one who realizes the presence of the same Supreme Brahma, God, the embodiment of knowledge and bliss in all.”

In the second Mantra, the Māṇḍūkyopaniṣad says—

सर्वॸ ह्येतद् ब्र ब्रह्मायमात्मा ब्रह्म सोऽयमात्मा चतुष्पात्।

“For all this world is the same as Brahma or God, and the Atmā, consisting of four feet (condition), is also the same as God.”

The Chāndogyopaniṣad also says—

तदिदमप्येतर्हि य एवं वेदाहं ब्रह्मास्मीति स इदॸ सर्वं भवति तस्य ह न देवाश्च ना भूत्या ईशते। आत्मा ह्येषाॸ स भवति।

“Whoever knows this Brahma even now as ‘I am Brahma’, turns into this all. The gods also fail to conquer him, because he becomes their Atmā or soul.” Again—

सर्वं खल्विदं ब्रह्म तज्जलानिति शान्त उपासीत।

(III. xiv. I)

“All this verily is Brahma, for therefrom doth it proceed, therein doth it merge, and thereby is it maintained; realizing this with a quiet and controlled mind one should practise adoration.”

And—

सदेव सोम्येदमग्र आसीदेकमेवाद्वितीयम्।

(VI. ii. 1)

“O child ! In the beginning there was only this Sat, without a second.”

In other words, one path upholds Dualism or Separateness and the other Unity or Identity.

Worship through separateness is called the path of devotion; in this path, the Jīva is regarded as the worshipper, and God as the object of worship. In this path, again, there are many divisions and sub-divisions. Sādhakas belonging to the various schools worship God either as śrī Rāma, or as śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, or śrī śiva, or śrī Viṣṇu, etc. All these Sādhakas regard their respective object of worship as the Supreme Lord Himself and attribute all the marks of God in Him and hold Him to be the agent of creation, sustenance and destruction of the universe. Just as people call water by different names, e.g., Nīra, p, Toya, Ambu, Pānī, etc., even so śrī Rāma, śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, śrī Viṣṇu, śrī śiva, śrī Durgā, śrī Gaṇeśa, śrī Sūrya, śrī Hari, Parameśwara, Allāh, Khudā, etc., stand for the name of one and the same God. The substance is one; though men belonging to the different sects worship Him by different names. This is how the worshippers of different schools worship the different forms of God. Some worship God with form, and others the formless aspect of God. Some worship God as possessed of two hands, some as possessed of four hands; and some others, as possessed of many hands. Some worship Him considering Him to be masculine in gender, some look upon God as feminine; and there are others who adore the Absolute in the neuter gender. All worship their respective forms of God according to their own faith and belief; but whatever descriptions of God are given by these worshippers, when they are all combined, cannot describe even a part of the real form of God. Truly speaking, it is never possible for anyone to give a full description of the complete form of God; for He is indescribable. Without direct realization, none can even know the real form of God. He can be understood only after realization. He is not the object of mind and speech—

यतो वाचो निवर्तन्ते अप्राप्य मनसा सह।

(Taittirīya. II. ix. 1)

For instance, there are Sādhakas who adore Bhagavān śrī Kr̥ṣṇa as the object of their worship; but these Sādhakas also adopt different forms of śrī Kr̥ṣṇa for their respective practices. Some meditate on the Child, or Gopāla Form of śrī Kr̥ṣṇa aged three years, and some on śrī Kr̥ṣṇa of eleven years, the player of the divine flute; and there is a third group, who meditate on śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, the Charioteer of Arjuna. All these three classes of Sādhakas meditate on three different forms of one and the same śrī Kr̥ṣṇa. In this also, there are many minor divisions and sub-divisions. For example, imagine there are a thousand Sādhakas, who meditate on śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, the divine flute-player, of eleven years of age. If all these Sādhakas are painters themselves, and each of them is asked to make a pictorial representation of his Deity, the pictures they will produce will be different in every case; that is, they will paint one-thousand different forms of śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, for all of them worship the Lord according to their own respective ideas about Him. That is the reason why the picture produced by one Sādhaka will be different from that of the other. No Sādhaka can truly know the real form of śrī Kr̥ṣṇa until he has directly seen the Lord. Similarly, the sentiment of devotion cultivated by devotees towards the Lord are also many. Some worship Him through the Servant-sentiment, and some through the Parental sentiment, some through the Friendly and some others through the Sweet sentiment. Mother Yaśodā cultivated the motherly sentiment towards śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, śrī Hanumān worshipped śrī Rāma through the Servant-sentiment, and Sugrīva through the Friendly-sentiment. The sentiments of śrī Sītājī towards śrī Rāma and of śrī Rukmiṇījī towards śrī Kr̥ṣṇa are of the sweet type. Dualistic worship is thus performed by devotees following various moods and sentiments.

Such differences should be understood to be present even in the case of Sādhakas who worship the attributeless and formless aspect of God. In this Path, the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā appear, separate when the Sādhaka is passing through the stage of practice; but in reality there is no difference. Like space within the pot and infinite space, the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā appear separate due to the existence of the body; but in reality they are one and the same. This form of worship, based on the idea of Identity, is called worship according to the Path of Identity. In this also, there are several divisions and sub-divisions according to the sentiment or mental outlook of the Sādhaka. The utterance of a single word “Saccidānanda” gives rise to different idea in the minds of different Sādhakas. The idea produced in one mind will not agree into with that of another. But whatever idea or form is produced by the word in the mind of the Sādhaka, he makes that his spiritual goal and goes on meditating thereon for the attainment of perfection, as the result of which he succeeds in realizing God; but, when logically considered, the picture of the goal formed by a Sādhaka in his mind can never be treated as real. But from the point of view of practice all such mental pictures are real; for they lead ultimately to the realization of God. The reality about God is known by the Sādhaka only when through the attainment of perfection in his practice he actually realizes God. In Dualistic Worship also, the reality about the Divine cannot be known and understood, until the Sādhaka has actually seen God face to face.

For the removal of this anomaly we may submit to God that He should either reveal to the Sādhaka His true form in the very beginning, and tell the Sādhaka that the latter would realize Him after the practice of meditation on that form for so many years; or He should send a true copy of His photograph so that the Sādhaka may know what His actual form is, or impart to the Sādhaka by some other means the Knowledge of His actual form. Unless this is done, whatever form the Sādhaka may meditate upon believing it to be the form of God should be regarded by the Lord as His own true form. As a matter of fact, God easily grants whatever the devotee may think about Him; according to this argument a Sādhaka may practise meditation on any form in the name of God, and may thereby realize God; for the Sādhaka has no other way but to follow the guidance of religious Teachers and the scriptures for the practice of meditation. Therefore, cultivating faith in the scriptures, one should go on with his spiritual practice according to them. In reality, God’s true form is altogether different in kind from whatever conceptions of God we may form in our mind based on the idea of identity or dualism, form or formlessness, attributes or attributelessness—after reading the scriptures, or hearing from the lips of saints, or on a study of the Gītā. Without direct realization, it is absolutely impossible for us to understand the real nature and form of God in our Sādhaka state by any means whatsoever. But what is the remedy? That is why the Lord says that everyone practising meditation on any of His aspects—Manifest or Unmanifest, dual or non-dual, with or without attributes, with or without form will certainly realize Him according to the sentiment he cultivates. This is clearly implied by the statement “ये यथा मां प्रपद्यन्ते तांस्तथैव भजाम्यहम्” (“Howsoever men approach Me, even so do I seek them”—Gītā IV. II).

The sum and substance of what has been stated above is this; according to the principle of Bhakti the Jīva (individual soul) is a part and God is the Total or Whole. Thus though one kind and essence they are separate. According to the principle of Identity or Unity, the tmā and the Paramātmā are one and the same; they appear separate due to nothing but error, in reality they are one. What the Sādhaka believes in should be regarded by him as principle or established truth; for a practitioner cannot reach a high state of development unless a belief is given the position of a principle. In reality, all the principles adopted by Sādhakas during the period of practice are nothing but concepts or ideas; the real principle or real substance is one and the same, which is beyond mind and speech and can be experienced only after God-realization. There is no other way of comprehending that reality. The Sādhaka first realizes God according to the principle he adopts for his practice, dual or non-dual. After this first realization, he gains knowledge of the real truth and real form of God, which is called Amr̥ta or ambrosia. This is beyond speech, mind and the intellect. The concept formed by the intellect, which derives its power from God, is only an intellectual conception about the form of God; the senses and mind cannot dive deep into and sound the depth of that reality. One can never obtain a reflection of the entire firmament in a mirror; for the firmament is infinite and the mirror is a small thing. Even so, God is infinite and the intellect is a small thing, hence the reality about God can never be comprehended by the intellect.

Teachers of Devotion like śrī Rāmānujācārya, śrī Madhwācārya, śrī Nimbārkācārya and śrī Vallabhācārya, etc., preached methods and practices of Bhakti which in their detail show differences from one another, either in more or less degree. But all of them gave allegiance to the principle that the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā are separate. Similarly, Teachers of the Adwaita school like śaṅkarācārya, also, though showing differences of opinion about the kind of unity between the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā, uphold the principle of Identity. In reality, both the principles of dualism and identity are true. For Sādhakas, who are inclined towards Devotion, the Jīvātmā and the Paramātmā are separate; and for Sādhakas who are inclined towards Identity, they are one and the same. Though these two are two distinct paths of practice, their fruit is one. God is both above Dualism and Identity, and possesses also those characteristics. Hence both the paths are right. Here, the question arises—what, then, is the real truth? The answer is that the Sādhaka will come to know the real truth only after direct realization of God, for actually it is something above and beyond both separateness and identity. It is a state which cannot be comprehended by the mind or explained by speech. Therefore, the best thing for the Sādhaka is to make his choice between these two paths according to his inclination and taste, and devote himself to the practices of his chosen path, for therein lies his spiritual good. Both the paths are approved by the scriptures and are supported by reason, and both lead to God-realization. After the realization of God, the actual truth and reality about the matter will be automatically comprehended by him.

For this supernatural, extraordinary and indescribable truth can never be completely described by the scriptures and saints. What they describe is concerned primarily with discipline, which infallibly leads to God-realization. But the spiritual goal to which they take the Sādhakas is beyond both mind and speech; it can only be experienced and is comprehended only after the Sādhaka has actually realized God. The scriptures and saints recognize both the paths of discipline and describe both as highly efficacious. The teachings of all religious sects are worthy of our respect and what they preach are all true, according to the rule of logic known as the “branch and the moon.” When as the result of the practice of these disciplines God is actually realized, there comes an experience, which is supernatural and extraordinary. This is the reality in its true sense. The teachings of the scriptures and saints are true in the sense that Sādhakas following the paths shown by them succeed in realizing God.

Now, we shall explain what constitutes the logic of ‘branch and the moon’. Suppose the moon has risen on the second day of the lunar fortnight; and one person has seen it and another cannot discover it with all his effort. Now, the person who has seen that faint curved line of the moon, pointing his finger to the branch of a tree, says—“Look, a cubit above that branch is the moon.” Turning his gaze according to the direction indicated, the second person succeeds in seeing the moon. But was the moon, really speaking, a cubit above that branch? Never. Similarly, the same person guides another friend by saying, “Look, the moon is close to that corner of this house.” And following his guidance, the second friend also succeeds in seeing the moon. But was the moon actually close to the corner of that house? Never. Even so he guides a third person saying, “Look, the moon is between the two paws of that flying bird”, and following that direction looking towards the paws of the bird the person sees the moon. But was the moon actually between the paws of that bird? Never. We should learn from this that though the literal senses of the words used are wrong, they lead to the attainment of the objective. Even so, though the disciplines laid down by the scriptures and saints may appear defective when tested by reason, they are all true, because, finally, they lead to God-realization. Just as following the direction of a guide and turning their gaze towards the direction indicated, the moon is discovered by a person, who is anxious to see it; even so, God is realized by a Sādhaka, who is anxious to realize Him, when, following the scriptures or the teaching of a saint, he adopts a discipline and carries it out with faith. And just as pointing his finger, a person can only say that the moon is above that branch by a cubit, or it is just close to the corner of that house, even so, the scriptures and saints, when they speak of the dual and non-dual forms of worship, only point the Sādhakas towards God; but there can be no doubt about the fact that unless one has actually realized God, he can never understand what constitutes the reality about God.

We shall try to make the point clearer by another example. Let us imagine a person belonging to this earth going to a world below the earth, which is totally covered by darkness, where people carry on their business with the help of the light given by fire and electricity, where the phenomenon called day and night never takes place, and the sun, moon and stars, etc., have no existence at all. There he asked the local people, “Is there no daybreak at all in this region?” The people of that world felt very much surprised at this question, and began to wonder what the day was. Then he explained to them that when the sun rises it is day, and when it goes down, it is night. This made them all the more curious. What is the sun? They asked. He replied, “It is a huge ball of light possessed of extraordinary brilliance, which, when it rises, totally drives away darkness, and lights up all the quarters.” Thereupon the residents of that nether world set up a bulb of one thousand candle power and enquired—“Is your sun similar to this?” He replied, “This is nothing compared to the sun.” Then those people set up a bulb of ten thousand candle power. “Is your sun like this?” They asked again. But still he replied that compared to the sun that light was nothing at all. In the end, those people made a light using all the electric power in their possession, but yet the man continued to assert that that light was nothing compared to the light of the sun. The residents of that world concluded from this that the man was a liar. How could there be any light more brilliant and powerful than the one they had just set up? The man, thereupon replied, “I am sorry I am unable to make you comprehend my point. What I mean to say cannot be described by words and is really inconceivable. So long as you have not actually seen the sun, you will not understand what it is.” Then, the residents of that world deputed one of their own men to come to this earth under the guidance of that man. When they both reached the earth, it was the midnight of the new-moon, and extreme darkness prevailed everywhere. To the resident of that nether world that very darkness appeared as light, and he asked his companion, “Is this what you call the day?” The companion replied, “No, this is the dense darkness of night.” After this when the darkness diminished a little, and there was twilight, he again asked—“Has the day broken now?” The companion replied, “No, we are still passing through the night.” Then, slowly the stars began to fade, and there was only half-an-hour before sunrise, when the resident of the nether world again enquired whether the day has dawned, but his companion still replied in the negative. “What are these stars?”—the newcomer asked. The companion replied, “Like this earth, they are so many separate worlds and balls of light.” Now, the light of the stars grew more dim, and when it was only five or six minutes before sunrise all of them, except the śukra (Venus) and the Br̥haspati (Jupiter), totally disappeared. And the moment the sun rose, śukra and Br̥haspati also disappeared from sight. The sun appeared so very bright that the resident of the nether world could not even look it—he saw the sun’s rays with his back turned towards the sun. At midday, when the sun became very bright, it was impossible for the poor soul even to gaze at it. His companion then handed to him a dark glass through which he was asked to look, even as we watch an eclipse of the sun, and told him that it was the thing he now observed in the sky, which made day and night on the earth. Thereupon the fellow exclaimed in wonder that he now really understood what are day and night, and what is the sun!

After this experience, the fellow returned to his own world. There, he was at once surrounded by his friends who eagerly asked, whether he had actually seen day, night and the sun? What kind of a thing was the sun? How bright was it? What are day and night like? They repeated the very same questions to their friend as they had put to the man who had originally come from the earth. Their friend replied, “The sun is a ball of light; it is infinitely brighter than our electric lights.” They again asked, “Explain this infinite degree of brightness to us? You know the language in which we speak.” He replied, “It is impossible to describe that thing to you. Unless you have seen it, you cannot know what it is. There is nothing in our world with which it can be compared, and referring to which I can say it is like this. You can comprehend it only after direct perception; without such experience, you can never expect to understand what it is.”

The illustration shows how difficult it is to understand even an ordinary thing like the sun without direct perception. When this is so, how is it possible to understand God without direct realization, and how can the nature of God be explained by one person to another? Nevertheless, the scriptures and saints try to describe as much as is possible of being described by words; but, truly speaking, none can make others comprehend the truth. The method of teaching of every religious teacher is different from that of other teachers. When tested by reason, no system will appear to be wholly unshakable, flawless and safely grounded on reason. For example, out of twenty sects, one sect affirms a particular principle, and the other nineteen deny it. Thus the principle of every sect is ruled out by the other nineteen; therefore nothing can be really established by logic or reason.

Similarly, there are hundreds of commentaries on the Gītā. Which of them should be regarded as true, and whose explanation should be adopted as correct? All of them show fundamental differences, and give different explanations to the verses. Take one commentary out of these hundred; you will find it is opposed by the rest of the commentaries. This proves that all of them are mutually contradictory. But one thing common among these commentaries is that they all show equal respect to the text of the Gītā. So far as the original text is concerned, there is no difference of opinion among them. Even so, God, who is the foundation of everything, is one; He is the supreme truth, beyond mind and speech.

In order to know God, the Sādhaka should take refuge in God as well as the saints. Addressing Arjuna, the Lord said—

तमेव शरणं गच्छ सर्वभावेन भारत।

तत्प्रसादात्परां शान्तिं स्थानं प्राप्स्यसि शाश्वतम्॥

(XVIII. 62)

“Seek refuge in Him alone with all your being, Arjuna. Through His grace you shall obtain supreme peace and the eternal abode.”

In the Viṣṇusahasranāma, it is said—

वासुदेवाश्रयो मर्त्यो वासुदेवपरायण:।

सर्वपापविशुद्धात्मा याति ब्रह्म सनातनम्॥

(130)

“One who has taken refuge in Vāsudeva (God) and has completely surrendered himself to Vāsudeva, being freed from sins and possessed of a pure heart, realizes the eternal, supreme Brahma or God.”

And referring to the need for seeking refuge with saints, the Lord says in the Gītā—

तद्विद्धि प्रणिपातेन परिप्रश्नेन सेवया।

उपदेक्ष्यन्ति ते ज्ञानं ज्ञानिनस्तत्त्वदर्शिन:॥

यज्ज्ञात्वा न पुनर्मोहमेवं यास्यसि पाण्डव।

येन भूतान्यशेषेण द्रक्ष्यस्यात्मन्यथो मयि॥

(IV. 34-35)

“Attain this knowledge by all means. If you prostrate yourself at the feet of the wise, render them all forms of service, and question them with a guileless heart, again and again, those wise seers of Truth will unfold that Knowledge to you. Acquiring that Knowledge you will no more be subjected to delusion like this, Arjuna, and through it you will see all beings first in your own self, and then in Me (the Oversoul).”

This is God-realization; and this has been variously described as attainment of the supreme abode, the supreme goal, the supreme indestructible and the unmanifest, etc.

The Lord says—

अव्यक्तोऽक्षर इत्युक्तस्तमाहु: परमां गतिम्।

यं प्राप्य न निवर्तन्ते तद्धाम परमं मम॥

(VIII. 21)

“This Unmanifest, spoken of as the indestructible, is called the Supreme God; that is My supreme abode, attaining which man does not return.”

This is realized through the practices stated above. Therefore, whatever may be the form or aspect of God for which a Sādhaka may have faith or liking, keeping that as the goal in view, he should go on with the practices of his discipline with a resolute heart, with a mind endowed with discrimination and dispassion.

 

Faith in God

The questions which have been asked about God are not at all surprising to me, for the subject is one which baffles human intellect. The wonder of wonders is that we do not really believe in God even though we profess theism. Those who believe in God without understanding His true nature declare Him to be omniscient (सर्वज्ञ), omnipotent (सर्वशक्तिमान्), just (न्यायकारी), the dispenser of the fruit of our actions (कर्मफलदाता), and a compact mass of reality, knowledge and bliss; but they do not obey the laws framed by Him. Their belief in God is only nominal; it is due to their folly that the existence of God is being questioned all over the world at the present day. Although I consider those who have such blind faith in God better than their friends who altogether deny His existence, I denounce them because it is these blind believers that are mainly responsible for having spread the cult of atheism. Their belief alone is praiseworthy who believe in God knowing Him what He really is. For the conduct of the man who has understood the reality of God can never transgress the bounds of propriety as laid down by God. On the contrary, his conduct serves as a model for others and compels their admiration. The Lord says—

यद्यदाचरति श्रेष्ठस्तत्तदेवेतरो जन:।

स यत्प्रमाणं कुरुते लोकस्तदनुवर्तते॥

(Gītā III. 21)

“Whatsoever a great man does, others try to follow him. The people act up to the standard set up by him.” It is these people who are the true exponents of theism—I am only an ordinary man. Although I find myself unable to answer questions relating to God, I venture to submit, for the perusal of my readers as well as for self-gratification, to the best of my knowledge, some of my views on the subject, which are the outcome partly of my association with pious men and partly of my own reflection. I hope the kind readers will overlook my failings as those of a child. Theology is a very profound and obscure subject and even the most erudite of scholars are confounded while trying to grasp its truths, to say nothing of ordinary persons like myself.

Question 1. Why should we believe in God?

Answer—(a) God showers His mercy on everyone as a matter of course, administers justice without any recompense and loves all equally. It is therefore obligatory on us to believe in Him, and humanity consists in discharging one’s obligation.

(b) Without belief in God it is not possible for anyone to investigate His true nature, and without investigating His true nature one cannot get an insight into it, and one cannot attain lasting happiness without knowing God.

(c) Belief in God stimulates a desire to know His virtues, loving nature and greatness and an endeavour to repeat His names, to meditate on His nature, and to hear of, and dwell on, His virtues, whereby one’s sins, weaknesses and afflictions are eradicated and supreme bliss is attained.

(d) One who believes in God after fully comprehending His nature cannot commit any immoral act. Those who are found indulging in immoral practices do not really believe in God. They are only impostors.

(e) Those who sincerely believe in God have always triumphed. The scriptures abound in remarkable instances of this, such as those of Dhruva, Prahlāda and many others. Even at the present day those who sincerely believe in God and approach Him in a spirit of dedication are seen flourishing.

(f) The Vedas, Smr̥tis, and other scriptures alike are useful only in so far as they believe in the existence of God. For all the scriptures aim at establishing His existence.

वेदे रामायणे चैव पुराणे भारते तथा।

आदौ मध्ये तथा चान्ते हरि: सर्वत्र गीयते॥

“In the Vedas, the Rāmāyaṇa, the Purāṇas as well as the Mahābhārata Hari (God) is extolled at the beginning, in the middle as well as at the end.

Q. 2. What harm is there in denying God?

Answer —(a) Denial of the existence of an All-pervading God who dispenses the fruit of our actions promotes unruliness in man. An unruly man develops in himself vices such as mendacity, duplicity, thieving, illicit love, violence etc., and evil propensities such as lust, anger, greed, infatuation, conceit, etc., and is thereby degraded, with the result that he grows all the more unhappy.

(b) If we do not believe in God, we cannot have a curiosity about His true nature and without that we cannot liberate our souls.

(c) Disbelief in God makes us guilty of ungratefulness. For there is no wonder if those who do not believe in God, the supreme Father who creates and maintains the whole world and is the friend of all, do not recognise even their parents who begot and brought them up. And is there a being more ungrateful than the man who does not recognise his own parents who served him from his very birth.

(d) By not believing in God a man loses his spirituality and imbibes bestiality. Careful observation will clearly show that those who do not believe in God gradually evince these characteristics.

There are numerous other serious evils resulting from disbelief in God; they are not mentioned here for want of space.

Q. 3. What are the strong arguments in favour of the existence of God?

Answer —This question is not at all surprising, nor does it reveal any wisdom. It is an ordinary thing for the people to ask this question. Nay, even an intelligent man is confronted with doubts concerning a subject which cannot be grasped by ordinary intellect, to say nothing of ordinary persons. But it should be borne in mind that to ask for proofs in favour of God, who is self-evident and furnishes the proof for everything else, is mere childishness. To question the existence of God is as absurd as to ask about one’s own self whether one exists or not. If it is urged that one’s own self is evident while God is not, our reply is that God is even more evident than our own self. If anyone asks in what way God is more evident than our own self, our rejoinder is this. One may argue that just as the objects and bodies that we behold in a dream no longer exist when we are wide wake, even so the objects and bodies that we perceive in our waking life may be only a dream to some one else. For we observe that just as the objects of our dreams undergo change in the dream, the objects of our waking life also undergo transformation in our waking life. But that immutable God who is responsible for the existence of all these objects and who persists even after all these have perished, who is the support (आधार) and foundation (अधिष्ठान) of everything is much more manifest than our individual existence. But He is manifest only to those noble souls who have been extolled in every sacred book. They alone who are endowed with a penetrating intellect can directly perceive God by means of their sharp intellect. The Vedas, the Smr̥tis, the Chronicles (the Rāmāyaṇa and the Mahābhārata), the Purāṇas and other scriptures, and the utterances of holy men bear testimony to this fact. Nay, those who desire to perceive God can do so by following the methods laid down in the Vedas and Smr̥tis and prescribed by holy men. Inferential proofs are also forthcoming in favour of the existence of God. Inference consists in deducing the existence of a cause from that of an effect. In this universe we do not find anything coming into existence or functioning without an efficient cause. From this we conclude that there is a tremendous force which created the earth, the ocean, the sun, the moon, the stars, the fire, the air, the sky, the directions (दिशा) and time and which controls them according to certain fixed laws. That force should be recognised as God. It is not correct to say that everything springs up of its own accord without any efficient cause, which it is not necessary to postulate, just as we find seeds originating from plants and plants growing from seeds. First of all let us consider whether it is the seed or the plant that came into existence first. If we are told that the seed came into existence first or that the tree preceded the seeds, we ask where it came from. And if it is urged that both came into being together, the question is how and by whom they were brought into existence. For an effect cannot come into being without a cause. He who created the seed and plants, etc., is God.

Our next question is, Is what you call nature sentient or insentient ? If you say it is insentient it is not possible for anything to come into existence or function without the existence or guidance of a sentient being. And if you say it is sentient we have no quarrel with you; for that sentient power which has brought this universe into existence is none else but God, call Him what you may. Without an intelligent guiding principle not only can the world not come into existence but it cannot function regularly. We do not find even the smallest machine worked without an engineer. Any organisation whatsoever will fall through if not supervised by a skilled organiser. Hence that power which runs the universe according to certain fixed laws should be recognised as God. Without an all-pervading, all-powerful and all-knowing God the various creatures cannot be made to reap the fruit of their respective actions according to their merits. It is not correct to say that an agent automatically reaps the fruit of his actions; for the actions themselves, insentient as they are, do not possess the faculty of adjusting the fruit of good and evil actions according to their merits and a sentient creature would not voluntarily reap the bitter consequences of his own misdeeds. A thief commits a burglary and the ruler in whose territory the theft is committed sentences him for the crime. But the thief never goes to the gaol of his own accord nor can his theft take him to that place. It is the officers appointed under the authority of the king who sentence him to a term of imprisonment commensurate with the seriousness of his crime. Similarly, the various deities or intelligences appointed by God bring woe to the sinners by way of punishment for their sins. In the same way a creature is himself unable to enjoy the fruit of his good actions in the shape of worldly pleasures. Just as a person obeying the laws of a king gets a fixed amount as reward for his good behaviour directly from the king or through the officers appointed by him, similarly, those who perform meritorious deeds get the reward for their actions, as ordained by God. The creatures themselves, infatuated as they are by ignorance, do not possess the necessary knowledge and capacity to migrate freely from one body to another according to their good or evil actions.

Besides this we find a purpose everywhere in the functions of the universe. Such a purposive universe can be created by none else than a highly intelligent sentient agent.

From the above exposition it is clear beyond doubt that without a God the world can neither come into existence nor function, nor can the creatures reap the fruit of their actions according to their merits, nor again is it possible for a purposive universe to come into being.

The above arguments have been advanced on the basis of inferential reasoning. As a matter of fact, God is self-evident because all proofs derive their validity from Him. Hence no other proofs are required to substantiate His existence.

The scriptures also bear testimony to the existence of God, all the four Vedas, the Smr̥tis, the Chronicles as well as the Purāṇas aim at establishing the existence of God. Numerous texts can be quoted from the above works to corroborate this fact, e.g.

ईशावास्यमिदॸ सर्वं यत्किंच जगत्यां जगत्।

(Yajurveda)

“Whatever exists in this universe is permeated by God.”

जन्माद्यस्य यत:। शास्त्रयोनित्वात्।

(Brahma Sūtras)

“That which creates, maintains and supports the universe is God, because He is the source of the scriptures. In other words, He who is the source i.e., the originator of the scriptures and who is proved by the scriptures is God.”

सर्वस्य चाहं हृदि संनिविष्टो

मत्त: स्मृतिर्ज्ञानमपोहनञ्च।

वेदैश्च सर्वैरहमेव वेद्यो

वेदान्तकृद्वेदविदेव चाहम्॥

(Gītā xv. 15)

“I am seated in the hearts of all (in the form of the inherent self). Memory, wisdom and the ratiocinative faculty emanate from Me. The four Vedas seek to know Me. Nay, I am the author of the Vedānta and knower of the Vedas.”

ईश्वर: सर्वभूतानां हृद्देशेऽर्जुन तिष्ठति।

भ्रामयन्सर्वभूतानि यन्त्रारूढानि मायया॥

(Gītā XVIII. 61)

“O Arjuna ! the Lord dwells as the inherent self (अन्तर्यामी) in the hearts of all beings, who are mounted on the wheel of this body, causing them by his enrapturing power (माया) to revolve according to their actions.”

ज्योतिषामपि तज्ज्योतिस्तमस: परमुच्यते।

ज्ञानं ज्ञेयं ज्ञानगम्यं हृदि सर्वस्य विष्ठितम्॥

(Gītā XIII. 17)

“He (God) is the illuminator of all lights and beyond the darkness (of ignorance). He is the very embodiment, as it were, of knowledge (wisdom), as also the object of knowledge and that which is attained by knowledge, and is seated in the hearts of all.”

उत्तम: पुरुषस्त्वन्य: परमात्मेत्युदाहृत:।

यो लोकत्रयमाविश्य बिभर्त्यव्यय ईश्वर:॥

(Gītā XV. 17)

“The Supreme Being is distinct from both (the destructible and the indestructible) and is known as the Universal Soul and the imperishable God, who, interpenetrating the three worlds sustains all.”

In the Yoga Sūtras of PataÛjali the following aphorisms occur—

क्लेशकर्मविपाकाशयैरपरामृष्ट: पुरुषविशेष ईश्वर:।

तत्र निरतिशयं सर्वज्ञबीजम्।

पूर्वेषामपि गुरु: कालेनानवच्छेदात्।

(Samādhipāda 24—26)

“God is one of such souls (the Supreme Soul), who is dissociated from the five afflictions (क्लेश), viz., Ignorance (अविद्या), Egoism (अस्मिता), Attachment (राग), Repugnance (द्वेष) and Fear of Death (अभिनिवेश), good and evil actions, the enjoyment of pleasure and pain and all sorts of desires.”

He is possessed of the highest degree of omniscience.

“He is the creator and instructor even of our remotest ancestors such as Brahmā and is unlimited by time.”

In the Upaniṣads as well the following texts are significant—

यतो वा इमानि भूतानि जायन्ते येन जातानि जीवन्ति यत्प्रयन्त्यभिसंविशन्ति। तद्विजिज्ञासस्व।

(Taittirīya Upaniṣad 3-1)

“Know Him who is the originator of all living beings, who sustains them after they are born and in whom they are all merged after death. He is the God (Brahma).”

एको देव: सर्वभूतेषु गूढ:

सर्वव्यापी सर्वभूतान्तरात्मा।

कर्माध्यक्ष: सर्वभूताधिवास:

साक्षी चेता केवलो निर्गुणश्च॥

“That one Deity (God) dwells in the hearts of all creatures, is All-pervading and the inner self of every living being. He is the supervisor of every action and the abode of all creatures, the witness, the sentient principle, absolute and free from attributes.”

In śrīmad Bhāgavata the Lord Himself says—

अहं ब्रह्मा च शर्वश्च जगत: कारणं परम्।

आत्मेश्वर उपद्रष्टा स्वयंदृगविशेषण:॥

आत्ममायां समाविश्य सोऽहं गुणमयीं द्विज।

सृजन् रक्षन् हरन् विश्वं दध्रे संज्ञां कियोचिताम्॥

“O Brāhmaṇa, I am Brahmā as well as śiva and the ultimate cause of the universe. I am the self as well as God, the inner ruler, self-perceiving and absolute. Entering my Māyā (enrapturing power), consisting of three attributes. I create, maintain and destroy the universe and assume different appellations according to this threefold function.”

In the Anuśāsanaparva of the Mahābhārata (Chapter 149) the following verses occur—

अनादिनिधनं विष्णुं सर्वलोकमहेश्वरम्।

लोकाध्यक्षं स्तुवन्नित्यं सर्वदु:खातिगो भवेत्॥ ६॥

ब्रह्मण्यं सर्वधर्मज्ञं लोकानां कीर्तिवर्द्धनम्।

लोकनाथं महद्भूतं सर्वभूतभवोद्भवम्॥ ७॥

परमं यो महत्तेज: परमं यो महत्तप:।

परमं यो महद्‍ब्रह्म परमं य: परायणम्॥ ९॥

पवित्राणां पवित्रं यो मङ्गलानाञ्च मङ्गलम्।

दैवतं देवतानाञ्च भूतानां योऽव्यय: पिता॥ १०॥

“He who always extols Him who has no beginning nor end, who is all-pervading and the Supreme Lord of all the three worlds and the controller of all the solar systems, who is the protector of the Brāhmaṇas, conversant with all the laws of morality, the promoter of the good name of the people, the master of the universe and the Great Being who creates all other beings, who is a great and supreme mass of lustre, the most austere of all austerities, the great Ultimate Reality and the ultimate and supreme resort of all, who is the most sacrosanct of all sacred objects, the most auspicious of all auspicious things, the presiding Deity of all deties and the Imperishable Father of all beings, surmounts all troubles.”

In the Rāmāyaṇa of Vālmīki God Brahmā says—

कर्त्ता सर्वस्य लोकस्य श्रेष्ठो ज्ञानविदां विभु:।

अक्षरं ब्रह्म सत्यञ्च मध्ये चान्ते च राघव॥

लोकानां त्वं परो धर्मो विष्वक्सेनश्चतुर्भुज:॥

“O Lord, You are the creator of all the three worlds, the wisest of the wise and All-pervading. You subsist at the beginning, in the middle and at the end of all creation, You are the indestructible supreme reality and truth; You are the supreme law governing all beings and the four-armed Viṣvaksena (Hari).”

Barring the scriptures of a few faiths such as Jainism, Budhism and the cult of Cārvāka there is not a single scripture or sacred book in which the existence of God has not been maintained. Mohammedans and Christians, too believe in God.

The Koran says—

“Both east and west are God’s. In whatever direction you turn your face you will find God’s face in that direction. God is all-powerful.”

Jesus Christ has said—

“He that believeth and is baptised shall be saved—but he that believeth not shall be condemned” (Aristion’s appendix—Mark 16-16).

Q. 4. Kindly relate some actual facts or incidents of your life which may have contributed to increase your faith in the existence and mercy of God.

Answer—

If we look around us with our eyes open we get proofs of the existence and mercy of God—Who is supremely merciful and just at every step. We find several incidents furnishing such proofs in the lives of a number of saints of ancient as well as the modern times. I do not know what to write about my own self. I can of course respectfully submit that it will surely do us good if we believe in the existence and mercy of the all-powerful God Who is the very embodiment, as it were, of knowledge and bliss, as well as incidents in the lives of holy men pointing to the same.

 

Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa

A friend has asked me how objects constituted of the three Guṇas—Sattva, Rajas and Tamas—evolve from Prakr̥ti (Primordial Matter) and on what grounds Prakr̥ti has been declared as the doer and the Puruṣa or the individual soul as the experiencer (of joys and sorrows). The following is our submission in reply to this question. The Lord says in the Gītā—

प्रकृतिं पुरुषं चैव विद्‍ध्यनादी उभावपि।

विकारांश्च गुणांश्चैव विद्धि प्रकृतिसम्भवान्॥

(XIII. 19)

“Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa, know both these as without beginning. And know all modifications such as likes and dislikes etc., and all objects constituted of the three Guṇas as born of Prakr̥ti.”

Here the modifications and the objects of the nature of the three Guṇas have been declared as evolved from Prakr̥ti. Therefore, the first thing to be ascertained is the number of the modifications. Regarding these Lord says in verse 6 of the same Discourse—

इच्छा द्वेष: सुखं दु:खं संघातश्चेतना धृति:।

एतत् क्षेत्रं समासेन सविकारमुदाहृतम्॥

“Desire, aversion, joy and sorrow, the physical body, consciousness, firmness; thus is the Kṣetra, with its evolutes (modifications) briefly stated.”

The verse immediately preceding it (XIII.5) enumerates the elements constituting the body. Of the modifications mentioned above, desire and aversion, joy and sorrow, firmness and consciousness are the modifications of the heart. The physical body is an evolute of the five elements. Being of the Sāttvika type, firmness and consciousness are welcome modifications. Being an evolute of attachment, desire is virtually the same as attachment. These attachment and aversion are the root of all other modifications. Our mental attitude with regard to something agreeable is ‘attachment’, while that in respect something of disagreeable is ‘aversion’. All that is disagreeable excites aversion, pain, fear and jealousy, rivalry and anxiety. Besides aversion itself gives rise to a number of other morbid feelings. Similarly, attachment too excites many a morbid feeling. Desire also leads to such feelings. The Gītā says;—

सङ्गात्संजायते काम: कामात्क्रोधोऽभिजायते॥

क्रोधाद्भवति सम्मोह: सम्मोहात्स्मृतिविभ्रम:।

स्मृतिभ्रंशाद् बुद्धिनाशो बुद्धिनाशात्प्रणश्यति॥

(II. 62-63)

All these are modifications of attachment and desire alone. From attachment springs up desire, and from desire (unfulfilled) ensues anger; then confusion of memory, thence loss of memory and from loss of memory one rushes headlong to complete degradation. All these modifications are the offspring of attachment and desire alone. That is why the Lord says in the (Gītā XIII. 6) that He referred to the modifications only briefly. Now let us come to the other issue. In the second half of (XIII. 19) the Lord says—

विकारांश्च गुणांश्चैव विद्धि प्रकृतिसम्भवान्।

“Know all modifications and all objects constituted of the three Guṇas as born of Prakr̥ti.”

Sattva, Rajas and Tamas are the three Guṇas. It is from Prakr̥ti that all these three Guṇas, whether of the nature of effects or of instruments, proceed and propagate themselves. Before the process of Creation (Mahāsarga) is set into operation only two entities, viz., Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa, exist. Puruṣa means the Supreme Spirit and Prakr̥ti His Energy. In other words, śakti and śaktimān are the only two entities present at that time. They are the parents of this entire creation. In verse 3 of Discourse (XIV) of the Gītā we are told that Prakr̥ti or Primordial Matter is the womb while He is the Procreator, who deposits the seed. Therefore, Prakr̥ti is the Mother of all creation and He is the Father who deposits the seed from which sprouts forth the entire universe. In this way all objects constituted of the three Guṇas evolve from Prakr̥ti alone.

At the beginning of each round of creation the universe is brought into existence and at the time of universal dissolution all beings merge in Prakr̥ti. The gross bodies of all beings disappear at the time of dissolution. The souls tenanting the subtle bodies persist, but their subtle bodies too get merged in their cause (Prakr̥ti). Thus at the time of universal dissolution everything disappears. Again at the time of creation everything is brought forth in same way. Some dissimilarities are noticed in the process of creation and dissolution described in various śāstras. The Gītā mentions the same number of categories as have been enumerated in the Yoga-Sūtras of the great sage PataÛjali and the Sāṅkhya-Sūtras of Lord Kapila. The process of creation as delineated in the latter two works is as follows—Prakr̥ti brought forth the Mahattattva; from the Mahattattva was evolved the cosmic ego; from the ego proceeded the mind and the five subtle elements and from the five subtle elements sprang up the ten Indriyas, namely, the senses of hearing, touch, sight, taste and smell, the tongue, the hands, the feet and the organs of procreation and defecation and the five gross elements, viz., ether, air, fire, water and earth. The same subject has been dealt with in Gītā (XIII. 5) as under—

महाभूतान्यहंकारो बुद्धिरव्यक्तमेव च।

इन्द्रियाणि दशैकं च पञ्च चेन्द्रियगोचरा:॥

That is to say, the five gross elements, the ego, the intellect, the Unmanifest or the Primordial Matter, the ten Indriyas and the mind plus the five objects of senses bring the number of categories to twenty-four. These very categories, numbering twenty-four, have been enumerated in the Sāṅkhya and Yoga systems. Of course, there is a slight difference in the order of their evolution because the creation of the universe does not follow the same order every time. The order slightly varies in every case. That is why the processes of evolution delineated in the Vedas, the Bhāgavata and other Purāṇas, the first chapter of the Manusmr̥ti and the Upaniṣads differ from one another in some respect or other. But the fundamental principle is the same, viz., that all material objects have evolved from Prakr̥ti or Matter. Now let us ascertain the order in which the evolution takes place according to the Gītā. For this we shall have to read the following line in a reverse order—

महाभूतान्यहंकारो बुद्धिरव्यक्तमेव च।

इन्द्रियाणि दशैकं च पञ्च चेन्द्रियगोचरा:॥

The Unmanifest (Avyakta) lies at the root of all. From this Unmanifest springs up everything. The Unmanifest stands for the undifferentiated Matter of the energy of God. Moving backwards from this energy of God in the form of the Unmanifest we find that from the Unmanifest came forth the intellect. It is the “Intellect” of the Gītā that has been referred to as the principle of Cosmic Intelligence or Mahattattva. The Vedānta speaks of it as the principle of Cosmic Intelligence, while the Sāṅkhya and Yoga call it “Mahattattva.” This intellect brought forth the ego from which evolved the five Mahābhūtas and so on. These five Mahābhūtas are the same as the five subtle elements and are causal in character because these five as well as the three foregoing ones are the causes of the other evolutes following therefrom. In the Sāṅkhya Philosophy too we are told that of the twenty-four categories, one, viz., Primordial Matter, is merely causal in character, seven are both causes and effects and the remaining sixteen are mere effects. In Gītā this topic has been dealt with as under—

भूमिरापोऽनलो वायु: खं मनो बुद्धिरेव च।

अहंकार इतीयं मे भिन्ना प्रकृतिरष्टधा॥

(VII. 4)

Earth, water, fire, air and ether—these five subtle elements plus mind, intellect and ego make the number eight. Here “mind” has been substituted for “Primordial Matter.” But mind is not the cause of any other category. It is for this reason that the great śaṅkarācārya, in his commentary on the above verse, has interpreted “Manas” not as “mind” but as “ego” and ego as the undifferentiated Māyā. We should take all these eight to be cause since the Lord has spoken of them as such. Therefore, the order of evolution should be understood as follows—

From the undifferentiated Māyā sprang up the intellect, which in its turn brought forth the ego and from the ego proceeded the five subtle elements and the mind. Again, from the five subtle elements evolved the ten Indriyas and thereafter the five sense-objects. There is no difference of opinion about the fact that the five sense-objects and ten Indriyas sprang up from the five subtle elements. Some take the five elements as evolutes of the mind while others believe them as having proceeded from the ego. The Vedas and the Bhāgavata delineate the process of the evolution of the five elements differently. One such order is that from ether sprang up the air, which in its turn brought forth fire; from fire proceeded water and from water appeared the earth. Recognizing all the processes of evolution mentioned above, one arrives at the conclusion that at the time of dissolution the earth is submerged in water, which is dried up by fire; fire in its turn is extinguished by the air, which gets lost in ether. In this way ether alone remains. At the time of final dissolution ether as well as all the other evolutes of the five subtle elements disappear in the ego, wherein the mind also loses itself. The ego gets merged in the intellect (Mahattattva) and the intellect in the Unmanifest Māyā. The Unmanifest Māyā is a potency of God. It persists even during the final dissolution. According to the same process when creation starts anew, it is the Mahattattva or cosmic intellect that appears first of all from Prakr̥ti. From the intellect springs up the cosmic ego, which in its turn brings forth the mind as well as the five Tanmātrās. It is these five Tanmātrās that are spoken of as the subtle elements, and when they are so-called, it is but meet and proper that they should be held to be the causes of the sense-objects. If, on the other hand, these five objects of sense are held to be the same as the five subtle elements, the five gross elements should be regarded as the evolutes of the subtle sense-objects which are the same as the subtle elements. The Yoga and the Sāṅkhya systems hold this view. This has been recognized as the process of evolution of all the categories. Prakr̥ti alone existed at the beginning of creation. At the time of dissolution the gross bodies of all beings disappear in the cosmic subtle nature, which itself is merged in the primordial nature. This primordial nature is the resting-place of all. It is the same as the cosmic causal body. Take for example the case of a cloud, which is composed of a number of molecules. Just as the cloud and each one of its molecules are the same, similar is the case with Prakr̥ti and all that is comprised in it. Of course Prakr̥ti has no molecules or atoms; it is only in order to elucidate the point that the analogy of a cloud has been given to it. Primordial Matter admits of no such divisions as to lead us to assume the existence of molecules in it. In case of ether too, there are some who hold that it does consist of atoms, while others do not hold this view. Ether, of course, can be so divided in this sense that the hundreds of birds that wing the air have each occupied a portion of the ether in the part of the sky which hold their bodies. Similarly, in order to clarify the point it can be held that the portion of Primordial Matter with which an embodied soul had clothed itself is its sheath or resting-place or it may be said to be its casual body. Thus the Jīva abides in Prakr̥ti along with the casual body. If he could get rid of the causal body, he would have realized God. Liberation or God-realization is not attained only because the Jīva remains established in the causal body. It is the causal body that comprises the subtle body and in the subtle body are stored the latencies of all his virtuous and evil deeds. The equilibrium of Prakr̥ti is disturbed only to enable the Jīvas to reap the consequences of their meritorious and evil deeds that are yet to be reaped. The periods of cosmic activity and of the suspension of such activity are as a rule of the same duration. It is after the end of the period of final dissolution and at the beginning of the next round of creation that the equilibrium of Primordial Matter gets disturbed. This disturbance causes a flutter in Prakr̥ti, which gives rise to ten evolutes and thirteen instruments. Says the Lord—

कार्यकरणकर्तृत्वे हेतु: प्रकृतिरुच्यते।

पुरुष: सुखदु:खानां भोक्तृत्वे हेतुरुच्यते॥

(Gītā XIII. 20)

“Prakr̥ti is declared to be responsible for bringing forth the evolutes and the instruments; while the Puruṣa (the individual soul) is said to bring about the experience of joys and sorrows.”

It has already been discussed how Prakr̥ti is responsible for bringing forth the evolutes and the instruments. The evolutes are ten in number—the five elements, viz., ether, air, fire, water and earth, and the five objects of sense-perception, viz., sound, touch, colour, taste and smell. The instruments or media are thirteen—the five organs of action, the five senses of perception, the mind, the intellect and the ego. Thus the ten evolutes and the thirteen instruments make a total of twenty-three. If Prakr̥ti is added to this, the number of the fundamental principles comes to twenty-four. Some recognize the mind, intellect, Citta and ego as the four aspects of the inner sense. The Gītā, however, enumerates only three aspects. The Yoga and Sāṅkhya systems also subscribe to the view of the Gītā. Citta and mind are believed to be one. Prakr̥ti alone is the cause of the other twenty-three categories. It is due to this that Prakr̥ti has been declared as responsible for the evolution of these twenty-three fundamental principles. These twenty-three are its evolutes. Now we have to consider how the individual soul brings about the experience of joys and sorrows.

It is the soul abiding in Prakr̥ti which is the experiencer of joys and sorrows. As a matter of fact it is neither the pure self nor Prakr̥ti that experiences joys and sorrows. Prakr̥ti is not the experiencer because it is insentient nor can the soul be the experiencer because it is pure. The experiencer is the embodied soul which is the union of Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa. Hence the experiencer of joys and sorrows is the Puruṣa (Spirit) identified with Prakr̥ti—पुरुष: प्रकृतिस्थो हि भुङ्‍क्ते प्रकृतिजान् गुणान् (XIII. 21). Here it is the embodied soul which has been referred to under the name of Puruṣa. It is the Jīva abiding in Prakr̥ti which is the experiencer of joys and sorrows and not the pure Self.

Mahar̥ṣi PataÛjali says in his “Aphorisms on Yoga”—हेयं दु:खमनागतम् (Yoga-Sūtras II. 16). “Pain which has yet to come is worth eschewing. ‘Anāgata’ is that which is not yet come. The sorrows that have already come, that is, those which are a thing of the past have not been declared here as worth eschewing, since they have come and gone. The sorrow existing at the present moment will also become a thing of the past the very next moment. That too need not be considered. But future sorrows should be avoided. Now the question arises as to what is the cause of pains not yet come. The reply is—द्रष्टृदृश्ययो: संयोगो हेयहेतु: (Ibid, II. 17). The union or identity of the knowing Self and the insentient Prakr̥ti is the cause of that which is to be avoided—the cause of sorrows and sufferings. Hence it is only the Self abiding in Prakr̥ti to whom joys and sorrows come. Now one should like to know what is the cause of this liaison between matter and Spirit. There must be some cause for that too. The reply to this is—तस्य हेतुरविद्या “Nescience is its cause.” This nescience is got rid of through spiritual wisdom. The dawn of spiritual wisdom eradicates ignorance and with the eradication of ignorance Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa get dissociated. Thus when they are disunited no experiencer of joys and sorrows remains, because the Puruṣa has his object accomplished. The question now arises—when the Puruṣa has his object accomplished, does the world still persist in his eyes or not? In reply to it the sage PataÛjali says—कृतार्थं प्रति नष्टमप्यनष्टं तदन्यसाधारणत्वात् (Yoga. II. 22). In the eyes of the man who accomplished his purpose matter ceases. The creation being intended for the commonality, does exist for them who have not attained liberation. To the realized soul this world is a non-entity.

Thus it is proved that it is the soul abiding in Prakr̥ti who experiences joys and sorrows. How does he experience them? Through ignorance. What is the nature of his ignorance? It consists in identifying oneself with the body and recognizing it as one’s own. A dreaming man identifies himself with the body of his dream or regards it as his own and so on. But when he wakes up, all his dream-world melts away. Then he comes to realize that the dream-world was mere thought, that it was nothing in reality. Nevertheless the objects of the dream appear as real while he is dreaming. Similarly, the world appears as real while one is buried in the sleep of ignorance. From the Advaitic point of view, really speaking, the body as well as the world is imaginary. All this creation is entirely imaginary. According to the Yoga and Sāṅkhya systems it is in a state of constant flux. It is ignorance rooted in the mind which is responsible for the experience of joys and sorrows. According to the Yoga and Sāṅkhya systems, as a matter of fact, the body and mind are not mere products of imagination. They do exist; but they are mutable, are always changing. The Spirit gets liberated when it breaks off with them. According to the Advaita Philosophy there is no such thing as breaking off. The world of matter is entirely dreamlike. It does not actually exist. Now one should like to know what kind of Puruṣa is the experiencer of joy and sorrow. The Lord says—

पुरुष: प्रकृतिस्थो हि भुङ्‍क्ते प्रकृतिजान् गुणान्।

कारणं गुणसङ्गोऽस्य सदसद्योनिजन्मसु॥

(Gītā XIII. 21)

“It is the Puruṣa abiding in Prakr̥ti who senses the objects of the nature of the three Guṇas evolved from Prakr̥ti. And it is contact with these Guṇas that is responsible for the birth of this soul in good and evil wombs.”

Contact here means attachment and union. Sattva, Rajas and Tamas are the three Guṇas. The embodied soul is cast into good or evil birth after death only according to the affinity it cherishes for a particular Guṇas and its contact with the same. He who is fond of Sattvaguṇa ascends to the higher worlds; he who conceives an affinity for Rajoguṇa attains a human or other similar birth—while one who is Tāmasika by nature is born into the nether worlds—into the infernal regions or is born among the worms and insects and other species of the sub-human creation. The higher species are termed as “Sat” while the lower ones are called “Asat.”

All the species lower than the human are “Asat” and those higher than that are “Sat.” Therefore, the man who abides in Sattvaguṇa alone goes up by the bright path and attains God. But he who is characterized by Sattvaguṇa mixed with Rajas travels by the path of smoke, goes to heaven (the abode of gods) and after having tasted the heavenly enjoyments returns to the mortal world. In other words, if the ascending soul cherishes some desire, he returns to this world, but the desireless soul knows no return.

It has been established above that the pure Self is not the experiencer; it is the Puruṣa (embodied Spirit) established in Prakr̥ti who is the experiencer.

Now one should like to know who is the doer of virtuous and sinful acts. If it be urged that Prakr̥ti is the agent, it naturally follows that Prakr̥ti alone should be the experiencer too. If, however, it is maintained that Puruṣa is the doer, then what kind of Puruṣa is the doer and where he is mentioned in the Gītā. Our reply is that the Gītā declares the following five factors as contributory to the accomplishment of an act—

पञ्चैतानि महाबाहो कारणानि निबोध मे।

सांख्ये कृतान्ते प्रोक्तानि सिद्धये सर्वकर्मणाम्॥

(XVIII. 13)

“In the branch of learning known by the name of “Sāṅkhya”, which prescribes means for neutralizing all actions, these five factors have been mentioned as contributory to the accomplishment of all actions, know them from Me, Arjuna.”

अधिष्ठानं तथा कर्ता करणं च पृथग्विधम्।

विविधाश्च पृथक्चेष्टा दैवं चैवात्र पञ्चमम्॥

(XVIII. 14)

“The following are the factors operating towards the accomplishment of actions, viz., the seat of action and the agent, the organs of different kinds and the separate movements of divergent types; and the fifth is Daiva or destiny.”

Here the body is the seat of action, the agent is the embodied Spirit. The five senses of perception, the five organs of action, mind, intellect and ego are the thirteen distinct organs or instruments. The activities of divergent types are the separate movements, and the fifth factor is Daiva. The latencies of good and evil deeds performed in the past constitute what is called “Daiva.” Some people construe Daiva to mean God, while others take it in the sense of destiny. But such an interpretation is not correct. In this way the above five factors have been recognized as contributory to all actions. Work accomplished by these five factors is of two kinds—virtuous and sinful. With regard to this the Lord says—

शरीरवाङ्मनोभिर्यत्कर्म प्रारभते नर:।

न्याय्यं वा विपरीतं वा पञ्चैते तस्य हेतव:॥

(XVIII. 15)

“These five are the contributory causes of whatever action, right or wrong, a man performs with the mind, speech and body.”

Thus, even though the above five factors are contributory to the accomplishment of all actions, he who holds the pure self as responsible therefore betrays his ignorance. This is rank stupidity. In reality the Self is a non-doer. The Lord says in the Gītā—

तत्रैवं सति कर्तारमात्मानं केवलं तु य:।

पश्यत्यकृतबुद्धित्वान्न स पश्यति दुर्मति:॥

(XVIII. 16)

“Notwithstanding this, however, he who, having an impure mind, regards the absolute, taintless Self alone as the doer, that man of perverse understanding does not view aright,”

He is undoubtedly an ignoramous and his view is not correct, because the pure Self is not the doer. All actions, whether good or bad, are accomplished by the above-mentioned five-factors. That is to say these five factors alone are contributory to the accomplishment of all actions. All activities proceed from them.

By thus realizing the true character of Prakr̥ti and Puruṣa ignorance is got rid of by the grace of God and once a man is assured of the fact that the pure Self is neither a doer nor the non-experiencer, he is rid of all shackles of Karma and realizes God.

 

Dualistic and Non-Dualistic Worship in the Upaniṣads

ॐ पूर्णमद: पूर्णमिदं पूर्णात् पूर्णमुदच्यते।

पूर्णस्य पूर्णमादाय पूर्णमेवावशिष्यते॥

(Br̥hadāraṇyaka. V.i.1)

That Brahman; this Brahman (universe) is also full. The full comes from the full (i.e., the latter effect comes from the former—the cause). If you accept the fullness of the full (effect) with the knowledge that this also is full then that is left behind is also full.

The Vedas constitute the root of the Hindu scriptures. They are the repositories of infinite Wisdom. The section of the Vedas, dealing with Knowledge or Wisdom, is the crown, or culmination of the Vedas. It is this portion which is famous throughout the world as the Upaniṣad or Vedānta. The characteristics of Brahma have been truly described in the Upaniṣads, and along with that they have laid down different practices for the realization of Brahma, to suit the taste and spiritual aptitude of different types or classes of Sādhakas. Symbolical worship, as described in them, has been recommended to be practised in both the ways—viz., (1) in one centre, and (2) universally, in all centres. It has been brought out that this worship may be pursued for either the enjoyments of this world, e.g., possession of wife, progeny, wealth, food, cattle, etc., or that of the other world, e.g., residence in the celestial garden amidst celestial damsels and drinking of the ambrosia, etc., and, together with this, many forms of practices have been laid down by them for the realization of God as well. In the present article, practices for the attainment of enjoyments of this world and the next will be left out. It will only deal briefly with the forms of worship for the realization of God, which have been elaborately described in the Upaniṣads.

Various practices of God-realization have been laid down by the Upaniṣads through various lines of reasoning and thought in the form of illustrations, anecdotes, metaphors, hints, and prescribed and prohibitory rules of conduct. Pursuing spiritual life following any of these practices, man can realize God. All these practices may be generally classified under two heads—(1) dualistic worship, and (2) non-dualistic worship. Dualistic worship, again, are of two kinds. One is that in which dualism is both the means and the end; and the other is that in which dualism is only the means, but the end is non-dual. We shall firstly deal with the dualistic form of worship.

DUALISTIC WORSHIP

In dualistic worship, three substances are considered to be eternal—(1) Māyā (Prakr̥ti); (2) Jīva (the individual soul) (3) the Lord of Māyā, God. Descriptions of these three substances occur in various places of the Upaniṣads. Prakr̥ti is inert, and its product, the objective world, is transient, perishable and subject to change. The individual soul and God—both are eternal, sentient and embodiment of Bliss; but the Jīva possesses finite knowledge, whereas God is omniscient; the Jīva is powerless, whereas God is almighty; the Jīva is a part and God is the Whole; the Jīva is the enjoyer, and God is the Witness; the Jīva is the worshipper, and God, the object of worship. That very God, manifesting Himself on earth from time to time, delivers His message to Jīva for the latter’s spiritual good.

The Kenopaniṣad records a story connected with this very subject. Once upon a time, through the grace and power of God, the celestials in heaven attained victory over the demons. But due to ignorance and pride, they began to believe that the victory had been attained by their own power. In order to remove this pride of theirs born of ignorance, and thus do them good, the Lord manifested Himself before those celestials as a Yakṣa, possessed of attributes and form. Curious to know who the Yakṣa was, Indra and the other celestial lords at first deputed Agni, the Fire-god, as their messenger. The Yakṣa asked Agni, “Who are you? What are your powers?” Agni replied, “I am the god of fire, if I want I can reduce the entire universe to ashes.” Placing a blade of grass before him, the Yakṣa invited Agni to burn it; but the latter failed to do so. Thereupon returning to the celestials, Agni reported that he had failed to know who the Yakṣa was. The celestials, then, sent the Wind-god. Him also the Yakṣa asked, “Who are you, and what are your powers?” He replied, “I am Wind, if I want I can blow away the entire universe.” The Yakṣa placed a blade of grass before him as well, and invited him to blow it. But the Wind-god failed to do so, and returning to the gods reported that he had failed to know who the Yakṣa was. Lastly, went Indra himself, the Chief of gods, but the moment he approached the Yakṣa, the latter disappeared from view. Then, finding Goddess Umā in that part of the sky, Indra questioned Her about the Yakṣa. She replied, “He was Brahma himself; it was the victory of this Brahma which you regarded as your own victory.” This instruction of the Goddess enlightened Indra about Brahma. Thereafter, the gods of Fire and Wind also recognized Brahma. Because they were the first to recognize Brahma, the three gods, viz., Indra, Agni and Vāyu, are regarded as superior to the other gods.

The story proves that whatever power, intelligence and greatness are exhibited by a living being are derived from God. In the Gītā also the Lord declared—

यद्यद्विभूतिमत्सत्त्वं श्रीमदूर्जितमेव वा।

तत्तदेवावगच्छ त्वं मम तेजोंऽशसम्भवम्॥

(X. 41)

“Every such creature as is glorious, brilliant or powerful, know that to be a manifestation of a spark of my effulgence.”

Thus the Upaniṣads describe the dualistic form of worship of God, now possessed of form, now formless, now possessed of attributes, and now in His absolute aspect. They also show that the Sādhaka derives success according to the motive with which he adores his object of worship. In the Kaṭhopaniṣad, describing the dualistic worship of Praṇava, which is both attributeless and possessed of attributes, the god of Death says to Naciketā—

एतद्‍ध्येवाक्षरं ब्रह्म एतद्‍ध्येवाक्षरं परम्।

एतद्‍ध्येवाक्षरं ज्ञात्वा यो यदिच्छति तस्य तत्॥

एतदालम्बनं श्रेष्ठमेतदालम्बनं परम्।

एतदालम्बनं ज्ञात्वा ब्रह्मलोके महीयते॥

(I. ii. 16-17)

“This Akṣaraṁ (Aum) is the very same as Brahma; this Aum means the supreme Brahma. Knowing this Aum, one attains whatever one may seek. This support is the best, it is the supreme refuge. Knowing this support well, the Sādhaka attains glory in Brahmaloka—the abode of Brahma.”

Therefore, men who seek their spiritual welfare should worship God only for the sake of God-realization, crossing for all time the ocean of misery in the form of existence in this world, and not for obtaining worldly objects. Within this body of ours, God is ever present in every heart in His formless aspect, but because of their ignorance about Him, people go through all their misery and sufferings. He who worships God comes to know Him, and hence crossing all misery and grief, he realizes God. In the Muṇḍakopaniṣad also it is said—

द्वा सुपर्णा सयुजा सखाया

समानं वृक्षं परिषस्वजाते।

तयोरन्य: पिप्पलं स्वाद्वत्त्य-

नश्नन्नन्यो अभिचाकशीति॥

समाने वृक्षे पुरुषो निमग्नो-

ऽनीशया शोचति मुह्यमान:।

जुष्टं यदा पश्यत्यन्यमीश-

मस्य महिमानमिति वीतशोक:॥

यदा पश्य: पश्यते रुक्मवर्णं

कर्तारमीशं पुरुषं ब्रह्मयोनिम्।

तदा विद्वान् पुण्यपापे विधूय

निरञ्जन: परम: साम्यमुपैति॥

(III. i. 1—3)

“Two birds (the individual soul and God), always living together, and friendly towards each other, dwell upon one and the same tree (the body). One of them enjoys the fruit of that tree (in the shape of Karma); but the other without partaking of that fruit only looks on as a witness. Dwelling on the same tree, the individual soul is merged in the deep attachment of the body, and feeling lack of power, and being deluded, grieves. But when through God’s causeless grace, it perceives God and His glory—God, who is ever worshipped by devotees and is different from it—then its grief ceases. And when this beholder (the individual soul) beholds the Supreme Person, the embodiment of divine effulgence, the ruler of all, the creator of even Brahmā, and the author of the world. Then, rising above virtue and vice, and attaining spotless purity, he as a devotee, who is also the possessor of Knowledge, attains the highest identity.”

God, who is both possessed of attributes and attributeless, though devoid of senses, is yet the knower of object of senses. Though the creator and maintainer of all, He is yet a non-doer. Praises and prayers should be offered to that formless God within the heart, who is omniscient, all-pervading, gracious without cause and the supreme lover. Taking refuge in that best object of worship, man rises above all forms of misery, suffering, sin and modification, and attains liberation in the form of supreme peace and the supreme state. Therefore, one should take refuge in God, for the sake of God-realization, through the knowledge of God, who is the creator, sustainer and destroyer of all, almighty, the substratum of all, all-pervading, subtler than the subtlest, greater than the greatest, and the universal friend.

The śvetāśvatara Upaniṣad contains a detailed description of the dualistic worship of God. Some Mantras taken from that Upaniṣad are being quoted below—

सर्वेन्द्रियगुणाभासं सर्वेन्द्रियविवर्जितम्।

सर्वस्य प्रभुमीशानं सर्वस्य शरणं बृहत्॥

(III. 17)

“He, who, though devoid of all the senses, knows all sense-objects, who is the lord of all, ruler of all, and the greatest support of all, should be resorted to.”

अणोरणीयान्महतो महीया-

नात्मा गुहायां निहितोऽस्य जन्तो:।

तमक्रतुं पश्यति वीतशोको

धातु: प्रसादान्महिमानमीशम्॥

(III. 20)

“Subtler than what is subtle, and greater than what is great, God dwells in the cavity of the heart of the Jīva. By the grace of the creator of all, he who sees that Lord, devoid of thought and His glory, becomes free from grief, and realizes Him; the embodiment of bliss.”

Again, it says—

मायां तु प्रकृतिं विद्यान्मायिनं तु महेश्वरम्।

तस्यावयवभूतैस्तु व्याप्तं सर्वमिदं जगत्॥

यो योनिं योनिमधितिष्ठत्येको

यस्मिन्निदं स च विचैति सर्वम्।

तमीशानं वरदं देवमीड्यं

निचाय्येमां शान्तिमत्यन्तमेति॥

(IV. 10-11)

“Prakr̥ti should be known as Māyā, and Maheśwara is the ruler of Māyā; the whole world is pervaded by cause and effect which are but parts of Māyā constituting the power of God. He who alone is the Lord of all wombs, and in whom the entire creation is dissolved at the time of destruction, and from whom, at the time of creation, all beings, in different forms, manifest themselves, knowing in truth that all-governing, benign, praiseworthy supreme Lord, man obtains peace in the form of liberation.”

सूक्ष्मातिसूक्ष्मं कलिलस्य मध्ये

विश्वस्य स्रष्टारमनेकरूपम्।

विश्वस्यैकं परिवेष्टितारं

ज्ञात्वा शिवं शान्तिमत्यन्तमेति॥

(IV. 14)
