“He who is subtler than what is subtle, residing within the secret cavity of the heart, the creator of the universe, the undertaker of various forms, the circumvallator of the universe, knowing Him the singular, all-blessed, supreme Lord, man attains everlasting peace.”

एको देव: सर्वभूतेषु गूढ:

सर्वव्यापी सर्वभूतान्तरात्मा।

कर्माध्यक्ष: सर्वभूताधिवास:

साक्षी चेता केवलो निर्गुणश्च॥

एको वशी निष्क्रियाणां बहूना-

मेकं बीजं बहुधा य: करोति।

तमात्मस्थं येऽनुपश्यन्ति धीरा-

स्तेषां सुखं शाश्वतं नेतरेषाम्॥

(VI. 11-12)

“The one God, who exists concealed in all beings, All-pervading, the inner soul of all, the ruler of all actions, the repository of all, the witness, the principle of life itself, ever pure and above the Guṇas, and who singly rules the many Jīvas, who are in reality, inactive and transforms into many forms the one seed of Prakr̥ti, the wise who ever perceives that God as residing within the heart, obtains everlasting Bliss, not others.”

यो ब्रह्माणं विदधाति पूर्वं

यो वै वेदांश्च प्रहिणोति तस्मै।

तं ह देवमात्मबुद्धिप्रकाशं

मुमुक्षुर्वै शरणमहं प्रपद्ये॥

(VI. 18)

“He who created Brahmā at the commencement of creation, and gave him the knowledge of all the Vedas, I, the seeker of liberation, take refuge in that Lord, the revealer of the intellect that gives knowledge of God”.

Thus, far we have described dualistic form of worship in which dualism is both the means and the end. Now, we shall deal with worship in which dualism is only the means, but the fruit or end is non-dual.

According to the dualistic form of worship, four types of liberation have been described in the scriptures—(1) Sālokya, (2) Sāmīpya, (3) Sārūpya and (4) Sāyujya. In the first three of these, dualism is both the means and the end; but in the last, the Sāyujya form of liberation, dualism is maintained during the period of practice, but in the state which represents the goal or fruit, there is no dualism. Reaching the supreme abode of God, when one is privileged to reside there, it is called the “Sālokya” form of liberation. Those who worship God cultivating the parental and similar other sentiments, attain the “Sālokya”form of liberation. He, who, reaching the supreme abode of God enjoys the privilege of staying near God, is said to have attained the “Sāmīpya” form of liberation. Sādhakas worshipping God through the cultivation of the servant-sentiment, or the lover sentiment, attain this “Sāmīpya” form of liberation. Reaching the supreme abode of God, when one lives there assuming God’s own form, it is called the “Sārūpya” form of liberation. Sādhakas who worship God cultivating the sentiment of a friend attain this “Sārūpya” form of liberation. These devotees of God come to possess all the qualities of God, except the special divine power of creation, protection and maintenance of the universe. Total absorption in and attainment of the non-dual state is called the “Sāyujya” form of liberation. Sādhakas who worship God cultivating the quiet-sentiment (through devotion mixed with Knowledge), attain this “Sāyujya” form of liberation, and those who worship God through the cultivation of enmity, hatred and fear, also, attain the “Sāyujya” form of liberation. Just as the various waters of the different rivers, renouncing their separate names and forms, falling into ocean, become the ocean itself, even so such a Sādhaka, merging himself in God, becomes the same as God Himself. This is proved by various statements in the Upaniṣads as well as in the other scriptures. In the Kaṭhopaniṣad the God of Death says to Naciketā—

यथोदकं शुद्धे शुद्धमासिक्तं तादृगेव भवति।

एवं मुनेर्विजानत आत्मा भवति गौतम॥

(II. 1. 15)

“Just as pure rain-water, dropped by the clouds on a sheet of pure water, becomes alike, even so, O Naciketā, the scion of Gautama, the soul of the Muni, who knows that God alone is everything, realizes God, that is, getting absorbed in God, becomes alike,”

The Muṇḍakopaniṣad also, says—

स वेदैतत् परमं ब्रह्म धाम

यत्र विश्वं निहितं भाति शुभ्रम्।

उपासते पुरुषं ये ह्यकामा-

स्ते शुक्रमेतदतिवर्तन्ति धीरा:॥

(III. ii. 1)

“That Sādhaka, possessed of the disinterested spirit, knows this holy, effulgent, abode of Brahma, on which the whole world appears as placed. The wise Sādhaka, free from desires, who adores the Supreme Being, will not take birth again in this world.”

यथा नद्य: स्यन्दमाना: समुद्रे-

ऽस्तं गच्छन्ति नामरूपे विहाय।

तथा विद्वान्नामरूपाद्विमुक्त:

परात्परं पुरुषमुपैति दिव्यम्॥

स यो ह वै तत्परमं ब्रह्म वेद ब्रह्मैव भवति नास्याब्रह्मवित्कुले भवति। तरति शोकं तरति पाप्मानं गुहाग्रन्थिभ्यो विमुक्तोऽमृतो भवति।

(III. ii. 8-9)

“Just as the flowing rivers, losing name and form, get absorbed by the sea, even so the wise, liberated from name and form, realizes the divine Supreme Being, who is greater than the great. Whoever knows the Supreme Brahma, becomes the same as Brahma. In his family there will be none ignorant of Brahma; he overcomes grief, he overcomes sin; liberated from the knots of the heart, he becomes immortal. That is, freed from birth and death, he becomes Brahma.”

The Sādhaka, who carries on spiritual practice, believing Māyā (Prakr̥ti), the individual soul and God as three different entities, and at the same time holds that Prakr̥ti and God are identical, inasmuch as energy and its possessor are identical, and the individual soul though different, yet being a part of God is also identical with God; hence God, though separate from both Prakr̥ti and Jīva, is identical with them—he, though following a spiritual discipline based on dualism, in the end realizes God as identical with him. This is corroborated by various statements in the Upaniṣads and other scriptures. For instance—

ज्ञाज्ञौ द्वावजावीशनीशा-

वजा ह्येका भोक्तृभोग्यार्थयुक्ता।

अनन्तश्चात्मा विश्वरूपो ह्यकर्ता

त्रयं यदा विन्दते ब्रह्ममेतत्॥

क्षरं प्रधानममृताक्षरं हर:

क्षरात्मानावीशते देव एक:।

तस्याभिध्यानाद्योजनात्तत्त्वभावाद्-

भूयश्चान्ते विश्वमायानिवृत्ति:॥

(śwetāśwatara I. 9-10)

“The Omniscient and the ignorant, the Omnipotent and the powerless—these two, God and the individual soul, are unborn; and for the enjoyer Jīva, unborn Prakr̥ti, composed of objects of enjoyment, is the third source of power; (among these, God is different from the other two) for He is infinite, embracing every form in the world without doership. When a person knows Brahma as thus threefold, viz., God, individual soul and Prakr̥ti—he attains liberation from bondage. The first, Prakr̥ti, is perishable; its enjoyer, the individual soul, is immortal and imperishable. God rules both perishable Prakr̥ti and the individual soul—knowing this, when one constantly meditates on God, and unites the mind again and again with Him, and gets absorbed in God, he at last, realizes God, when Māyā totally ceases to exist for him.”

Showing thus the two processes of dualistic worship as laid down by the Upaniṣads, we shall next proceed to discuss non-dualistic worship according to the Upaniṣads.

NON-DUALISTIC WORSHIP

There are four principal forms of non-dualistic worship. The first two of them have “That” as their object, and with the remaining two “Thou” is the goal or object, as briefly described below—

1. Whatever appears to the senses in this animate and inanimate world is Brahma. There is nothing, which is different from God, who is solidified Existence, Knowledge and Bliss. Thus the Sādhaka should worship.

2. That God, who is attributeless, formless, actionless and changeless is wholly beyond the transient, perishable, material, objective world, or Māyā. Thus he should worship.

3. The entire material and sentient, movable and immovable, animate and inanimate world is Brahma, and that Brahma am I. Hence all these are my forms—thus the worship should be practised.

4. God, the Supreme Brahma, who is beyond the perishable, transient and illusory objective world, and is formless, changeless, attributeless, eternal Knowledge and Bliss solidified, is verily my tmā or soul, that is, the same as my Self—thus the worship should be practised.

For a better comprehension of these practices, they are being discussed below in some detail, with authoritative references from the Upaniṣads.

(1) In the beginning of creation; Brahma alone, Existence, Knowledge and Bliss solidified, existed. He desired, “Let Me become many, let Me appear” (सोऽकामयत। बहु स्यां प्रजायेयेति. Taittirīya II vi. 1.)—Thus the one Brahma assumed many forms. Hence whatever there is in the world, material and sentient, movable and immovable, is God. The śruti says—

ब्रह्मैवेदममृतं पुरस्ताद्‍ब्रह्म

पश्चाद्‍ब्रह्म दक्षिणतश्चोत्तरेण।

अधश्चोर्ध्वं च प्रसृतं ब्रह्मै-

वेदं विश्वमिदं वरिष्ठम्॥

(Muṇḍ. II. ii. 11)

“The immortal Brahma is before, Brahma is behind, Brahma is to the right and Brahma is to the left, below and above is also the all-pervading Brahma, the entire world is nothing but the highest Brahma.”

संप्राप्यैनमृषयो ज्ञानतृप्ता:

कृतात्मानो वीतरागा: प्रशान्ता:।

ते सर्वगं सर्वत: प्राप्य धीरा

युक्तात्मान: सर्वमेवाविशन्ति॥

(Muṇḍ. III. ii. 5)

“The ¤Rṣis, who are wholly devoid of attachment and pure of heart, realizing God get satisfied with knowledge and attain supreme peace. These men of wisdom, who unite themselves with God, finding all-pervading God everywhere, enter Him (the all-formed God) wholly.”

सर्व ॸ ह्येतद् ब्रह्मायमात्मा ब्रह्म सोऽयमात्मा चतुष्पात्।

(Māṇḍūkya 2)

“For all this (the entire world) is Brahma; and this the soul, possessed of four feet, is also Brahma.”

सर्वं खल्विदं ब्रह्म तज्जलानिति शान्त उपासीत।

(Chāndogya. III. xiv. 1)

“All this verily is Brahma; from Brahma does it proceed, Brahma sustains it, and in Brahma does it merge. Comprehending this, one should adore it with a quiet mind.”

(2) It has been shown that the entire material and sentient, movable and immovable, animate and inanimate world, which is the object indicated by the word “That”, is Brahma. Now, the object of the same word “That”, Brahma, in its attributeless aspect, will be described. That attributeless, formless, actionless, and changeless Brahma is wholly beyond transient, perishable, material and objective Māyā. Whatever object appears to the senses is born of ignorance. In reality, there is nothing beyond Brahma, which is Knowledge and Bliss solidified, infinite and free from attributes. The Sādhaka who comes to experience this gets freed from birth and death, and realizes Brahma, the solidified form of infinite Knowledge and Bliss. This is confirmed by the scriptures and by the Upaniṣads at many places.

In the Kaṭhopaniṣad, while describing the characteristics of Supreme Brahma, the Lord of Death said—

अशब्दमस्पर्शमरूपमव्ययं

तथारसं नित्यमगन्धवच्च यत्।

अनाद्यनन्तं महत: परं ध्रुवं

निचाय्य तन्मृत्युमुखात्प्रमुच्यते॥

(I. iii. 15)

“Knowing Him, who is without sound, without touch, without form, without taste and without smell, and who is imperishable, eternal, without beginning, infinite, beyond Mahat-Tattva and wholly Truth itself, man escapes for all time from the jaws of death.”

मनसैवेदमाप्तव्यं नेह नानास्ति किंचन।

मृत्यो: स मृत्युं गच्छति य इह नानेव पश्यति॥

(II. i. 11)

“God is realized only through the pure mind. Except God there is nothing else in the world. Therefore, he who beholds something else in this world proceeds from death to death, that is he goes through the processes of birth and death, again and again.”

In the Muṇḍakopaniṣad also it is said—

न चक्षुषा गृह्यते नापि वाचा

नान्यैर्देवैस्तपसा कर्मणा वा।

ज्ञानप्रसादेन विशुद्धसत्त्व-

स्ततस्तु तं पश्यते निष्कलं ध्यायमान:॥

(III. i. 8)

“That attributeless, formless Supreme Brahma, or God, is neither apprehended by the eye, nor by speech, nor by the other senses, nor by askesis, nor by devotional rites. He is beheld only by the Sādhaka, possessed of a pure heart, performing constant meditation through that pure heart, by his intellect purified by the light of Knowledge.”

The Taittirīyopaṇiṣad also says—

ब्रह्मविदाप्नोति परम्। तदेषाभ्युक्ता। सत्यं ज्ञानमनन्तं ब्रह्म।

(II. i. 1)

“The knower of Brahma attains Supreme Brahma. With reference to this (Knowledge), the following śruti has been uttered—“Brahma is Truth, Knowledge and Infinity”.

(3) Describing thus the process of worship of the word “That”, we shall now take up the process of worship of the word “Thou”. Whatever material and sentient, movable and immovable objects appear in the world, they are all Brahma, and who Brahma is, that I am. Hence man should see the Self in all beings, and all beings in the Self. That is to say, the Sādhaka who cultivates the habit of feeling, that whatever exists is identical with the Self, transcending grief and delusion, attains Brahma, which is Knowledge and Bliss solidified.

This is confirmed by the scriptures and supported by various Mantras of the Upaniṣads. The Gītā says—

सर्वभूतस्थमात्मानं सर्वभूतानि चात्मनि।

ईक्षते योगयुक्तात्मा सर्वत्र समदर्शन:॥

(VI. 29)

“The Yogī, who is united in identity with the All-pervading, infinite Consciousness, and looks on all with an equal eye, sees the Self present in all beings, and all beings as imaginary objects in the Self.”

The ºśāvāsyopaniṣad says—

यस्तु सर्वाणि भूतान्यात्मन्येवानुपश्यति।

सर्वभूतेषु चात्मानं ततो न विजुगुप्सते॥

यस्मिन् सर्वाणि भूतान्यात्मैवाभूद्विजानत:।

तत्र को मोह: क: शोक एकत्वमनुपश्यत:॥

(6-7)

“Whoever beholds all beings in the Self, and the Self in all beings, in other words, regards all beings as the Self, does no more look down on any creature. Whom will he hate, and how?”

“Similarly, to the saint who knows the secret of the Self, everything appears as Self and that beholder of the unity of the soul, suffers neither from delusion, nor from grief. That is to say, modifications like grief and delusion, etc., totally disappear from him who sees Brahma alone, the embodiment of Knowledge and Bliss, pervading all beings.”

The secret of this has been explained by means of a story in the Chāndogya-Upaniṣad. The grandson of Aruṇa, and Uddālaka’s son, śwetaketu, had gone to his teacher in his twelfth year for the acquisition of Knowledge, and on the completion of his study, returned home when he was in his twenty-fourth year. Regarding himself as a man of intellect, and a good orator, he entered home devoid of any humility, and out of intellectual pride did not bow even to his father. Upon this, his father asked him.

श्वेतकेतो यन्नु सोम्येदं महामना अनूचानमानी स्तब्धोऽस्युत तमादेशमप्राक्ष्य:। येनाश्रुतॸश्रुतं भवत्यमतं मतमविज्ञातं विज्ञातमिति।

(VI. i. 2-3)

“O śwetaketu! O youngman Since you are, child, so confident of your knowledge, and so proud, have you enquired of your teacher about that subject which makes the unheard as heard, the unthought as already thought, that is, the unconsidered subject as good as considered, and the unknown known.”

śwetaketu replied, “Lord! What is that subject?”

Then, Uddālaka said—

यथा सोम्यैकेन मृत्पिण्डेन सर्वं मृन्मयं विज्ञातॸस्याद्वाचारम्भणं विकारो नामधेयं मृत्तिकेत्येव सत्यम्।

(Chāndogya VI. i. 4)

“Youngman! Even as from the Knowledge of a single clod of earth, one obtains knowledge of all earthen objects and comes to know that the various modifications are only different words and names, and the truth is only the earth—

यथा सोम्यैकेन लोहमणिना सर्वं लोहमयं विज्ञातॸस्याद्वाचारम्भणं विकारो नामधेयं लोहमित्येव सत्यम्।

(Chāndogya VI. i. 5)

“Youngman, even as the knowledge of a single lump of gold brings the knowledge of all articles of gold, and the various modifications are nothing but different words and names, and the truth is gold alone—

यथा सोम्यैकेन नखनिकृन्तनेन सर्वं कार्ष्णायसं विज्ञातॸस्या द्वाचारम्भणं विकारो नामधेयं कृष्णायसमित्येव सत्यमेवॸसोम्य स आदेशो भवतीति।

(Chāndogya VI. i. 6)

“Youngman, just as the knowledge of a nail-cutter brings the knowledge of all things made of iron, and the modifications of that substance differ only in name and words, but the real truth is iron alone; even so, O child is that subject.”

न वै नूनं भगवन्तस्त एतदवेदिषुर्यद्‍ध्येतदवेदिष्यन्। कथं मे नावक्ष्यन्निति भगवाॸस्त्वेव मे तद्‍ब्रवीत्विति तथा सोम्येति होवाच।

(Ibid. VI. i. 7)

“Verily, my revered teacher did not know that subject. Had he known it, why should he have omitted to impart it to me ? Lord, please impart the knowledge to me.” There-upon, the father said, “Be it so, child.”

सदेव सोम्येदमग्र आसीदेकमेवाद्वितीयम्।

(Ibid. VI. ii. 1)

“Child, in the beginning there was “Sat” alone, without a second.”

Thereupon, śwetaketu said, “Father, please explain this in more detail.” Uddālaka, the son of Aruṇi, replied—“Child, just as when the curd is churned, its fine particles forming butter, come to the surface; even so, when food is consumed, its fine particles, form the mind. The fine particles of water form the Prāṇa (life-breath), and the fine particles of fire, butter, oil form speech. In reality, the mind, breath and speech, and their cause food, etc., being cause and effect, are in their ultimate analysis nothing but Sat. For Sat is the primordial cause of everything, it is the foundation or supreme support. All the various forms, which are the products of Sat, are nothing but mere different words and names. This Sat is minuter than the atom and is the soul of everything. O śwetaketu, thou art That—Tattvamasi.”

śwetaketu said—“Lord, explain this once again to me.” The father, Aruṇi replied, “All right, bring me a fruit of the Indian Banyan tree; then I shall explain the subject further to you.” śwetaketu brought the fruit as desired. The father said, “Break it, and see what is there in it.” Breaking the fruit, śwetaketu replied “Lord, there are some very small seeds in it.” “Break one of them”, said ¤Rṣi Uddālaka. Breaking that small seed; śwetaketu reported, “Nothing can be perceived within it.” Then, Aruṇi said, “Child, you do not perceive the subtle truth within the small seed, from this extremely subtle truth, the huge tree comes into being. Just as the minute seed is the cause of the mighty tree, even so the subtle Sat is the cause and substratum of the external universe. O child, I say the truth, have faith in my words. This subtle substance is the soul; it is Sat, and it is the Universal Soul. O śwetaketu, thou art That—तत्त्वमसि.”

(VI. xii. 3)

Thus, Uddālaka explained the truth in detail through various examples and arguments. In this article, we have given only a brief summary of the same. For a complete study of these arguments, the reader should look up the ChāndogyaUpaniṣad.

The Br̥hadāraṇyakaUpaniṣad discusses the same subject in the following words—

ब्रह्म वा इदमग्र आसीत्तदात्मानमेवावेत्। अहं ब्रह्मास्मीति। तस्मात्तत्सर्वमभवत्तद्यो यो देवानां प्रत्यबुध्यत स एव तदभवत् तथर्षीणां तथा मनुष्याणां तद्धैतत्पश्यन्नृषिर्वामदेव: प्रतिपेदेऽहं मनुरभव सूर्यश्चेति। तदिदमप्येतर्हि य एवं वेदाहं ब्रह्मास्मीति स इदॸसर्वं भवति तस्य ह न देवाश्च नाभूत्या ईशते। आत्मा ह्येषाॸस भवति।

(I. iv. 10)

“In the beginning, there was only Brahma; He reliaged Himself that He was verily Brahma. Therefore, He became all. Whosoever among the gods comprehended Him, became the same as Brahma. Even so, whosoever among the ¤Rṣis and men comprehended Him became Brahma. Seeing Him as the Self, ¤Rṣi Vāmadeva realized—“I became Manu, I am the Sun.” Whosoever knows Brahma even now as “I am Brahma” becomes this all. Even the gods fail to vanquish him; for he becomes the soul of them all.”

In order to explain this secret truth, we find another story in the Br̥hadāraṇyakaUpaniṣad. Sage YājÛavalkya had two wives—Maitreyī and Kātyāyanī. At the time of taking to the fourth order of Saṁnyāsa, sage YājÛavalkya said to Maitreyī—“Behold, I propose to raise myself from the present order of a householder’s life and enter the Saṁnyāsa, order; therefore, it is proper for me to divide my property between you and Kātyāyanī”. Maitreyī replied, “O venerable one, if the whole of this world with all its wealth were mine, could I become immortal thereby?” YājÛavalkya said, “No, your life will become just like the life of other wealthy men of the world. Wealth cannot be expected to bring immortality”. Maitreyī replied, “Of what use would that wealth be to me, if it does not bring immortality? O venerable one, please tell me the means of obtaining immortality.” Hearing this, YājÛavalkya said, “Maitreyī, you are, indeed, blessed; you were dear to me before, and now what you say is also dear to me. Come, I will explain the subject to you; endeavour to comprehend what I say.”

Then, YājÛavalkya continued—

‘न वा अरे सर्वस्य कामाय सर्वं प्रियं भवत्यात्मनस्तु कामाय सर्वं प्रियं भवति। आत्मा वा अरे द्रष्टव्य: श्रोतव्यो मन्तव्यो निदिध्यासितव्यो मैत्रेय्यात्मनो वा अरे दर्शनेन श्रवणेन मत्या विज्ञानेनेदॸसर्व विदितम्।

(II. iv. 5)

“Behold, Maitreyī, not for the sake of the universe, the universe is dear, but for the sake of the Self, it is dear. This Self (tmā) is verily to be seen, heard, reflected and meditated upon. O Maitreyī, by seeing, hearing, meditating on and knowing the Self, all this (universe) is, comprehended.”

Again,—

‘इदं ब्रह्मेदं क्षत्रमिमे लोका इमे देवा इमानि भूतानीदॸसर्वं यदयमात्मा।’

(II. iv. 5)

“O Maitreyī, the Brāhmaṇa, the Kṣatriya, the worlds, these gods, these elements, and whatever else there is in the universe, are the same as the Self.”

And—

‘यत्र हि द्वैतमिव भवति तदितर इतरं जिघ्रति तदितर इतरं पश्यति तदितर इतॸशृणोति तदितर इतरमभिवदति तदितर इतरं मनुते तदितर इतरं विजानाति यत्र वा अस्य सर्वमात्मैवाभूत्तत्केन कं जिघ्रेत् तत्केन कं पश्येत्तत्केन कॸशृणुयात्तत्केन कं कमभिवदेत्तत्केन कं मन्वीत तत्केन कं विजानीयात्। येनेदॸसर्वं विजानाति तं केन विजानीयाद् विज्ञातारमरे केन विजानीयादिति।’

(II. iv. 14)

“Where (in the state of ignorance) there is duality, as it were there one sees another, smells another, hears another, salutes another, reflects on another and knows another. But to one for whom everything is tmā, who will smell whom by what, see whom by what, hear whom by what, salute whom by what, meditate on whom by what, and know whom by what? How should he know him by whom he knows this all? O Maitreyī, how should he know the knower?”

Thus, the subject has been discussed in detail in Chapters II and IV of the Br̥hadāraṇyakaUpaniṣad, a short summary of which has been given above in this article.

(4) God, the Supreme Brahma, who is beyond the perishable, transient, illusory-objective world, and is formless, changeless, attributeless, eternal, Knowledge and Bliss solidified, is my soul or tmā, that is, the same as my Self; knowing thus the identity between himself and God, man realizes God. The śruti says—

योऽकामो निष्काम आप्तकाम आत्मकामो न तस्य प्राणा उत्क्रामन्ति ब्रह्मैव सन् ब्रह्माप्येति।

(Br̥hadāraṇyaka IV. iv. 6)

“He who does not desire, has no desires, is beyond desires, and whose desire is centred in the soul, where Great do not depart (from the body). Becoming Brahma, he attains Brahma.”

In order to explain the secret of this truth, there appears a story in the Br̥hadāraṇyakaUpaniṣad. Janaka, the King of the Videhas, once performed a sacrifice in which large gifts were given to Brāhmaṇas. In that connection, many Brāhmaṇas from the Kuru and PāÛcāla countries assembled there. Desiring to know who among those Brāhmaṇas was the best enlightened, he brought ten thousand cows from his stable, the horns of each of whom were ornamented with ten Padas of gold, and addressing the Brāhmaṇas, he said—“O venerable ones, whoever among you is the best knower of Brahma, may take away these cows”. The Brāhmaṇas heard this announcement of the king but none ventured to come forward. Then, YājÛavalkya ordered his disciples to drive away the cows. When the disciples of YājÛavalkya started doing so, the Brāhmaṇas became angry, and Janaka’s Hotā Aśvala enquired, “Are you the only knower of Brahma among us?” YājÛavalkya replied, “I bow to him Who is the knower of Brahma; I am desirous of only getting the cows.” Hearing this, Aśvala, rtabhāga and Bhujyu put many questions to him one after another, and YājÛavalkya answered them all to their great satisfaction.

Then, Uṣasta, the son of Cakra, asked, “YājÛavalkya, do explain to me that Brahma, who is directly realizable, the soul, which is within every soul.” YājÛavalkya replied—

एष त आत्मा सर्वान्तर: कतमो याज्ञवल्क्य सर्वान्तरो य: प्राणेन प्राणिति स त आत्मा सर्वान्तरो योऽपानेनापानिति स त आत्मा सर्वान्तरो यो व्यानेन व्यानीति स त आत्मा सर्वान्तरो य उदानेनोदानिति स त आत्मा सर्वान्तर एष त आत्मा सर्वान्तर:।

(III. iv. 1)

“It is thy soul, which is within everyone.” Uṣasta asked, “Which soul is within everyone ?” “That which breathes through breaths is thy soul, which is within every being. That which drives the life-wind downwards and out through the anus is thy soul, which is within every being. That which makes the life-wind function as the vital air that is diffused through the whole body, is thy soul which is within every being. That which makes the life-wind rise up the throat and enter the head is thy soul, which is within every being.”

Then, Uṣasta, again, asked, “Which soul is within every being?” YājÛavalkya replied again—

‘.....सर्वान्तर:। न दृष्टेर्द्रष्टारं पश्येर्न श्रुते: श्रोतारॸशृणुया न मतेर्मन्तारं मन्वीथा न विज्ञातेर्विज्ञातारं विजानीया:। एष त आत्मा सर्वान्तरोऽतोऽन्यदार्तं ततो होषस्तश्चाक्रायण उपरराम।’

(III. iv. 2)

“This thy soul is within every being. You cannot behold the beholder of the process of beholding; you cannot hear the hearer of the process of hearing; you cannot reflect on the thinker who carries on the process of thought; you cannot know the knower of the process of knowing. This thy soul is within every being—whatever is different from it is transient.” Hearing this, Uṣasta, the son of Cakra, became silent.

अथ हैनं कहोल: कौषीतकेय: पप्रच्छ याज्ञवल्क्येति होवाच यदेव साक्षादपरोक्षाद्‍ब्रह्म य आत्मा सर्वान्तरस्तं मे व्याचक्ष्वेत्येष त आत्मासर्वान्तर:। कतमो याज्ञवल्क्य सर्वान्तरो योऽशनायापिपासे शोकं मोहं जरां मृत्युमत्येति।

(III. v. 1)

“Then, Kahola, the son of Kauṣītaka, asked, “Yāj×avalkya, do explain to me that directly realizable Brahma, which is the soul of every being.” YājÛavalkya replied, “It is your soul, which is within every being.” Kahola enquired, “Which soul, O YājÛavalkya, is within every being?” Thereupon, YājÛavalkya replied, “That which is beyond hunger, thirst, grief, delusion, old age and death, it is that your soul, which is within every being.”

“Then, Uddālaka, the son of Aruṇa, told YājÛavalkya, “If you are ignorant, O YājÛavalkya, of the Inner Ruler running as the Thread within all, and yet take away the cows which rightly belong to the knower of Brahma, your head will certainly drop down from your neck.” YājÛavalkya replied, “Verily I know that Inner Ruler running as the Thread within all.”

“O Gautama, the wind is that Thread. This world, the other world, and all beings are held together by the wind, as by a thread.” Agreeing with this, Uddālaka, now asked YājÛavalkya to explain the Inner Ruler.

YājÛavalkya said—

‘य: पृथिव्यां तिष्ठन् पृथिव्या अन्तरो यं पृथिवी न वेद यस्य पृथिवी शरीरं य: पृथिवीमन्तरो यमयत्येष त आत्मान्तर्याम्यमृत:।’

(III. vii. 3)

“He who dwelling in the earth is within the earth, whom the earth does not know, whose body is the earth, who living within rules the earth, is your soul, the Inner Ruler, immortal.’’

Again—

‘अदृष्टो द्रष्टाश्रुत: श्रोतामतो मन्ताविज्ञातो विज्ञाता नान्योऽतोऽस्ति विज्ञातैष त आत्मान्तर्याम्यमृतोऽतोऽन्यदार्तं ततो होद्दालक आरुणिरुपरराम।’

(III. vii. 23)

“Unseen, He sees, unheard He hears, though beyond thought, He thinks, unknown He knows. That is your soul, the Inner Ruler, Immortal. Whatever is different from Him is perishable.” Hearing this, Uddālaka, the son of Aruṇa, become silent.

Then, Gārgī, the daughter of Vacaknu, and Vidagdha, the son of śakala, put many other questions, which were immediately answered by YājÛavalkya. In the end, addressing Vidagdha, YājÛavalkya said, “I shall now ask you about the Puruṣa in the Upaniṣads, and if you fail to explain Him to me, your head will fall off.” Vidagdha knew Him not, hence his head fell off.

Then, YājÛavalkya said, “O venerable Brāhmaṇas, let anyone among you, who wishes it, question me; or I will question anyone among you, who desires it.” But the Brāhmaṇas dared not take up his challenge.

In order to explain the secret of this truth, the Br̥hadāraṇyakaUpaniṣad further said—

स वा एष महानज आत्माजरोऽमरोऽमृतोऽभयो ब्रह्माभयं वै ब्रह्माभयॸहि वै ब्रह्म भवति य एवं वेद।

(IV. iv. 24)

“This great unborn tmā (soul), undecaying, immortal and fearless, is Brahma; Brahma is verily fearless; he who knows becomes verily the fearless Brahma.”

This is a short description of what constitutes the worship of attributeless Brahma, indication of the word “Thou,” beyond the external universe, which is the very same as Self.

Through the careful practice of any of the forms of worship mentioned in this article, man can realize God. Firstly, as between dualistic and non-dualistic forms of worship, whatever course the Sādhaka chooses, he may start the practice either according to his own taste or comprehension, or under the instruction of some teacher; but if his goal is truly God-realization, whatever may be the sentiment he cultivates for the sake of his worship, he is bound in the end to realize God, for the final result of all the practices is one and the same. In the Gītā, also, the Lord says—

यत्सांख्यै: प्राप्यते स्थानं तद्योगैरपि गम्यते।

एकं सांख्यं च योगं च य: पश्यति स पश्यति॥

(V. 5)

“The supreme state, which is reached by the Sāṅkhyayogī, is attained also by the Karmayogī. Therefore, he who sees Sāṅkhyayoga and Karmayoga as one, so far as their result goes, really sees.”

Again, it has been said—

ध्यानेनात्मनि पश्यन्ति केचिदात्मानमात्मना।

अन्ये सांख्येन योगेन कर्मयोगेन चापरे॥

(XIII. 24)

“Some by meditation behold the Self (Paramātmā) in their own heart with the help of their pure reason; others by proceeding along the path of Knowledge, and others, again, by treading the path of Action.”

All the practices, which have been described in the Gītā, the Upaniṣad and other scriptures bear the same fruit, that is, their final result is one and the same. This result is indescribable, and none can explain it in any way. It is much beyond whatever is described and explained.

Thus the Saguṇa and Nirguṇa forms of worship of God, as dualistic and non-dualistic worship, have been discussed very briefly in this article. The subject of worship as described in the Upaniṣads is very extensive and very abstruse. Owing to shortness of space, only a bird’s eye-view of it has been given in this article. Cultured and inquisitive readers, who may desire to know the subject more in detail should themselves study the Upaniṣads, and by properly reflecting on, and adopting the practices described in them, make their lives truly successful.

 

Meditation on the Absolute

SUBLATION OF THE PHENOMENAL

All that is objective is illusory, of the nature of a dream. Just as on waking from a dream no trace is left of the dream-world, the phenomenal has an absolute non-existence even more certain than that. In the eyes of him who has woken from a dream the dream-world persists in a conceptual form, or in other words it continues to exist as a mere concept. But to him who has woken into God-consciousness, i.e., realized God, the world ceases to exist even as a concept. For the mind and intellect in which the dream-world appeared persist even after one’s waking from the dream, with this difference that the dream-world no longer abides in them; that is why it is said to have a conceptual existence. Subsequent to God-realization, however, the mind and intellect, being products of Māyā (illusion), are left behind in this world of illusion. Hence this world is absolutely non-existent in the essential nature of God. But in the mind of the individual who is believed to have realized God the world exists as a dream—this is what the scriptures as well as the seekers of truth ever. Hence viewing this world as a mere illusion or as analogous to a dream or a mirage, a mere appearance having no reality whatsoever, the Sādhaka should banish it altogether from his mind, or in other words he should give up all thought of the world, should forget the world altogether.

A Hindī poet says—

“Free your mind from thoughts of the world, by whatever method it may be possible. Sublate the world, whether it be through devotion, knowledge or Yoga.”*

* मन फुरना से रहित कर, जौनहि बिधि से होय।

चाहे भक्ति चाहे ग्यान से, चाहे योग से खोय॥

The sum and substance of Advaita philosophy or the monistic doctrine is that beyond the one Divinity, who is truth, knowledge and bliss combined, nothing ever was or is. Realizing the absolute non-existence of this phenomenal world in the past, present and future, and even of these three divisions of time, the Sādhaka should merge himself into the one eternal Divinity, who is solidified knowledge and bliss.

THE ESSENTIAL NATURE OF GOD

God is truth, knowledge and bliss combined. These three constitute the essential nature of God. They are no qualifications of God, because there is no distinction of qualifications of God, because there is no distinction of either; for God admits of no such difference as attribute and substratum. Nor again are they characteristics or distinguishing marks of God; for there is no such thing as distinguisher and distinguished in God.

THE TRUTH ASPECT

To us Truth appears as a distinct entity. Consciousness as an altogether different entity and Bliss as a separate entity, but the truth is far from it. That which is real is conscious as well and that which is conscious is of the nature of bliss at the same time. To put it the other way, that which is conscious is also real and that which is real is of the nature of bliss as well. Or to put it in a third form, that which is of the nature of bliss is also conscious; and that alone is real, which is conscious at the same time. That which we hold as real is not in essence the same as God; for the essential nature of God is different from it.

The Lord says in the Gītā—

“Now I shall speak to you at length about that which ought to be known, and knowing which one attains immortality; that beginningless supreme Brahma is said to be neither Sat (being) nor Asat (non-being).”*

* ज्ञेयं यत्तत्प्रवक्ष्यामि यज्ज्ञात्वामृतमश्नुते।

अनादिमत्परं ब्रह्म न सत्तन्नासदुच्यते॥

(XIII. 12)

A reality which can be grasped through the intellect is illusory; hence it is a product of Māyā (illusion). A Sādhaka undoubtedly succeeds in realizing God by viewing it as identical with Him, but in reality it is not the same as God; for the human intellect, being a product of Māyā, is incapable of comprehending God, who is beyond the realm of Māyā. Such a reality can be declared to be the same as God qualified by the intellect, but not as Absolute Divinity.

THE CONSCIOUS ASPECT

The conscious principle which we recognize as such is not the same as God either, God is entirely different from it. That is why the Gītā says—

“The light of all lights, it is said to be beyond the darkness of Māyā. It is knowledge itself, as well as the object of knowledge, and is also worth attaining through knowledge; it is specially seated in the hearts of all.”*

* ज्योतिषामपि तज्ज्योतिस्तमस: परमुच्यते।

ज्ञानं ज्ञेयं ज्ञानगम्यं हृदि सर्वस्य विष्ठितम्॥

(XIII. 17)

Higher than the lights that are perceived through the senses of perception are those that are grasped through the intellect; that is to say, discrimination and knowledge are higher than physical light etc. Even higher than intellectual perception is the apprehension by the conscious self of a spiritual reality. For just as the intellect has knowledge of the eye and the objects perceived by the eye, but the eye cannot perceive the intellect or things comprehended by the intellect, even so the self knows the intellect as well as the objects perceived through intellect, but the intellect is incapable of knowing the self. All the same the self too, when it knows its own essential nature, knows it through a refined and sharpened intellect.

The Kaṭhopaniṣad says—

“The self hidden in the heart of all beings does not become manifest to all, but it is perceived by men of acute vision through a sharp and penetrating intellect.”*

* एष सर्वेषु भूतेषु गूढोत्मा न प्रकाशते।

दृश्यते त्वग्रॺया बुद्‍ध्या सूक्ष्मया सूक्ष्मदर्शिभि:॥

Hence the self which is known through the intellect is the same as God qualified by the intellect. As for the absolute aspect of God, it is even higher than the latter and cannot be known by any means.

We read in the Upaniṣads—

“Through what organ can it be possible to know that by which one knows everything? It is not that, not that. The soul does exist, but it cannot be grasped; it is proof against decay; it does not decay; it is unattached, it does not cleave; it is unbound, it does not feel vexed or afflicted; it does not perish. Well, through what organ can one know the knower itself ?”*

*.....येनेदॸ सर्वं विजानाति तं केन विजानीयात् स एष नेति नेत्यात्मागृह्यो न हि गृह्यतेऽशीर्यो न हि शीर्यतेऽसङ्गो न हि सज्यतेऽसितो न व्यथते न रिष्यति विज्ञातारमरे केन विजानीयात्॥

(Br̥hadāraṇyaka-Upaniṣad IV. v. 15 )

All that is knowable is a product of intellect. Knowledge is more subtle and higher than the knowable and, though a product of intellect, is the same as intellect. The knower or the subject is infinitel than knowledge and is quite dissimilar from the intellect. In other words, knowledge is more comprehensive, higher, greater and subtler than the knowable, and conscious and infinite at the same time. Maharṣi PataÛjali says in his famous “Aphorisms on Yoga”—

“On the cessation of the Kleśas and Karmas when the whole impurity in the shape of obstruction disappears, the knowable appears very small in the infinitude of knowledge.”*

* तदा सर्वावरणमलापेतस्य ज्ञानस्यानन्त्याज्ज्ञेयमल्पम्।

(IV. 21)

And the self (knower) is even more subtle, conscious and blissful, and at the same time greater and higher than knowledge. The real nature of absolute extremely God is even distinguishing than what has been said above of the Self. It cannot be known, because there is no distinction of knower, knowledge and knowable in God. He is worth attaining.

THE BLISS ASPECT

The joy too which we recognize as such is not in essence the same as God; for the bliss aspect of God is entirely chilinghing from it. The Gītā says—

“He whose mind remains unattached to sense-objects derives through meditation the Sāttvika joy which dwells in the mind; then that Yogī, having completely identified himself through meditation with Brahma, enjoys eternal bliss.”*

* बाह्यस्पर्शेष्वसक्तात्मा विन्दत्यात्मनि यत्सुखम्।

स ब्रह्मयोगयुक्तात्मा सुखमक्षयमश्नुते॥

(V. 21)

The joy born of sleep, indolence etc., is Tāmasika in character. Higher than that is the joy arising from contact of the senses with their objects, which is Rājasika in character. The Sāttvika form of joy which proceeds from spiritual practice is even higher than the latter; but the essential nature of God, which is characterized as all bliss, transcends all these. The Rājasika form of joy is superior to that born of sleep, indolence etc., in so far as it is conscious whereas in the latter there is no consciousness at all. Again, the Rājasika type of joy arising from contact of the senses with their objects is momentary, transient and painful in consequence; whereas the Sāttvika joy is comparatively more lasting and nectar-like in the end. Hence the Rājasika form of joy is worth rejecting when compared with the Sāttvika joy resulting from spiritual practice. But even this latter type of joy proceeding from meditation on the spirit is of a lower order when looked at from the viewpoint of the essential nature of God. Hence even the Sāttvika joy is worth giving up when compared with God. The Gītā says—

“Of these Sattva, being immaculate, is illuminating and flawless; it binds through self-identification with joy and knowledge, O sinless Arjuna.”*

* तत्र सत्त्वं निर्मलत्वात्प्रकाशकमनामयम्।

सुखसङ्गेन बध्नाति ज्ञानसङ्गेन चानघ॥

(XIV. 6)

Even the joy proceeding from meditation, limited as it is by time and space and enjoyable through the intellect, is material, finite and transient.

The knower or the subject is as a rule conscious, whereas the object of knowledge is material. Only that which is finite is capable of being known; and that which is finite is necessarily bound by time and space. God, however, is beyond time and space. Hence the blissful nature of God is extraordinary than all these.

Material joy is not cognized by itself, it is known by someone else. The joy, however, which is the essential nature of God is Self-conscious. It is not cognizable by others, hence it is conscious; and it is beyond the limits of time and space, hence it is eternal. But the real nature of God is entirely different from that which is thus understood. What has been described above is the nature of God qualified by the intellect. The nature of even such a God can be understood only through a refined and sharpened intellect. It is this nature of God which has been called supersensuous and comprehensible by the intellect. The Lord says in the Gītā—

“That in which the soul experiences the eternal and supersensuous joy which can be apprehended only through a refined and sharp intellect, and wherein established the said Yogī moves not from Truth on any account.”*

सुखमात्यन्तिकं यत्तद् बुद्धिग्राह्यमतीन्द्रियम्।

वेत्ति यत्र न चैवायं स्थितश्चलति तत्त्वत:॥

(VI. 21)

That reality of God cannot be described either through affirmation or through negation—nevertheless, if a Sādhaka follows a particular course of spiritual discipline with the object of attaining that which is aimed at and discussed by the Vedas and other scriptures, he succeeds in realizing God even as one is able to locate the new moon with the help of the bough of a tree—शाखाचन्द्रवत्. Suppose anyone catches sight of the new moon and wants to show it to another who is unable to locate it. The former takes the help of a bough of a tree appearing close to the moon and tells his companion that the moon stands just four inches above the bough. He tells a third person that the moon appears exactly beside the corner of a particular roof. Really speaking, both these descriptions are wrong; yet they enable the other fellows to discover the moon. Even so a Sādhaka who strives for God-realization following a particular path pointed out to him as leading to God and with his eyes fixed on what has been described to him as God succeeds in realizing Him.

The various descriptions of God such as God with form and without form, God with attributes, and without attributes, God as manifest, and unmanifest, are all correct as well as incorrect at the same time. They are correct in so far as those who strive for God-realization with their eyes fixed on Him as described by saints and holy men, the Vedas and other scriptures, are enabled to realize Him. And they are incorrect in the sense that really speaking God is entirely different from what He has been described to be; for words fail to describe the real nature of God.

Hence those who meditate on God without form should strive diligently with their eyes fixed on God as described in the śāstras, no matter what method they follow. This may enable them to realize the truth as a matter of course.

 

Meditation on the All-Blissful Aspect of God

Squatting in a secluded place, which is free from dirt and other impurities, in a steady and easy posture, one should meditate on God as follows. There is no spiritual discipline as good as meditation in the world. The Lord says—

संकल्पप्रभवान् कामांस्त्यक्त्वा सर्वानशेषत:।

मनसैवेन्द्रियग्रामं विनियम्य समन्तत:॥

शनै: शनैरुपरमेद् बुद्‍ध्या धृतिगृहीतया।

आत्मसंस्थं मन: कृत्वा न किंचिदपि चिन्तयेत् ॥

(Gītā VI. 24-25)

“Completely renouncing all desires arising from thoughts of the world, and fully restraining the whole pack of the senses from all sides by the mind, he should through gradual practice attain tranquillity; and fixing the mind on God through reason controlled by steadfastness, he should not think of anything else.”

God has been characterized as All-truth, all-consciousness and infinite—‘सत्यं ज्ञानमनन्तं ब्रह्म’ (Taittirīya Upaniṣad II.i.1). God is all-conscious. The whole of this universe is comprised in the idea of that all-conscious God. If He were to give up the idea of the world, the one All-conscious God alone would remain. There are three categories in world—viz., (1) JÛāta, (2) JÛāna and (3) JÛeya. Of these, the JÛāna and JÛeya are unconscious, the JÛāta alone being conscious. That which is capable of being known is called JÛeya (the knowable); the instrument of knowledge is called “JÛāna” (the means of knowledge) and the knowing subject is the JÛāta. The object as well as the means of knowledge depend on the knower. The means as well as the object of knowledge—these are all assumed. Even as the dream-world is only assumed, it has no real existence, it is a mere idea, so this visible universe too is a mere idea. If, on the other hand, it does exist, it should not perish; for the Lord says in the Gītā—

नासतो विद्यते भावो नाभावो विद्यते सत:।

(II. 16)

“The unreal has no existence, and the real does not cease to be.”

But all objects are perishing before our very eyes. Because of this perishable nature they are all transient; and, being transient, they do not really exist, are mere ideas or fictitious. That which ideates them is the conscious substance and that is the soul.

The soul is conscious by nature; and that alone which is conscious constitutes bliss. Of course, consciousness is cognized by us, but not so bliss; for the soul being associated with “JÛāna” and “JÛeya”, its true nature is veiled. Even as the sun, though consisting of a huge mass of light, is not perceived when screened by clouds, so the soul, even though conscious by nature, is not cognized due to its being veiled by ignorance. The soul is a ray of the Oversoul. Hence from the monistic point of view the soul and the Oversoul are one. The veiling is only subjective, a mere idea. When this is negated, the one God remains.

God exists. He is great, infinite, unbounded, conscious, and consisting of knowledge, cognition and bliss. One should meditate on these lines. God is uniformly present everywhere—above and below, inside as well as outside this universe consisting of mobile and immobile creation. The Lord says in the Gītā—

बहिरन्तश्च भूतानामचरं चरमेव च।

(XIII. 15)

“It exists without and within all beings, and constitutes the animate and inanimate creation as well.”

Just as the sky is unmanifest and formless, God too is unmanifest and formless. Really speaking, however, the sky stands no comparison with God; for, while the sky is material (unconscious) and void, God is conscious and bliss solidified. That is why they speak of God as truth, consciousness and bliss crystallized. By calling Him truth we only mean that He exists. “Conscious” means that which consists of knowledge or cognition. That conscious entity in itself is bliss. Hence He is spoken of as consciousness and bliss condensed.

The aforesaid bliss is of the nature of never-ending happiness. That happiness, again, is known to that all-blissful God alone. Hence that all-blissful God is characterized as consisting of bliss. That bliss itself is consciousness and that consciousness itself is bliss. The idea is that the said bliss is known to none else; that all-blissful God alone is conscious of His own existence. Such is the nature of God, who is spiritual in essence and bliss solidified. Nothing exists beyond that one divine essence. In other words, the world has no reality apart from God. Entirely forgetting the world in this way, one should cease to entertain any idea whatsoever. Meditation on that Nirguṇa and formless God consists of this alone.

A man treading the path of devotion should fell this tree of mundane life with the axe of strong dispassion. In other words, he should forget the world and empty the mind of all thoughts by dint of dispassion. And the Sādhaka following the path of knowledge should look upon the world as an empty dream and altogether negate it. The world impinges on our vision even though it has no reality. It appears as real because of its being an idea of God; in fact, it has no reality whatsoever. The world would dwindle into nothingness if God were to give up this idea.

Hence the Sādhaka should picture to his mind that God has given up the idea of the world and that the entire universe has vanished of its own accord. God alone now remains. Nothing and none exists apart from that absolute and formless Reality, consisting of truth, consciousness and bliss crystallized. That bliss is spiritual bliss, unmixed and absolute bliss, bliss and bliss alone. There is no entity apart from that bliss. In this way one should meditate on that All-blissful God.

The Sādhaka following the path of knowledge coupled with Devotion should picture to his mind that while God has given up the idea of the entire universe, he alone persists in His idea. Since he is engrossed in the thought of God, the latter too is thinking of him; for the Lord says—

ये यथा मां प्रपद्यन्ते तांस्तथैव भजाम्यहम्।

(Gītā IV. 11)

“Arjuna, howsoever men seek Me, even so do I approach them.”

When God ceases to think of the Sādhaka, God alone will remain in his place too; for God alone has existed forever and everywhere from time immemorial. The word “everywhere” brings in the notion of space. That space too exists in the idea of God; really speaking, there is no space in God. The observation that God is eternally present is expressive of time. This time as well exists in the idea of God. As a matter of fact, God is beyond space and time. The consciousness of time and space that persists during the period of “Sādhanā” (spiritual discipline), being an idea of God, is no other than God; in fact, there is no entity other than God. The one undifferentiated Brahma alone exists.

While practising meditation, we should repeatedly urge on our mind that God is present on all our sides. By us we mean our body. In other words, we should feel that God is present all round our body. In fact, the body does not exist at all; God alone exists in place of it. Just as there is the sky and sky alone on every side of a cloud, God alone exists on every side of our body. As a matter of fact, the cloud springs up from and gets dissolved in the sky alone. Hence the cloud has no existence apart from the sky. Similarly there is no entity apart from God. The body and God stand in the same relation to each other as the cloud and the sky. Really speaking, however, the sky stands no comparison with God, who is all consciousness and all bliss; while the sky is material and unconscious. Consciousness is the same as bliss and bliss is identical with consciousness. Hence the two are no separate entities. It is in order to distinguish the aforesaid bliss from worldly joy that we say such bliss is transcendental, unearthly, unique and spiritual, that it is the same as consciousness or knowledge.

That All-blissful God is perfect in Himself—that is why He is spoken of as “perfect bliss”. Because He is unbounded, we characterize Him as “unbounded joy”. Since He is all-tranquil, we refer to Him as “tranquil joy”. That bliss is most compact and voluminous; it has no room for any other substance. Hence it is spoken of as “solid bliss”. It is unvarying and unremittent; hence it is known as “constant bliss”. It continues for ever; hence it is called “perpetual bliss”. It never ceases to be and exists in reality; hence it is designated as “real bliss.” The bliss in question is conscious; hence it is referred to as “conscious bliss” or “bliss identical with knowledge”. It uniformly extends everywhere—above and below, outside as well as inside; hence it is spoken of as “uniform bliss”. It cannot be conceived by any—is beyond the scope of the intellect; hence it is designated as “bliss inconceivable”. To know it is to recognize that it is beyond conception. As a matter of fact, the essential character of God is altogether different from the all-conscious and All-blissful God conceived by us; from that which forms the object of our conception. That alone which is united with the intellect is conceived through the intellect. Hence it is Brahma qualified by reason that comes to be conceived by us. In other words, that alone which is capable of being grasped with the intellect is conceived by means of the intellect. That is why the Gītā characterizes it as ‘बुद्धिग्राह्यम्’—so subtle that it can be comprehended with the intellect alone (VI. 21).

The aforesaid bliss is great indeed; hence it is spoken of as “great bliss.” It is the highest of all; hence it is characterized as “supreme bliss.” It is conscious by nature; hence it is designated as “spiritual bliss.” A striver should remain constantly and uninterruptedly merged in the All-blissful God referred to above.

In the course of meditation a striver should mentally repeat the following ideas in his mind and actually feel like that;—“Oh, what ineffable peace is reigning all round! There is nothing but peace. God alone is becoming manifest in the form of peace. Oh, how profuse is the light of knowledge spreading all round! There is enlightenment and enlightenment alone. Nothing but enlightenment. God alone is being revealed in the form of enlightenment. Oh, how unspeakable is the consciousness that is being experienced! There is nothing but consciousness. God alone is manifesting Himself in the form of consciousness. Oh, what ineffable joy! We find that joy and joy alone pervades our mind, intellect, senses and body, inside, as well as outside. In other words, the All-blissful God alone is visibly permeating every pore of our body, nay, every atom of our being, the form of our body being a mere idea. Really speaking, joy and joy alone manifests itself through it. That bliss alone stands manifested in the form of peace. That bliss alone is revealed in the form of knowledge and that bliss alone is perceived in the form of consciousness. All these are no more than synonyms of joy. In fact, they all represent the one All-blissful Divinity.

How blissful! Oh, what a bliss! What a joy! Integral Bliss! Unbounded Bliss! Tranquil Bliss! Solid Bliss! Constant Bliss! Eternal Bliss! Conscious Bliss! Supreme Bliss! Great Bliss! Never-ending Bliss! Inconceivable Bliss! Absolute Bliss! Unmixed Joy! OË! Peace! Peace!! Peace!!!

 

Intensity of Spiritual Practice

Just as the process of respiration goes on without interruption and knows no pause, the striving for God-realization should continue day and night without remission. He who is incessantly and habitually engaged in adoration and meditation is sure to attain blessedness. For the Lord Himself says in corroboration of this truth—

यं यं वापि स्मरन्भावं त्यजत्यन्ते कलेवरम्।

तं तमेवैति कौन्तेय सदा तद्भावभावित:॥

(Gītā VIII. 6)

“Arjuna, thinking of whatever entity one leaves the body at the time of death, that and that alone one attains, being ever absorbed in its thought”.

Nay, the Lord goes on to declare that He is easily accessible to him who is constantly engrossed in His thought.

अनन्यचेता: सततं यो मां स्मरति नित्यश:।

तस्याहं सुलभ: पार्थ नित्ययुक्तस्य योगिन:॥

(Gītā VIII. 14)

In the couple of verses immediately preceding the above, the Lord has already indicated the means of controlling the senses, the mind and the vital airs. Such a practice can very well be pronounced as exceedingly difficult; Yogīs alone can undertake it. As for constant remembrance of God, anyone and everyone can practise it. “Think of Me at all times and fight,”* says the Lord.

* मामनुस्मर युध्य च।

Of course steadfastness is most essential for carrying on this practice. If a man does not succeed in realizing God during his lifetime even through diligent practice, he is sure to do so at the last moment.

The mind, speech and body—all these three should be employed in carrying on one’s Sādhana. The body should be employed in rendering service to others, the mind should be devoted to meditation and the organ of speech should be employed in muttering the Divine Name. No action should be performed with the motive of attaining worldly objects; everything should be done as a duty (Gītā II. 47). Whenever the Sādhaka is reminded of God, he should take it as a great favour of God and should feel transported with joy. Similarly he should bitterly rue the moment when God slips out of his memory, and should shudder to think what a sorry plight he would have been reduced to had he met his end that very moment.

We should go in for all-round improvement. If we visit a temple, we should entertain a very high notion of the image enshrined therein. We should look upon the latter as superior to the decorations surrounding it. Mental worship is more valuable than external worship. Therefore, installing a mental image of the Deity in the heart, one should go on worshipping it with articles of one’s own imagination. This practice should continue at all times and one should get absorbed in it. One should repeatedly remember the divine virtues of God, such as compassion, forbearance, equanimity, impartiality and so on.

One should contemplate on the loving nature, and glory of God. As a lover God has no compeer. As for glory, whatever there is glorious, brilliant and powerful, should be regarded as a spark of the Lord’s glory (Gītā X. 41). Thus perceiving His glory in everything one should offer praises and prayer to Him and meditate on Him. Of these three practices, again, meditation is the most valuable. When there is no one equal to God, how can anyone excel Him. A Sādhaka should offer praises to Him on the above lines. He should only pray that he may remain ever engrossed in His sweet thought and rememberance and may ever continue to behold His divine sports. It should, however, be noted in this connection that the Rāmalīlā that we witness is only an external imitation of śrī Rāma’s divine play. In the same way we should select all the Līlās depicted in the Rāmacaritamānasa, for instance, meditate on them and behold them with our mental eyes. The Caupāīs bearing on those beautiful episodes should be memorized in order to facilitate meditation on and mental perception of the divine sports. This constitutes an excellent and easy way of practising meditation and fixing the mind on God. A large proportion of man’s time is being constantly frittered away in idle thought. On his addressing himself to this practice, however, the mind gets a good occupation by way of reflection and contemplation. This constitutes the most remarkable feature of this practice.

The mind should be kept fully engaged in such pursuits, it should be allowed no respite for indulging in any other thought. The Gītā, the Rāmāyaṇa and the Bhāgavata should form the constant objects of his study. Turning away from other vain pursuits of the livelong day, one should remain engaged in such occupations only.

The main obstacle which slackens our practice, which arrests the progress of our Sādhana, is our attraction for sense-objects. Hence we should carefully eliminate our attachment to worldly objects. Viewing the world and its objects as perishable, ephemeral and sorrowful, we should divert our mind from them and cultivate dispassion. Repeated practice and dispassion are the only means of subduing the mind. Even if it is urged that the generality of men do not feel inclined to undertake such practices, we for our part should take to them without fail, minding not what others do.

The Sādhaka should note one thing more in this connection, viz., that human nature has an innate tendency to lapse into sin. That is why an attempt to encourage the demoniac propensities in men begins to bear fruit immediately, whereas the noble and good work of popularizing the divine virtues bristles with great difficulties. Realizing this he should be ever vigilant and should not waste a single moment. He should never remain idle. An empty mind falls an easy victim to error, sloth and other vices. Hence so long as the mind does not get emptied of worldly thoughts and fixed on God, there is grave risk for him.

One should raise a crop of the Divine Name. The Divine Name is the seed which should be sown in the soil of the heart. The thought-current of the mind represents water. This thought-current is ever flowing in the direction of the ocean of worldly existence. Stemming its flow in that direction the field of the heart has to be irrigated thereby as a field of paddy. Even as the flow of the mind is diverted from that channel and directed towards this field, the latter will begin to thrive. A field of paddy requires an abundant supply of water. It gets dry if we cease watering it. But the field of which we are speaking here never gets dry; that is the speciality of it. Nevertheless the process of watering it should never be discontinued. We should go on watering it all times. That is our paramount duty. And when there is no occupation higher than this, what else should be done. This alone should be persevered in.

Through this process of continually watering the field when the tiny plants of paddy get mature and are crowned with ears, or in other words, as we begin to develop a taste for adoration of God, association with holy men, meditation, dispassion and self-abnegation etc., the field should be scrupulously guarded against destructive birds in the shape of lust, anger, honour and fame etc. Extra vigilance is needed at this stage. We should always be on our guard lest we may be lost in their lovely and sweet warbling, and our ripening crop may be destroyed by them.

For quickening the speed of our Sādhana disinterestedness is most efficacious. This latter ensures speedy success. We have grown very selfish; that is why we are not making rapid progress in our Sādhana. In every matter and at every step we are guided by self-interest. If we get something from the market for our family consisting of say, five members, we shall feel inclined to have the best of the lot for ourselves. If our property is partitioned, we shall try to appropriate the better share. While travelling by rail we try to secure a more comfortable seat for ourselves. Although we soar high in our professions, our eyes, like those of a vulture, are directed towards nasty things. This accounts for our abject degeneration. Hence we can gain much if we attend to our worldly duties in a disinterested spirit.

A man of scanty means should not imagine that only moneyed men can practise self-denial and that he is incapable of it. Had it been so, God-realization would be open to the wealthy alone. In the majority of cases, however, the reverse only is true. The more material resources one possesses, the more self-centred he is. Taking note of the above facts, therefore, everyone should improve one’s Sādhana and accelerate its progress.

 

God-realization through Intense Longing

How can we see the Lord at the earliest possible moment? Our reply to this question is that if we develop faith in our heart, there will be no delay in seeing Him. One should have faith in one’s mind that “God exists, that he who longs for a sight of the Lord is sure to meet Him, that many people have seen Him in the past, that people see Him even today and will continue to do so in future as well. Why should I, then, remain deprived of His sight ?” He who comes to develop this kind of faith will not rest till he has seen God. His sleep as well as appetite will take leave of Him. A number of friends, both men and women, betake themselves to ¤Rṣikeṣa for the annual Satsaṅga in the hope that they will return this time only after realizing God. But they did so on many an occasion in the past, but returned without realizing their ambition. This has led them to believe that even though they harbour such a feeling in their heart, there is little hope of their seeing God. As we believe, so we realize. That is why such people fail to see God and return empty-handed. He who is firm in his belief that the Lord will surely meet him cannot but see the Lord. Prince Bharata was positive in his belief that śrī Rāma would surely meet Him. In the words of Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa, he says—

मोरें जियँ भरोस दृढ़ सोई।

मिलिहहिं राम सगुन सुभ होई॥

“I have firm belief in my heart that śrī Rāma will surely meet me. The omens too are propitious.”

This conviction is based on the tender disposition of the Lord. Bharata continues to say—

जौं करनी समुझै प्रभु मोरी।

नहिं निस्तार कलप सत कोरी॥

जन अवगुन प्रभु मान न काऊ।

दीन बंधु अति मृदुल सुभाऊ॥

“If the Lord were to consider my doings, there would be no redemption for me even after a thousand million Kalpas (cycles). But the Lord never takes into account the faults of His devotees, a friend of the afflicted and most tender-hearted as He is.”

Still, if he does not succeed in seeing the Lord, he says —

बीतें अवधि रहहिं जौं प्राना।

अधम कवन जग मोहि समाना॥

“If however, my life lingers even after the expiry of the term of His exile, who would be so vile in this world as I.”

In other words, he suggests that his life too will depart with the expiry of the term of śrī Rāma’s exile. He had, as a matter of fact, vowed that he would enter a blazing fire in case śrī Rāma did not return even after the expiry of the term of His exile. But such a course was not at all difficult; anyone could attempt it. Moreover, that would be tantamount to suicide. If, on the other hand, he actually loved the Lord, it would be impossible for him to survive without seeing the Lord. Till then his life hung on the hope of śrī Rāma’s return at the end of the term of His exile; but if He did not return even after that, it would be clear that he had no love for the Lord and that what he professed was a mere travesty of love. While he was musing this, Bharata was overcome with pangs of separation from the Lord. Says Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa—

राम बिरह सागर महँ भरत मगन मन होत।

बिप्र रूप धरि पवनसुत आइ गयउ जनु पोत॥

The poet compares the vastness of the agony of separation from śrī Rāma to an ocean in which the mind of Bharata was just going to sink. In other words, Bharata was just on the verge of succumbing to his pangs of separation from the Lord, when Hanumān (son of the wind-god) appeared as a bark in the disguise of a Brāhmaṇa and rescued him.

What we seek to convey through this illustration is that if, on the analogy of Bharata, we too persuade ourselves that the Lord will surely meet us; nay, if we exert ourselves day and night with this end in view, fully determined to see Him at any cost, we are sure to behold him. When the Lord has revealed Himself in the past to thousands of devotees, whose stories are available in our scriptures and other books, it cannot be believed that the stories are all fictitious. When so many people have seen Him with their own eyes heretofore, we should rest assured that we too shall behold Him. If it is urged that we are not worthy of His sight, our reply is that the Lord does not as a rule appear before the deserving alone. Not only do the deserving behold Him; on the other hand, all those who have faith in His mercy are able to see Him, be they deserving or non-deserving or even undeserving. Let us cast a glance into the previous life of those who were blessed with His sight. For example, what sort of life did the celebrated devotee Bilvamaṅgala lead in the prime of his youth? Even Bāli, the monkey-chief of Kiṣkindhā, the elder brother of Sugrīva, whom Lord śrī Rāma Himself characterizes as a great sinner, was blessed by the Lord with His sight. Thus, the scope of His mercy is indeed very wide.

We should turn to account the supreme compassion that the Lord cherishes for all living beings—the dominant feeling of His heart towards us all. He who comes to know His true nature can no longer remain deprived of His realization. Lord śiva says—

उमा राम सुभाउ जेहिं जाना।

ताहि भजन तजि भाव न आना॥

(Rāmacaritamānasa)

“He who comes to realize the true nature of śrī Rāma, O Pārvatī, will not like any other pursuit than His adoration.”

“The Lord has a most tender disposition. Anyone who takes refuge in the Lord can realize Him, no matter if he is the worst sinner. The very thought of the Lord rids one of all sins. But men of an exalted nature do not worship the Lord with the motive of wiping out their sins. They hold that the adoration of the Lord is so very valuable and great that it should never be devoted to the cause of wiping out one’s sins. To stamp out one’s sins by means of adoration of the Lord is as preposterous as to crush stones by means of a diamond. Should a precious stone like diamond be devoted to the cause of crushing stones? That is why men of an exalted type worship God, not for the sake of wiping out their sins but with a view to develop love for the Lord, which is something supremely high. There is no comparison between the two. One’s stock of sins is automatically wiped out by the mere semblance of the Divine Name. Darkness can never stand in the face of the rising sun any more than ignorance can stand before the light of wisdom. Even so sin cannot stand the dazzling glory of the adoration of God. Anyone worshipping the sun-god with a view to securing his vision need not request him to disperse the mist of darkness. We would only pray—“Be good enough to reveal yourself before us.” If darkness persists even after that, we have no objection. We should likewise persist in our longing to see the Lord, no matter if our sins continue unbated and even if the Lord makes us reap their bitter fruit, which we shall be only to glad to do. Even if we are cast into hell as a punishment for our sins, we should not mind. We seek only to behold Him even in hell. We would rather see Him in hell; for in that case all the denizens of hell would have the chance of being redeemed in a body. It is the sight of the Lord, and not the annihilation of our sins, that we are concerned with.

We should never be deluded into the belief that the Lord will appear before us only when we get ripe for His sight. They who believe like that can be blessed with His sight in consonance with their belief only when they get qualified for it. But the divine law is quite the reverse of it. Whoever holds that “the Lord is supremely compassionate, that His mercy descends not only on the deserving, but even on the most undeserving; nay, whosoever holds that, judged by the standard of individual merit, he is not at all worthy, but from the point of view of the Lord’s kind disposition, His sacred vow of redeeming the fallen, His infinite mercy and unmotived love, not only he but even those more degraded than he, rather all of them, however debased they may be, are entitled to His realization, though unworthy of it,” is quite deserving in the Lord’s eye, though undeserving from the point of view of personal merit. Hence, one should never regard oneself as undeserving in the face of the Lord’s unbounded compassion.

In the case of those who realize, are alive to the glory of His compassion, even their disqualification in the shape of unworthiness cannot stand in the way of their God-realization. On the strength of God’s compassion, he who firmly believes that he is sure to realize God this time stands every chance of realizing Him.

It may be urged here that although what has been stated above is quite true and one should undoubtedly persuade oneself like that, yet considering one’s sins and failings one is prone to throw the entire blame on one’s Prārabdha (destiny) and remains deprived of His sight. Our reply to the objector is that such handicaps are of his own making. One should never allow one’s destiny to stand in the way of one’s God-realization. Destiny is no stumbling-block to God-realization. They alone who are blind to this truth lay the blame at the door of their evil destiny. As a matter of fact, unpropitious destiny can interfere with God-realization. If destiny could stand in the way of God-realization, none would be able to realize God before reaching the end of one’s destiny. This is, however, not the case.

One may ask here—How long does one take to realize God? Our reply to this question is that time is no determining factor in God-realization. Man can realize God in an instant if he comes to perceive His true character and unravel His mystery. Failing this, he may not be able to realize Him after years and years together. It may be asked here—“Is it on the strength of one’s personal endeavour or on the strength of God’s kind-heartedness that He can be realized? In other words, does God-realization depend on one’s own striving or on His loving and kindly disposition! Our reply to this is that it depends on self-endeavour in the case of the individual who believes that God will be realized through his or her own endeavour; whereas it depends on the kind-heartedness of God in the case of the aspirant who holds that God is the disinterested friend of all living beings and that he or she is one of such beings. The Lord proclaims in the Gītā—

सुहृदं सर्वभूतानां ज्ञात्वा मां शान्तिमृच्छति॥

(V. 29)

“Having known Me (in reality) as the disinterested friend of all created beings, My devotee attains peace.”

The Lord’s compassion and love unaccountably extends to all; hence He is the disinterested friend of all. He who takes refuge in the Lord with this belief can never remain deprived of God-realization, he is sure to attain God, who is of the nature of supreme and ineffable peace. Hence, the above assertion of the Lord is quite in order and in consonance with the scriptures.

God is supremely compassionate and loving by nature. He cannot bear the sight of others, suffering and none can vie with Him, much less surpass Him, in requiting love. The Lord offers Himself to him who, conscious of the above secret, dedicates himself to the Lord. Tell me, who else than God is so benign and kind-hearted in the world?

Nowhere in the wide world can we find such a unique bargain. A multimillionaire cannot be expected to offer his all in return to a pauper who has offered his all to the multimillionaire. This type of magnanimity exists in God alone. The Lord knows that one’s own self is dearer to one than everything else. He says to Himself, “Lo, this man has offered his all to Me along with his very self. Now even if I offer My all to him in return, I cannot repay the obligation he has put Me under—for it is he who took the initiative in offering his all and I would only be reciprocating. I would have excelled him only if I took the lead. Hence, even though I reciprocate his friendly gesture, he would still be outstripping Me by virtue of the initiative he has taken.” In this way the supremely magnanimous Lord feels beholden to His devotee.

There is one thing more worth noting in this connection. He who comes to realize the kind-heartedness of the Lord becomes kind-hearted himself; for kind-heartedness occupies a unique place in the disposition of the Lord. Realizing that it is entirely in one’s hand to mould one’s own disposition, he shapes his disposition according to the disposition of the Lord. The Lord now betakes Himself to him of His own accord; for he is a devotee of the Lord, acquainted with His virtues, glory, reality, mystery and disposition and because he cherishes supreme reverence for and integral faith in the Lord. How can the Lord remain aloof from such a loving devotee and how can such a loving devotee remain aloof from his beloved Lord ? The Lord says in the Gītā—

यो मां पश्यति सर्वत्र सर्वं च मयि पश्यति।

तस्याहं न प्रणश्यामि स च मे न प्रणश्यति॥

(VI. 30)

“He who sees Me (the Universal Soul) present in all beings, and all beings existing within Me, never loses sight of Me, and I never lose sight of him.”

He who perceives ether in all and all in ether can never lose sight of ether. Even so how can he who sees God pervading everywhere as ether and sees all in God lose sight of Him and how can God lose sight of him either? On the other hand, they continue to behold each other at every time, and on every occasion. How enviable and exalted this position is ! The Lord can never evade the sight of a loving devotee who has grasped His truth and mystery. It is His reality that predominates in the path of knowledge; while it is His mystery that is uppermost in the path of devotion. The cowherdesses of Vraja knew the secret of the false appearance assumed by Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa to deceive them. They could easily make out that it was their beloved śrī Kr̥ṣṇa who appeared before them in the guise of a female companion. The mystery would thus be unravelled and śrī Kr̥ṣṇa would then reveal His identity.

Now let us consider the question once more as to how to realize God at the earliest moment. Once a man has developed the desire to see God at the earliest moment, the only means of realizing this ambition is to intensify the desire to the utmost degree. When the desire reaches the highest pitch, the Lord can no longer tarry. The Lord tarries in the case of him alone who brooks the delay. The Lord is postponing His visit to us only because we bear separation from Him. When our agony of separation from Him grows so acute that we cannot do without Him even for a moment, the Lord too will find it impossible to defer His visit to us even for a moment. The Lord will find Himself in the same plight to which we shall be reduced.

“We do at times grow restless for the Lord,” it may be contended; “does the Lord too become restless for us in those moments?” My reply is that He does grow restless as it were for us. By way of illustration let us take the case of Sītā, who actually grew restless for the Lord during her separation from Him. Now śrī Rāma too become restless for her as it were during that period. Judged from our standard His agony grew so acute that He actually addressed even trees and animals and asked them if they had seen His beloved spouse. Nay, he even raved as it were like a madman. As a matter of fact, however, the Lord does not actually go mad nor does He lose His balance of mind. Certainly He does outwardly behave like one distracted or whose mental equilibrium has been disturbed. He showed to the world that Sītā was as restless for Him as He was for her. When Lakṣmaṇa was pierced in the heart with a javelin on the battle-field of Laṅkā, the Lord began to rave in His agony at the impending loss of His beloved younger brother. By the aforesaid pastime of His the Lord showed to the world that Lakṣmaṇa could be reduced to a similar plight in his agony of separation from Him. The Lord began to lament and rave in a state of excitement the like of which Lakṣmaṇa would have experienced in his agony of separation from his divine Brother.

Hence, the best means of realizing God is to develop an intense longing to meet Him. In the event of our developing such longing the Lord cannot help meeting us. Adoration and meditation are not so efficacious in drawing the Lord to us as an intense longing to behold Him. When we find no rest without seeing Him, the Lord too will find no rest without seeing us. When both God and His devotee are restless to meet eachother, the more powerful of the two will be held more liable to take the initiative. We for our part are quite helpless in the matter, hence we are unable to approach the Lord. The Lord, on the other hand, is All-powerful, so He can certainly come to us. Say, for example, a friend of mine is seriously ill; as such he is unable to come to me. I can, however, go to him because I am physically fit. Nay, suppose my friend sends word to me saying that he is ill and therefore, unable to see me, but that he is all the same anxious to see me.
