Under such circumstances, if I have an iota of love in me, I shall do my level best to go to him at all events. But in spite of my best efforts it is not certain that I shall be able to see him; for we are altogether helpless and dependent on our destiny in this matter. The Lord, on the other hand, is absolutely independent, all-powerful, supremely compassionate and the disinterested friend of all. How can He, under such circumstances, tarry even for a moment ? But the deficiency in our faith and love must be supplied by us. Of course the Lord too can help us if we implore Him to bless us with reverence and love.

We can meet the Lord if we love Him even as we love honour and praise, wife and children, wealth and body etc. Wealth etc., are material objects. It is we that seek them; they do not seek us. Moreover, their acquisition by us is dependent on our destiny and is therefore uncertain. God, however, is a conscious Being; He loves those that love Him and destiny too cannot stand in the way of our meeting Him. Therefore, the Lord surely meets those who are seized with an intense longing to see Him.

If we persuade ourselves that the Lord will meet us even if our love for Him is scant, the most compassionate and supremely loving Lord can meet us in spite of our meagre love, overlooking our faults. Nay, the Lord meets even those who long to see Him under the belief that He takes into account neither our eligibility nor our love but only expects us to long for Him. Hence, we should develop an intense longing for the Lord—the same type of thirst as a greedy man has for money. All the endeavours of a greedy man are solely directed towards money, His mind is ever obsessed with the idea of money and money alone.
He may not have the word “money” on his lips all the twenty-four hours; but his mind is ever seized with an insatiable thirst for money. Hence he is designated as a true worshipper of mammon. He can renounce his wife and children, his faith and duty, nay, even God for the sake of money. He is always found saying, “Money must be acquired at any cost, no matter if we get nothing else. Even so all the endeavours of a true devotee of God are directed solely towards God and the Lord surely meets him. It is therefore that Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa says—

कामिहि नारि पिआरि जिमि लोभिहि प्रिय जिमि दाम।

तिमि रघुनाथ निरंतर प्रिय लागहु मोहि राम॥

“May You be ever so dear to me, O Rāma, as woman is dear to a lustful man, and as money is dear to the greedy, O Lord of the Raghus!”

The Lord is sure to meet one who has developed such love for Him, He can meet us even if we have faith in and reverence for Him; for faith and reverence are the chief factors in His realization. Hence, he who develops the firm conviction that the Lord will undoubtedly meet him that very night will have no wink of sleep during that night; for he will expect Him every moment with supreme eagerness. If he falls asleep, he cannot be said to have faith in the possibility of His coming to him during the said night; for there are no hard and fast rules as to at what particular moment He will be coming. Therefore, he should naturally be on the tiptoe every moment. Even a devoted wife keenly awaits the arrival of her husband who has gone out during the daytime, if he does not return till late at night. The devotee who expects the Lord in the course of a particular night awaits Him even more eagerly than that devoted wife. The aforesaid wife does fall asleep sometimes; for her husband often comes back and at times he does not. A devotee of the Lord, however, gets no sleep; for he expects him every moment and keeps himself ready to offer worship and pay homage to Him.

When a high official or any other venerable personage or honourable guest or some esteemed friend visits our house, we keep things ready for his use even before his arrival. Similarly a devotee expecting the Lord keeps articles such as sandalpaste, flowers, incense and food ready for His worship and his heart overflows with enthusiasm and joy at the prospect of His arrival. The enthusiasm of a devoted wife who receives information about her husband returning from abroad that very night knows no bounds. A lover of the Lord is even more enthusiastic; for there is nothing so valuable as God. Intense longing is therefore the only requisite for God-realization; no other spiritual discipline is called for. Longing automatically draws out what little discipline is necessary; no other independent discipline is required.

When a fish is taken out of water, it is seized with an insatiable thirst for water. It writhes without water and is unable to forget it. If we are similarly seized with an intense longing for the Lord and grow impatient to meet Him, the Lord will be compelled to appear before us that very moment. If there is no such thirst and uneasiness in us and if we but develop the constancy and eagerness of the Cātaka bird, even then we can realize God. A Cātaka would never stoop to drink water lying on the ground, no matter if it is the water of the holy Gaṅgā; it would quench its thirst only with rain-drops falling from above. Such isits wonderful constancy. The Lord too maintains the life of the Cātaka by pouring showers from above. Just visualize the state of mind of a Cātaka. When clouds begin to rumble in the sky, the Cātaka feels enraptured as it were. Even if there is a hail-storm and the wings of the Cātaka get injured by pellets of hail, the bird unflinchingly endures it and eagerly looks forward to a delightful shower. Even so if a devotee pants to see the Lord, the latter is sure to appear before him and bless him with His transcendent vision. If we fail to develop the ideal constancy of this strange bird, let us imitate the ways of the Cakora bird, which is noted for its love for the moon. The Cakora, it is traditionally believed, rivets its eyes on the full moon as soon as it perceives it. If we likewise fix our eyes on a mental image of the Lord and get absorbed in that exhilarating vision, the Lord will feel compelled to meet us and bless us with His divine presence.

Let us emulate in this behalf the lofty example of the celebrated sage Sutīkṣṇa figuring in the Rāmacaritamānasa of Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa. When Sutīkṣṇa got absorbed in the loving thought of śrī Rāma, the Lord manifested Himself first in his heart and then before his eyes too. And if we develop the loving attitude of the Gopīs, there can be nothing like it.

Therefore, we should spare no pains to develop exclusive love and supreme reverence for the Lord, who eagerly awaits the blessed hour when love illumines our heart and He gets a suitable opportunity to take possession of it. We only profess our love and reverence for the Lord. But the Lord, who indwells all hearts, knows how sincere we are in our profession. Where there is genuine love and reverence for the Lord, the Lord does not lag behind in manifesting Himself. When the electric installation in a house is complete in every respect, there can be no delay in illuminating it. Even so, the Lord reveals Himself as soon as genuine love and reverence appear in our heart. All our unworthiness forthwith disappears and we are no longer regarded as unfit for His sight. Love and reverence are the only requisites for God-realization.

We attend religious discourses, enjoy the company of saints and godmen and follow a prescribed course of spiritual discipline under the belief that God-realization will take place sooner or later as a sequel to our spiritual practice. We, however, do not believe that the Lord is going to meet us this very moment. God-realization is really difficult for him who feels that judging by his record of past and present actions it is difficult for him to realize God. But the Lord surely meets him who feels in his heart of heart that the Lord has an extremely tender disposition and that He will undoubtedly see him in order to justify His own kind disposition. He who persuades himself that the Lord will surely meet him will be filled with enthusiasm and will find his life transforming every moment and his love for the Lord growing from more to more.

As has been pointed out above, the acquisition of all worldly things is doubtful; for it depends on one’s destiny, and because they are insentient. But an unpropitious destiny does not stand in the way of God-realization and there is no one so merciful and loving as God. Therefore, to entertain doubt about one’s meeting the Lord betrays total ignorance of His disposition and glory. A little thought would reveal that as a matter of fact meeting with none is so easy as that with the Lord. None of the created beings, whether animate or inanimate, possesses a disposition similar to the Lord’s and inanimate objects can have no desire to meet us. They can be had only through one’s Prārabdha (destiny).

The entire stock of compassion in this world taken together would hardly equal a single drop of the ocean of mercy that God is. So supremely comely compassionate is He. He is an inexhaustible storehouse of compassion, love and other virtues. Our love is insignificant as compared to His, which is immeasurable. Our knowledge of the true significance and secret of love is negligible as compared to His. It is sheer folly on our part to accuse such a God of delaying His visit to us. It is lack of love and reverence on our part which is responsible for delaying His visit.

Fancy couple of individuals loving each other, one of them being a mediocre Lover and the other an extraordinary one. The ordinary lover can afford to remain without his beloved. But how can the other, who knows in a special degree the true meaning and secret of love, tarry and help seeing his beloved? God is omnipotent and the inner controller of all. Hence, as has been pointed above, it is lack of love and reverence on our part which is responsible for delaying the Lord’s visit to us.

Therefore, let us strengthen our conviction that God exists and that He meets those who are anxious to meet Him. It is love alone that counts in His eyes. Intense longing to meet Him in all that is required. A thirsty man longing for water may or may not get water; for that depends on his destiny. The Lord, however, is sure to meet us if we have an ardent desire to see Him. When a man feels thirsty, he is put in mind of water again and again. He does not deliberately recall it; on the other hand, he cannot forget it due to his agonizing thirst. Similarly when thirst for God appears in us, He will automatically continue in our thought. The Lord surely meets him who remembers God in the same way as a thirsty man longing for water recalls water. Just as a hungry man is automatically reminded of food again and again, similarly, when we hunger for the Lord, He will automatically be remembered by us. But when there is no hunger for Him, how can He meet us? He who is running temperature loses his appetite due to that fever. In our case unbelief and impiety and impurity of mind occasioned by our past sins is the threefold fever which has dulled our appetite. The appetite of a man suffering from disinclination for food is roused only when he consults a physician and takes some medicine, an appetizing powder or digestive pills and so on, under his expert advice. In our case saints and godmen are physicians to be consulted and the scriptures are the standard books on medicine to be referred to. And the practice of Devotion consisting in the repetition of the Divine Name, meditation on His divine Form and action for his sake is the unfailing remedy. Just as the use of medicine is calculated to rouse one’s appetite, the practice of adoration and medicine and action for the sake of God is expected to rouse our appetite, i.e., to intensify our desire to see God. One feels inclined to undertake such practice by cultivating the fellowship of saints and godmen or where this is not possible by pursuing the study of sacred books. If we hear discourses on the excellence, glory, truth and secret of God from the lips of His devotees or read their accounts in sacred books and put faith in them, we can no longer help seeing God. The Lord is delaying His visit to us only so long as we bear separation from Him.

Therefore, we should constantly practise Nāma-Japa and meditation, cultivate the fellowship of saints and holy men and pursue the study of holy books and undertake action for the sake of God in a disinterested spirit with reverence and devotion.

 

God-realization through Self-discipline

The great sage PataÛjali has laid down eight limbs of Yoga for the edification and redemption of the soul, They are Yamas, Niyamas, Asanas, Prāṇāyāma, Pratyāhāra, Dhāraṇā, Dhyāna and Samādhi.* It goes without saying that their practice purifies the mind in the first instance and eventually leads to the attainment of JÛāna (spiritual enlightenment) resulting in final beatitude. Nay, man can be redeemed even through the practice in all their details of the first two limbs (Yamas and Niyamas) alone.

* यमनियमासनप्राणायामप्रत्याहारधारणाध्यानसमाधयोऽष्टावङ्गानि।

(Yoga-Darśana II. 29)

Yamas— Ahiṁsā (non-violence), Satya (truthfulness), Asteya (non-thieving), Brahmacarya (continence) and Aparigraha (non-acquisition of material possessions)—these five taken together constitute what they call Yamas.1 These five Yamas when practised with reference to all species of living beings, at all times and places and under all circumstances come to be known by the appellation of Mahāvratas!2 In Jain scriptures too these five Yamas have been referred to under the name of Mahāvratas. Now let us consider each of these separately.

2-अहिंसासत्यास्तेयब्रह्मचर्यापरिग्रहा यमा:।

(Yoga-Darśana II. 30)

3-जातिदेशकालसमयानवच्छिन्ना: सार्वभौमा महाव्रतम्।

(Yoga-Darśana II. 31)

Ahiṁsā—Total abstinence from Hiṁsā or injury to any living being in any form constitutes Ahiṁsā. Evils like Hiṁsā (violence) have been classified under various heads. First of all Hiṁsā has been classified under three categories: (1) Kr̥ta (done) (2) Kārita (get done) and (3) Anumodita (abetment). An injury inflicted by one’s own self is known as Kr̥ta; that which is caused through the instrumentality of another falls under the category of “Kārita”; while “Anumodita” Hiṁsā consists in countenancing its perpetration by another who does it of his own accord. All these three types of Hiṁsā are actuated by greed, anger or folly. In this way Hiṁsā can be subdivided under nine categories. Violence committed from some selfish motive is spoken of as actuated by greed; that which is motivated by hatred or malice has anger at its back; while that, which is committed through ignorance is actuated by folly. This ninefold violence can be further divided into twenty-seven types when classified again as Mr̥du (mild), Madhya (moderate), or Adhimātra (excessive). A simple injury falls under the category of “Mr̥du” Hiṁsā, a grievous injury can be termed as “Madhya” Hiṁsā; while a fatal injury constitutes what they call “Adhimātra” Hiṁsā. Similarly, there are many more categories of violence.

But if anyone refrains from homicide or cowslaughter but commits violence upon other species of living beings, his non-violence is only partial. Similarly, that non-violence is also partial which is observed only at sacred places but not elsewhere. In the same way if anyone practises violence on other days but abstains from it on a Saṅkrānti day (the day on which the sun passes from one zodiacal sign to another), the day of a solar or lunar eclipse and on other sacred days, such non-violence too is partial. Likewise, if someone does not practise violence except on the occasion of a wedding, funeral and so on, his non-violence too is only partial and not absolute or universal. Universal non-violence is only that which is observed irrespective of any species of living beings, place, time or occassion, i.e., which eschews violence completely. Therefore universal violence excludes all violence, whether mental, vocal or physical and perpetrated against any living being knowingly, unknowingly or through listlessness in anyway and in any measure at any time or place and on any account.

As in the case of violence, lying, theft, copulation and acquisition of material possessions too should be considered as capable of being classified under similar heads.

Satya—To relate an occurrence, to represent a situation or reproduce a talk precisely as it was observed, heard or understood without reserving or adding anything and without an effort to convey a different sense in spite of a faithful reproduction—such artless veracity of speech constitutes Satya or Truthfulness.

Telling a lie and instigating or encouraging anyone to tell a lie from greed, anger or folly in a slight, moderate or excessive degree—all this constitute a falsehood. Therefore, to make it a point never to tell a lie nor to instigate or encourage anyone to do so at any place, for any reason in the slightest degree, nor to give a false hint, nor to behave falsely or even to make a false resolve—in other words, to eschew all these varieties of falsehood for good and in everyway constitutes what goes by the name of “Satya” or Truthfulness.

Asteya—To make it a point never to appropriate to oneself any movable or immovable property such as land or soil, house, money, cattle etc., through mendacity, deceit, perfidy, fraud, violence or any other means constitutes “Asteya” or non-thieving.

Thieving or instigating or encouraging anyone to commit theft from greed, anger or folly in a small, moderate or large measure—all this constitutes theft. Therefore, to make it a point never to thieve nor to instigate or encourage anyone to commit a theft of any category at any place on any account through mind, speech or body in any measure constitutes what goes by the name of “Asteya” or non-thieving.

Brahmacarya—(Continence) Abstinence on the part of a man from passionately looking at, talking with, touching the person of, remaining closetted with, remembering, hearing the voice of or cutting jokes with a woman, indulging in sexual commerce and having any other contact with her constitutes what goes by the name of “Brahmacarya”. Brahmacarya on the part of a woman similarly excludes of these acts in relation to a man.

To indulge in coition and instigate or encourage anyone to do so from greed, anger or folly constitutes a stigma on Brahmacarya. Therefore, abstinence from indulging in any indecent practice with the hand or any other part of the body with reference to any human being or animal etc., at any place on any account and in anyway in the slightest degree and a vow not to use obscene language or even too harbour obscene thoughts in one’s mind or make an obscene gesture or incite or encourage anyone to do so all this constitutes the observance of “Universal Brahmacarya”.

Aparigraha—Refraining from acquiring with the intention of enjoyment, movable or immovable property in the form of luxuries and wealth, house, cattle etc., beyond what is barely necessary to keep one’s body and soul together constitutes what goes by the name of “Aparigraha”.

“Parigraha” consists in hoarding luxuries from greed, anger and folly in a small, moderate or large measure or inciting or encouraging others to do so. Therefore, refraining from hoarding or inciting or encouraging anyone to hoard even in a small measure any object of sense-enjoyment or any movable or immovable property on any account at any time or place in anyway, and making it a point not to ask anything of anybody or even crave for, or throw a hint about, anything—this constitutes what goes by the name of “Aparigraha”.

Niyamas—śauca (Purity), Santoṣa (Contentment), Tapas (Askesis), Swādhyāya (Scriptural Study) and ºśwara-Praṇidhāna (Surrender to God)—these five are known by the name of Niyamas.* Let us consider them separately now.

* शौचसंतोषतप: स्वाध्यायेश्वरप्रणिधानानि नियमा:।

(Yoga-Darśana II. 32)

śauca (Purity)—Purity is of two kinds—(1) external and (2) internal. External purity is subdivided into two categories—(i) śaucācāra (cleanliness of body) and (ii) Sadācāra (purity of conduct). Cleanliness consists in cleaning the body with clay and water, cleaning one’s house etc., by brooming and dusting it and in ensuring the purity of food by procuring it through rightfully earned money; while “purity of conduct” consists in lovingly and politely behaving with all, eschewing selfishness and egotism, as well as in maintaining an excellent conduct. This is external purity. Internal purity, on the other hand, consists in rooting out vices like egotism, sense of possession, passion, anger, greed, infatuation, attachment and aversion etc., abiding in the heart, through the practice of Japa, austerities, undertaking pilgrimages, vows and fasts etc., as well as through the performance of disinterested action and entertaining noble thoughts.

Santoṣa—To remain free from all desires, balanced in mind and cheerful, viewing the consequences—whatever they may be—while discharging one’s duties as well as pleasure and pain, gain and loss and favourable and unfavourable objects or circumstances as a benign dispensation of God—this constitutes what goes by the name of “Santoṣa”.

Tapas—To endure bodily and mental suffering when faced with it and to purify the body, mind and senses by discharging one’s sacred obligations and observing vows and fasts after subduing the mind and senses and becoming free from attachment and aversion—this constitutes what goes by the names of “Tapas”.

Swādhyāya—To read sacred books and the utterances of exalted souls—the study of which is conducive to the realization of God in one’s own desired form—with an eye to their meaning and inner sense—this constitutes what goes by the name of “Swādhyāya”.

ºśwara-Praṇidhāna—To recognize God as the supreme goal, the final resort, the supreme asylum, nay, one’s be-all and end-all after ascertaining with faith the excellences, glory, essential character and substance of God, who is Omnipotent and Omniscient and who is absolute Truth, Consciousness and Bliss solidified; to throw oneself entirely at His mercy, regarding Him as one’s Master, Supporter, Director, Protector and supreme Well-wisher, and become fearless; to obey Him considering everything as belonging
to Him; to remain ever contented taking every pleasurable and painful experience coming to one as a reward sent by God; to dwell at all times without interruption on His name, attributes, glory, substance and essential character with excessive reverence and love; and to dedicate one’s all and one’s own self to Him; this constitutes what goes by the name of “ºśwara-Praṇidhāna”.

It goes without saying that God can be realized through the purification of one’s mind brought about by the practice of these two limbs of Yoga—the Yamas and the Niyamas—alone. Nay, God can be realized by duly observing even a part of these limbs with reverence, devotion and application. The great sage PataÛjali says—

संतोषादनुत्तमसुखलाभ:।

(Yoga. II. 42)

“Through contentment one is able to attain happiness higher than which there is no other happiness.”

Similarly, God-realization can be attained through scriptural study alone—

स्वाध्यायादिष्टदेवतासम्प्रयोग:।

(Yoga. II, 44)

In the same way Samādhi in the form of cessation of the (outward) tendencies of the mind, which is said to follow from repeated effort and aversion (to the pleasure of sense) and which has been declared as conducive to ability in one’s own self, (i.e., Self-realization) is attained through Surrender to God-alone—

ईश्वरप्रणिधानाद्वा।

(Yoga. I. 23)

Besides, perfect Samādhi is possible through Surrender to God also—

समाधिसिद्धिरीश्वरप्रणिधानात्।

(Yoga-Darśana II. 45)

Nay, Japa of His Name and pondering over its connotation alone has been declared as conducive to Self-realization by brushing aside all impediments to the same.

तज्जपस्तदर्थभावनम्।

तत: प्रत्यक्चेतनाधिगमोऽप्यन्तरायाभावश्च।

(Yoga. I. 28-29)

The attainment of superlative happiness, realization of God in the form of one’s own choice, stability in the Self perfection of Samādhi, which is the same as the cessation of modifications of the mind and Self-realization all these are synonymous with final beatitude.

Thus, it has been proved that the due observance of even a part of the aforesaid limbs of Yoga—Yamas and Niyamas—is capable of redeeming the soul; for perfection in a part brings perfection in the whole as a matter of course. Therefore, grasping the true significance of the Yamas and Niyamas, we should betake ourselves to their due observance with reverence, devotion and diligence in a disinterested spirit.

 

Value of Faith and Reverence in God-realization

The Lord says in the Gītā—

सत्त्वानुरूपा सर्वस्व श्रद्धा भवति भारत।

श्रद्धामयोऽयं पुरुषो यो यच्छ्रद्ध:स एव स:॥

(XVII. 3)

“The faith of all men conforms to their mental constitution, O Arjuna. Man consists of faith; whatever the nature of his faith, he is verily that.”

Some people complain that they have been attending the discourses of saints for many years past and are also engaged in spiritual discipline according to their own poor lights and capacity, but are not progressing as they ought to. They are at a loss to understand the reason and desire to know what they should do in order to achieve progress in Sādhana. Our reply to them is that lack of faith and reverence alone hampers our progress in Sādhana. Therefore, pondering deeply on the question of reverence one should heighten one’s reverence. The following four are worthy of reverence—(1) God (2) Enlightened souls (3) Scriptures like the Gītā and (4) one’s own higher self. A man can easily attain blessedness if he develops consummate faith in and reverence for any one of these. The Gītā says—

श्रद्धावाँल्लभते ज्ञानं तत्पर: संयतेन्द्रिय:।

ज्ञानं लब्ध्वा परां शान्तिमचिरेणाधिगच्छति॥

(IV. 39)

“He who has mastered his senses, is exclusively devoted to self-discipline and is full of reverence attains Knowledge; having had the revelation of Truth, he immediately attains supreme peace (in the form of God-realization).”

From this it should be concluded that earnestness is the criterion of reverence, control over the mind and senses being the criterion of earnestness. For, a man’s earnestness in the matter of Sādhana is commensurate with his reverence and control over his mind and senses is commensurate with his earnestness.

I. FAITH IN GOD

Firm faith in and deep-rooted reverence for God consists in the belief that God exists, that He has been realized by many, is realized even at present and will continue to be realized in future as well, and that He is all-pervading, the knower of all hearts, omniscient, the inner controller of all, supremely compassionate, supremely loving, endowed with all transcendent virtues, capable of bringing the impossible into the region of possibility, omnipotent, birthless and immortal, the Supreme Person, the Supreme Reality and the Supreme Spirit. His realization eradicates all sufferings, vices, immoral conduct, evil addictions, sloth, error and ignorance of man, disengages him from the snare of mundane existence and enables him to attain supreme peace and supreme bliss, which has been referred to in the Gītā under the names of God-realization (XIII. 34), realization of Brahma, who is all peace (V. 24-25), attainment of immortality (XIII. 12), attainment of unending bliss (VI. 28), attainment of the imperishable supreme State (XV. 5), Brahma-realization (XIII. 30), attainment of the supreme Goal (VIII. 13; XIII. 28) and so on.

When a man develops devout faith in the aforesaid God as though He were manifest, whatever is done by him conforms to the Lord’s commandments and no action of his ever runs counter to His injunctions. If anyone’s actions run counter to His commandments, it should be concluded that the man has no faith even in the existence of God. For, faith and reverence do not appear in the heart of a man obsessed with doubt as to whether God exists or not, nay, if he does exist, whether He is open to realization, and if so whether He will lend Himself to his realization; again, if He comes to be realized by him, whether he will attain blessedness or not and finally whether He is rightly proclaimed as benevolent to all, omniscient, the dispenser of fruit to all, supremely loving and supremely compassionate. That is the reason why no progress is visible even though one attends the discourses of saints and remains engaged in self-discipline. Therefore we should cultivate firm faith in God combined with reverence and devotion. Not only do the śrutis, the Smr̥ti texts and the other śāstras bear testimony to the existence of God but there are strong logical grounds too to believe in it. Nay, He can even be directly perceived by means of spiritual endeavour. That of course depends on faith.

In the śukla Yajurveda we read—

ईशा वास्यमिदॸ सर्वं यत्किंच जगत्यां जगत्।

(XL, first half of verse 1)

“Whatever (movable or immovable) existence there is in the world, all that stands permeated by God.”

The Manusmr̥ti says—

प्रशासितारं सर्वेषामणीयांसमणोरपि।

रुक्माभं स्वप्नधीगम्यं विद्यात् तं पुरुषं परम्॥

(XII. 122)

“One ought to realize the Supreme Spirit which holds absolute sway over all, which is subtler even than the subtle, is refulgent and pure as gold and can be perceived through reason even in the dream state.”

The Gītā proclaims—

उत्तम: पुरुषस्त्वन्य: परमात्मेत्युदाहृत:।

यो लोकत्रयमाविश्य बिभर्त्यव्यय ईश्वर:॥

(XV. 17)

“Yet other than these is the Supreme Person, who, having entered all the three worlds, upholds and maintains all, and has been spoken of as the imperishable Lord and the Supreme Spirit.”

In the Viṣṇu-Purāṇa we read—

स ईश्वरो व्यष्टिसमष्टिरूपो

व्यक्तस्वरूपोऽप्रकटस्वरूप:।

सर्वेश्वर: सर्वदृक् सर्वविच्च

समस्तशक्ति: परमेश्वराख्य:॥

(VI. v. 86)

“That God (alone) constitutes the microcosm as well as the macrocosm. He (alone) is manifest as well as unmanifest. He is the Lord of all, All-witnessing and All-knowing. That All-powerful Being (alone) is termed as Parameśwara (the supreme Lord).”

Sage PataÛjali says in his Aphorisms on Yoga—

ईश्वरप्रणिधानाद् वा।

(Yoga-Sūtra I. 23)

“The state of super-conscious Absorption can as well be (speedily) attained through self-surrender to God.”

क्लेशकर्मविपाकाशयैरपरामृष्ट: पुरुषविशेष ईश्वर:।

(Ibid., I. 24)

“God is the foremost Person (the highest soul), who is untouched by the five Kleśas or sources of affliction (viz,. Nescience, Egoism, Attachment, Aversion and Fear of Death), (all the four types of) Karma (viz. virtuous, sinful, both virtuous and sinful and those which are neither virtuous nor sinful), the fruit of actions and the seeds of actions.”

तत्र निरतिशयं सर्वज्ञबीजम्।

(Ibid., I. 25)

“There exists in Him the seed of omniscience which is unsurpassed.”

पूर्वेषामपि गुरु: कालेनानवच्छेदात्।

(Ibid., I. 26)

“He is older even than the forbears of all, since He cannot be measured by Time.”

तस्य वाचक: प्रणव:।

(Ibid., I. 27)

“The word OË is His appellation.”

तज्जपस्तदर्थ भावनम्।

(Ibid., I. 28)

“Repetition of the aforesaid Name and contemplation on the substance denoted by It (viz., God) constitute a spiritual discipline in themselves.”

तत: प्रत्यक्चेतनाधिगमोऽप्यन्तरायाभावश्च।

(Ibid., I. 29)

“From the aforesaid (discipline) follow the elimination of all obstacles and Self-realization too.”

Now let us consider the logical grounds warranting faith in the existence of God. He who is the originator (source), maker, preserver and director of ether, air, fire, water and earth, the sun and the moon, the lunar mansions and the other stars and other numerous mighty phenomena is God; for there can be no effect without any cause. The movements of the sun and moon etc., cannot be efficiently, dextrously, consciously and properly controlled without a maker and controller. Even the smallest machine would perish without someone to control it. How, then, can this mighty machine of a universe persist without a controller? And if there is none to dispense the fruit of our good and evil actions no living being would be willing to suffer punishment for its own misdeeds of its own accord even as no offender is ever seen courting punishment for his offence of his own will in the absence of a ruler or government. Action itself being inanimate, it is incapable of punishing an animate being. Nor can Nature, which is also inanimate, reward or punish anyone according to its merits without the help of an animate being. An animate being alone can reward or punish anyone according to his or her deserts consciously and fairly. God of course is the principle animating of all conscious beings, the All-seeing witness, omniscient and omnipotent. This is in consonance with logic and sacred lore.

If someone denies the existence of God, it is presumptuous on his part to do so. Let him prove what he says. It may be urged by him in reply that “If the non-existence of God cannot be proved, there is no satisfactory proof for His existence either; hence it is doubtful whether God exists or not.” Let us, however, turn this doubt to account by intelligently bestowing our thought on this doubt. Suppose there is no God and yet there are people who believe in His existence. Now if really there is no God, He is equally non-existent for those who believe in Him as well as for those who do not. Neither does the believer stand to lose nor does the unbeliever stand to gain anything under such circumstances. Both stand on an equal footing. It is not that the unbeliever would get any reward for his unbelief or be able to realize God by denying His existence or that the believer will be punished or deprived of God-realization by believing in Him. There being no God, His realization is out of the question. On the other hand, if the belief of the believer comes out to be true, the latter would take to spiritual endeavour according to the divine commandments and will be rewarded with God-realization. Nay, rid of all trouble, obligations and sorrows, he will attain supreme peace and supreme bliss. The unbeliever, however, will remain deprived of all these and will have to suffer great affliction in the shape of the tortures of hell. Hence it is beneficial in everyway to believe in the existence of God even though it may be doubtful. Nay, believing in the existence of God, intelligent and thoughtful men should take refuge in Him with reverence, faith and devotion. In the Gītā the Lord says—

ईश्वर: सर्वभूतानां हृद्देशेऽर्जुन तिष्ठति।

भ्रामयन् सर्वभूतानि यन्त्रारूढानि मायया॥

तमेव शरणं गच्छ सर्वभावेन भारत।

तत्प्रसादात् परां शान्तिं स्थानं प्राप्स्यसि शाश्वतम्॥

(XVIII. 61-62)

“Arjuna, God abides in the heart of all creatures, causing them to revolve according to their Karma by His illusive power, seated as those beings are in the vehicle of the body. Take shelter in Him alone with all your being, O Arjuna! By His mere grace you shall attain supreme peace and the eternal state.”

II. FAITH IN AND REVERENCE FOR
AN ENLIGHTENED SOUL

We must also believe and take it on trust that there have been and still exist many an enlightened soul and many more will come to be in the future as well. Those exalted souls who attain God-realization by following the discipline of action, devotion or Knowledge are believed to be men of wisdom of a high order. The following have been declared in the Gītā as the characteristics of those that have attained the heights of God-realization through the discipline of Action—

प्रजहाति यदा कामान् सर्वान् पार्थ मनोगतान्।

आत्मन्येवात्मना तुष्ट: स्थितप्रज्ञस्तदोच्यते॥

दु:खेष्वनुद्विग्नमना: सुखेषु विगतस्पृह:।

वीतरागभयक्रोध: स्थितधीर्मुनिरुच्यते॥

(Gītā II. 55-56)

“Arjuna, when one thoroughly dismisses all cravings of the heart, and is satisfied in the self through (the joy of) the self, then he is called stable of mind. The sage whose mind remains unperturbed amid sorrows, whose thirst for pleasures has altogether disappeared, and who is free from passion, fear and anger, is called stable of mind.”

The characteristics of such exalted souls continue to be discussed in verses 57 and 58 as well as in many succeeding verses of Discourse II. They are further delineated in the following verses of Discourse VI—

जितात्मन: प्रशान्तस्य परमात्मा समाहित:।

शीतोष्णसुखदु:खेषु तथा मानापमानयो:॥

ज्ञानविज्ञानतृप्तात्मा कूटस्थो विजितेन्द्रिय:।

युक्त इत्युच्यते योगी समलोष्टाश्मकाञ्चन:॥

सुहृन्मित्रार्युदासीनमध्यस्थद्वेष्यबन्धुषु।

साधुष्वपि च पापेषु समबुद्धिर्विशिष्यते॥

(VI. 7—9)

“The Supreme Spirit established in the knowledge of the self-controlled man whose mind is perfectly serene in the midst of pairs of opposites, such as cold and heat, joy and sorrow, honour and ignominy. The Yogī whose mind is sated with JÛāna (Knowledge of Nirguṇa Brahma) and VijÛāna (Knowledge of manifest Divinity), who is unmoved under all circumstances, whose senses stand completely mastered, and to whom earth, stone and gold are all alike, is spoken of as a God-realized soul. He who looks upon well-wishers and neutrals as well as upon meditators, friends and foes, relatives and objects of hatred, the virtuous and the sinful with the same eye, stands supreme.”

The characteristics of those that have attained God-realization by treading the path of Devotion have been delineated in the following verses—

अद्वेष्टा सर्वभूतानां मैत्र: करुण एव च।

निर्ममो निरहंकार: समदु:खसुख: क्षमी॥

संतुष्ट: सततं योगी यतात्मा दृढनिश्चय:।

मय्यर्पितमनोबुद्धिर्यो मद्भक्त: स मे प्रिय:॥

(XII. 13-14)

He who is free from malice towards all beings, friendly and compassionate, rid of the sense of ‘I’ and ‘mine’, balanced in joy and sorrow, forgiving by nature, ever-contented and mentally united with Me, nay, who has subdued his mind, senses and body, has a firm resolve, and has surrendered his mind and reason to Me—that devotee of Mine is dear to Me.”

The description of these characteristics is continued still further in verses 15 to 19 of Discourse XII—

Even so the characteristics of those who have attained God-realization by treading the path of Knowledge have been delineated in the following verses—

विद्याविनयसम्पन्ने ब्राह्मणे गवि हस्तिनि।

शुनि चैव श्वपाके च पण्डिता: समदर्शिन:॥

इहैव तैर्जित: सर्गो येषां साम्ये स्थितं मन:।

निर्दोषं हि समं ब्रह्म तस्माद् ब्रह्मणि ते स्थिता:॥

(V. 18-19)

“The wise look with the same eye on a Brāhmaṇa endowed with learning and culture, a cow, an elephant, a dog, and a pariah too. Even here is the mortal plane conquered by those whose mind is established in unity; since the Absolute is untouched by evil and knows no distinction, hence they are established in the Absolute.”

समदु:खसुख: स्वस्थ: समलोष्टाश्मकाञ्चन:।

तुल्यप्रियाप्रियो धीरस्तुल्यनिन्दात्मसंस्तुति:॥

मानापमानयोस्तुल्यस्तुल्यो मित्रारिपक्षयो:।

सर्वारम्भपरित्यागी गुणातीत: स उच्यते॥

(XIV. 24-25)

“He who is ever established in the Self, takes sorrow and joy alike, regards a clod of earth, a stone and a piece of gold as equal in value, is possessed of wisdom, receives the pleasant as well as the unpleasant in the same spirit, and views censure and praise alike, he who is indifferent to honour and ignominy, is alike to the cause of a friend as well as to that of an enemy, and has renounced the sense of doership in all undertakings, is said to have risen above the three Guṇas.”

The characteristics of exalted souls of this category have also been described earlier in verses 22 and 23 of Discourse XIV as well as in other chapters. We should try to emulate the conduct of enlightened souls endowed with the characteristics mentioned above and should carry out their injunctions with reverence and devotion. The Lord says in the Gītā—

यद्यदाचरति श्रेष्ठस्तत्तदेवेतरो जन:।

स यत्प्रमाणं कुरुते लोकस्तदनुवर्तते॥

(III. 21)

“For whatever a great man does, that very thing other men also do; whatever standard he sets up, the generality of men follow the same.”

Even an ignorant man lacking in critical acumen can be redeemed by emulating in this way the conduct of enlightened souls as our ideal or by carrying out their injunctions with reverence and devotion. The Lord says in the Gītā—

अन्ये त्वेवमजानन्त: श्रुत्वान्येभ्य उपासते।

तेऽपि चातितरन्त्येव मृत्युं श्रुतिपरायणा:॥

(XIII. 25)

“Not knowing thus, other dull-witted persons, however, worship even as they have heard from others; and even those who are thus devoted to hearing, are able to cross the ocean of mundane existence in the shape of death.”

Of course it is true that such an enlightened soul can hardly be found even among millions of men. This is borne out by the Lord, who says in the Gītā—

मनुष्याणां सहस्त्रेषु कश्चिद्यतति सिद्धये।

यततामपि सिद्धानां कश्चिन्मां वेत्ति तत्त्वत:॥

(VII. 3)

“Hardly one among thousands of men strives to realize Me; of those striving Yogīs, again, some rare one (devoting himself exclusively to Me) knows Me in reality.”

From this it follows that such exalted souls do exist although they are rarely to be found. Exalted souls have existed in the world in all ages and exist even now; but they can be discovered only through reverence and love. God too cannot be realized without reverence and love. Hence, enlightened souls endowed with the aforesaid characteristics should be reverently sought for. How are we to recognize them?—it may be asked. Our reply to this is that, the existence of an exalted soul can be found out through a process of very powerful reasoning. For our purposes an exalted soul is he whose sight, touch and thought and talk with whom bring out in us the aforesaid characteristics of exalted souls. No other means of finding out an exalted soul is open to us. If any exalted soul declares himself to be such, no one would believe him; on the other hand, such a claim would detract from the reverence one may already have for the latter. And we do not possess the capacity to find out an exalted soul by applying the aforesaid standards; for, the presence of these characteristics can be judged by one’s own selfand not by others. Exalted souls are known to themselves alone. It is their inner characteristics alone that count. Outward characteristics can be exhibited even by a pretender through dissimulation. Exalted souls look on all with an equal eye; they are unaccountably kind and full of love. Their relations with others are never guided by self-interest. All their actions are intended for the good of the world. Even then they cannot be recognized by the ordinary run of men. Anyone who can know them a little can do so only through devotion and utmost reverence attended with faith. When a man has come to recognize an exalted soul, he should seek his presence and receive instruction from him with an inquisitive mind. The Lord says to Arjuna—

तद्विद्धि प्रणिपातेन परिप्रश्नेन सेवया।

उपदेक्ष्यन्ति ते ज्ञानं ज्ञानिनस्तत्त्वदर्शिन:॥

(IV. 34)

“Understand the true nature of that knowledge by approaching illumined souls. If you prostrate at their feet, render them service, and question them with an open heart. those wise seers of Truth will instruct you in that Knowledge.”

A striver who carries out with reverence and devotion the injunctions of an exalted soul worthy of reverence is spoken of as a reverent soul. More reverent than the latter is he who follows the indirect suggestions of an exalted soul. And still more reverent is he who acts up to the principles of an exalted soul and most reverent of all is he who follows the mind of an exalted soul. How can it be possible, it may be asked, to read the workings of the mind of an exalted soul ? Our reply to this is that the reverent soul who carries out the injunctions as well as the indirect suggestions of an exalted soul and also acts up to the latter’s principles comes to know his mind as well. Such a supremely reverent soul believes the words of an exalted soul even more than he does his own eyes. Suppose a house evidently built of bricks, lime and cement stands before him. Now if the exalted soul declares it to be of gold, the house will actually begin to appear to the reverent soul as built of gold. This is an illustration of supreme faith in the words of an exalted soul. To such a supremely reverent soul, the sight, speech and thought of an exalted soul, the touch of his feet and talk with him, everything is conducive to blessedness.

To quote noteworthy examples of supreme reverence from ancient history, Ekalavya, who was a Bhīla by birth, cherished the highest reverence for Droṇācārya, by virtue of which he outdid even Arjuna in archery (vide Mahābhārata, di., CXXXI). Satyakāma, son of Jabālā, had supreme faith in Maharṣi Gautama, son of Haridrumān, by carrying out whose command the former attained Brahma-realization (vide ChāndogyaUp., IV. 4—9) Upakosala cherished supreme reverence for Satyakāma and attained oneness with Brahma through the sacred fires by virtue of services rendered to his preceptor (Ibid., IV. 10—14). Again, king Drupada and his consort cherished supreme faith in the words of Lord śiva, by virtue of which a daughter, named śikhaṇḍī, born to them, was transformed into a boy and become known as śikhaṇḍī (vide Mahābhārata, Udyoga., CLXXXVIII to CXCII). We should likewise cherish supreme reverence for an exalted soul and derive benefit from him.

It is not easy to develop reverence even for an exalted soul endowed with the aforesaid characteristics; it is through the grace of God or through the good-will of saints alone that such reverence could be developed. On the other hand a setback in one’s faith is very easy. One’s reverence for an individual naturally declines through censoriousness, hearing of his faults or through the fellowship of unbelievers and irreverent souls. Therefore one should make it a point neither to expose the faults of another nor to hear of them nor even to hold them in one’s heart. Far from conducing to one’s benefit censoriousness etc., invariably lead to harm. By prying into others’ faults, hearing of and exposing them and by dwelling on them one allows them to take root in one’s heart, thus making them liable to appear in that very individual. Nay, the man who is reviled feels aggrieved at heart; hence it is sinful to expose the faults of others. He who mentions others’ faults also receives a share of those faults. This is with reference to ordinary human beings. As regards God and enlightened souls they are intrinsically free from faults. It is still more sinful to see fault in those who are innocent. Hence a seeker of blessedness should ever be on his guard against prying into others’ faults.

III. FAITH AND BELIEF IN THE Gºt AND OTHER SACRED BOOKS

Our faith in and reverence for the Gītā and other sacred books should lead us to believe that the teachings contained in them are based on truth are salutary. Convinced thus, and full of reverence, faith and devotion, one should try to translate into practice the kernel of the lessons taught in the Vedas and other sacred books written by the ¤Rṣis of old—such as Smr̥ti texts, the Itihāsa, the Purāṇas and the Gītā—lessons bearing on Devotion, Self-Knowledge, Dispassion, the discipline of Action, good Morals etc. For example take the case of śrīmad Bhagavadgītā, which is an epitome of all the śāstras. The Mahābhārata says—

सर्वशास्त्रमयी गीता सर्वदेवमयो हरि:।

सर्वतीर्थमयी गङ्गा सर्ववेदमयो मनु:॥

(Bhīṣma., XLIII. 2)

“The Gītā embodies the teachings of all the śāstras, śrī Hari represents all the gods in His person, the river Gaṅgā embodies all the sacred waters and the Code of Manu is the essence of all the Vedas.”

The Gītā is the cream of all sacred books as well as of all the Upaniṣads—

सर्वोपनिषदो गावो दोग्धा गोपालनन्दन:।

पार्थो वत्स: सुधीर्भोक्ता दुग्धं गीतामृतं महत्॥

“All the Upaniṣads are so many cows, while śrī Kr̥ṣṇa (the Son of a cowherd) is the milkman milking them. Arjuna is the calf (for whose sake the cows were originally milked), while a man of good understanding is the enjoyer of the rich milk extracted in the form of the Bhagavadgītā.”

Exceptionally great is the glory of the Gītā. The Lord is worshipped by means of a JÛāna-YajÛa (an offering in the form of spiritual wisdom) by him who makes a study of the Gītā with an eye to its meaning and underlying idea. The Lord Himself says—

अध्येष्यते च य इमं धर्म्यं संवादमावयो:।

ज्ञानयज्ञेन तेनाहमिष्ट: स्यामिति मे मति:॥

(XVIII. 70)

“Whosoever studies this sacred dialogue of ours (in the form of the Gītā), by him (too) shall I be worshipped through offering in the form of wisdom. Such is my mind.”

The Gītā is even holier than the holy Gaṅgā. He who takes a dip in the waters of the Gaṅgā gets emancipated himself; whereas a man taking a plunge into the wisdom of the Gītā is rendered capable of liberating others. He who makes an intelligent study of the Gītā is greater than he who repeats it mechanically everyday. He however, is the foremost of all, who moulds his life according to the teachings of the Gītā. Therefore, with a view to assimilating the teaching of the Gītā we should make a through study of this scripture. śrī Vedavyāsa says—

गीता सुगीता कर्तव्या किमन्यै: शास्त्रसंग्रहै:।

या स्वयं पद्मनाभस्य मुखपद्माद् विनिःसृता:॥

(Mahābhā. Bhīṣma., XLIII. 1)

“What need is there for a collection of other sacred books? One should make a thorough study of the Gītā alone with an eye to its meaning and underlying idea; for it has emanated from lotus-like lips of Lord Viṣṇu Himself (who bears a lotus sprung from His navel).”

One who has made a thorough study of the Gītā has no need for other śāstras. This does not mean anything derogatory to the other scriptures. It is only intended to show the superiority of the Gītā over the other scriptures.

In the Gītā-Māhātmya too we read—

गीताशास्त्रमिदं पुण्यं य: पठेत् प्रयत: पुमान्।

विष्णो: पदमवाप्नोति भयशोकादिवर्जित:॥

“The man who, having controlled his mind and senses, reads this sacred book of the Gītā is rid of fear, grief etc., and attains the supreme state of Viṣṇu.”

In the Gītā itself the Lord Himself declares it to be a scripture—

इति गुह्यतमं शास्त्रमिदमुक्तं मयानघ।

एतद्बुद्ध्वा बुद्धिमान्स्यात् कृतकृत्यश्च भारत॥

(Gītā XV. 20)

“O sinless Arjuna, in this way this most esoteric teaching has been imparted by Me; assimilating this man becomes wise and his mission (in life) is accomplished.”

The Lord extols in even more glowing terms the man who popularizes the Gītā in the world—

य इमं परमं गुह्यं मद्भक्तेष्वभिधास्यति।

भक्तिं मयि परां कृत्वा मामेवैष्यत्यसंशय:॥

(XVIII. 68)

“He who, offering the highest love to Me, shall impart this most secret teaching of Mine to My devotees, shall attain Me; there is no doubt about it.”

न च तस्मान्मनुष्येषु कश्चिन्मे प्रियकृत्तम:।

भविता न च मे तस्मादन्य: प्रियतरो भुवि॥

(XVIII. 69)

“Among men there is none who does me a more loving service than he; nor shall anyone be dearer to Me than he on this earth.”

The Gītā is replete with lessons on Bhaktiyoga, Karmayoga, JÛānayoga and good morals. Hence there is no room for doubt regarding the redemption of the man who, having heard discourses on, and studied and pondered the meaning of, the Gītā with faith, reverence and devotion, moulds his conduct according to it. Even the pious acts, on the other hand, of the man who having transgressed the bounds of morality set up by the śāstras, follows his own will, yield no good result—

य: शास्त्रविधिमुत्सृज्य वर्तते कामकारत:।

न स सिद्धिमवाप्नोति न सुखं न परां गतिम्॥

(Gītā XVI. 23)

“He, who, having cast aside the injunctions of the scriptures, acts according to his own sweet will, neither attains perfection nor the highest goal, nor even happiness.”

Some are endowed with natural faith, unmindful of the scriptural ordinance of the śāstras. On the subject of faith Arjuna asks a question in the opening verse of Discourse XVII as follows—“Is the action of the man who undertakes a sacrificial performance or worship etc., unmindful of the rules laid down for its conduct by the śāstras Sāttvika, Rājasika or Tāmasika? This question leads to the following further questions—(1) What type of Karma is that which does not follow the rules of conduct laid down by the śāstras and which is devoid of reverence? (2) What is the class of Karmas which follow the injunctions of the śāstras and are also inspired by reverence? (3) What is the category of Karmas that conform to the injunctions of the śāstras but are devoid of reverence? (4) What is the type of Karmas that are inspired by reverence but do not follow the injunctions of the śāstras? In reply to these questions the Lord says that actions which neither follow the injunctions of the śāstras nor are inspired by reverence are Tāmasika in character (vide Gītā XVII, 13); actions which are both inspired by reverence and conform to the injunctions of the śāstras can as well be Sāttvika as Rājasika. Actions which, while following the injunctions of the śāstras, are not only inspired by reverence but are done in a disinterested spirit too are Sāttvika in character (vide Gītā XVII. 11). Actions which follow the injunctions of the śāstras but are devoid of reverence are of no value (vide Gītā XVII. 28). Actions which are inspired by reverence but do not follow the injunctions of the śāstras can partake of the character of Sattva, Rajas or Tamas (vide Gītā XVII. 2).

Hence we should earnestly perform our duties in a disinterested spirit with reverence, faith and devotion according to the injunctions of the śāstras such as the Gītā.

IV. FAITH IN AND REVERENCE FOR ONE’S OWN SELF

First of all one should take it for granted that whatever act a man does—whether good, bad or mixed, he is sure to reap its fruit in this life as well as thereafter. Of course an act which is done in a disinterested spirit does not yield any fruit either here or hereafter. The Lord says—

अनिष्टमिष्टं मिश्रं च त्रिविधं कर्मण: फलम्।

भवत्यत्यागिनां प्रेत्य न तु संन्यासिनां क्वचित्॥

(Gītā XVIII. 12)

“Welcome, unwelcome and mixed—threefold, indeed, is the fruit that accrues hereafter from the actions of the unrenouncing; but there is none ever for those who have renounced (the fruit).”

From this it is proved that there is life even after death. He who realizes this cannot perpetrate a sinful act; for he knows that the fruit of good and evil acts committed in this life must be reaped—

अवश्यमेव भोक्तव्यं कृतं कर्म शुभाशुभम्।

नाभुक्तं क्षीयते कर्म जन्मकोटिशतैरपि॥

“One needs must reap the fruit of one’s good and evil deeds. An action can never be neutralized even in millions of years unless its fruit has been reaped.”

To believe and put faith in the sure existence of the soul even after death—this is what is meant by faith and belief in the Self. The soul surely exists after death. The Lord says—

न त्वेवाहं जातु नासं न त्वं नेमे जनाधिपा:।

न चैव न भविष्याम: सर्वे वयमत: परम्॥

(Gītā II. 12)

“In fact, there was never a time when I did not exist, or when you or these kings did not exist. Nor is it a fact that hereafter we shall all cease to be.”

देहिनोऽस्मिन् यथा देहे कौमारं यौवनं जरा।

तथा देहान्तरप्राप्तिर्धीरस्तत्र न मुह्यति॥

(Ibid. II. 13)

“Just as boyhood, youth and old age are attributed to the soul through this body, even so it attains another body, The wise man does not get deluded about this.”

न जायते म्रियते वा कदाचि-

न्नायं भूत्वा भविता वा न भूय:।

अजो नित्य: शाश्वतोऽयं पुराणो

न हन्यते हन्यमाने शरीर॥

(Ibid. II. 20)

“The soul is never born nor dies; nor does it become only after being born. For it is unborn, eternal, everlasting and ancient; even though the body is slain, the soul is not.”

In the Vedas too we read—

असुर्या नाम ते लोका अन्धेन तमसाऽऽवृता:।

ताॸस्ते प्रेत्याभिगच्छन्ति ये के चात्महनो जना:॥

“Men who kill their soul (by sacrificing their spiritual interests) repeatedly attain after death demoniac wombs as well as the frightful realms of the demons, which are enveloped in utter darkness in the form of suffering and sorrow.”

Reason too points to life after death. A human babe begins to cry as soon as it is born; for it is evident that it undergoes great suffering at the time of birth. Sometimes it even laughs, which leads us to conclude that it experiences joy too. The child does so only because it has cried whenever it underwent suffering and has laughed whenever it experienced joy in his past life. It was never taught to cry or laugh in its present life nor in its mother’s womb. This action is traceable to its habit of crying and laughing formed in its previous lives. This too proves the existence of a past life, and with that the existence of a future life too in the shape of his present birth is proved. When a mother gives suck to her babe, the milk of her breasts does not reach its stomach by her merely putting the nipple in its mouth. This requires the effort of the suckling to suck the milk. The babe is accustomed to this act of sucking from its previous birth. The babe also goes to sleep and at times even shudders from fear of death; for it has suffered the pangs of death in many a past life. All this he had no occasion to learn in its present life. Hence all these habitual actions prove the existence of a past life.

In this world some are born as birds or beasts and others as human beings; some lead a happy life, while others drag a miserable existence; some are rich and others destitute; some are healthy and others sick. If a child has acquired any of these states from his very birth, it cannot be attributed to any action on the part of the child, which has done nothing to deserve it in its present life. Therefore, the conclusion is forced upon us that this diversity is attributable to actions done in one’s previous lives; for it cannot be accounted for in any other way. All these arguments go to prove the existence of past and future lives.

Hence a seeker of one’s own spiritual wellbeing must reverently follow the path of devotion, spiritual knowledge and dispassion, cultivate noble virtues and practise good conduct in a disinterested spirit, giving up vices, immorality and evil addictions. By doing so one can attain blessedness in this very life. If something is found lacking and he dies beforehand, he is sure to attain his goal in the next birth.

The Lord says in the Gītā—

तत्र तं बुद्धिसंयोगं लभते पौर्वदेहिकम्।

यतते च ततो भूय: संसिद्धौ कुरुनन्दन॥

(VI. 43)

“Arjuna, there he (automatically) regains in that birth the spiritual insight of his previous births (i.e., the latencies of the Yoga of even-mindedness are roused in him), and through that he strives, with greater vigour than before, for perfection (in the form of God-realization).”

पूर्वाभ्यासेन तेनैव ह्रियते ह्यवशोऽपि स:।

जिज्ञासुरपि योगस्य शब्दब्रह्मातिवर्तते॥

(VI. 44)

“The other one (who takes birth in a rich family), though subject to the sense-objects feels drawn towards God by force of the habit acquired in his previous birth; nay, even the seeker of enlightenment on Yoga in the form of even-mindedness also transcends the fruit of actions performed with some interested motive as laid down in the Vedas.”

Even if it is urged by the objector that he does not believe in the authority of the scriptures nor in reason and that the existence of life after death is still open to question, it is wiser and more profitable to believe in such life even in the face of grave doubts; for even as it has been proved in the foregoing paragraphs on logical grounds that a believer in the existence of God positively stands to gain and that the unbeliever remains a loser, so does a believer in life after death too stands to gain and the unbeliever remains a loser.

Supposing one does not believe in the existence of God, exalted souls and the śāstras and even in reason, he should at least believe in the existence of his own soul. That a man should practise what he thinks good in his heart and should totally abstain from that which he considers undesirable, is also tantamount to believing in the existence of one’s soul. It is a matter of direct experience that a man who does wrong to you or ill-treats you rankles in your heart, nay, such a man falls in your estimation. From this we should take a lesson that one should never misbehave towards anyone. We directly perceive that another man who behaves well with us, renders service to us, gratifies us and acts in conformity with our mind or wishes well of us is looked upon by us as an exceedingly noble soul. This should teach a lesson to us that we too should likewise behave well towards others. If any other duty apart from this comes before us, we should ponder about it in our mind and completely abandon that which our conscience pronounces as undesirable and diligently pursue that which our inner self approves of.

For example, suppose you have to make a choice between continence, on the one hand, and adultery, on the other. If you refer to your own self in the matter you will get the answer that practice of continence is highly commendable, while adultery is an abominable act. Hence one should practise continence and should never take to adultery. Similarly suppose there is a conflict between gratifying others, doing a good turn to others, rendering service to others and doing a friendly act to another, on the one hand, and doing an unfavourable act to others, inflicting suffering on others and harming another, on the other. On referring to our inner self we shall get the reply that the act of gratifying others, doing a good turn to others, rendering service to others and doing a friendly act to another is highly commendable, while doing an ill turn to another, inflicting suffering on others and harming another, on the other hand, is most undesirable. Therefore one should do a good turn to others, render service to others, do a friendly act to another and gratify others and should never do an unfavourable act to another nor inflict suffering on others, much less harm others. Similarly if there is a choice between speaking truthful, agreeable and wholesome words, on the one hand, and uttering untruthful, harsh and harmful words, on the other, we shall on referring to our inner self get the reply that speaking truthful, agreeable and wholesome words alone is desirable and uttering untruthful, harsh and harmful words is highly deprecable. Hence, we should always take care to speak truthful, agreeable and wholesome words alone and should never utter untruthful, unpalatable and harmful words. Similarly in every other matter, one should ponder with an untainted and unbiased mind and should resolutely and diligently pursue that which is judged to be morally right and should altogether renounce that which is pronounced as undesirable. Even he who puts faith in his own self and after careful investigation as indicated above conducts himself in the world in a selfless, unattached and unegotistic way attains blessedness.

One can attain blessedness by putting faith in any of the above-mentioned four, viz., God, exalted souls, scriptures like the Gītā and one’s own self; much more can one attain blessedness by putting faith in all the four. Faith and reverence grow as a result of our estimating the worth of a thing and they dwindle if we entertain a low opinion about it. Hence a seeker of blessedness should value everything and should never despise anything. Says Nārāyaṇa Swāmī—

तज पर अवगुन नीर कौं छीर गुननि सौं प्रीति।

हंस संत की सर्वदा ‘नारायन’ यह रीति॥

‘Ignoring others’ faults like water contained in milk, they love to pick up good points like milk. Such is the way of good men, who resemble swans in this respect, says Nārāyaṇa.”

 

God-realization through Disinterested Action

Karmayoga (the Discipline of Action), Bhaktiyoga (the Discipline of Devotion) and JÛānayoga (the Discipline of Knowledge)—all these disciplines have been recognized as superior in their own place, easy to practise and quickly leading to blessedness. Occasion is taken here to write something about Karmayoga. Karmayoga is easy to practise. The Lord says in the Gītā—

ज्ञेय: स नित्यसंन्यासी यो न द्वेष्टि न काङ्क्षति।

निर्द्वन्द्वो हि महाबाहो सुखं बन्धात्प्रमुच्यते॥

(V. 3)

“The Karmayogī, who neither hates nor desires, should be ever considered a Saṁnyāsī (renouncer). For, Arjuna, he who has transcended the pairs of opposites is easily freed for ever from bondage.”

Nay, although the heights of JÛānayoga are difficult to ascend without the ladder of Karmayoga, the practice of the latter quickly leads to God-realization. The Gītā says—

संन्यासस्तु महाबाहो दु:खमाप्तुमयोगत:।

योगयुक्तो मुनिर्ब्रह्म नचिरेणाधिगच्छति॥

(V. 6)

“Without Karmayoga, however, Sāṅkhyayoga (or renunciation of doership in relation to all activities of the mind, the senses and the body) is difficult to attain; whereas the Karmayogī who keeps his mind fixed on God, reaches Brahma in no time, Arjuna.”

In the Gītā all the three disciplines—Bhaktiyoga, JÛānayoga and Karmayoga—have been declared by the Lord as independent of one another—

ध्यानेनात्मनि पश्यन्ति केचिदात्मानमात्मना।

अन्ये सांख्येन योगेन कर्मयोगेन चापरे॥

(XIII. 24)

“Some by meditation behold the Self (Paramātmā) in their own heart with the help of their pure reason; others by proceeding along the path of Knowledge; and others, again, by treading the path of Action.”

यत्सांख्यै: प्राप्यते स्थानं तद्योगैरपि गम्यते।

एकं सांख्यं च योगं च य: पश्यति स पश्यति॥

(V. 5)

“The supreme state which is reached by the Sāṅkhyayogī is attained also by the Karmayogī. Therefore, he who sees Sāṅkhyayoga and Karmayoga as one (so far as their result goes) really sees.”

Nay, Karmayoga has been pronounced as superior even to the practice of Bhaktiyoga and JÛānayoga—

श्रेयो हि ज्ञानमभ्यासाज्ज्ञानाद्‍ध्यानं विशिष्यते।

ध्यानात्कर्मफलत्यागस्त्यागाच्छान्तिरनन्तरम्॥

(XII. 12)

“Knowledge is better than practice (carried on without proper insight), meditation is superior to knowledge, and renunciation of the fruit of actions is even superior to meditation; for peace immediately follows from renunciation.”

संन्यास: कर्मयोगश्च नि:श्रेयसकरावुभौ।

तयोस्तु कर्मसंन्यासात् कर्मयोगो विशिष्यते॥

(V. 2)

“The Yoga of Knowledge and the Yoga of Action both lead to supreme bliss. Of the two, however, the Yoga of Action (being easier to practise) is superior to the Yoga of Knowledge.”

From this it follows that Disinterested Action is superior even to meditation on the Supreme Spirit. A comparison is made here between meditation on God divorced from renunciation of its fruit, on the one hand, and disinterested Action in the form of renunciation of the fruit of one’s action, devoid of meditation on God, on the other. There is renunciation of fruit in Disinterested Action; that is why it leads to supreme peace in the form of God-realization. Meditation on God in which there is no such renunciation of fruit can only accomplish the object for which the meditation is being done; it cannot lead to supreme peace. In praise of Karmayoga the Lord says—

अनाश्रित: कर्मफलं कार्यं कर्म करोति य:।

स संन्यासी च योगी च न निरग्निर्न चाक्रिय:॥

(VI. 1)

“He who does his duty without expecting the reward of actions is a Saṁnyāsī (Sāṅkhyayogī) and a yogī (Karmayogī) both. He is no Saṁnyāsī (renouncer) who has merely renounced the sacred fire; even so, he is no Yogī, who has merely given up all activity.”

Therefore Disinterested Action consists only in renouncing the fruit of and attachment to one’s actions* enjoined by the śāstras with due regard to one’s grade in society and stage in life.

* For a detailed exposition of Varṇāśrama-Dharma a reference is invited to the writer’s article under the caption “Humanity and Varṇāśrama-Dharma” published in the form of a serial in the foregoing four ordinary numbers of the “Kalyana-Kalpataru” consecutively.

A man should discharge the duties peculiar to his grade in society as well as all other duties enjoined by the śāstras with regard to his stage in life, the duties shared by him in common with others and those binding on him in times of distress, renouncing the fruit as well as attachment to them. This is how one is able to pursue the discipline of Disinterested Action. The essence of Disinterested Action has been given in a nutshell in the following verse of śrīmad Bhagavadgīta—

कर्मण्येवाधिकारस्ते मा फलेषु कदाचन।

मा कर्मफलहेतुर्भूर्मा ते सङ्गोऽस्त्वकर्मणि॥

(II. 47)

“Your right is to work only, but never to its fruit. Be not instrumental in your actions bear fruit, nor let your attachment be to inaction.”

Pointing out a man’s right and freedom to act by the words ‘कर्मण्येवाधिकारस्ते’ forming the first quarter of the above-quoted verse emphasis has been laid on the performance of duty—on the obligatory nature of duty. The words ‘मा ते सङ्गोऽस्त्वकर्मणि’ representing the fourth quarter also seek to lay emphasis on action alone by forbidding renunciation of action. The words ‘मा फलेषु कदाचन’ representing the second quarter are intended to show that man has no power over the fruit of actions, rather he is helpless in the matter; while by asking Arjuna not to allow his actions to be motivated by their reward in the words ‘मा कर्मफलहेतुर्भू:’ forming the third quarter of the above-quoted verse, the Lord enjoins him to give up the craving for pleasures of sense, attachment, the sense of possession and pride. The same idea has been elucidated in the following verse—

विहाय कामान् य: सर्वान् पुमांश्चरति नि:स्पृह:।

निर्ममो निरहंकार: स शान्तिमधिगच्छति॥

(II. 71)

“He who gives up all desires, and moves free from attachment, egotism and the thirst for enjoyment attains peace.”

In the above verse the Lord speaks of renouncing desire, the thirst for enjoyment, the sense of possession and egotism. Attachment and craving should be understood as covered by Spr̥hā (the thirst for enjoyment) and pride should be taken as included in egotism.

Side by side with the renunciation of attachment the Lord also speaks of remaining balanced in success and failure—

योगस्थ: कुरु कर्माणि सङ्गं त्यक्त्वा धनंजय।

सिद्‍ध्यसिद्‍ध्यो: समो भूत्वा समत्वं योग उच्यते॥

(II. 48)

“Arjuna, perform your duties dwelling in Yoga, relinquishing attachment and remaining indifferent to success and failure—even-mindedness is called Yoga.”

As a sequel to remaining balanced in success and failure, pairs of opposites like attachment and aversion, joy and grief etc., automatically disappear. Attachment and aversion alone are the root of all evils. Therefore the shaking off of attachment and aversion implies the shaking off of all evil propensities. Just as giving up egotism and the sense of mine implies the shedding of all evil propensities, similarly even when even-mindedness is acquired all evil propensities disappear. The Gītā emphasizes at some places the renunciation of attachment to actions as well as to the pleasures of sense (VI. 4); there relinquishment of the fruit of actions is covered by the renunciation of attachment to the objects of senses. At other places the Lord talks of renunciation of attachment alone (II. 48); in that context renunciation of the desire for fruit should be taken as covered by the renunciation of attachment, since from attachment alone springs up the desire for the objects of senses—सङ्गात् संजायते काम: (II. 62). Hence the renunciation of an effect is naturally implied by the renunciation of its cause. Just as the felling down of a tree is implied in the act of cutting its roots, so the renunciation of desire, thirst for enjoyment, craving etc., is naturally implied in the renunciation of attachment; for attachment alone is the root of all these. Hence God-realization can be attained by performing one’s duties giving up attachment to them. The Lord says to Arjuna—

तस्मादसक्त: सततं कार्यं कर्म समाचर।

असक्तो ह्याचरन् कर्म परमाप्नोति पुरुष:॥

(III. 19)

“Therefore, always efficiently do your duty without attachment. Doing work without attachment, man attains the Supreme.”

Similarly renunciation of the fruit of one’s actions too should be taken to cover the renunciation of attachment, the thirst for enjoyment, craving and the sense of possession. Generally people take to action for the sake of reward only. Hence the glory of giving up the reward of one’s duties has been particularly stressed in the Gītā (VI. 1).

Really speaking, actions which are performed regardless of their fruit and without attachment are no actions at all—

त्यक्त्वा कर्मफलासङ्गं नित्यतृप्तो निराश्रय:।

कर्मण्यभिप्रवृत्तोऽपि नैव किंचित् करोति स:॥

(IV. 20)

“He, who, having totally given up attachment to actions and their fruit, has got over the dependence on the world, and is ever satisfied, does nothing at all, though he may remain ever engaged in action.”

For such actions are a mere semblance of actions and not actions in the real acceptance of the term. This is what is meant by seeing inaction in action. It is the glory of such actions alone that has been referred to in IV. 18 as the glory of seeing inaction in action. This alone constitutes the secret of actions and here lies the skill in action;—

बुद्धियुक्तो जहातीह उभे सुकृतदुष्कृते।

तस्माद्योगाय युज्यस्व योग: कर्मसु कौशलम्॥

( II. 50)

“Endowed with even-mindedness one sheds in this life both good and evil. Therefore, exert yourself for this Yoga of even-mindedness. Skill in action lies in (the practice of this) Yoga.”

The idea is that Yoga consists in even-mindedness and this even-mindedness alone constitutes the skill in action. This even-mindedness alone enables a man to attain blessedness.

Here it may be urged that according to the scriptures emancipation is out of the question without Knowledge. Admitted that it is so; but that supreme knowledge of the reality of God, which has been glorified by the Gītā and other sacred books, dawns as a matter of course upon the consciousness of Karmayogī on his mind getting purified through the practice of Disinterested Action.

न हि ज्ञानेन सदृशं पवित्रमिह विद्यते।

तत् स्वयं योगसंसिद्ध: कालेनात्मनि विन्दति॥

(IV. 38)

“In this world, there is no purifying agency like Knowledge; he who has attained purity of heart through practice of Karmayoga automatically realizes it in the self in course of time.”

The use of the word ‘Swayam’ in the abovequoted verse clearly proves that the Karmayogī need not fall back upon any other discipline or individual, that he is directly able to attain the aforesaid knowledge.

Nay, all his sins being washed away as a sequel to such Disinterested Action, his mind too gets automatically purified—

यज्ञायाचरत: कर्म समग्रं प्रविलीयते॥

(Second half of IV. 23)

“....... All actions of that liberated man who works for the sake of sacrifice melt away.”

That is why—

कायेन मनसा बुद्‍ध्या केवलैरिन्द्रियैरपि।

योगिन: कर्म कुर्वन्ति सङ्गं त्यक्त्वाऽऽत्मशुद्धये॥

(V. 11)

“The Karmayogīs perform action only with their senses, mind, intellect and body as well, withdrawing the feeling of meum with respect to them and shaking off attachment, (simply) for the sake of self-purification.”

On the mind being purified he is able by virtue of such Disinterested Action to attain that supreme peace, characterized by being firmly established in God, which follows from God-realization—

युक्त: कर्मफलं त्यक्त्वा शान्तिमाप्नोति नैष्ठिकीम्।

(First half of V. 12)

“Offering the fruit of actions to God, the Karmayogi attains peace in the shape of God-realization.

In this way we have shown above how Karmayoga leads to speedy realization of Brahma (V.6), to God-realization (III. 19), to the dawn of spiritual enlightenment (IV. 38), to the dissolution of merit and sin (IV. 23), to the purification of the mind (V.11) and to the attainment of peace (II. 71; V. 12), as also how Karmayoga is easy to practise (V. 3) and how it excels all other disciplines (V. 2; VI. 1; XII. 12). Besides this the performance of sacrifices, the practice of austerities and the undertaking of other noble pursuits enjoined by the śāstras have been declared by the Lord as conducive to the purification of men of discrimination.

यज्ञदानतप:कर्म न त्याज्यं कार्यमेव तत्।

यज्ञो दानं तपश्चैव पावनानि मनीषिणाम्॥

(XVIII. 5)

“Acts of sacrifice, gift and austerity should not be relinquished, they must be performed at all events. For sacrifice, gift and austerities performed by wise men purify the heart.”

Nay, the Lord has pronounced it to be his settled opinion that sacrificial performances, charity, austerities and other pursuits enjoined by the śāstras must be undertaken giving up attachment and their fruit—

एतान्यपि तु कर्माणि सङ्गं त्यक्त्वा फलानि च।

कर्तव्यानीति मे पार्थ निश्चितं मतमुत्तमम्॥

(XVIII. 6)

“Therefore, Arjuna, My considered and best opinion is that these acts of sacrifice, gift and penance, and all other duties must be performed relinquishing attachment and fruit.”

While engaging in action a man invariably thinks of the return which it is expected to yield. In this way a man’s actions are invariably actuated by self-interest. This is what they call an interested motive. Giving up such interested motives a man should, on the other hand, think what good his action will bring to the entire living creation; for altruism constitutes the highest virtue, while maleficence to others is a sin.*

* Says Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa—

परहित सरिस धर्म नहिं भाई।

परपीड़ा सम नहिं अधमाई॥

(Rāmacaritamānasa VII. l. 1)

Hence for him whose heart is set on the good of others nothing is difficult to achieve in this world—

परहित बस जिन्ह के मन माहीं।

तिन्ह कहुँ जग दुर्लभ कछु नाहीं॥

(Rāmacaritamānasa III. XXX. 5)

Pointing out the characteristics of exalted souls, Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa says the same thing in the Gītā too—

लभन्ते ब्रह्मनिर्वाणमृषय: क्षीणकल्मषा:।

छिन्नद्वैधा यतात्मान: सर्वभूतहिते रता:॥

(V. 25)

“The seers whose sins have been washed away, whose doubts have been dispelled by Knowledge, whose mind is firmly established in God and who are actively engaged in promoting the welfare of all beings, attain Brahma, who is all Peace.”

Therefore with the movements of our mind, speech and body we should perform such deeds alone as are conducive to the good of others, and should refrain from such actions as are not conducive to the good of others. Nay, we should never do a thing which is injurious to others. On the other hand, we should devote our life to pursuits which are conducive to the supreme good of others—constitute the highest service to them. To cater for the mundane pleasure or sensual gratification of others is service, whereas to do a thing which rebounds to the spiritual benefit of another constitutes the highest form of service to the latter. If, however, a man renders any kind of service whatsoever to another in a disinterested spirit, such service is the highest service done by him. To render the highest service to all and sundry in a disinterested spirit while remaining devoted to the supreme good of all should be the paramount duty and supreme goal of a seeker of blessedness.

Therefore giving up the sense of possession, attachment, pride and self-interest with respect to all worldly objects and enjoyments, to his body and all his actions, a man should do well to strive till his last breath to perform whole-heartedly with his mind, body, money and men his duties enjoined by the śāstras in the shape of sacrificial performances, charity, austerities, visiting sacred places, observing sacred vows and fasts, practising the five forms of abstinence (Yamas) and the five religious observances (Niyamas), service to his parents, behaving well with others and doing a good turn to the afflicted and forlorn, nay, to the entire living creation.

God-realization is attained even through disinterested action alone and much more so if Bhakti (Devotion) is added it. The Lord says in śrīmad Bhagavadgītā—

श्रेयो हि ज्ञानमभ्यासाज्ज्ञानाद् ध्यानं विशिष्यते।

ध्यानात् कर्मफलत्यागस्त्यागाच्छान्तिरनन्तरम्॥

(XII. 12)
