“Knowledge is better than practice (carried without proper discernment), meditation on God is superior to knowledge, and renunciation of the fruit of actions is superior even to meditation; for peace immediately follows from renunciation.”

The idea is that knowledge is superior to repeated practice without discernment, repeated study of sacred books or any other spiritual practice repeatedly pursued. But that scriptural knowledge alone which is not accompanied by such practice is superior as compared to that practice which does not follow from scriptural knowledge. Meditation on God is superior to scriptural knowledge devoid of such meditation, and not to direct or immediate knowledge. And renunciation of the fruit of actions is superior to meditation which is not disinterested. In other words, disinterested action or action which is free from longing for its reward is superior to meditation on God practised from some interested motive; for the act of self-abnegation involved in renouncing the reward of actions leads immediately to lasting peace. He attains that very peace which follows from God-realization. Hence disinterested action is superior to all; that is why renunciation of the fruit of actions has been declared as superior even to meditation. The renunciation of the fruit of actions referred to in the aforesaid verse, is not accompanied by meditation; for meditation on God stands comprised in Devotion to God, which is unquestionably superior to everything else. But disinterested action, even when it is not accompanied by meditation, is superior to meditation, which is not characterized by renunciation of the fruit of actions but is carried on from some interested motive. This constitutes the discipline of Disinterested Action unmixed with Devotion.

Verse 19 of Discourse III of śrīmad Bhagavadgītā too speaks of Disinterested Action unmixed with Devotion. God-realization has been declared in that verse as following from renunciation of attachment. The Lord says to
Arjuna—

तस्मादसक्त: सततं कार्यं कर्म समाचर।

असक्तो ह्याचरन्कर्म परमाप्नोति पूरुष:॥

(III. 19)

“Therefore, always efficiently do your duty without attachment. Doing work without attachment, man attains the Supreme.”

Disinterested action as inculcated in the foregoing verse is not accompanied by Devotion and the striver is enjoined in this verse to do his obligatory duties incessantly. The verse therefore implies that the striver should incessantly do his duty in a detached spirit at all times. The absence of attachment rules out desire as a matter of course; for—

सङ्गात्संजायते काम:।

(Gītā II. 62)

“From attachment proceeds desire.”

Where attachment, the cause, is absent, desire, which is the effect, is out of the question.

Similarly the opening verse of Discourse VI as well speaks of Disinterested Action unmixed with Devotion. Says the Lord—

अनाश्रित: कर्मफलं कार्यं कर्म करोति य:।

स संन्यासी च योगी च न निरग्निर्न चाक्रिय:॥

(VI. 1)

“He who does his duty without expecting the fruit of actions is a Saṁnyāsī (Sāṅkhyayogī) and a Yogī (Karmayogī) both. He is no Saṁnyāsī (renouncer) who has merely renounced the sacred fire; even so, he is no Yogī, who has merely given up all activity.”

A man who is really actuated by lofty motives, who cherishes the spirit of renunciation in his heart, nay, whose heart is illumined by the light of wisdom, who inwardly practises Yoga and meditation on the Supreme Spirit has not been declared unfit for the title of a Saṁnyāsī (recluse) or Yogī.

Even so at some places the Gītā speaks of people renouncing attachment and at others of those eschewing desire. There is no mention of Devotion accompanying such renunciation. Even such Karmayoga (which is not accompanied by Devotion) is conducive to Liberation. There is a reference to this type of Karmayogī even in verses 11 and 12 of Discourse V of the Gītā. The Lord says—

कायेन मनसा बुद्‍ध्या केवलैरिन्द्रियैरपि।

योगिन: कर्म कुर्वन्ति सङ्गं त्यक्त्वात्मशुद्धये॥

(V. 11)

“The Karmayogīs perform action only with their senses, mind, intellect, and body as well, withdrawing the feeling of meum in respect of them and shaking off attachment, simply for the sake of self purification. Their mind getting purified thereby, they attain God-realization.”

The Lord clearly mentions this in the following verse—

युक्त: कर्मफलं त्यक्त्वा शान्तिमाप्नोति नैष्ठिकीम्।

अयुक्त: कामकारेण फले सक्तो निबध्यते॥

(V. 12)

“Offering the fruit of actions to God, the Karmayogī attains peace that follows from God-realization; whereas he who works with a selfish motive, being attached to the fruit of actions through desire, gets tied down.”

In this way the type of Disinterested Action which has been referred to in the Gītā as having no connection whatsoever with Devotion is Disinterested Action purely as such (having nothing to do with Devotion). In other words, action which is free from desire and attachment and is performed as a matter of duty is Disinterested Action purely as such. Even such action makes for redemption of the soul and more so when it is accompanied by Devotion.

Now let us consider what that action is. That work or duty which has been assigned by the śāstras to a man or woman belonging to a particular grade of society or stage in life, if performed in a disinterested spirit, leads to his or her salvation. The duty of a married woman, for example, is to follow the code of conduct prescribed for a devoted wife. She can realize God merely by following such conduct in a disinterested spirit. Similarly a man whose parents are living can attain the highest goal merely by serving them in a disinterested spirit. Service to his parents is the highest duty of such a man; all other duties are secondary to him. In the Manusmr̥ti we read—

त्रिष्वेतेष्विति कृत्यं हि पुरुषस्य समाप्यते।

एष धर्म: पर: साक्षादुपधर्मोऽन्य उच्यते॥

(II. 237)

“The entire duty of a man is fully accomplished when these three (viz., the parents and the preceptor) are propitiated through service. For such service is the paramount duty of a man, directly devolving on him; every other duty is declared to be subordinate.”

A reference to our sacred books will show what a large number of women have been redeemed by following the conduct prescribed for a devoted wife. While reproducing the advice tendered by Anasūyā (the consort of sage Atri) to Sītā in the Araṇya-Kāṇḍa of śrī Rāmacaritamānasa, Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa too says—

एकइ धर्म एक ब्रत नेमा।

कायँ बचन मन पति पद प्रेमा॥

***

बिनु श्रम नारि परम गति लहई।

पतिब्रत धर्म छाड़ि छल गहई॥

(III. iv. 5, 9)

“Devotion through body, speech and mind to her lord’s feet is the only duty, sacred vow and penance for a woman ........ The woman who sincerely takes a vow of fidelity to her husband easily attains the highest state.”

Devotion to one’s husband has likewise been glorified at numerous places in our sacred books. Anasūyā herself followed the course of conduct prescribed for a devoted wife so faithfully that Brahmā, Viṣṇu and śiva (the Creator, Protector and Destroyer respectively of the universe) took descent from her in their partial being. There are many other such examples of wifely devotion forthcoming in our sacred books.

Similarly a man can attain the highest state by serving all; of course he must be actuated by a disinterested spirit. He must not have even a trace of selfishness of any kind whatsoever lurking anywhere in his mind. Our duty in everyway is to serve all. He too who undertakes sacrificial performances and other noble pursuits and practices charity and austerities in a disinterested spirit is sure to attain blessedness.

It is the duty of a śūdra (a member of the labouring class) to serve all. He attains the highest state by serving the members of all the other three classes (viz., the priestly class, the warrior class and the mercantile class). But he too must be actuated by a disinterested spirit. A Vaiśya (a member of the mercantile class) can similarly be redeemed by carrying on the pursuit of agriculture, rearing cows, and trade in a disinterested spirit; but not so the Vaiśya who is a seeker of wealth and undertakes any of these pursuits for the sake of hoarding money. That Vaiśya alone is actuated by a disinterested spirit, who has no interest of his own and is actuated by the motive of gratifying all by undertaking the pursuits of agriculture, trade or rearing the bovine race, and who seeks no return whatsoever even after gratifying all in the aforesaid manner. If while serving others you seek honour, praise or prestige, your service is not disinterested. If you feel flattered to find others bestowing honour on or showing respect to you or singing your praises, although you do not seek honour, praise and respect nor do you aim at receiving these, even then you are actuated by a selfish motive. Such gratification in a way detracts from your disinterested spirit. A man should altogether banish from his mind the idea that others should compliment him by calling him a man of highly disinterested spirit and utterly selfless. Such compliments should not sound pleasing to his ears; on the other hand he should feel abashed and pained at heart to hear them.

In the course of every undertaking a man should reflect and see for himself whether he has any craving or attachment behind that pursuit. Where there is attachment or craving at the back of some undertaking it should be taken for granted that the pursuit is not actuated by a disinterested spirit. In any case it is supremely good to serve others, even if such service is not disinterested. Even if a man renders good offices to others, and practises charity and beneficence in anyway and does so, say, out of fear or shame or with a craving for honour and praise, his doing so is far better than not doing all this. Of course an action which is disinterested is indeed most exalted.

A man should always remain wide awake. A craving of any kind whatsoever detracts from one’s disinterested spirit. There is a world of difference between an action done from an interested motive and a disinterested action. They are poles asunder. Bearing this in mind a man should act in a disinterested spirit.

Before a man undertakes to do a thing the thought strikes his mind as to what he will gain thereby. This is what we call an interested motive. Therefore a man should keep in view the benefit that is likely to accrue to the world at large from an action of his. The good of the greatest number should be his ideal and he should seek no return whatsoever for this benefit; he should rather not accept a return even if it is offered to him. It should, however, be borne in mind that in some cases even the acceptance of a return turns out to be a disinterested act. If, for example, a man bestows a gift in a disinterested spirit as a matter of duty on a hermit, saint, Brāhmaṇa or any other householder as something due to him, to accept such a gift in a disinterested spirit does good to the donor and the donee both. To take another example, suppose a hermit is in need of a piece of cloth to cover his body with and a donor pressingly offers a piece to him and suppose the hermit has no craving for it for all practical purposes but feels inclined to pull on with his worn out and tattered cloth; yet if, when pressed by the donor, he accepts his offer for the donor’s gratification and pleasure, no blame attaches to him. If the donor is likely to feel pained by the thought that the hermit has declined his offer, because the donor is good for nothing, to accept his offer just to spare him that pain is as good as declining it. To take a third example, suppose a man sends to a householder friend of his some fruits and other articles saying that they were received as a present in connection with the wedding of his own son, which he would fain share with his friend, and if the man sending those fruits etc., is likely to feel pained at their non-acceptance by his friend, their acceptance would be as good as declining the offer. To take a fourth example, suppose we call on a friend of ours and the latter proceeds to show hospitality to us and if he is likely to be pained by our not accepting it, our acceptance of his hospitality would be as unobjectionable as to decline it.

To accept for one’s own gain a service done by another is surely an act prompted by self-interest; whereas it is free from blemish to accept it for the latter’s pleasure. In the course of every undertaking we should always be on our guard to see that we have no selfish motive behind it. A selfish motive is the only noxious factor. Suppose we take work from a son, wife or servant and if they feel pained by our declining to take such service from them, to take work or service from them is as unobjectionable as not to accept it. Before accepting any gift or service from another we should consider whether we accept that gift or service for his or her gratification or for our own gratification. We are actuated by an interested motive only where there is some self-interest behind our work. Such an interested motive is a blot on one’s disinterested spirit. But there is nothing wrong in our accepting that which is really our legitimate due. The best thing is that we accept something not for our own pleasure but for the gratification of others. This constitutes our disinterested spirit.

Moreover, it should be particularly borne in mind that we conceive a liking for whatever comes to us reacting agreeably on our mind, while we loathe that which reacts unfavourably on our mind. But where there are attraction and aversion at play, no disinterested spirit is there. Therefore likes and dislikes should be altogether eschewed while undertaking any and every pursuit. It does good to one’s soul even to move among the pleasures of sense provided one is free from likes and dislikes. To enjoy the pleasures of sense in a legitimate way without conceiving a liking or zest for them is what is meant by moving among them. Taking food and drink, for example, is a legitimate act inasmuch as it is necessary and desirable for keeping the body and soul together. The expression “moving among the objects of sense” smacks of attachment; hence we feel compelled to add a note of warning that the phrase “moving among the objects of senses” should not be taken in the sense of enjoying the pleasures of sense with a feeling of attachment or zest. An act of moving among the pleasures of sense such as taking food and drink in a legitimate way for the sake of bare maintenance of body without any craving whatsoever is an example of what they call a disinterested spirit. We read in the Gītā—

रागद्वेषवियुक्तैस्तु विषयानिन्द्रियैश्चरन्।

आत्मवश्यैर्विधेयात्मा प्रसादमधिगच्छति॥

प्रसादे सर्वदु:खानां हानिरस्योपजायते।

प्रसन्नचेतसो ह्याशु बुद्धि: पर्यवतिष्ठते॥

(II. 64-65)

“But the self-controlled striver, while utilizing the various sense-objects through his senses, which are disciplined and free from likes and dislikes, attains placidity of mind. With the attainment of such placidity of mind, all his sorrows come to an end; and the intellect of such a person of tranquil mind, soon withdrawing itself from all sides, becomes firmly established in God.”

Such is the glory of a disinterested spirit. There is no attachment, no desire, no likes and dislikes in the act of moving among the objects of senses referred to in the above-quoted verses. The striver mentioned here performs legitimate acts only as a matter of duty. He moves among the objects of senses by his senses alone without attachment or aversion. Such conduct on his part ensures his salvation. The Lord says—

विहाय कामान्य: सर्वान्पुमांश्चरति नि:स्पृह:।

निर्ममो निरहंकार: स शान्तिमधिगच्छति॥

(II. 71)

“He who gives up all desires, and moves free from attachment, egotism and thirst for enjoyment attains peace.”

In the verse preceding this as well we read—

स शान्तिमाप्नोति न कामकामी॥

“He in whom all enjoyments merge themselves attains peace; not he who hankers after such enjoyments.”

Hence the verse quoted above also refers to the attainment of the same real peace.

In this way the salvation of a man is ensured even through mere disinterested action, much more so when Devotion is added to it.

 

Exclusive Surrender—An Easy

Means to God-realization

To surrender oneself in everyway exclusively at the feet of the Lord is a most convenient method of attaining God-realization for all mankind. For those, however, who are incapable of pursuing the Path of Knowledge, the Path of Action, the Eightfold Path of Yoga and other such courses of discipline, to fall at the mercy exclusively of God is profitable in everyway, the Lord being supremely loving, the greatest befriender and unaccountably compassionate. He never looks into the faults of His servants. śrī Bharata says—

“But the Lord never takes into account the faults of His devotees, being a friend of the humble and the most tender of disposition.”*

* जन अवगुन प्रभु मान न काऊ।

दीनबंधु अति मृदुल सुभाऊ॥

(Rāmacaritamānasa, Caupāī 3 of Uttara-Kāṇḍa)

Therefore the Lord rids him of all fear, who surrenders himself to Him. The Lord Himself says—

सकृदेव प्रपन्नाय तवास्मीति च याचते।

अभयं सर्वभूतेभ्यो ददाम्येतद् व्रतं मम॥

(Vālmīki-Rāmāyaṇa VI. xviii. 33)

“I rid him of fear from all created beings, who, having come to Me for protection (only) once, saying ‘I am Yours’, seeks fearlessness; such is My vow.”

The Lord does not renounce anyone, however great a sinner one may be. The Lord says in śrī Rāmacaritamānasa—

“I will not abandon even him who is guilty of having killed tens of millions of Brāhmaṇas, if he comes to Me for protection.”*

* कोटि बिप्र बध लागहिं जाहू।

आएँ सरन तजउँ नहिं ताहू॥

(V. xliv. 1)

Therefore, for the beatitude of a man who is destitute of any Sādhana or spiritual discipline Self-surrender to the Lord is the only simple course within his easy reach. The Lord says in the Gītā too—

मां हि पार्थ व्यपाश्रित्य येऽपि स्यु: पापयोनय:।

स्त्रियो वैश्यास्तथा शूद्रास्तेऽपि यान्ति परां गतिम्॥

(IX. 32)

“Arjuna, womenfolk, Vaiśyas (members of the trading and agriculturist classes), śūdras (those belonging to the labouring and artisan classes) as well as those of vile birth (such as the pariah), whoever they may be, taking refuge in Me, they too attain the supreme goal.”

A man attains the highest goal through self-surrender (devotion) to the Lord, even if he belongs to the lowest grade of society. The Lord says to Uddhava—

भक्त्याहमेकया ग्राह्य: श्रद्धयाऽऽत्मा प्रिय: सताम्।

भक्ति: पुनाति मन्निष्ठा श्वपाकानपि सम्भवात्॥

(XI. xiv. 21)

“I, the beloved Self of the righteous, can be captured (only) through exclusive Devotion coupled with reverence. Devotion concentrated on Me absolves even the pariahs (who cook and eat the flesh of dogs) from the stigma attaching to their birth.”

Similarly he who is devoid of good conduct speedily becomes virtuous and attains supreme peace. The Lord says to Arjuna—

अपि चेत्सुदुराचारो भजते मामनन्यभाक्।

साधुरेव स मन्तव्य: सम्यग्व्यवसितो हि स:॥

(Gītā IX., 30)

“Even if the vilest sinner worships Me with exclusive devotion, he should be accounted a saint; for he has rightly resolved (He is positive in this belief that there is nothing like devoted worship of God).”

क्षिप्रं भवति धर्मात्मा शश्वच्छान्तिं निगच्छति।

कौन्तेय प्रति जानीहि न मे भक्त: प्रणश्यति॥

(IX. 31)

“Speedily he becomesvirtuous and secures lasting peace. Know it for certain, Arjuna, that My devotee never falls.”

It is all the more easy for those who belong to the higher grades of society and are of noble conduct to get so redeemed.

Therefore one should seek the protection in everyway of the Lord alone, who is unaccountably loving and supremely compassionate. What is meant by seeking the protection in everyway of the Lord is found mentioned in the Gītā as follows—

मन्मना भव मद्भक्तो मद्याजी मां नमस्कुरु।

मामेवैष्यसि युक्त्वैवमात्मानं मत्परायण:॥

(IX. 34)

“Fix your mind on Me, be devoted to Me, adore Me and salute Me; thus linking yourself with Me and entirely depending on Me, you shall come to Me.”

In the foregoing verse the Lord prescribes four modes of seeking the protection of the Lord. The import of these should be understood. The following are the four principal instruments in our possession—the mind, reason, the senses and the body. By saying ‘मन्मना भव’ the Lord calls upon us to direct our mind to Him in the same way as the cowherdesses of Vraja thought ceaselessly of Him alone. śrīmad Bhāgavata says—

या दोहनेऽवहनने मथनोपलेप-

प्रेङ्खेङ्खनार्भरुदितोक्षणमार्जनादौ ।

गायन्ति चैनमनुरक्तधियोऽश्रुकण्ठ्यो

धन्या व्रजस्त्रिय उरुक्रमचित्तयाना:॥

(X. xliv. 15)

“Blessed are the women of Vraja, who celebrate Him in song while milking their cows, pounding the corn, churning their curds, plastering their floor (with cowdung etc.), rocking the cradles, lulling their crying babes, sprinkling and sweeping their houses and attending to other such duties with a mind full of love and their throat choked with tears, (nay, all) whose movements are inspired by the thought of śrī Kr̥ṣṇa (who is no other than Lord Viṣṇu, who took long strides while measuring the three worlds during His descent as the Divine Dwarf).”

To focus the mind on the Lord as indicated above is what is meant by seeking His protection with the mind.

By saying ‘मद्भक्तो भव’ the Lord asks us mainly to direct our reason to Him. Intellectually grasping the reality and inwardness of His virtues and glory, which consist in cognizing that He alone is our greatest friend, extremely compassionate, supremely loving and benevolent, that none can compare with Him, and that the Lord alone is worth seeking the protection of, worthy of being recognized as one’s own and worth loving, one should treat Him alone as one’s own and bestow one’s exclusive and unalloyed love on Him alone in the same way as Prahlāda did. True Devotion merely consists in loving the Lord exclusively. In the Bhakti-Sūtras of sage śāṇdilya we read—

सा परानुरक्तिरीश्वरे।

(Sūtra 2)

“Bhakti (Devotion) is supreme love for God.”

Directing one’s reason as pointed out above is what is meant by seeking the protection of the Lord through reason.

By saying ‘मद्याजी भव’ (offer worship to me), the Lord seeks to convey that worship should be offered to Him recognizing the fact that He alone is the enjoyer of all sacrificial performances, austerities and other pursuits—‘भोक्तारं यज्ञतपसाम्’ (Gītā V. 29), that He is the Self seated in the heart of all created beings, O Arjuna (‘अहमात्मा गुडाकेश सर्वभूताशयस्थित:’ Ibid., X. 20) and that He alone pervades all
(‘मया ततमिदं सर्वम्’ Ibid., IX. 4). Later on the Lord Himself points out how worship should be offered to Him—

यत: प्रवृत्तिर्भूतानां येन सर्वमिदं ततम्।

स्वकर्मणा तमभ्यर्च्य सिद्धिं विन्दति मानव:॥

(Gītā XVIII. 46)

“Man attains the highest perfection by worshipping through his own natural duties Him from whom the tide of creation has streamed forth and by whom all this universe is pervaded.”

To gratify all through service recognizing the Lord as indwelling all living beings and offering respect, attentions and honour with humility and love by means of one’s organs to all as so many images of God and guarding against pride and selfishness is what is meant by worship here. This is how comprehensive worship is offered to the Lord. While doing so one is mainly required to devote all one’s senses to the Lord. Hence by saying ‘मद्याजी भव’ the Lord calls upon us to seek His protection by the senses.

The mandate ‘मां नमस्कुरु’ (offer salutation to Me) is intended to call upon us to offer our body to the Lord. The idea is that one should have exclusive dependence on the Lord. One should rejoice, taking everything that the Lord does—whether it is agreeable or repulsive to our mind, in other words, whatever event, object or situation confronts us, no matter whether it is to our liking or distasteful to us—as a dispensation of God or a reward from Him and should continue to perceive His grace in that dispensation of His at every step. It is but natural to perceive His grace and find solace in an event, object or situation agreeable to one’s body, senses, mind, reason, scripture or creed; but he alone correctly comprehends the true import and inwardness of His compassion, who perceives His grace even in that which is repellent to him. The gratification and solace that one enjoys in an agreeable situation is experienced by all; but that is something Rājasika (adulterated). That solace alone which continues as a result of one’s recognizing adversity as helpful to God-realization is Sāttvika (or unmixed). On being confronted with an ailment distasteful to one’s mind or loss of a wife, progeny, fortune etc., a man should feel that even though the situation is distressing on the face of it, it has been brought about by God for his benefit and that it will conduce to his good in many ways. By undergoing suffering a man is purged of his sins and his heart gets purified. Misfortune puts one in mind of God. Once we recognize that the suffering undergone by us is an outcome of sin our proneness to sin gets arrested. Forbearance in suffering enhances our power of endurance. The solace one derives from such recognition is also Sāttvika. The mental peace in the form of God-realization or the supreme peace which transcends the three Guṇas or modes of Prakr̥ti is, however, different from all these types of Sāttvika peace. He who follows a course of discipline after recognizing this truth finds his delight in the pleasure of God, recognizing every event, object or situation that confronts him as a dispensation or decree of the Lord, and ever remains merged in peace. That is the highest peace. Such a man remains balanced in favourable and adverse circumstances; nay, he is completely rid for ever of anxiety and fear, delight and grief, partiality and prejudice, lust and anger, greed and infatuation and all other morbid affections and he continues to enjoy gratification, peace and joy everywhere at every step.

The aforesaid devotee of the Lord has already dedicated his mind, intellect, senses and bodywholly to the Lord; the grace of the Lord alone continues to be perceived by him in every action that comes to be performed through the instrumentality of his body, there being no doership in him. Therefore, whatever is done through his agency is done according to a hint from the Lord alone. Whatever the Lord gets done through him according to His own will, he remains equipoised in the midst of joy and sorrow, gain and loss, victory and defeat—whatever comes to him as a consequence of his action. Whatever is done through the agency of a man who has offered his all to the Lord is in conformity with the injunctions of the śāstras; no action which is repugnant to the spirit of the śāstras or useless is done by him; for all his activities are controlled by the Lord. Hence all the actions of that man are akin to the pastimes of the Lord. They are highly beneficial for the world too inasmuch as they are exemplary in character. Hence other man get liberated only by following them. It is with reference to such an exalted soul that the Lord says—

यद्यदाचरति श्रेष्ठस्तत्तदेवेतरो जन:।

स यत्प्रमाणं कुरुते लोकस्तदनुवर्तते॥

(Gītā III. 21)

“For whatsoever a great man does, that very thing other men also do; whatever standard he sets up, the generality of men follow the same.”

In short it should be understood that he continues to perceive at every step the infinite and unbounded grace of the Lord in every event, object or circumstance that confronts him against his will (by the will of Providence) or through the will of another and even in all actions that are voluntarily done through the agency of his body. Realizing the presence of the Lord in all objects and beholding His pastimes in all actions, he continues to perceive His boundless grace and remains unaffected, equipoised, gratified, cheerful and tranquil under all circumstances.

To devote one’s mind, intellect, senses, body and all exclusively to the Lord in the aforesaid manner is what is meant by taking refuge solely in Him. To take refuge solely in the Lord and exclusive devotion to Him are, theoretically speaking, just the same. In the Gītā, while talking of surrender, the Lord speaks of Devotion as a principal constituent of Surrender. In the verse analyzing the constituents of surrender, quoted in the previous issue, for example, the Lord refers to Devotion as a principal constituent by calling upon Arjuna to become a devotee. Similarly, in verses 54-55 of Discourse XI of the Gītā, the Lord speaks of exclusive Devotion in the following words—

भक्त्या त्वनन्यया शक्य अहमेवंविधोऽर्जुन।

ज्ञातुं द्रष्टुं च तत्त्वेन प्रवेष्टुं च परंतप॥

(XI. 54)

“Through single minded devotion, however, I can be seen in this form (with four arms), and known in essence and even entered into, O valiant Arjuna.”

मत्कर्मकृन्मत्परमो मद्भक्त: सङ्गवर्जित:।

निर्वैर: सर्वभूतेषु य: स मामेति पाण्डव:॥

(XI. 55)

“Arjuna, he who performs all his duties for My sake, depends on Me, is devoted to Me, has no attachment, and is free from malice towards all beings, reaches Me.”

In order to point out what is meant by exclusive Devotion in the latter of the two verses the Lord speaks of Surrender as a principal constituent of Devotion by characterizing a devotee inter alia as मत्परम:—(thoroughly dependent on the Lord). From this it should be concluded that Exclusive Surrender and Exclusive Devotion are, really speaking, one and the same.

A devotee of God who takes refuge solely in the Lord as aforesaid is rid of fear in everyway and at every place. He fears none. The messengers of Death and others dare not even approach him. In the Viṣṇu-Purāṇa Bhīṣma says—

किंकरा: पाशदण्डाश्च न यमो न च यातना:।

समर्थास्तस्य यस्यात्मा केशवालम्बन: सदा॥

(III. vii. 38)

“Neither Yama (the god of punishment) nor his servants, noose and staff, nor even his tortures prevail over him whose mind ever hangs on śrī Kr̥ṣṇa (the Ruler even of Brahmā and Lord śiva).”

Recapitulating in this context to the highminded Nakula the instructions given by Yama to his messengers, Bhīṣma points out in some detail the characteristics of a devotee, which are worth studying, pondering and imbibing. Neither Yama (the god of punishment) nor his messengers have any access to the place where such a devotee of the Lord is breathing his last, Bhīṣma says—

स्वपुरुषमभिवीक्ष्य पाशहस्तं

वदति यम: किल तस्य कर्णमूले।

परिहर मधुसूदनप्रपन्नान्

प्रभुरहमन्यनृणामवैष्णवानाम्॥

(Ibid., III. vii. 14)

“Looking intently on his servant carrying a noose in his hand, Yama, they say, whispers to him—Shun those who have sought for protection of Lord Viṣṇu (the Slayer of the demon Madhu). I am the ruler (only) of other men, who are not devoted to Lord Viṣṇu.”

हरिममरवरार्चिताङ्घ्रिपद्मं

प्रणमति य: परमार्थतो हि मर्त्य:।

तमपगतसमस्तपापबन्धं

व्रज परिहृत्य यथाग्निमाज्यसिक्तम्॥

(Ibid., III. vii. 18)

“Even as one would pass by a fire fed with ghee, slip past the man who bows down reverentially, regarding Him as the highest truth, to Lord śrī Hari—whose lotus-feet are worshipped by the foremost of immortals—and who has been rid of all bondage occasioned by sin.”

कलिकलुषमलेन यस्य नात्मा

विमलमतेर्मलिनीकृतस्तमेनम्।

मनसिकृतजनार्दनं मनुष्यं

सततमवेहि हरेरतीवभक्तम्॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 21)

“Know the man to be a very great devotee of Lord śrī Hari, whose mind has not been soiled with dirt in the shape of the impurities of the Kali age, endowed as he is with an untarnished intellect, and who has eternally established in his mind Lord Viṣṇu (Who is solicited by all men).”

कनकमपि रहस्यवेक्ष्य बुद्‍ध्या

तृणमिव य: समवैति वै परस्वम्।

भवति च भगवत्यनन्यचेता:

पुरुषवरं तमवेहि विष्णुभक्तम्॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 22)

“Know that jewel among men to be a devotee of Lord Viṣṇu, who on perceiving gold belonging to another even in a lonely place actually looks upon it through his intellect as (no more valuable than) a blade of grass and has his mind exclusively set on the Lord.”

विमलमतिरमत्सर: प्रशान्त:

शुचिचरितोऽखिलसत्त्वमित्रभूत:।

प्रियहितवचनोऽस्तमानमायो

वसति सदा हृदि तस्य वासुदेव:॥

वसति हृदि सनातने च तस्मिन्

भवति पुमाञ्जगतोऽस्य सौम्यरूप:।

(Ibid. III. vii. 24-25)

“The All-pervading Lord Viṣṇu ever dwells in the heart of a man who is endowed with an unsullied intellect, who is free from jealousy and perfectly tranquil, who is upright in his conduct, who has come to be the friend of all creatures, who speaks agreeable and wholesome words, and from whom pride and deceit have disappeared (for good). And when that Eternal Being takes up His abode in the heart, the man assumes a gentle aspect in the eyes of the world.”

यमनियमविधूतकल्मषाणा-

मनुदिनमच्युतक्तसमानसानाम्।

अपगतमदमानमत्सराणां

त्यज भट दूरतरेण मानवानाम्॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 26)

“O valiant one, avoid from a respectable distance the presence of men whose sins have been washed away through the practice of the five Yamas and the five Niyamas, whose mind remains attached to the immortal Lord day in and day out and from whom pride and jealousy have departed (for ever).”

हृदि यदि भगवाननादिरास्ते

हरिरसिशंखगदाधरोऽव्ययात्मा।

तदबमघविघातकर्तृभिन्नं

भवति कथं सति चान्धकारमर्के॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 27)

“If Lord śrī Hari—who has no beginning and knows no decay, (nay) who carries a sword, conch and a mace (in His hands)—abides in the heart, sins attaching to it are dispelled by the Lord who destroys the sins of His devotees. How can darkness exist so long as the sun shines ?”

सकलमिदमहं च वासुदेव:

परमपुमान् परमेश्वर: स एक:।

इति मतिरचला भवत्यनन्ते

हृदयगते व्रज तान् विहाय दूरात्॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 32)

“Slip away shunning from a distance those who are endowed with a firm conviction about the immortal Lord dwelling in their heart that the whole of this (visible) universe as well as themselves are the same as the one Supreme Person and the Supreme Ruler, the All-pervading Lord Viṣṇu.”

कमलनयन वासुदेव विष्णो

धरणिधराच्युत शंखचक्रपाणे।

भव शरणमितीरयन्ति ये वै

त्यज भट दूरतरेण तानपापान्॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 33)

“Keep away, O gallant one, at a respectable distance those sinless men who utter the following prayer—O lotus-eyed Lord Viṣṇu. O All-pervading Acyuta, O Supporter of the Earth, O Lord carrying a conch and a discus in Your hands, be my refuge.”

वसति मनसि यस्य सोऽव्ययात्मा

पुरुषवरस्य न तस्य दृष्टिपाते।

तव गतिरथ वा ममास्ति चक्र

प्रतिहतवीर्यबलस्य सोऽन्यलोक्य:॥

(Ibid. III. vii. 34)

“Neither you nor I, whose prowess and might stand frustrated by the discus of the Lord, have any access to the range of vision of that jewel among men in whose mind the said undecaying Lord abides. He (really) belongs to other (ethereal) realms.”

Such is the exalted glory of exclusive and unalloyed Devotion (Self-surrender) to the Lord. Therefore, applying our mind, intellect, senses, body and all exclusively to the Lord in the aforesaid manner and perceiving His boundless grace at every step, we should remain extremely delighted and supremely gratified in His pleasure alone. The highest goal can thereby be attained most speedily. It is for this reason that sage Kavi, the chief among Yogīs, speaks as follows—

कायेन वाचा मनसेन्द्रियैर्वा

बुद्‍ध्याऽऽत्मना वानुसृतस्वभावात्।

करोति यद् यत् सकलं परस्मै

नारायणायेति समर्पयेत् तत्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata XI. ii. 36)

“Whatever he does with his body, tongue, mind or senses, reason or intellect or by force of his inherited nature, he should consecrate all as an offering to the supreme Lord Nārāyaṇa.”

 

Love All Equally in a Disinterested Spirit for God-realization

God is all love and consciousness. Although love, beloved and lover are stated to be distinct terms and entities, the reality is that God alone is manifest in all the three forms. When a lover of God attains God, he gets absorbed in Him; he is no longer recognized as distinct from God. God alone is the beloved of all, while Love constitutes His very being. It is just the same whether we say that Love alone stands manifested in the triple form of love, beloved and lover or that God alone exists in these forms—even as earrings, necklace, bangles and so on have distinct names and forms only, while essentially all is gold.

यथा सोम्यैकेन लोहमणिना सर्वं लोहमयं विज्ञातॸस्वाद् वाचारम्भणं विकारो नामधेयं लोहमित्येव सत्यम्।

(Chāndogya. VI. i. 5)

Says Uddālaka to his son, śwetaketu—

“My good son, just as when the one substance, viz., gold (the best of all metals) is known, everything made of gold is fully known; for a modification is only a name, hanging on speech alone. Gold alone is real.”

Similarly, so far as common parlance goes, love, beloved and lover are distinct terms and concepts. Really speaking, however, Love and Love alone exists and this Love is the same as God, spiritual and transcendental. Earthly love is a mere reflection of a ray of this Love, which is synonymous with God. Hence it should not be termed as love but should be designated as attachment or affection.

Exclusive and unmixed love for God makes for speedy God-realization. Exclusive love is that which is not directed towards anyone other than God; while ‘unmixed’ Love is that which is free from any other desire—is fully disinterested. For developing this genuine love strivers should look upon all as so many manifestations of God. In the Gītā the Lord says—

बहूनां जन्मनामन्ते ज्ञानवान्मां प्रपद्यते।

वासुदेव: सर्वमिति स महात्मा सुदुर्लभ:॥

(VII. 19)

“In the very last of all births, the man of realization worships Me, realizing that all this is God. Such a great soul is very rare.”

If it is not possible to recognize all as so many manifestations of God, one should at least conclude that the Lord is present in all. Says the Lord—

यो मां पश्यति सर्वत्र सर्वं च मयि पश्यति।

तस्याहं न प्रणश्यामि स च मे न प्रणश्यति॥

(VI. 30)

“He who sees Me (the Universal Self) present in all beings, and sees all beings existing within Me, never loses sight of Me, nor do I lose sight of him.”

If we fail to arrive at this conclusion either, we can develop love for God and attain God-realization even by rendering service to all taking everything as belonging to Him. The Lord says in the Gītā—

यत: प्रवृत्तिर्भूतानां येन सर्वमिदं ततम्।

स्वकर्मणा तमभ्यर्च्य सिद्धिं विन्दति मानव:॥

(XVIII. 46)

“Man attains the highest perfection by worshipping through his own natural duties Him from whom the tide of creation has streamed forth and by whom all this universe is pervaded.”

Therefore, if we love all in a disinterested spirit viewing all alike, it is as good as loving God Himself. It is natural for exalted souls of a high order to look upon all with the same eye, while a striver has to develop this even-mindedness through effort and culture. Hence a striver should emulate such exalted souls. The even-mindedness of exalted souls has been described as follows in the Gītā—

विद्याविनयसम्पन्ने ब्राह्मणे गवि हस्तिनि।

शुनि चैव श्वपाके च पण्डिता: समदर्शिन:॥

(V. 18)

“The wise look with the same eye on a Brāhmaṇa endowed with learning and culture, a cow, an elephant, a dog and a pariah too.”

‘Recognizing as equal’ is what is meant by ‘Samadarśana’ in the abovequoted verse. What is inculcated in the above verse is ‘Samadarśana’ and not ‘Samavartana’ (treating alike); for it is not even possible to treat all alike. Treatment can be meted out only according to rules of propriety. The milk of a cow alone is fit for human consumption; that of a bitch or a she-elephant cannot be used as human food. An elephant or horse alone can be used for riding, not a cow nor a dog. The removal of night-soil can be got done only by a sweeper and not by a learned and pious Brāhmaṇa. Grass can be given as food only to a cow or an elephant and not to a dog, a pariah or a Brāhmaṇa. Again we assign to our head the same place of honour as we do to a Brāhmaṇa in the Hindu society, to our arms a place corresponding to the position of a Kṣatriya, to the thighs a place similar to the position occupied in the Hindu social structure by a Vaiśya and to our feet a position corresponding to the status of a śūdra. We bow our head at the feet of or join our palms before a man to whom we intend to show special reverence; and we join our palms by way of apology before a man whom we happen to touch with our foot by mistake. Even though we use our limbs differently in this way, our feeling of mineness and love for each one of them is just the same—it does not vary in anyway. Similarly, even though we should treat all differently according to their deserts, our feeling of identity with them, our consciousness of their being so many manifestations of God and our feeling of love for them should be uniform. Exalted souls naturally cherish the same feeling towards all living beings; hence they alone are worthy of eulogy. Says the Lord in the Gītā—

सुहृन्मित्रार्युदासीनमध्यस्थद्वेष्यबन्धुषु।

साधुष्वपि च पापेषु समबुद्धिर्विशिष्यते॥

(VI. 9)

“He who looks upon well-wishers and neutrals as well as mediators, friends and foes, relatives and objects of hatred, the virtuous and the sinful with the same eye stands supreme.”

Ordinary men bear ill-will towards an enemy and cherish love for their friend. A friend evokes love and an enemy evokes hatred. A man whose mind is set on piety evokes love, while a sinner evokes hatred. One’s relations and kinsmen evoke love, while others evoke hatred or a feeling of indifference. Exalted souls who have realized God, however, always cherish the same feeling towards all; they never make any distinction in the case of any individual. Hence we should love all equally in a disinterested spirit.

Love which is bestowed on any living being whatsoever in a disinterested spirit, in obedience to the injunctions of God, with a view to God-realization or earning His pleasure or for His sake alone results in God-realization itself. Hence to recognize all as so many manifestations of God or to look upon God as present in all or to regard everything as belonging to God—whichever of these three ideals is agreeable to an individual, whichever evokes his or her reverence and catches his or her fancy, is supreme for him or her. Adopting that very ideal, he or she should love all.

Some time back it was seen that strivers got pleased and transported with joy to see a fellow striver and thought highly of him. They never belittled him. Their fellow striver appeared to them as excelling them in devotion, spiritual enlightenment, dispassion, virtues and good conduct and they wished to see these transplanted in themselves. Nowadays, however, many people are censorious towards their fellow strivers and see faults alone in them; this is evidently a road to degradation. That is why a majority of strivers do not make headway in their spiritual endeavour.

Being a concept of God alone, this entire cosmos is just the same as God. Therefore, if, recognizing all as so many manifestations of God, we serve all in an unegotistic and disinterested spirit, it is no doubt a most excellent spiritual discipline. If, however, we fail to recognize all as so many manifestations of God, the notion that God is all-pervading as ether is of course quite logical and consistent with the findings of the scriptures, and we should naturally conceive all living beings, nay, the whole of the animate and inanimate creation as permeated by God. He who comes to recognize this can never do any harm to another; on the other hand, he naturally begins develop even-mindedness and love towards all. It is certain that ill-will towards another grows only when we are censorious towards him or her and love follows as a matter of course where there is good opinion about another. Whatever living being comes before us, to say nothing of man, we should feel delighted recognizing it to be permeated by God. Faults we should see only in ourselves in order to correct ourselves. Faults do not stand our gaze. We should entertain a good opinion only about others, and should not be censorious towards them. We stand much to lose by being hyper critical about others. Faults are like filth. A cloth or limb gets polluted on coming into touch with filth. If we see the faults of others with our eyes, our eyes get polluted; if we hear about them with our ears, our ears get defiled; if we talk of others’ faults with our tongue, our tongue gets polluted and if we dwell on others’ faults with our mind, our mind gets defiled. Getting polluted by contact with others’ faults in this way one becomes dirty and goes down in the scale of human evolution.

Nay, there are many other evils in mentioning, hearing, seeing and dwelling on others’ faults—

(1) If we entertain a bad opinion about others we begin to hate them and our self-esteem grows, which is exceedingly harmful.

(2) By reviling others and hearing them being reviled we outrage the feelings of the man whom we revile or hear being reviled and thereby incur sin.

(3) As a sequel to our dwelling on, seeing, hearing and speaking of others’ faults the impressions of those faults get imprinted on our heart in the form of seeds which develop in due course into a tree and make of us an abode of those very faults.

(4) By criticizing, hearing and speaking of others’ faults we have to share the sin of that sinner, or in other words, to share the consequences of that sin.

Taking all these facts into consideration a man would be well-advised never to observe, criticize, ponder, speak or hear of others’ faults, vices, immoral conduct or evil addictions; for all these actions are sinful, most harmful and degrading. Hence a seeker of beatitude should eschew them in everyway; for so long as these evil habits persist hatred alone must grow, love for all being out of the question. Love is possible only when a good opinion is entertained about others.

Even if a man loves anyone with an interested motive, he achieves his end in the world, enjoys fame and attains heaven after death. If, however, love is extended equally to all in a disinterested spirit, God-realization itself follows.

If anyone loves us, we must reciprocate his love under all circumstances; for, to say nothing of man, even birds and beasts love those who love them. Strivers, however, should love all. They should love even those who do not love them. On the other hand, they should bestow love alone even on those who hate them.

In order to cultivate love towards all and develop it one should serve all in an unegotistic and disinterested spirit, to gratify them in everyway and, entertaining a good opinion about them, to perceive, ponder, mention and celebrate their virtues alone. Thereby love can be evoked in the highest and the lowest individual. Love is automatically evoked in God through service to Him and singing His praises, in exalted souls through service to them and singing their praises and in a common man through service to him and celebrating his virtues. The long and short of it is that if we render services to, gratify and celebrate the real virtues even of a wicked, sinful and vile man, love can be evoked even in him, much more in others. Nay, we should render good offices even to him who illtreats us, harms us or does a disservice to us. Even though Dhr̥tarāṣṭra, for example, contrived to burn Kuntī alive with her sons in the city of Vāraṇāvata and repeatedly wronged her. Kuntī, for her part, served Dhr̥tarāṣṭra and his wife, Gāndhārī, all their life, forgetting their wrong-doing (vide Mahābhārata, di., CXLIV—CXLVII; sramavāsika., I-II, XVXIX). Following this conduct of Kuntī as our ideal, let us also render good offices even to him who has done a disservice to us.

Whoever comes up before us, whomsoever we meet, let us serve him in a disinterested spirit, thinking at the same time how his interests may be served. Nay, let us feel enraptured, delighted and transported with joy to observe his good points and let us appreciate his virtues alone. There is no one in this world who is full of faults alone and devoid of any virtue; nor is there anyone other than God and exalted souls who has virtues alone and not a single fault.

Therefore, as a matter of principle, a man should make it his goal to love all equally in a disinterested spirit. With this end in view, one should unegotistically and selflessly behave with humility and love towards all. Love bestowed on anyone for the pleasure of God is love bestowed on God. Therefore, strivers would do well to develop disinterested love towards all for the pleasure of God, looking upon all as so many manifestations of God or viewing God as present in all or recognizing everything as belonging to God. All sorts of evils will be completely eradicated thereby and realization of God, who is the same as Supreme Love, will take place. That is why Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa says to His loving devotee, Uddhava—

मामेव सर्वभूतेषु बहिरन्तरपावृतम्।

ईक्षेतात्मनि चात्मानं यथा खममलाशय:॥

इति सर्वाणि भूतानि मद्भावेन महाद्युते।

सभाजयन् मन्यमानो ज्ञानं केवलमाश्रित:॥

ब्राह्मणे पुल्कसे स्तेने ब्रह्मण्येऽर्के स्फुलिङ्गके।

अक्रूरे क्रूरके चैव समदृक् पण्डितो मत:॥

नरेष्वभीक्ष्णं मद्भावं पुंसो भावयतोऽचिरात् ।

स्पर्धासूयातिरस्कारा: साहङ्कारा व्रियन्ति हि॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata XI. xxix. 12—15)

“Pure of mind, he should behold Me alone, the Supreme Self, as pervading within as well as without and unconditioned as the sky in all created beings as also in his own self. He who, embracing thus the point of view of Self-knowledge alone, regards all created beings as identical with Me,
O highly enlightened one, and honours them as such, (nay) who looks with an equal eye upon a Brāhmaṇa and a lowborn individual, a thief and a devotee of Brāhmaṇas, the sun and a spark, a tender-hearted and a cruel man is accounted wise. ( The spirit of ) rivalry (with equals), fault-finding (with one’s superiors) and contempt (for one’s inferiors) coupled with self-conceit surely disappear before long from the mind of a man incessantly (engaged in) looking upon men and women as (no other than) Myself.”

 

The Various Means of God-realization

The goal of human existence is realization of God. The scriptures, saints and Mahātmās have laid down various means for the same. Through the practice of any of these scriptural methods, according to one’s qualification and temperament, in a disinterested way, i.e., without the motive of gaining worldly happiness, and merely for the sake of God’s pleasure, man may in due course realize God and fulfil the object of his birth and existence. Lord Manu has laid down the ten characteristics of Dharma. Anyone who disinterestedly practises these ten aspects of Dharma may attain freedom from the bondage of Māyā, and realize God.

The Ten Ways

धृति: क्षमा दमोऽस्तेयं शौचमिन्द्रियनिग्रह:।

धीर्विद्या सत्यमक्रोधो दशकं धर्मलक्षणम्॥

“The ten characteristics of Dharma are Fortitude, Forgiveness, Mind-control, Non-theft, Purity of body and mind, Sense-control, Wisdom, Knowledge, Truth and Non-anger.”

Briefly explained, they are as follows—

1. Fortitude means firmness of character in the face of danger or failure in obtaining a desired thing. He who practises fortitude can save his Dharma, and attains success in this world and the next.

2. Forgiveness is expressed through patient bearing of another’s persecution without entertaining the least thought of retaliation, although possessing full power of retaliation, and making proper effort to change the attitude of the persecutor and remove for all time his propensity for the commission of guilt.

3. Dama ordinarily means control of the senses, but sense-control having been covered by another word in the verse, it should be taken in the sense of ‘śama’ or mind-control. Without control over the mind God-realization is almost an impossibility (Gītā vi, 36). The Lord has said that the mind can be brought under control through discipline and practice of dispassion (Gītāvi. 35).

4. Non-theft means non-commission of any form of theft, through mind, speech and body.

5. Purification, both external and internal. Honest means and trade in pure things for livelihood, purity of food gained through honest livelihood, purity of conduct and cleaning of the body through the use of water, earth, etc., are called external purity. Attainment of transparency of the mind through total removal of modifications like attraction and hatred, hypocrisy and falsehood, enmity and pride, etc., is called internal purity.

6. Sense-control means not allowing the senses to run after objects of sight, taste, sound, smell and touch of their own accord, their withdrawal from harmful objects and application to beneficial objects.

7. Wisdom is the highest Sāttvika type of intelligence, born of the study of elevated scriptures, practice of Bhajana and investigation into the character of Self, through which the mind gets attached to God and true knowledge is awakened.

8. Knowledge is that spiritual knowledge which God has described as identical with Him, and which liberating man from the bondage of nescience brings the attainment of the supreme state.

9. Truth is statement of facts, as they are, in words which are pleasant. Whatever the mind and the senses have ascertained as fact has to be expressed in sweet words, bearing in mind that it does not lead to injury to any innocent creature. The characteristics of truth are that it conforms to fact, is pleasant, untainted by deceit and non-injurious to every being.

10. Non-anger means want of the least trace of fire in the mind even against one who causes injury. The difference between non-anger and forgiveness lies in this that in the former there is no action; it is a passive state of bearing everything, without allowing the mind to undergo any modification. But this does not necessarily mean forgiveness of the guilt of the wrong-doer, who reaps the fruit of his action, either in this world or the next, through the operation of God’s justice and dispensation. In forgiveness, the guilt is altogether forgiven.

THE NINE WAYS

If one is unable to utilize the ten ways mentioned above, he may try to realize God through the nine forms of practice known as the nine forms of devotion. These are as follows—

श्रवणं कीर्तनं विष्णो: स्मरणं पादसेवनम्।

अर्चनं वन्दनं दास्यं सख्यमात्मनिवेदनम्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata VII. v. 23)

“Hearing of the Names and description of the Form, Virtues and Glory of Lord Viṣṇu, Loud chanting (Kīrtana) practice of Remembrance, Serving the Lord’s sacred feet, Worship, Prostration before Him, Cultivation of the Servant-sentiment (towards God), Cultivation of the Friend-sentiment and surrender these are the nine forms of devotion.”

1. Hearing—This form of devotion is cultivated through the constant practice of hearing with reverence of God’s character, glories, virtues, sports, names and love, and attempting to mould conduct accordingly. By mere hearing of the ‘śrīmad Bhāgavata’ a sinner like Dhundhukārī crossed the ocean of existence. As a devotee, king Parīkṣit is considered to belong to this class.

2. Kīrtana—This is loud chanting of God’s sports, power, glory, character, virtues, names etc., filling the heart with the sentiment of love. Devarṣi Nārada, Vyāsadeva, Vālmīki, śukadeva and śrī Caitanya, etc., are considered to be devotees of this class.

3. Remembrance—This is the practice of exclusive remembrance of the divine form with all its virtues and glory and feeling, again and again, charmed with it. śrī Prahlāda, śrī Dhruva, śrī Bharata, Bhīṣma and the Gopīs are devotees of this class.

4. Serving the Lord’s feet—This is either service of the feet of the divine form, which is the object of worship of the devotee, or service of the feet of every being regarding him to be the abode of the Lord. śrī Lakṣmī, śrī Rukmiṇī and śrī Bharata are devotees of this class.

5. Worship—This is offering of constant worship to either a particular image of God or to a divine form conceived in the mind, according to the liking or inclination of the devotee. For the details of the practice of mental worship, the reader is referred to the article entitled “Lessons in Devotion and Divine Love” appearing in the Kalyana-Kalpataru, May 1944. Rendering service to all beings regarding them as divine manifestations is also a form of worship of Unmanifest God. Kings like Pr̥thu and Ambarīṣa belong to this class of devotees.

6. Salutation—This form of devotion is cultivated through offering of salutations to an image of God, or to all beings, regarding the universe itself as God’s image. śrī Akrūra is classified as a devotee of this type.

7. The Servant-sentiment—This is expressed through constant service rendered to God by a devotee with utmost reverence and faith, regarding God as the sole Lord and Master and himself as God’s eternal servant, without entertaining any worldly desire, and treating the joy of salvation as quite insignificant in comparison with the delight of personal service to God. śrī Hanumān, śrī Lakṣmījī, etc., are devotees of this class.

8. The Friend-sentiment—This is offering of love to God with an open heart, regarding Him to be the best friend and well-wisher. God performs even the humblest service of His friend with very great pleasure. śrī Arjuna, Uddhava, Sudāmā, śrīdāmā and others are devotees of this class.

9. Self-surrender—It means, being freed from the ego, offering everything which one may call as one’s own, to God. Mahārāja Bali and the Gopīs are examples of this type of devotion.

THE EIGHT WAYS

If one is unable to follow the nine ways mentioned above, he may adopt for practice the eight limbs of Yoga laid down by Maharṣi PataÛjali, and thereby attain God. Those eight Limbs of Yoga are as follows—

यमनियमासनप्राणायामप्रत्याहारधारणाध्यानसमाधयोऽष्टावङ्गानि।

(Yogasūtra 11.29)

“Yama, Niyama, Asana, Prāṇāyāma, Pratyāhāra, Dhāraṇā, Dhyāna and Samādhi—these are the eight Limbs of Yoga.”

1. Yamas are five in number—

अहिंसासत्यास्तेयब्रह्मचर्यापरिग्रहा यमा:।

(Ibid. 30)

“Ahiṁsā (non-injury to beings), truth, non-theft, Brahmacarya and non-possession—these five constitute Yama.”

(a) Ahiṁsā is the practice of refraining from infliction causing to inflict or supporting infliction of the least amount of pain and injury, through mind, speech and body, to any being whatsoever under the influence of greed, infatuation or anger.

(b) Truth is the correct expression of whatever is seen, heard or understood, keeping the good of others as the motive in view.

(c) Non-theft is the practice of ever refraining through mind, speech and body from taking possession of anything belonging to another.

(d) Total abstinence from the eight forms of sexual indulgence is Brahmacarya.

(e) The practice of non-possession means refraining wholly from accumulation of things for the purpose of enjoyment, or refraining from accumulation of things with the sense of personal possession.

When one has fully developed Ahiṁsā, his presence will pacify the ferocious propensity of even untamed beasts (Yogasūtras II. 35).

Words uttered by one in whom truth has been fully established come to pass; that is to say, his words never fail (Y.S. II. 36).

Through success in the practice of non-theft, one attains mastery over wealth or jewels (Y.S. II. 37).

Fully established Brahmacarya brings immense physical and mental strength (Y.S. II. 38).

Success in the practice of the vow of Non-possession brings awareness of the facts of past life (Y. S. II. 31).

2. Niyamas also are five in number—

‘शौचसन्तोषतप: स्वाध्यायेश्वरप्रणिधानानि नियमा:’।

(Yogasūtra II. 32)

“Purity, contentment, penance, study of scriptures and surrender to God—these five are Niyamas.”

(a) Purification of the body through the uses of earth, water, etc., Purification of food and conduct through earning by means of trade in pure commodities and proper behaviour, and purification of the mind through abandonment of attraction and hatred—these are the practices of purity.

(b) Satisfaction with whatever is obtained through the grace of God is contentment.

(c) Sufferings endured for the practice of Dharma, observances of vows like Candrāyaṇa, etc., which involve much hardship, or practices of bearing heat and cold, etc., are called penance.

(d) Study of the Vedas, Upaniṣads, the Gītā and other scriptures written by the ¤Rṣis, repetition of sacred formulas like the Gāyatrī and practice of Japa of the divine name—all these are included in the study of scriptures.

(e) Offering of one’s possessions to God and complete dependence on God mean surrender to God.

Through complete observance of external purity the sense of loathsomeness of the body is developed leading to growth of dispassion for physical contact with another. Internal purity brings mental purity, brightness, concentration, victory over the senses and the qualifications for vision of the Self (Y.S. II. 40-41).

Complete success in the practice of contentment leads to the attainment of the highest form of happiness.

Through success in penance impurities are eradicated, and Siddhis of the body and senses like the powers of becoming as small as the atom, etc., and of hearing and seeing from a distance, are attained (II. 43). Through success in study of the scriptures and practice of Japa direct vision of the Deity is obtained.

Success in the practice of self-surrender leads to the attainment of Samādhi (II. 45).

3. Asana—The practice of sitting in one posture, without movement of the limbs, for a sufficient length of time is called Asana. Asanas are of various kinds, e.g., Siddhāsana, Padmāsana, Sukhāsana, etc. Success in the practice of Asanas brings immunity from the struggle between contraries like heat and cold, etc.

4. Prāṇāyāma—The practice of controlling the breath is called Prāṇāyāma. Exhalation, Inhalation and Pause—these are the three forms of Prāṇāyāma. They should be practised after learning the processes from a Guru or spiritual instructor. The practice of Prāṇāyāma leads to the attenuation of the screen which covers the light of knowledge. The power of the mind is developed, so as to make it ripe for the practice of Dhāraṇā.

5. Pratyāhāra—Through dissociation of the senses from their corresponding objects, their transformation or absorption into the mind is called the state of Pratyāhāra. Success in the practice of Pratyāhāra brings one complete mastery over the senses.

6. Dhāraṇā—Fixing of the mind on one object is called Dhāraṇā.

7. Dhyāna—Flow of all the mental faculties towards the object of meditation, similar to the uninterrupted or incessant flow of oil, is called Dhyāna or meditation.

8. Samādhi—When Dhyāna or meditation becomes complete, the triangular difference between the meditator, act of meditation and the object of meditation is removed, and a state of identity is established, which is called the state of Samādhi. This is, again divided into two types, e.g., with seed and seedless. In the first type of Samādhi with seed, though the triangular difference is lost, the seeds of subtle impressions or latencies remain; whereas in seedless Samādhi there is total annihilation of even the subtle impressions.

THE SEVEN WAYS

If the eight ways mentioned above cannot be carried out, the following seven ways may be adopted, and their disinterested practice may lead to God-realization.

“In this unsubstantial world, seven things are of worth, Association, Bhajana, Service, Kindness, Meditation, Humility and Benevolence.”*

* इस असार संसारमें सात वस्तु हैं सार।

संग, भजन, सेवा, दया, ध्यान, दैन्य, उपकार॥

1. Association—It means good association. The benefit which man derives from the association of saints and lovers of God, and scriptures dealing with God, cannot be properly described. The greatness and glory of God can be understood only from such association. It is through the influence of Satsaṅga alone that the darkness of ignorance of the Jīva is removed. Goswāmī Tulasīdāsajī says—

“Except in the company of saints there is no talk about Hari; without this talk ignorance will not be dispelled; and without dispelling ignorance, it is not possible to develop strong attachment for the feet of śrī Rāma.”

“O dear, if heavenly enjoyments and the bliss of absolution (Mukti) are placed on the same scale of the balance; they combined cannot equal the joy of a moment’s association with a saint.”*

* बिनु सतसंग न हरिकथा तेहि बिनु मोह न भाग।

मोह गएँ बिनु रामपद होइ न दृढ़ अनुराग॥

तात स्वर्ग अपबर्ग सुख धरिअ तुला इक अंग।

तूल न ताहि सकल मिलि जो सुख लव सतसंग॥

In the same strain, śaunaka and the other ¤Rṣis say in the śrīmad Bhāgavata—

तुलयाम लवेनापि न स्वर्गं नापुनर्भवम्।

भगवत्सङ्गिसङ्गस्य मर्त्यानां किमुताशिष:॥

(I. xviii. 13)

“In comparison with a moment’s contact with a lover of God, we consider the attainment of heaven or even salvation (Mokṣa) as quite insignificant, what to speak of other worldly things.”

The Lord Himself, addressing śrī Uddhava says—

न रोधयति मां योगो न सांख्यं धर्म एव च।

न स्वाध्यायस्तपस्त्यागो नेष्टापूर्तं न दक्षिणा॥

व्रतानि यज्ञश्छन्दांसि तीर्थानि नियमा यमा:।

यथावरुन्धे सत्सङ्ग: सर्वसङ्गापहो हि माम्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata XI. xii. 1-2)

“O Uddhava, neither Yoga nor Sāṅkhya, nor righteousness, nor the study of the Vedas, nor askesis, nor renunciation, nor sacrifices, nor construction of wells and tanks, nor charities, nor payments of sacrificial fees, nor self-denying vows, nor worship of gods, nor visit to sacred places, nor the disciplines of Yama and Niyama can so gain Me over as does the association of a saint, which puts an end to all worldly attachments.”

But, it is necessary, to consider what should be the characteristics of the Sādhu whose company should be cultivated for Satsaṅga. The Gītā, chapter II, contains the marks of a man of stable mind. Similarly, chapter XII contains the marks of the devotee, and chapter XIV the marks of one, who has risen above the Guṇas. He in whom these marks are developed in a more or less pronounced form is a saint in the real sense of the term. Describing, again, the marks of a saint, śrī Kapiladeva says to his mother in the Bhāgavata—

तितिक्षव: कारुणिका: सुहृद: सर्वदेहिनाम्।

अजातशत्रव: शान्ता: साधव: साधुभूषणा:॥

मय्यनन्येन भावेन भक्तिं कुर्वन्ति ये दृढाम्।

मत्कृते त्यक्तकर्माणस्त्यक्तस्वजनबान्धवा:॥

मदाश्रया: कथा मृष्टा: ऋण्वन्ति कथयन्ति च।

तपन्ति विविधास्तापा नैतान्मद्गतचेतस:॥

त एते साधव: साध्वि सर्वसङ्गविवर्जिता:।

सङ्गस्तेष्वथ ते प्रार्थ्य: सङ्गदोषहरा हि ते॥

(III. xxv. 21—24)

“O mother, Sādhus who are full of forbearance and mercy, friends to all embodied beings, and enemies to none and are tranquil, who possess good disposition, as their ornament and cherish firm devotion to Me (God) with an unswerving heart and have renounced all works and even their kith and kin for My sake, have taken refuge under Me and regard My stories as sweet and are constantly engaged in either listening to or telling My stories, and whose mind is set on Me, are not harassed by the afflictions of the world. They are the people who are rid of all attachments and can cure the evil of worldly attachments in others. O virtuous lady, their association should be cultivated.”

Therefore, Satsaṅga should be sought after with constant love and faith even at the risk of great loss in worldly pursuits.

2. Bhajana—Goswāmī Tulasīdāsajī says—

बारि मथें घृत होइ बरु सिकता ते बरु तेल।

बिन हरि भजन न भव तरिअ यह सिद्धांत अपेल॥

“Butter may be rather churned out of water, and oil may be pressed out of sand; but without the practice of Bhajana none can cross the ocean of existence; this truth is unshakable.”

The statement is absolutely correct. Practice of Bhajana is the primary means of crossing the ocean of metempsychosis. When this is done, all other qualifications follow as a matter of course. It is through the power of Bhajana, devotees like Dhruva, Prahlāda and Mīrā, etc., that God blessed them with His sight.

3. Service—It is man’s primary duty to render service in any form or shape to another being. Regarding the whole world as the manifestation of God, one should render disinterested service to all through the mind, speech and body. When an opportunity of service occurs, it should be regarded as a favour done by God as well as the person who receives the service. For the latter by accepting the service, and God by creating the opportunity of service, show us exceptional favour, indeed. By rendering service to another, one should neither feel that he has conferred an obligation nor regard the recipient of service as in anyway inferior.

4. Kindness—Melting of the heart at the sight of misery of a distressed being and awakening in the mind of the desire to remove his misery is called kindness. Ahiṁsā or non-violence is a negative or passive quality, and kindness is positive and active. Ahiṁsā means only restraint from doing injury, but kindness involves doing something so as to contribute to the happiness of another. The heart of the person who possesses no kindness is like a piece of hard stone. Human life should be made a success through the exhibition of kindness to the indigent, the cripple, the diseased and the helpless. Mahāprabhu śrī Caitanya expressed his entire teachings in a nutshell by pointing, in conclusion, to three things only—

नामे रुचि जीवे दया वैष्णवसेवन।

इहा छाड़ा आर नाहिं जानि सनातन॥

“Sanātana! taste in the Name of God, kindness to beings, and association of devotees—beyond these three, I know of no other way.”

5. Meditation—The very key to unlock the gate leading to God-vision is meditation. When the first attempt to meditation is made, the mind being not broken to it will feel tired and worried, but if the practice is resolutely continued daily and regularly, the mind will be habituated to the practice. Then the joy, which meditation will bring will be found in no other work. Therefore, meditation on the form of one’s cherished deity should be practised daily with a resolute heart. The practice of meditation is the best of all means.

6. Humility—Pride is the one enemy of man, which inevitably leads to his fall. If he can replace it by cultivating humility, and being meek and lowly before God, he can certainly draw the compassion of God on him. Therefore, humility should be cultivated abandoning all forms of crookedness and twists of character.

7. Benevolence—It is said—

अष्टादशपुराणेषु व्यासस्य वचनद्वयम्।

परोपकार: पुण्याय पापाय परपीडनम्॥

“In all the eighteen Purāṇas, Maharṣi Vedavyāsa inculcated only two teachings, viz., benevolence is the cause of virtue, and tyranny the cause of sin.”

Goswāmī Tulasīdāsajī also says—

“There is no virtue equal to benevolence, and nothing so despicable as tyranny. For a man possessing benevolence in his heart, there is nothing unattainable in the world.”

Therefore, one should constantly devote oneself to the practice of benevolence renouncing pride, selfishness and desire.

THE SIX WAYS

If the seven ways mentioned above cannot be practised, the six ways described below should be adopted and followed realization of God is possible through their disinterested practice.

संध्या, पूजा, यज्ञ, तप, दया सु-सात्त्विक दान।

इन छ: के आचरणसे निश्चय हो कल्यान॥

“The six practices of Sandhyā, adoration, sacrifice, askesis, compassion and charities of the Sāttvika type lead to blessedness without fail.”

1. Sandhyā—Every twice-born should practise Sandhyā three times a day. In case three times of practice is not possible, there should be no failure at least in the performance of the morning and evening Sandhyās. Through the practice of Sandhyā, God is worshipped in the forms of sun, fire and water. Lord Manu says—

न तिष्ठति तु य: पूर्वां नोपास्ते यश्च पश्चिमाम्।

स शूद्रवद्‍बहिष्कार्य: सर्वस्माद् द्विजकर्मण:॥

“The twice-born who fails in the performance of the morning and evening Sandhyās, should be kept at a distance like the śūdra in all works enjoined for the twice-born.”

Therefore, the practice of Sandhyā should never be abandoned. During periods of uncleanliness caused by the birth or death of a relative, or journey by rail, Sandhyā should be mentally performed. The times fixed for the performance should be kept up.

These times are as follows—

उत्तमा तारकोपेता मध्यमा लुप्ततारका।

कनिष्ठा सूर्यसहिता प्रात:सन्ध्या त्रिधा स्मृता॥

उत्तमा सूर्यसहिता मध्यमा लुप्तभास्करा।

कनिष्ठा तारकोपेता सायंसन्ध्या त्रिधा स्मृता॥

(Devī-Bhāgavata XI. xvi. 4-5)

“There are three types of morning Sandhyā, the best is performed when there are stars in the sky, the middling is performed when stars are invisible, the worst is performed after sun-rise. Similarly, evening Sandhyā is of three types, the best is performed before sun-set, the middling is after sun-set, and the worst after stars are visible.”

In the morning, God approaches our continent in the form of the sun, and in the evening goes to another part of the world. Just as when an honoured guest comes to us we make arrangements for his reception before his arrival, go to the station before the train arrives and stand waiting on the platform with flowers and bouquets to welcome and salute him, and when he leaves us, make all necessary arrangements for his departure and go to the station to bid him farewell, even so obeisances are offered to the sun through the practice of Sandhyā for which the right time should be observed. Through the practice of adoration in the form of Sandhyā all sins are dispelled, and, finally, God can be realized. If we offer our reception and farewell to the sun disinterestedly throughout life regularly both the times, then on our death, the sun also will be compelled to help us in our liberation from bondage.

The scripture says—

सन्ध्यामुपासते ये तु सततं संशितव्रता:।

विधूतपापास्ते यान्ति ब्रह्मलोकं सनातनम्॥

“The twice-born who resolutely performs Sandhyā regularly everyday, being released from sin, attains the eternal abode of Brahmā.”

2. Adoration—External or mental worship of the image of the Deity should be performed by all regularly everyday. In every home there should be either an image or a picture of the Deity to whom women and children may offer their worship. This will create good mental impressions and develop the spirit of devotion in them. Mīrābāī and Dhannā, the Jāṭa, and others became famous devotees only through the practice of adoration.

3. Sacrifice—The Gītā has described many forms of sacrifice, and among them God has described the sacrifice consisting of Japa as His very Self ‘यज्ञानां जपयज्ञोऽस्मि’ (X.25). Therefore, Japa of the Divine Name should be practised by all. Since man generally takes, in the course of a day and night, 21,600 breaths, that number of utterance of the Name of God should be practised without fail. Inaudible or Upāṁśu Japa is the best of all. Besides this, it is indispensable for a householder to perform the five great obligatory sacrifices. At least, there should be no lapse in the performance of the Balivaiśwadeva, the rules of which will be elsewhere described.

4. Askesis—To undergo suffering in the practice of one’s particular Dharma is askesis. In chapter XVII, verses 14 to 19 of the Gītā, austerities of the body, mind and speech are described, in following which the Sāttvika form of austerity should be performed.

5. Compassion—The Smr̥ti says—

परे वा बन्धुवर्गे वा मित्रे द्वेष्टरि वा सदा।

आपन्ने रक्षितव्यं तु दयैषा परिकीर्तिता॥

(Atri Smr̥ti 41)

“Whether a person is a relative or a stranger, a friend or a foe, when he is a victim of misery, attempt should be made to remove the same. This is called compassion.”

At the sight of another’s distress, the heart of a man of compassion will at once melt. It is said, “Compassion is the root of virtue, and the root of sin is pride; says Tulasī, so long as there is breath in the body, do not give up compassion.”

6. Charity—The practice of charity is the common duty of man. Wealth, learning, intelligence, food, water, cloth, good words, whatever of these one may possess should be gifted away to the proper person at the proper place and time, and the gift should be made in the purest Sāttvika spirit. A gift made without consideration of the propriety of place, time and person, without welcome to the recipient, and in a spirit of reproach to him, is a Tāmasika form of gift. And the Rājasika forms of gift are made with mental unwillingness, in a spirit of receiving something in return, or for gaining honour, fame, prestige, or progeny, for removal of illness or for the attainment of happiness in the heaven. The gift which is made as a matter of duty, without any sense of return, and after proper consideration of the place, time and person is the Sāttvika type of gift. This last type of gift acts as a great help in one’s progress towards God-realization. If one possesses a thing for the lack of which other persons are suffering in a particular place and time, then the service rendered to those persons in that place and time by making them gifts of that thing, is called practice of charity after due consideration of the place and time. The hungry, the helpless, the distressed and generally all indigent beggars are always worthy subjects of charity to whom services should be rendered by supplying them with food, clothing, medicine, or whatever else they may require. The value of a gift does not depend on the amount of money spent, but on the spirit or motive of the giver. The greater the sacrifice involved in a gift, the more is its value. That is why in the Aśwamedha Parva of the Mahābhārata, the mongoose reproved the unlimited charities performed by the Pāṇḍavas and praised the value of the gift of a handful of parched grain made by a poor Brāhmaṇa who lived by gleaning corn. (Mahābhārata, Aśwa. 90. 7). A person gives away a lakh of rupees out of his accumulated balance of crores of rupees in order to earn name and fame, or from any other selfish motive, and another poor individual parts with, from a sense of duty, half a piece of bread and goes empty in stomach himself. Out of these two, the gift of the latter is more valuable. Nevertheless, charity performed in a good cause even with a motive, is better than total absence of the practice of charity.

THE FIVE WAYS

If the six ways mentioned above cannot be practised, one may take to the following five ways. The practice of these five also may bring one the attainment of the supreme object of life.

“Gāyatrī, Govinda, Gau (the Cow),

Gītā and Ganges-bath

The grace of these five quickly brings

God-realization.”

1. Gāyatrī—The scriptures sing the glory of the Gāyatrī in the highest of terms. The Japa of Gāyatrī has to be performed observing bodily purity and silence adding the Praṇava and Vyāhr̥ti to the formula. The formula of Gāyatrī contains the praise of All-pervading Brahma, the embodiment of Truth, Knowledge and Bliss, and meditation on His Divine Light and a prayer to Him.

Lord Manu writes the glory of the Gāyatrī in the following terms—

ओंकारपूर्विकास्तिस्रो महाव्याहृतयोऽव्यया:।

त्रिपदा चैव सावित्री विज्ञेयं ब्रह्मणो मुखम्॥

एतदक्षरमेतां च जपन् व्याहृतिपूर्विकाम्।

संध्ययोर्वेदविद्विप्रो वेदपुण्येन युज्यते॥

सहस्रकृत्वस्त्वभ्यस्य बहिरेतत् त्रिकं द्विज।

महतोऽप्येनसो मासात्त्वचेवाहिर्विमुच्यते॥

योऽधीतेऽहन्यहन्येतास्त्रीणि वर्षाण्यतन्द्रित:।

स ब्रह्म परमभ्येति वायुभूत: खमूर्तिमान्॥

(II. 81, 78, 79, 82)

“The Gāyatrī, containing three Vyāhr̥tis and three divisions, together with OË, should be known as the mouth of the Vedas. The twice-born, possessed of Knowledge of the Vedas, who performs Japa of the Gāyatrī, with Praṇava (OË) and the Vyāhr̥tis (Bhūḥ, Bhuvaḥ, śwaḥ), every morning and evening, obtains the fruit of reading the entire Veda. The twice-born, who retiring to a lonely retreat performs a thousand Japas (repetitions), of the Gāyatrī everyday continuously for a month, gets freed from the effect of the greatest of sins, even as the snake throws off its slough.
