He who, abandoning idleness, performs the Japa of Gāyatrī, regularly everyday, for three years at a stretch, becomes as light of speed as air, and as stainless as ether, and attains Supreme Brahma.”

Therefore, the Japa of Gāyatrī should be regularly practised everyday without fail.

2. Govinda—Through constant meditation on śrī Govinda, there is nothing which cannot be achieved. The Lord Himself says—

अनन्याश्चिन्तयन्तो मां ये जना: पर्युपासते।

तेषां नित्याभियुक्तानां योगक्षेमं वहाम्यहम्॥

(Gītā IX. 22)

“To devotees, who knowing no one else constantly think of Me, and worship Me in a disinterested way, to those ever united in thought with Me, I bring full security and personally attend to their needs.”

Therefore, with a strong resolution, and with faith and love in the heart, God should be meditated upon.

3. The Cow—The Hindu scriptures glorify the cow in highest terms. Through service of the cow all desires of men can be fulfilled. The five products of the cow, viz., milk, curd, ghee (clarified butter) urine and dung are pure and destructive of sins. The number of cows is being reduced in India nowadays with extreme cruelty. All religious-minded men, who seek the country’s welfare, should make it a point to protect and serve the cow.

4. The Gītā—The Gītā is, indeed, the very heart of God. Bhagavān śrī Vyāsa says—

गीता सुगीता कर्तव्या किमन्यै: शास्त्रविस्तरै:।

या स्वयं पद्मनाभस्य मुखपद्माद्विनि:सृता:॥

(Mahābhārata, Bhīṣma-Parva 43.1)

“Emanating from the lotus-like lips of God Himself, the Gītā alone should be studied well. What is the use of studying so many scriptures?”

How can one express the glory of the Gītā? It can only be felt by one, who devotes himself to a study of it with attention. There are many verses in the Gītā, half or even a quarter of whose instructions, when reduced to practice, may easily bring one God-realization. It has been said—

सर्वोपनिषदो गावो दोग्धा गोपालनन्दन:।

पार्थो वत्स: सुधीर्भोक्ता दुग्धं गीतामृतं महत्॥

“The entire range of the Upaniṣads constitutes the cow, the milker is the divine cowherd śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, Arjuna is the calf, the wise man of intelligence is the enjoyer and the Gītā is the nectar-like milk.”

Therefore the Gītā should be read with attention everyday, as an unfailing duty, properly understanding the meaning of the verses studied.

5. Ganges-bath—The glory of the Ganges is infinite. She is, in fact, styled by the devotees as liquid Brahma, That is, Brahma Itself transformed into water from its formless state, and flowing as a stream. It is stated by the scriptures at numberless places that through bath in the Ganges, and drinking of its water, sins are eradicated and liberation is attained. Even today, there is hardly a place found as pure and sacred as the bank of the Ganges. There are many great saints who live only on the bank of the Ganges. Western medical experts after analysing the water of the Ganges have declared that it contains natural prophylactic properties and disease germs coming in contact with it are at once destroyed. It is well-known that Ganges water even if preserved for years does not rot. It is the established view of the scriptures that he who resorts to the Ganges and bathes in it and drinks its water, with reverence and faith, attains the supreme state.

THE FOUR WAYS

If the five ways mentioned above cannot be adopted for practice, one may take recourse to the four ways mentioned below—

“Self-restraint, service, spiritual practices and association of saints—

These four, when adopted, quickly chase away the night of delusion.”

1. Self-restraint—Disallowing the mind, speech and body to acts they like, and withdrawing them from enjoyments of the world, and to devote them to practices which lead to blessedness is called practice of self-restraint. According to Lord Manu, he who keeps restraint over the mind, speech and body is a Tridaṇḍī in the true sense of the term—

वाग्दण्डोऽथ मनोदण्ड: कायदण्डस्तथैव च।

यस्यैते निहिता बुद्धौ त्रिदण्डीति स उच्यते॥

त्रिदण्डमेतन्निक्षिप्यसर्वभूतेषु मानव।

कामक्रोधौ तु संयम्य तत: सिद्धिं नियच्छति॥

(XII. 10-11)

“He who carefully practises control over speech, control over the mind and control over the body is a Tridaṇḍī (bearer of three staffs, an ascetic). He, who has attained restraint over mind, speech and the body with reference to all beings, and for the exercise of this restraint has brought under control his lust and anger, attains salvation.”

He who moves in the world keeping his mind, senses and the body under control, and without being influenced by feelings of attraction and repulsion, obtains Bliss. Men of restraint alone are healthy, strong, virtuous, long-lived and fit for salvation.

2. Service—He who serves his elders, and all other beings, disinterestedly, and in the spirit of serving God, serves God Himself at every step, and, finally, attains God.

3. Spiritual Practices—Practices of Bhajana, Meditation, etc., done for God-realization are all spiritual practices or Sādhanā. One should adopt, for the realization of God, a course of discipline or Sādhanā for regular practice, suited to his qualifications, faith, nature as well as taste.

4. Association with saints—The Bhāgavata says—

दुर्लभो मानुषो देहो देहिनां क्षणभङ्गुर:।

तत्रापि दुर्लभं मन्ये गोविन्दप्रियदशर्नम्॥

(XI. ii. 29)

“The human body is attained with difficulty by Jīvas, and is transient; and in that life, too, it is rarer to meet a devotee dear to God; for devotees and saints are, in a sense, the same as God Himself.”

Goswāmī Tulasīdāsajī speaks of the devotee as even greater than God—‘राम तें अधिक राम कर दासा’. Association with saints leads to the destruction of sins and purification of the heart. It helps one in fixing the mind on God and, uprooting doubts, leads to God-realization. Therefore the company of saints should be cultivated without fail.

Addressing king Rahūgaṇa, Mahātmā Bharata says—

रहूगणैतत्तपसा न याति

न चेज्यया निर्वपणाद् गृहाद्वा।

नच्छन्दसा नैव जलाग्निसूर्यै-

र्विना महत्पादरजोऽभिषेकम्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata V. xii. 12)

“O king, the Supreme Knowledge can be attained only by bathing the body with the dust of the feet of saints. It cannot be attained through penance, sacrifices, charity, benevolent acts done while in the family, study of the Vedas or worship of the Gods of water, fire and the sun.”

Therefore, service of saints and their association is an infallible means to salvation.

THE THREE WAYS

If the four methods detailed above cannot be practised then the following three methods may be adopted—

“Practice of truth in speech, subordination of self, looking upon women in general (except one’s wife) as a mother. If it is doubted that these will bring realization of Hari, Tulasīdāsa stands surety for it.”

1. Truth in speech—It is said—

“There is no austerity equal to Truthfulness, and no

sin equal to falsehood;

He whose heart is the abode of Truth, has God

Himself there.”

Truth is synonymous with God. Where there is Truth, there is God. Because of his being a votary of Truth, the name of śrī Hariścandra has been remembered even to this day. When a man observes truth in speech, whatever escapes his lips turns out to be True. Falsehood should never be uttered out of a selfish motive, or as a matter of habit or in joke, or in the form of a promise about future action or conduct.

2. Subordination of self—It means making oneself subordinate to the will of God, i.e., to become a true servant of God, giving up the spirit of independence, and maintaining life for carrying out the Lord’s behests and following His promptings. Performance of duties in the world, e.g., service of the parents by the son, of the preceptor by the disciple, of the husband by the wife, of the master by the servant, and looking upon the object of service as God, also constitute subordination of self to God.

On the self becoming subordinate to God, it develops almost all the qualities of God. With the strength derived from God, it finds itself possessed of an immense accession of strength. Just as on the strength of the king an ordinary constable challenges and arrests men of wealth and position in society, even so subordinating himself to God man can attain victory over all sins on the strength of God and can be a great lover of God.

3. Looking upon all women (except one’s wife) as a mother—Realizing that every woman is a part of the Divine Mother, one should mentally bow in reverence to every woman except his wife and show respect to all. This practice is extremely beneficial. He who cultivates this sentiment towards women in general will have an accession of energy and merit and being saved from sins may realize God.

It is said that Goswāmī Tulasīdāsajī is the author of this couplet, and he stands surety for the fact that one adopting these three ways for practice is sure to cross the sea of existence.

THE TWO WAYS

If the above-mentioned three ways cannot be practised, one should take to the following two ways—

“Never forget two things, if you are anxious for salvation; One is Death, the other is God, says Nārāyaṇa.”

1. Remembrance of Death—Everything in this world is perishable; whatever has an origin is bound to have an end. This body and its relations, and all other objects, will one day be devoured by death. Then, why this attachment for them? Why so much decoration for the body, which is steadily advancing towards death every moment? Man does not develop dispassion for things of the world, because he remains forgetful of death. King Yudhiṣṭhira, in answer to the query of the Yakṣa said—

अहन्यहनि भूतानि गच्छन्तीह यमालयम्।

शेषा: स्थिरत्वमिच्छन्ति किमाश्चर्यमत: परम्॥

(Mahābhārata, Vanaparva 3.3-316)

“Everyday men go to the abode of death, yet those who are left behind seek to live eternally. What can be more surprising than this?”

That is why ‘Nārāyaṇa Swāmī’ advises us to remember death—for remembrance of death will save us from the commission of new sins, and generate dispassion for things of the world.

2. Remembrance of God—With the cultivation of dispassion, ‘practice’ also is necessary. The Lord prescribed in the Gītā both dispassion and practice the means to attain mind control. Constant remembrance of death will no doubt generate dispassion, but it will fail to bring joy to the heart. The world will appear blank and perishable. That is why it should be accompanied by the thought of God. The whole world is permeated by God; and whatever happens is but the sport of the Lord. He is supreme Bliss, supreme Consciousness and the very embodiment of Knowledge. Practice of constant remembrance of Him destroys sins, and the mind being transformed into the likeness of God, Mokṣa or salvation is easily attained.

THE ONE AND ONLY WAY

If the two ways described above cannot be adopted, then all must take to the one great way revealed by Bhagavan śrī Kr̥ṣṇa through His sacred lips, Which is the very essence of all the ways. The character of this one way is such that when properly practised, it automatically brings success to the Sādhaka. It is as follows—

सर्वधर्मान् परित्यज्य मामेकं शरणं व्रज।

अहं त्वा सर्वपापेभ्यो मोक्षयिष्यामि मा शुच:॥

“Surrendering all duties to Me, seek refuge in Me alone, O Arjuna; I shall absolve you of all sins, grieve not.”

Bhagavān śrī Rāma says—

सकृदेव प्रपन्नाय तवास्मीति च याचते।

अभयं सर्वभूतेभ्यो ददाम्येतद् व्रतं मम॥

(Vālmīki Rāmāyaṇa VI. xviii. 83)

“He who even once surrendering himself to Me says, Lord, I am Thine’, I remove his fears from all beings, this is My solemn vow.”

Thus, surrender to the Lord in every respect is the best and supreme way. One who has surrendered himself to God has become God’s own and is free from fear and anxiety for all time. Therefore, giving up dependence on others’ support, one should surrender oneself to God, who is man’s best and sole lover and ever devoted to the promotion of his good.

 

The Various Ways of God-realization

According to the ancient and modern systems of Vedantic thought dirt (मल), distraction (विक्षेप), and obstruction (आवरण)—these three are the inherent defects. Among them, the means of destruction of dirt has been declared to be the adoption of Karmayoga, that is, performance of actions like sacrifice, charity, askesis, service, adoration etc., in a disinterested spirit. Such actions lead to purification of the heart through the removal or destruction of mental ‘dirt’. Some friends assert Japa of the Divine Name alone as the principal means of immediate attainment of purification of the heart. Both these views are correct. After purification of the heart, meditation on God for the removal of the defect of ‘distraction’, and association of saints and study of elevating scriptures for the removal of ‘obstruction’ have been declared as the primary ways. This proves that it is man’s spiritual duty to realize Truth, first through the practice of disinterested action, then through the practice of worship, and then through the association of saints.

According to the Vedānta, man should adopt the fourfold practice in order to acquire the eligibility for liberation. This fourfold practice is—(1) discrimination between the real and the unreal1 (2) strong indifference towards all forms of enjoyment of this world and the next2; (3) The six endowments beginning with śama—(a) śama—mind-control3, (b) Dama—control of the senses4, (c) Uparati—withdrawal of the mind and senses from their respective objects5 (d) Titikṣā—endurance in the face of dualities like heat and cold, joy and grief, etc.6 (e) śraddhā—faith in God, scriptures, saints and the other world, as if, they were present before the eyes7, (f) śamādhāna—complete fixation of the mind and intellect on God8 and (4) Strong desire for liberation.9

1-नासतो विद्यते भावो नाभावो विद्यते सत:।

उभयोरपि दृष्टोऽन्तस्त्वनयोस्तत्त्वदर्शिभि:॥

(Gītā II. 16)

2-ये हि संस्पर्शजा भोगा दु:खयोनय एव ते।

आद्यन्तवन्त: कौन्तेय न तेषु रमते बुध:॥

(Gītā V. 22)

3-अभ्यासेन तु कौन्तेय वैराग्येण च गृह्यते।

(Gītā VI. 35)

4-तस्मात्त्वमिन्द्रियाण्यादौ नियम्य भरतर्षभ।

पाप्मानं प्रजहि ह्येनं ज्ञानविज्ञाननाशनम्॥

(Gītā III. 41)

5-न रूपमस्येह तथोपलभ्यते

नान्तो न चादिर्न च संप्रतिष्ठा।

अश्वत्थमेनं सुविरूढमूल-

मसङ्गशस्त्रेण दृढेन छित्त्वा॥

(Gītā XV. 3)

6-मात्रास्पर्शास्तु कौन्तेय शीतोष्णसुखदु:खदा:।

आगमापायिनोऽनित्यास्तांस्तितिक्षस्व भारत॥

(Gītā II. 14)

7-श्रद्धावाँल्लभते ज्ञानं तत्पर: संयतेन्द्रिय:।

ज्ञानं लब्ध्वा परां शान्तिमचिरेणाधिगच्छति॥

(Gītā IV. 39)

8-जितात्मन: प्रशान्तस्य परमात्मा समाहित:।

(Gītā VI. 7)

9-अभ्यासयोगेन ततो मामिच्छाप्तुं धनञ्जय।

(Gītā XII. 9)

After qualifying himself through the fourfold practice, the Sādhaka should take to (1) hearing (the Truth), (2) reflection and (3) deep meditation, and thereby realize God. “Hearing”1 means approaching with proper humility a saint, who has realized truth, and hearing the words of wisdom falling from his lips, and ‘reflectionf’2 means study of the scriptures and devotion of the mind constantly to the thought of God, or Brahma, the embodiment of Existence, Knowledge and Bliss, as expounded by the great God-realized souls and ‘deep meditation’3 means complete absorption of the mind in the meditation on God. Through the adoption of this practice, the Sādhaka succeeds in realizing God. The realization in identity of attributeless and formless supreme Brahma alone, the embodiment of Sat, Cit and nanda, means direct realization of God4. Out of these three forms of practice, complete success in only one may lead to God-realization; therefore, it is no wonder if such realization is attained through the practice of all the three, one after another.

1-तद्विद्धि प्रणिपातेन परिप्रश्नेन सेवया।

उपदेक्ष्यन्ति ते ज्ञानं ज्ञानिनस्तत्त्वदर्शिन:॥

(Gītā IV. 34)

2-ये त्वक्षरमनिर्देश्यमव्यक्तं पर्युपासते।

सर्वत्रगमचिन्त्यं च कूटस्थमचलं ध्रुवम्॥

संनियम्येन्द्रियग्रामं सर्वत्र समबुद्धय:।

ते प्राप्नुवन्ति मामेव सर्वभूतहिते रता:॥

(Gītā XII. 3-4)

3-शनै: शनैरुपरमेद् बुद्‍ध्या धृतिगृहीतया।

आत्मसंस्थं मन: कृत्वा न किञ्चिदपि चिन्तयेत्॥

(Gītā VI. 25)

4-योऽन्त:सुखोऽन्तरारामस्तथान्तर्ज्योतिरेव य:।

स योगी ब्रह्मनिर्वाणं ब्रह्मभूतोऽधिगच्छति॥

(Gītā V. 24)

This view is held by both the ancient and modern teachers of religion, and it is quite excellent in its own way, but this should not lead one to the conclusion that it is the last word on the subject, in other words, it is the only way, and there is no other. For, besides it, man can realize God even by the practice of disinterested action alone, or by the practice of worship, or devotion to God, alone. Specially in the present Kali age, the path of devotion is much easier of practice by the common man than the paths of Yoga and Knowledge.

In the Gītā, the Lord declared at several places, that He could be realized by the practice of Karmayoga (disinterested action) alone. He said—

कर्मजं बुद्धियुक्ता हि फलं त्यक्त्वा मनीषिण:।

जन्मबन्धविनिर्मुक्ता: पदं गच्छन्त्यनामयम्॥

(II. 51)

“For wise men endowed with equanimity, renouncing the fruit of actions, and freed from the shackles of birth, attain the blissful supreme state.”

तस्मादसक्त: सततं कार्यं कर्म समाचर।

असक्तो ह्याचरन् कर्म परमाप्नोति पूरुष:॥

(III. 19)

“Therefore, always efficiently do your duty without attachment. Doing work without attachment, man attains the Supreme.”

युक्त: कर्मफलं त्यक्त्वा शान्तिमाप्नोति नैष्ठिकीम्।

अयुक्त: कामकारेण फले सक्तो निबध्यते॥

(V. 12)

“Offering the fruit of actions to God, the Karmayogī attains peace in the shape of God-realization; whereas he who works with a selfish motive, being attached to the fruit of actions through desire, gets tied down.”

श्रेयो हि ज्ञानमभ्यासाज्ज्ञानाद्‍ध्यानं विशिष्यते।

ध्यानात्कर्मफलत्यागस्त्यागाच्छान्तिरनन्तरम्॥

(XII. 12)

“Knowledge is better than practice (carried on without proper insight), meditation is superior to Knowledge, and renunciation of the fruit of actions is even superior to meditation; for peace immediately follows from renunciation.”

Apart from this, just as JÛānayoga has been declared to be an independent means of God-realization, even so Karmayoga has been pronounced to be an independent means. The Lord says—

सांख्ययोगौ पृथग्बाला: प्रवदन्ति न पण्डिता:।

एकमप्यास्थित: सम्यगुभयोर्विन्दते फलम्॥

यत्सांख्यै: प्राप्यते स्थानं तद्यौगैरपि गम्यते।

एकं सांख्यं च योगं च य: पश्यति स पश्यति॥

(V. 4-5)

“It is the ignorant, not the wise, who say that Sāṅkhyayoga and Karmayoga are productive of divergent results. For one, who is firmly established in either, gets the fruit of both (viz., God-realization). The supreme state, which is reached by the Sāṅkhyayogī, is attained also by the Karmayogī. Therefore, he, who sees Sāṅkhyayoga and Karmayoga as one, so far as their result goes, really sees.”

Through the continued practice of Karmayoga in a disinterested spirit, the heart becomes pure, and thereby automatically true Knowledge of God dawns on the Sādhaka, and it is not necessary for him to take to any other practice for this Knowledge. The Lord says—

न हि ज्ञानेन सदृशं पवित्रमिह विद्यते।

तत्स्वयं योगसंसिद्ध: कालेनात्मनि विन्दति॥

(Gītā IV. 38)

“In this world, there is no purifier like Knowledge; he, who has attained purity of heart through practice of Karmayoga, automatically realizes it in the self in course of time.”

When the supreme state is attainable only through the practice of action in a disinterested spirit, what, then, is there to wonder, if the devotee of God, through God’s grace, should attain the same ! The Gītā, the Rāmāyaṇa, the Bhāgavata, the Mahābhārata and all other scriptures proclaim this truth repeatedly, here and there. The Lord Himself, also, stated in the Gītā at several places that the supreme state was attainable through the practice of devotion alone—

दैवी ह्येषा गुणमयी मम माया दुरत्यया।

मामेव ये प्रपद्यन्ते मायामेतां तरन्ति ते॥

(VII. 14)

“For this wonderful illusion of Mine consisting of the three Guṇas (modes of Nature) is extremely difficult to get over; those, however, who take refuge in Me alone cross it.”

अनन्यचेता: सततं यो मां स्मरति नित्यश:।

तस्याहं सुलभ: पार्थ नित्ययुक्तस्य योगिन:॥

(VIII. 14)

“Arjuna, he who always and constantly thinks of Me with undivided mind, to that Yogī always absorbed in Me, I am easily attainable.”

मन्मना भव मद्भक्तो मद्याजी मां नमस्कुरु।

मामेवैष्यसि युक्त्वैवमात्मानं मत्परायण:॥

(IX. 34)

“Fix your mind on Me, be devoted to Me, adore Me and make obeisance to Me; thus uniting yourself to Me and entirely depending on Me, you shall come to Me.”

मच्चित्ता मद्गतप्राणा बोधयन्त: परस्परम्।

कथयन्तश्च मां नित्यं तुष्यन्ति च रमन्ति च॥

तेषां सततयुक्तानां भजतां प्रीतिपूर्वकम्।

ददामि बुद्धियोगं तं येन मामुपयान्ति ते॥

(X. 9-10)

“With their mind fixed on Me, with their lives surrendered to Me, enlightening one another about My greatness and speaking of Me, they ever remain contented and take delight in Me. On those ever united through meditation with Me and worshipping Me with love, I confer that Yoga of wisdom through which they attain Me.”

भक्त्या त्वनन्यया शक्य अहमेवंविधोऽर्जुन।

ज्ञातुं द्रष्टुं च तत्त्वेन प्रवेष्टुं च परंतप॥

(XI. 54)

“Through single-minded devotion, however, I can be seen in this Form (with four arms), and known in essence and even entered into, O valiant Arjuna.”

तेषामहं समुद्धर्ता मृत्युसंसारसागरात्।

भवामि नचिरात्पार्थ मय्यावेशितचेतसाम्॥

(XII. 7)

“These, Arjuna, I speedily rescue from the ocean of birth and death, their mind being fixed on Me.”

तमेव शरणं गच्छ सर्वभावेन भारत।

तत्प्रसादात्परां शान्तिं स्थानं प्राप्स्यसि शाश्वतम्॥

(XVIII. 62)

“Seek refuge in Him alone with all your being, Arjuna. Through His grace you shall obtain supreme peace and the eternal abode.”

In the Rāmacaritamānasa, śrī Kākabhuśuṇḍi says—

“O Garuḍa! the ocean of existence cannot be crossed without developing the sentiment of master and servant. Reflecting on this truth, adore the lotus-feet of śrī Rāma. Butter may well be churned out of water, and oil may be pressed out of sand, but without the adoration of śrī Hari, there is no crossing of the ocean of existence; this conclusion is unshakeable.*

* सेवक सेब्य भाव बिनु भव न तरिअ उरगारि।

भजहु राम पद पंकज अस सिद्धांत बिचारि॥

बारि मथें घृत होइ बरु सिकता ते बरु तेल।

बिनु हरि भजन न भव तरिअ यह सिद्धांत अपेल॥

Again,

विनिश्चितं वदामि ते न अन्यथा वचांसि मे।

हरिं नरा भजन्ति येऽतिदुस्तरं तरन्ति ते॥

“I am stating to you a truth about which I am quite certain—my statement cannot be untrue—it is this that he who adores śrī Hari very easily crosses the extremely rough ocean of worldly existence.”

“In the Mahābhārata, Anuśāsana Parva, in the course of his description of the thousand names of Viṣṇu, the patriarch Bhīṣma says—

वासुदेवाश्रयो मर्त्यो वासुदेवपरायण:।

सर्वपापविशुद्धात्मा याति ब्रह्म सनातनम्॥

(Viṣṇusahasranāma 130)

“One who has taken refuge in Vāsudeva, and is a devotee of Vāsudeva, being purified of all sins, attains the eternal Brahma.”

In the Bhāgavata, addressing Uddhava, Bhagavān śrī Kr̥ṣṇa says—

भक्त्योद्धवानपायिन्या सर्वलोकमहेश्वरम्।

सर्वोत्पत्त्यप्ययं ब्रह्म कारणं मोपयाति स:॥

(XI. xviii. 45)

“O Uddhava, by such unswerving devotion a person attains Me, the Brahma, the Supreme Ruler of all the worlds, the cause of birth and destruction of all.”

सर्वं मद्भक्तियोगेन मद्भक्तो लभतेऽञ्जसा।

स्वर्गापवर्गं मद्धाम कथञ्चिद् यदि वाञ्छति॥

(XI. xx. 33)

“Paradise, liberation and even My own Abode, or whatever other object My devotee may desire, he may fully attain all this through the one course of devotion to Me.”

एवमेतान् मयाऽऽदिष्टाननुतिष्ठन्ति मे पथ:।

क्षेमं विन्दन्ति मत्स्थानं यद् ब्रह्म परमं विदु:॥

(XI. xx. 37)

“Those who follow these ways of devotion indicated by Me (in this chapter), reach My supreme Abode, which is the very embodiment of Bliss; for they come to know the truth about the Supreme Brahma.”

Many such verses are to be found in the scriptures, but they are not being quoted here because it is not intended to lengthen the article.

In short, disinterested action and the Yoga of devotion, both are independent paths of discipline, and firstly, Karmayoga, then Adoration and, lastly Knowledge—this is also an order of procedure. And these three disciplines are each capable of leading to God-realization, when practised separately and independently. The Lord has made this point clear in verse 24 of Chapter XIII of the Gītā—

ध्यानेनात्मनि पश्यन्ति केचिदात्मानमात्मना।

अन्ये सांख्येन योगेन कर्मयोगेन चापरे॥

“Some by meditation behold the Self (Paramātmā) in their own heart with the help of their pure reason; others by proceeding along the path of Knowledge and others, again, by treading the path of Action.”

Besides these, in Chapter IV, verses 24 to 29 of the Gītā, in the name of the different forms of sacrifice, the Lord has laid down many other means of God-realization. In his Yoga-darśana, Maharṣi PataÛjali also has described various practices which lead to God-realization. Out of these, man should select a discipline for himself and earnestly take to its practice. The fruit of all disciplines is attainment of ‘true Knowledge’ of God, and the fruit of this ‘true Knowledge’ is God-realization.

 

Need of Dispassion and Devotion for God-realization

For the sake of God-realization every striver needs must get rid of attachment. Love for gold, woman, honour, fame, prestige, progeny, riches, dainties, smartness, luxury, comfort and all other worldly enjoyments induces evil propensities like lust and anger, greed and infatuation, jealousy and hatred; vices such as lying, deceit, thieving, gambling, violence, adultery, eating prohibited food, drinking and so on; evil habits such as visiting picture-dromes and indecent plays and shows, playing at dice and cards, use of intoxicating drugs, smoking, idling etc., as well as suffering of every description—bodily distemper and mental anguish, those brought about by divine agency and those caused by other living beings; in short, it is the root of all evils. Hence love or attachment for objects of senses must be totally given up. In other words, one should conceive an intense aversion for all these objects. So long as a strong disinclination for the enjoyments of the world and one’s body does not appear, a striver cannot achieve success in any spiritual endeavour; for in all spiritual endeavour; dispassion is most essential. No spiritual endeavour can bear fruit without dispassion.

In his Aphorisms on Yoga the great sage PataÛjali, has recommended only two sovereign recipes for setting at rest the functioning of the mind—repeated practice and dispassion—

अभ्यासवैराग्याभ्यां तन्निरोध:।

(1.12)

“Complete cessation of the functioning of the mind is brought about only through Abhyāsa (continued practice) and dispassion.”

तत्र स्थितौ यत्नोऽभ्यास:।

(I. 13)

“Of the two, Abhyāsa consists in endeavouring to steady the mind.”

स तु दीर्घकालनैरन्तर्यसत्कारासेवितो दृढभूमि:।

(I. 14)

“That (Abhyāsa), however, gets firmly rooted (only) when duly carried on for a long time uninterruptedly with reverence.”

Then the sage proceeds to define Vairāgya (dispassion) as follows—

दृष्टानुश्रविकविषयवितृष्णस्य वशीकारसंज्ञा वैराग्यम्।

(I. 15)

“The state known by the name of Vaśīkāra (subjugation) of a mind which is free from covetousness with regard to (all) objects of senses—whether (actually) perceived or known through the Vedas—is called Vairāgya (dispassion).

तत् परं पुरुषख्यातेर्गुणवैतृष्ण्यम्।

(I. 16)

“Total absence of covetousness with regard to the modes of Prakr̥ti, following from the realization of the Puruṣa (Spirit), is known as Para Vairāgya (supreme dispassion).”

Thus it is proved that without dispassion the functioning of the mind does not cease and that there is no Self-realization so long as the functioning of the mind does not cease. As a matter of fact, Self-realization comes only when the mind has been fully subdued.

The sage PataÛjali says—

योगश्चित्तवृत्तिनिरोध:।

(I. 2)

“Yoga is complete cessation of the functioning of the mind.”

तदा द्रष्टु: स्वरूपेऽवस्थानम्।

(I. 3)

“Then the subject (the perceiver) is established in his own reality.”

Even the realization, according to the principles of Vedānta philosophy or the doctrine of Monism that there is only one entity (viz., the Absolute), is not possible without dispassion. It is most essential for a follower of the doctrine of Monism to be equipped with the four requisites (साधनचतुष्टय), of which the first two, viz., Viveka and Vairāgya are the foremost. The four requisites are—

(1) Viveka—True knowledge distinguishing the real from the unreal and the everlasting from the transient.

(2) Vairāgya—Total absence of attachment for the body and all mundane objects.

(3) The sixfold endowment (षट्सम्पत्ति)—Consisting of 1. śama, 2. Dama, 3. Uparati, 4. Titikṣā, 5. śraddhā and 6. Samādhāna.

1. śama—through tameness, steadiness and quietude of the mind.

2. Dama—through tameness and abstinency of the senses.

3. Uparati—complete cessation of fidgetiness or distraction of the mind in the shape of thoughts of various kinds.

4. Titikṣā—enduring pairs of opposites such as heat and cold (Gītā II. 14).

5. śraddhā—reverent faith of the same type as we have for things seen by us in the Vedas and other sacred books, God and holy men as well as in the other world;

6. Samādhāna—complete withdrawal of the mind and intellect from all worldly objects and their absorption into the object of adoration.

(4) Mumukṣutva—a keen desire to the exclusion of every other desire to redeem one’s soul.

None of these requisites can be had without Viveka and Vairāgya and without these requisites it is not possible to carry on the practice of hearing discourses on, cogitating and contemplating on Brahma (the Absolute)—which is truth, consciousness and bliss combined—much less to attain God-realization in the shape of direct knowledge of Brahma. Hence Vairāgya is most essential even for the cultivation of JÛāna (spiritual enlightenment). The Lord too says in the Gītā—

बाह्यस्पर्शेष्वसक्तात्मा विन्दत्यात्मनि यत् सुखम्।

स ब्रह्मयोगयुक्तात्मा सुखमक्षयमश्नुते॥

(V. 21)

“He whose mind remains unattached to sense-objects derives through meditation the Sāttvika joy which dwells in the mind; then, having completely identified himself through meditation with Brahma, that Yogī enjoys eternal bliss.”

For,

ये हि संस्पर्शजा भोगा दु:खयोनय एव ते।

आद्यन्तवन्त: कौन्तेय न तेषु रमते बुध:॥

(V. 22)

“The pleasures which are born of sense-contacts are, really speaking, a source of misery alone (though appearing as enjoyable to worldly-minded people). They have a begin-ning and an end (they come and go). Arjuna, it is for this reason that a wise man does not indulge in them.”

How these sensuous enjoyments are a source of agony is pointed out by the sage PataÛjali in the following aphorism—

परिणामतापसंस्कारदु:खैर्गुणवृत्तिविरोधाच्च दु:खमेव सर्वं विवेकिन:।

(II.15)

“Because every enjoyment (in this world) not only ends in agony but is attended with agony and because its impression on the mind (of the enjoyer) too is painful (both here and hereafter) and because the functions of the three Guṇas conflict with one another (even at the time of enjoyment), all enjoyment is affliction in the eye of the discriminating.”

It is therefore that, while discussing the factors helpful to the attainment of spiritual enlightenment, the Lord dwells on aversion to the pleasures of sense in the following verses—

इन्द्रियार्थेषु वैराग्यमनहंकार एव च।

जन्ममृत्युजराव्याधिदु:खदोषानुदर्शनम्॥

असक्तिरनभिष्वङ्ग: पुत्रदारगृहादिषु।

नित्यं च समचित्तत्वमिष्टानिष्टोपपत्तिषु॥

“Aversion towards the objects of enjoyment of this world and the next and also absence of egotism; pondering again and again on the pain and evils inherent in birth, old age and disease; absence of attachment and the feeling of mineness with respect to son, wife, home etc., and constant equipoise of mind both in favourable and unfavourable circumstances.”

Further, while discussing the topic of JÛānayoga in Discourse XVIII, the Lord says—

बुद्‍ध्या विशुद्धया युक्तो धृत्यात्मानं नियम्य च।

शब्दादीन्विषयांस्त्यक्त्वा रागद्वेषौ व्युदस्य च॥

विविक्तसेवी लघ्वाशी यतवाक्कायमानस:।

ध्यानयोगपरो नित्यं वैराग्यं समुपाश्रित:॥

(XVIII. 51-52)

“Endowed with an untarnished intellect and partaking of a light, Sāttvika and regulated diet, living in a lonely and undefiled place, having rejected sound and other objects of sense and controlled in his mind, speech and body by restraining the mind and senses through firmness of a Sāttvika type, and taking a resolute stand on dispassion, a man ever devoted to the Yoga of meditation (becomes qualified for oneness with Brahma).”

The practice of Karmayoga too cannot proceed without dispassion. It is only by shaking off attachment that one gets firmly established in Yoga (the path of disinterested action). The Lord says in the Gītā—

तस्मादसक्त: सततं कार्यं कर्म समाचर।

असक्तो ह्याचरन् कर्म परमाप्नोति पूरुष:॥

(III. 19)

“Therefore, go on doing your duty well without attachment. Doing work without attachment, man reaches the Supreme.”

That is why—

कायेन मनसा बुद्‍ध्या केवलैरिन्द्रियैरपि।

योगिन: कर्म कुर्वन्ति सङ्गं त्यक्त्वाऽऽत्मशुद्धये॥

(Gītā V. 11)

“Karmayogīs perform action with their senses, mind, intellect and body as well, giving up the feeling of mineness with respect to them and shaking off attachment, simply for the sake of self-purification.”

यदा हि नेन्द्रियार्थेषु न कर्मस्वनुषज्जते।

सर्वसंकल्पसंन्यासी योगारूढस्तदोच्यते॥

(Ibid., VI. 4)

“When a man ceases to have any attachment either for the objects of senses or for actions, and has renounced all thought of the world, he is said to have climbed to the heights of Yoga.”

As for the Yoga of devotion one gets established in it only through deep-rooted disinclination for the enjoyments of the world and exclusive love for the Lord. Hence he who treads the path of devotion should develop deep-rooted disinclination for the enjoyments of the world and exclusive love for the Lord. If a man cherishes love for anything other than the Lord, his devotion is divided. It is through undivided devotion that the Lord is directly perceived; nay, His reality too can be truly known through such devotion. The Lord says—

भक्त्या त्वनन्यया शक्य अहमेवंविधोऽर्जुन।

ज्ञातुं द्रष्टुं च तत्त्वेन प्रवेष्टुं च परंतप॥

(Ibid., XI. 4)

“Through single-minded devotion, however, I can be seen in this form (with four arms), nay, known in essence and even entered into (attained in terms of identity), O valiant one !”

Exclusive devotion is only another name for undivided love—love which is absolutely disinterested and imbued with supreme reverence and which is characterized by the notion that the Almighty Lord alone is supreme, that He alone is our master, worth approaching for protection, nay, our highest goal and supreme refuge, our father and mother, brother and relation, greatest benefactor, our only possession, that we have none else to call our own. In other words, that love which is entirely free from the blemish of self-interest, pride and aberration, which is always and in every way integral and unflinching, which is absolutely undivided and which makes it unbearable for the love to forget the Lord even for an instant, goes by the name of exclusive devotion.

In his Aphorisms on Bhakti the sage Nārada says—

नारदस्तु तदर्पिताखिलाचारता तद्विस्मरणे परमव्याकुलतेति।

(Bhakti-Sūtras, 19)

“According to Nārada, however, Bhakti (devotion) consists in offering all activities to God and feeling supremely restless at the slightest interruption in the thought of God.”

Therefore, developing a strong disinclination towards the enjoyments of the world and one’s body, and cultivating exclusive love for the Lord, one must take refuge in Him in every way. Figuratively referring to the world as a tree, the Lord too stresses in the following verses the advisability of cultivating dispassion and taking refuge in Him;—

न रूपमस्येह तथोपलभ्यते

नान्तो न चादिर्नं च सम्प्रतिष्ठा।

अश्वत्थमेनं सुविरूढमूल-

मसङ्गशस्त्रेण दृढेन छित्त्वा॥

तत: पदं तत् परिमार्गितव्यं

यस्मिन् गता न निवर्तन्ति भूय:॥

तमेव चाद्यं पुरुषं प्रपद्ये

यत: प्रवृत्ति: प्रसृता पुराणी॥

(Gītā XV. 3-4)

“The nature of this cosmic tree does not on mature thought turn out what it is represented to be; for it has neither beginning nor end nor (even) stability. Therefore, cutting down this Peepul tree, which is most firmly rooted, with the strong axe of dispassion, a man should then diligently make an enquiry about that supreme state, viz., God, attaining to which they return no more to this world; and having concluded that he stands dedicated to that primeval Being (God Nārāyaṇa) Himself, from whom the flow of this beginningless creation has progressed, he should meditate on Him.”

The Lord now announces the reward of this Sādhana in the following verse—

निर्मानमोहा जितसङ्गदोषा

अध्यात्मनित्या विनिवृत्तकामा:।

द्वन्द्वैर्विमुक्ता: सुखदु:खसंज्ञै-

र्गच्छन्त्यमूढा: पदमव्ययं तत्॥

(XV. 5)

“Those wise men who are free from pride and infatuation, who have conquered the evil of attachment, who are in eternal union with God, whose cravings have altogether ceased and who are entirely free from all pairs of opposites going by the name of pleasure and pain, attain to that immortal state.”

Hence one should do well to conceive some relationship with the Lord and develop love for Him. The feeling that ‘the Lord is my master and I His servant’ is known by the name of Dāsya-bhāva; the feeling of Bharata and Hanumān towards śrī Rāma was of this type. The feeling that ‘the Lord is my greatest friend’ goes by the name of Sakhya-bhāva; this type of feeling characterized the Devotion of Arjuna towards śrī Kr̥ṣṇa. The feeling that ‘the Lord is my beloved son’ is designated as Vātsalya-bhāva; such was the feeling of mother Yaśodā towards śrī Kr̥ṣṇa. The feeling that ‘the Lord is my own husband’ is known as Swakīya Mādhurya-bhāva or Kanta-bhāva; the feeling of Rukmiṇī towards śrī Kr̥ṣṇa was of this type. And the feeling that ‘Lord is my darling and friend’ is known as the Parakīya Mādhurya-bhāva, a most sublime and innocent feeling that characterized the devotion of śrī Rādhā andthe other cowherdesses of Vraja. Inspired by any of these feelings, one should enter into a relationship with the Lord. When some sort of relation marked with supreme reverence and devotion is established with the Lord, one gradually comes to develop exclusive love for the Lord. It is no longer possible for such a lover of the Lord to be disunited from Him; for a man is sure to develop constant and abiding love for an object that he truly regards as his own and such an object never escapes from his heart. Nay, his mind automatically ceases to have any attachment for everything other than that. Loving remembrance of the Lord is ever present in the mind of such a lover, as a result of which he is speedily enabled to realize Him.

Hence we should cultivate undivided love for the Lord in the aforesaid manner and, observing the strict vow of a Cātaka bird and cherishing exclusive devotion to Him, get impatient for His sight. Though dying of thirst, a Cātaka would never stoop to touch water fallen on the ground, but would turn to the cloud and utter its shrill notes of ‘Pihu!’ ‘Pihu!!’. It would begin to warble even at the sight of a waterless cloud and would centre its hopes in the cloud alone. Even if the latter rained hailstones, smashing its wings thereby, it would continue to direct its eyes towards the cloud and pine for rain-drops. Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa says—

उपल बरषि गरजत तरजि डारत कुलिस कठोर।

चितव कि चातक मेघ तजि कबहुँ दूसरी ओर॥

“Even if the cloud rains hailstones and thunders in a menacing way with a terrible flash of lightning, will the Cātaka ever turn its eyes towards anyone other than the cloud?”

Therefore,

जौं घन बरसै समय सिर जौं भरि जनम उदास।

तुलसी या चित चातकहि तऊ तिहारी आस॥

“O cloud in the guise of śrī Rāma, whether You rain (shower Your grace) at the appropriate time or remain indifferent throughout my life, the Cātaka of my mind, says Tulasīdāsa, will (ever) have its hopes centred in You.”

In the same way we too should long for the Lord’s sight and rivet our hopes on Him, awaiting His grace every moment. Whatever trials and tribulations we may have to face, undaunted like the Cātaka bird, which keeps yearning for rain drops alone, let us crave for the sight of the Lord and for nothing else.

To cite another illustration, just as a fish gets uneasy without water and finds itself in throes of death if it fails to get water even for a short while, we should experience a similar state of uneasiness due to separation from the Lord. The longing for a sight of the Lord grows very intense in the agony of separation and such intense longing is attended with a joyful experience of a unique kind, the like of which is never had in union.

When a striver gets impatient for a sight of the Lord, the latter feels compelled to appear before his eyes immediately, even as He reached the home of Rukmiṇī and took her to Dwārakā when the latter wept in agony caused by separation from Him. When a man grows altogether impatient in his agony of separation from the Lord, and finds himself on the verge of death, the Lord can no long tarry even for a moment. When the cowherdesses of Vraja wailed in their agony of separation from the Lord and were about to die, Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa appeared in their midst and enacted the great Rāsa dance with them.

We should experience the same sort of agony for the Lord as Bharata did due to his separation from Lord śrī Rāma. Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa portrays his agony and lament in the following words—

रहेउ एक दिन अवधि अधारा।

समुझत मन दुख भयउ अपारा॥

कारन कवन नाथ नहिं आयउ।

जानि कुटिल किधौं मोहि बिसरायउ॥

अहह धन्य लछिमन बड़ भागी।

राम पदार बिंदु अनुरागी॥

कपटी कुटिल मोहि प्रभु चीन्हा।

ताते नाथ संग नहिं लीन्हा॥

जौं करनी समुझै प्रभु मोरी।

नहिं निस्तार कलप सत कोरी॥

जन अवगुन प्रभु मान न काऊ।

दीनबंधु अति मृदुल सुभाऊ॥

मोरे जियँ भरोस दृढ़ सोई।

मिलिहहिं राम सगुन सुभ होई।

बीतें अवधि रहहिं जौं प्राना।

अधम कवन जग मोहि समाना॥

राम बिरह सागर महँ भरत मगन मन होत।

बिप्र रूप धरि पवनसुत आइ गयउ जनु पोत॥

बैठे देखि कुसासन जटा मुकुट कृस गात।

राम राम रघुपति जपत स्रवत नयन जलजात॥

(śrī Rāmacaritamānasa, UttaraKāṇḍa)

“The term of śrī Rāma’s exile, which was the sole hope of his life, was going to expire only a day hence the thought filled Bharata’s mind with untold grief. How is it that the Lord did not turn up? Has He cast me out of His mind, knowing me to be crooked? Ah! how blessed and fortunate is Lakṣmaṇa, who is truly devoted to śrī Rāma’s lotus-feet. The Lord knew me to be false and perverse; that is why He refused to take me along with Him. If the Lord were to consider my doings, there would be no redemption for me even after countless cycles. But the Lord never takes into account the faults of His devotees, being a friend of the humble and most tender-hearted. I have a firm conviction in my heart that śrī Rāma will surely meet me; for the omens are so propitious. But, if I outlive the expiry of the time-limit, no one would be so despicable in this world as I. “While Bharata’s mind was thus sinking in the ocean of separation from śrī Rāma, the son of the wind-god, disguised as a Brāhmaṇa, came like a bark to his rescue. He found Bharata seated on a mat of Kuśa grass, emaciated in body, with a coil of matted hair for a crown and the words ‘Rāma, Rāma, Raghupati’ on his lips, his lotus-eyes streaming with tears.”

What a sublime picture of dispassion and undivided love! When the Lord contemplated this state of mind of Bharata, he first despatched Hanumān and then went personally to him.

Thus overwhelmed with the agony of separation from the Lord, the striver should invoke His presence by uttering His most sacred names—and, mentally perceiving Him, should meditate on that mental image of the Lord. Thus enjoying His sight and touch with his mind and conversing with Him, he should get immersed in love for the Lord like the sage Sutīkṣṇa of the Rāmacaritamānasa.

Sutīkṣṇa was so overwhelmed with love for śrī Rāma that he ceased to have an idea of the four quarters or of the intermediate points of the compass. Witnessing this love-lorn state of Sutīkṣṇa, the Lord revealed Himself in his heart. The sage now sat down motionless on the road, his body bristling all over like a jack-fruit with its hair standing on end. Lord śrī Rāma thereupon drew near and was enraptured to see his ecstatic mood. The Lord tried His best to rouse the sage from his trance; but the latter would not wake. The Lord then withdrew His image from his heart. Overwhelmed with agony, the sage now opened his eyes and found the Lord before his eyes enraptured through love, the sage fell at His feet. The Lord lifted him up with His arms and pressed him to His bosom. Sutīkṣṇa fixed his gaze exclusively on the Lord, extolled Him and conducting Him to his hermitage, offered Him homage in ways more than one.

In this way when the striver gets absorbed in meditation on the Lord, He sometimes appears in person and reveals Himself before his very eyes. Following the noble example of the loving devotee Sutīkṣṇa, therefore, we should get absorbed in meditation on the Lord.

Then the striver should picture to his mind that the Lord has appeared in the sky and is incessantly shedding on him rays of His forgiveness, compassion, tranquillity, contentment, wisdom, dispassion, even-mindedness, love, joy and other divine virtues; that the nectarean rays of light emanating from those divine virtues of the Lord are saturating every atom of his mind, senses and body with divine joy and pervading the entire space in such a way that those transcendent virtues are permeating every pore of his being and infusing in him liveliness, animation, joy and tranquillity that know no bounds; and that the striver is feeling enraptured again and again to behold that divine personality of the Lord and is gazing on it with unwinking eyes. He should then picture to himself that the Lord has descended on the earth and set His feet on the ground; that, enraptured at His sight, the striver is falling prostrate at His feet and that the Lord is lifting him up and clasping him to His bosom in the same way as Lord śrī Rāma hugged the sage Sutīkṣṇa and later on His own young brother, Bharata. The striver should also feel that the aforesaid sight, touch, speech and talk, nay, the very thought of the Lord is supremely delightful, infinitely enjoyable, sweet as nectar, surcharged with love and full of joy.

While mentally touching the Lord’s person with his hands, the striver should feel as if the hair all over his body are standing on end a thrill of joy is running through his body. And while pressing Him to his bosom he should feel as if an electric current of joy is passing through his entire frame. While gazing on Him the striver should feel as if his eyes are drinking divine nectar. He should conceive the face and eyes of the Lord as blooming like a full-blown lotus. Just as a bee flies from one flower to another and thence to a third, his eyes now rest on the Lord’s mouth and now on His cheeks; while at another moment they meet the Lord’s eyes and enjoy nectarean bliss. Just as the Cakora bird remains constantly gazing on the full moon so long as it is visible in the sky, the striver should continue to scan with his mind’s eye the eyes and cheeks, nay, the entire face of the Lord and feel transported with joy while enjoying their blissful sight. The Lord’s speech should likewise be conceived as extremely soft, most charming, exceedingly sweet, nay, full of nectar, delightful and most agreeable to the ear. An ethereal fragrance should be conceived as emanating from His body—a fragrance which is full of nectar and enjoyable to the nose. Similarly His touch should be conceived as full of nectar and sweetness to one’s bosom and hands. Even as the cowherdesses of Vraja revelled in the Lord through their sight, speech, touch, dance and music and were drowned in an ocean of joy even while contemplating on the Lord with their mind and comprehending His greatness with their reason, so the striver too should revel in Him with his mind, intellect, senses and body and enjoy His transcendent sweetness. In the Gītā too the Lord says—

मच्चित्ता मद्गतप्राणा बोधयन्त: परस्परम्।

कथयन्तश्च मां नित्यं तुष्यन्ति च रमन्ति च॥

(X. 9)

“With their mind fixed on Me and their lives surrendered to Me, (nay). enlightening one another about My greatness and speaking of Me, My devotees ever remain gratified and revel in Me.”

To fix one’s mind on the Lord and to commune with Him with one’s mind, body and senses—this is what is meant by revelling in Him; and to enjoy the transcendent bliss arising out of such loving communion—this is what is meant by being gratified. Just as water is the mainstay of life to a fish, the Lord is the prop of life to a devotee. And just as the Gopīs of Vraja talked with one another about the true character and secret of Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa and thereby apprised one another of His truth and mysteries, so should the striver feel as if he is realizing the truth and secret of His divine character by talking with the Lord Himself and also as if he is speaking to the Lord about His virtues, glory and essential character.

When a striver gets absorbed in this way in meditation on the Lord and mentally communes with Him, the hair on his body stand on end, tears begin to flow from his eyes, his throat gets choked and speech fails him and his heart expands with joy. The sage Nārada says—

भक्ता एकान्तिनो मुख्या:। कण्ठावरोधरोमाञ्चाश्रुभि: परस्परं लपमाना: पावयन्ति कुलानि पृथिवीं च।

(Aphorisms on Devotion 67-68)

“Devotees who are exclusively attached to the Lord are the best. Devotees of this type, conversing with each other in a voice choked with emotion, the hairs on their body standing on end and eyes wet with tears, sanctify not only their race but the whole earth.”

The Lord Himself says in śrīmad Bhāgavata—

वाग् गद्गदा द्रवते यस्य चित्तं

रुदत्यभीक्ष्णं हसति क्वचिच्च।

विलज्ज उद्गायति नृत्यते च

मद्भक्तियुक्तो भुवनं पुनाति॥

(XI. xiv. 24)

“The person rich in devotion to Me, whose speech fails him and heart melts, who repeatedly weeps and sometimes laughs and sings loudly and dances without shyness, purifies the (whole) world.”

Describing the devotion of such a devotee, who is overwhelmed with love, the saint Sundaradāsa says in his Sundaravilāsa—

न लाज तीन लोक की, न वेद को कह्यौ करै।

न संक भूत प्रेत की न देव जच्छ तें डरै॥

सुनै न कान और की द्रसै न और इच्छना।

कहै न मुख और बात, भक्ति प्रेम लच्छना॥

“When a devotee gets absorbed in loving devotion, he feels shy before none in all the three worlds. In other words, he loses all sense of bashfulness. He is no longer capable of respecting the commandments of the Vedas and other scriptures; for, being full of love, he ceases to have any consciousness of the outside world. He entertains no fear of ghosts and other evil spirits nor does he feel afraid of gods or demigods. He hears no other talk than that of God-love and sees none else than his beloved Lord nor does he crave for anything else. He utters with his tongue nothing else than the praises of the Lord. This is what goes by the name of loving devotion to the Lord.”

By thus offering loving adoration to the Lord in the form of revelling in His company the striver comes to attain true knowledge of God and is enabled, through such knowledge vouchsafed by the Lord Himself, to perceive Him directly. At the moment of such perception he gazes on the Lord with unwinking eyes; his ego-sense vanishes, or in other words he forgets his very self and like one spell-bound remains conscious of the Lord’s personality alone. At the time of one’s meeting with the Lord all sense of modesty and diffidence, self-esteem, fear, honour and respect leaves him. The striver rises above such feelings. Here the lover, the beloved Lord and the love subsisting between them are all one from the point of view of their spiritual substance, although they have a separate existence of their own. From the point of view of the Lord, He Himself is the lover, the devotee being His beloved; whereas, from the point of view of the devotee, he is the lover, the Lord being the beloved; and their relation with one another goes by the name of love. It seems as if love alone manifests itself in the dual form of the Lord and His devotee and exhibits its sport. Every effort of the Lord is intended to enrapture the devotee and vice versa. Their love as depicted above is ever on the increase and is never dormant. Both stand on an equal footing; nay, there is absolute identity between them. There is no feeling of honour or respect for each other. Honour and respect, self-esteem and fear, modesty and diffidence characterize the relation of master and servant. Although bashfulness and diffidence are absent in the relation of husband and wife, honour and respect, pride and fear continue even there. The relation of parent and son is also marked by honour and respect, fear, bashfulness and diffidence. Although honour and respect and fear too are absent in the attitude of friendliness, diffidence is present even there. In the domain of love, however, absolute identity and equality reign supreme. Hence there is no tinge of modesty and diffidence, pride and fear, honour and respect in this relation inasmuch as they constitute a stigma on pure, unalloyed love. The nature of such transcendent love cannot be depicted in words. The sage Nārada says—

अनिर्वचनीयं प्रेमस्वरूपम्। मूकास्वादनवत्॥

(Bhakti-Sūtras 51-52)

“Divine Love is indefinable in nature. It is like the pleasure of the palate enjoyed by the dumb.”

प्रकाशते क्वापि पात्रे।

(Ibid., 53)

“This Love manifests itself in some rare qualified souls (devotees in the path of Love).”

गुणरहितं कामनारहितं प्रतिक्षणवर्धमानमविच्छिन्नं सूक्ष्मतरमनुभवरूपम्।

(Ibid., 54)

“This Love is devoid of guṇas, seeks no return, grows from more to more every moment, knows no break, is subtler than the subtlest and is of the nature of experience.”

तत्प्राप्य तदेवावलोकयति तदेव शृणोति तदेव भाषयति तदेव चिन्तयति।

(Ibid., 55)

“Attaining this Love, the loving devotee sees nothing but Love, hears only about Love, speaks only of Love and thinks of Love alone.”

A devotee endowed with such unalloyed and undivided love is rid of infatuation once for all. Such a devotee comes to realize by His grace in due course, the essential character of the Lord—whatever it is and whatever it is like, whether with or without form, saguṇa or Nirguṇa. The Lord says in the Gītā—

तेषां सततयुक्तानां भजतां प्रीतिपूर्वकम्।

ददामि बुद्धियोगं तं येन मामुपयान्ति ते॥

(X. 10)

“On those ever united through meditation with Me and worshipping Me with love, I confer that Yoga of wisdom through which they come to Me.”

Then we should extol the Lord in the following words of Arjuna—

परं ब्रह्म परं धाम पवित्रं परमं भवान्।

पुरुषं शाश्वतं दिव्यमादिदेवमजं विभुम्॥

आहुस्त्वामृषय: सर्वे देवर्षिर्नारदस्तथा।

असितो देवलो व्यास: स्वयं चैव ब्रवीषि मे॥

(Gītā X. 12-13)

“You are the transcendent Eternal, the supreme abode and the greatest purifier; all the seers speak of you as the eternal divine Puruṣa, the primal Deity, unborn and All-pervading. Likewise speak the celestial sage Nārada, the sages Asita and Devala and the great sage Vyāsa, and Yourself too proclaim this to me.”

Let us conclude with the following prayer of the sages Sanaka and his three brothers as occurring in śrīRāmacaritamānasa—

परमानंद कृपायतन मन परिपूरन काम।

प्रेम भगति अनपायनी देहु हमहि श्रीराम॥

(Uttara-Kāṇḍa 34)

“You are supreme bliss personified and the abode of mercy and fulfil the desire of Your devotees’ heart. Pray, grant me the boon of unceasing love and devotion (to Your feet), O graceful Rāma.”

 

Means of Attaining Supreme Peace

Generally speaking, all men long to attain peace, but they search for it in the pleasures of sense alone, peace does not, however, lie in the pleasures of sense. On the other hand, they are brimful with disquietude alone. When peace does not at all exist in the pleasures of sense, how can it be found when sought for in them? Just as coolness is impossible to get in fire, peace is impossible to get in the pleasures of sense. Through ignorance many men imagine peace to lie in the advancement of worldly happiness, affluence and power. Peace, however, does not lie in worldly happiness. The momentary peace that appears in it is a mere reflection of an iota of it. A semblance of peace alone appears in it through misconception; in reality there is no peace in it. Had there been real peace in it, it would abide; for that alone which is real abides—such is the positive conclusion of exalted souls. The Lord says in the Gītā—

नासतो विद्यते भावो नाभावो विद्यते सत:।

उभयोरपि दृष्टोऽन्तस्त्वनयोस्तत्त्वदर्शिभि:॥

(II. 16)

“The unreal has no existence and the real never ceases to be; the reality of both has thus been perceived by the seers of truth.”

Therefore the peace which appears to lie in worldly enjoyments is only apparent, illusory and not real, just as joy has been classified under various heads—the delight coming from sleep, languor and obstinate error is Tāmasika ( Gītā XVIII. 39); that proceeding from the contact of the senses with their objects is Rājasika (Ibid. XVIII. 38), while the cheerfulness following from the placidity of mind in Sāttvika (Ibid. XVIII. 36-37), so in the same way peace should be classified under three heads. The tranquillity which appears to lie in sleep, languor and obstinate error is Tāmasika, that which appears in enjoyments proceeding from the contact of the senses with their objects is Rājasika, while that which appears in the absence of distraction or restlessness of mind is Sāttvika (Ibid. XVI. 2).

Though helpful in God-realization, even these Sāttvika joy and peace prove to be a source of bondage (Gītā XIV) and interfere with the spiritual discipline of the striver and retard its progress if the striver develops an attachment for them; for, being identified with the body and looking upon it as his own, the striver comes through ignorance to esteem himself as superior. Nay, self-esteem sometimes leads to the striver’s downfall. Hence a striver should never be proud of his or her virtues. Exalted souls who have realized God for their part never pride themselves on their excellences. Through ignorance a striver, however, in some cases comes to regard himself as superior. He should therefore always beware of this contingency and should give up the notion of superiority.

God is most tranquil by nature. In the benedictory verse at the beginning of Sundarakāṇḍa of śrī Rāmacaritamānasa Goswāmī Tulasīdāsa says—

शान्तं शाश्वतमप्रमेयमनघं निर्वाणशान्तिप्रदम्।

“I bow to śrī Rāma, who is placid, eternal, beyond the ordinary means of cognition and sinless, the bestower of supreme peace in the form of final beatitude.”

That supreme peace which is beyond the three Guṇas or modes of Prakr̥ti comes to him who has realized the supremely placid God. Lord śrī Rāma has declared ‘śānti’ (placidity) to be the main characteristic of saints among other such characteristics—

“Having no interested motive of their own, they are devoted to My Name and are abodes of tranquillity, dispassion, humility and good humour.”*

* बिगत काम मम नाम परायन।

सांति बिरति बिनती मुदितायन॥

The tranquillity that reigns in the heart of the striver at the time of his spiritual endeavour is of the Sāttvika type and leads to supreme peace. That peace in the form of God-realization has been referred to in the Gītā and other scriptures as śāśwatī śānti (Everlasting Peace), Paramā śānti (Supreme Peace), Naiṣṭhikī śānti (Perpetual Peace), Amr̥ta (immortality), Brahma (the Eternal), Paramātmā (the Supreme Spirit), Parama Pada (the Supreme Goal), Parama Sthāna (the Supreme State), Parama Dhāma (the Supreme Abode of God). Paramā Gati (the Supreme Destiny) etc. Several means for attaining the aforesaid Supreme Peace have been recommended in the Gītā and other holy books. The following three, viz., Karmayoga (the Path of Action), JÛānayoga (the Discipline of Knowledge) and Bhaktiyoga (the Path of Devotion) are the foremost among them. A passing reference is made to them in the following paragraphs.

This Supreme Peace is natural in God-realized souls while it is acquired by strivers through endeavour. This alone is the reward even of actions done in a disinterested spirit by strivers pursuing the Path of Action; for it is not actions but the disinterested spirit that counts in the Path of Action. And the mind of the striver pursuing the Path of Action gets purified when he develops a fully disinterested spirit. His reverence for the course of discipline followed by him then becomes stable and through true knowledge he attains Supreme Peace and Supreme Bliss. But peace cannot be attained without endeavour in a reverential spirit. The Lord says in the Gītā—

नास्ति बुद्धिरयुक्तस्य न चायुक्तस्य भावना।

न चाभावयत: शान्तिरशान्तस्य कुत: सुखम्॥

(II. 66)

“He who has not controlled his mind and senses can have no determinate reason, nor can such an undisciplined man have belief (in God). The unbelieving man can have no peace, and how can there be happiness for one lacking peace of mind ?” For peace comes through disinterestedness—

युक्त: कर्मफलं त्यक्त्वा शान्तिमाप्नोति नैष्ठिकीम्।

अयुक्त: कामकारेण फले सक्तो निबध्यते॥

(V. 12)

“Offering the fruit of his actions to God, the Karmayogī attains everlasting peace in the shape of God-realization; whereas he who works with a selfish motive, being attached to the fruit of actions through desire, gets tied down.”

Just as in the foregoing paragraphs peace has been declared as attainable by reverently treading the Path of Action, it is likewise attained by reverently pursuing the Path of Knowledge too.

श्रद्धावाँल्लभते ज्ञानं तत्पर: संयतेन्द्रिय:।

ज्ञानं लब्ध्वा परां शान्तिमचिरेणाधिगच्छति॥

(IV. 39)

“He who has fully controlled his senses, is exclusively devoted to spiritual practice and is full of faith attains Knowledge; having gained Knowledge, he immediately attains Supreme Peace in the form of God-realization..”

Getting rid of one’s sins and developing an aversion for the enjoyments of the world through intense practice carried on in a reverential spirit, the striver acquires full control over his senses and mind. True knowledge of the reality of God also dawns on him, whereby he is able to attain Supreme Peace.

The ripeness of spiritual discipline attained by treading the Paths of Action and Knowledge has been spoken of in the Gītā by the name of Niṣṭhā. The highest goal in the shape of Supreme Peace and Supreme Joy is the reward.

लोकेऽस्मिन्द्विविधा निष्ठा पुरा प्रोक्ता मयानघ।

ज्ञानयोगेन सांख्यानां कर्मयोगेन योगिनाम्॥

(III. 3)

“In this world two courses of Sādhana (spiritual discipline) have been enunciated by Me in the past. In the case of the Sāṅkhyayogī the Sādhana proceeds along the Path of Knowledge, whereas in the case of the Karmayogī it proceeds along the Path of Action.”

He further says—

यत्सांख्यै:प्राप्यते स्थानं तद्योगैरपि गम्यते।

एकं साख्यं च योगं च य: पश्यति स पश्यति॥

(V. 5)

“The (highest) state which is reached by Sāṅkhyayogīs is attained also by Karmayogīs. Therefore, he alone who sees Sāṅkhyayoga and Karmayoga as one, so far as their reward is concerned, really sees.”

The Lord delineates the Path of Knowledge in verses 11 to 30 and the Path of Action in verses 40 to 53 of Discourse II. The Path of Devotion too is the same as the Path of Action.

In the following verses the Lord declares eternal peace as attainable through the Path of Devotion as well—

अपि चेत्सुदुराचारो भजते मामनन्यभाक्।

साधुरेव स मन्तव्य: सम्यग्व्यवसितो हि स:॥

क्षिप्रं भवति धर्मात्मा शश्वच्छान्तिं निगच्छति।

कौन्तेय प्रति जानीहि न मे भक्त: प्रणश्यति॥

(IX. 30-31)

“Even if the vilest sinner worships Me with exclusive devotion, he should be accounted a saint, for he has rightly resolved; (he is positive in his belief that there is nothing like devoted worship of the Lord). Speedily he becomes virtuous and secures lasting peace. Know it for certain, O Arjuna, that My devotee never falls.”

Therefore it is most essential for a striver treading the Path of Devotion exclusively to worship and remember the Lord at all times without interruption with reverence and love.

For God-realization all the three Paths of Action, Devotion and Knowledge are equal. Diverse courses of discipline have been recommended only in view of diversity in fitness. A striver is qualified for that course of discipline alone for which he has reverence, faith, love and aptitude. The reward of all the three disciplines being the same, they are really identical. In the Gītā, the Lord says—

ध्यानेनात्मनि पश्यन्ति केचिदात्मानमात्मना।

अन्ये सांख्येन योगेन कर्मयोगेन चापरे॥

(XIII. 24)

“Some by meditation behold the Supreme Spirit in the heart with the help of their refined and sharp intellect; others realize it through the discipline of knowledge, and others, again, through the discipline of action.”

It has been pointed out at various places in the Gītā that a striver attains supreme peace on developing disinterestedness in a full measure through the practice of Karmayoga. The Lord says—

आपूर्यमाणमचलप्रतिष्ठं

समुद्रमाप: प्रविशन्ति यद्वत्।

तद्वत्कामा यं प्रविशन्त सर्वे

स शान्तिमाप्नोति न कामकामी॥

(II. 70)

“As the waters of different rivers enter the ocean, which, though full on all sides, remains undisturbed, likewise he in whom all enjoyments merge themselves attains peace, not he who hankers after enjoyments.”

विहाय कामान् य: सर्वान् पुमांश्चरति नि:स्पृह:।

निर्ममो निरहंकार: स शान्तिमधिगच्छति॥

(II. 71)

“He who has given up all desires and moves free from attachment, egotism and the thirst for enjoyment attains peace.”

The idea is that a man hankering after sense-enjoyments never attains peace inasmuch as his mind remains constantly agitated through a craving for various kinds of enjoyments; how can there be happiness where there is restlessness? Disquietude, worry, heart-burning and grief alone abide there. Therefore, giving up the thirst for all kinds of enjoyments of this world as also of the next and freed from egotism, the sense of mine and attachment, one should perform one’s duties enjoined bythe śāstras. This too, constitutes a valuable means for attaining supreme peace.

Supreme peace is attained even by knowing God in reality, practising exclusive devotion to Him and taking refuge in Him. Says the Lord—

भोक्तारं यज्ञतपसां सर्वलोकमहेश्वरम्।

सुहृदं सर्वभूतानां ज्ञात्वा मां शान्तिमृच्छति॥

(V. 29)

“Having known Me in reality as the enjoyer of all sacrifices and austerities, the supreme Lord of all the worlds, and the disinterested friend of all beings, My devotee attains peace.”

In the Kaṭhopaniṣad, Yama (the god of retribution) says to Naciketā—

नित्यो नित्यानां चेतनश्चेतनाना-

मेको बहूनां यो विदधाति कामान्।

तमात्मस्थं येऽनुपश्यन्ति धीरा-

स्तेषां शान्ति: शाश्वती नेतरेषाम्॥

(II. ii.13)

“Lasting peace comes to those wise men alone who constantly perceive the spirit dwelling in their mind—the spirit who is constant among the constant, (nay), conscious among the conscious and who singly gratifies the desires of many (according to their past actions), and not to others.”

We read in the śwetāśvatara Upaniṣad too—

यो योनिं योनिमधितिष्ठत्येको

यस्मिन्निदं स च वि चैति सर्वम्।

तमीशानं वरदं देवमीड्यं

निचाय्येमां शान्तिमत्यन्तमेति॥

(IV. 11)

“One attains the peace referred to above (in the form of final beatitude) without end on coming to know in reality that all-ruling Lord, the Bestower of boons, who is worth extolling, who alone controls all causes and into whom all this (creation) disappears (at the time of final dissolution) and again comes forth (at the time of creation).”

सूक्ष्मातिसूक्ष्मं कलिलस्य मध्ये

विश्वस्य स्रष्टारमनेकरूपम्।

विश्वस्यैकं परिवेष्टितारं

ज्ञात्वा शिवं शान्तिमत्यन्तमेति॥

(IV. 14)

“One attains peace without end on knowing in reality Lord śiva, the subtlest of the subtle, abiding in the cave of the heart, the creator of the universe, who is endowed with diverse forms and who alone pervades the universe.”

In śrīmad Bhāgavata, Sage Kavi, a lord of yogīs, says to King Nimi—

इत्यच्युताङ्घ्रिं भजतोऽनुवृत्त्या

भक्तिर्विरक्तिर्भगवत्प्रबोध:।

भवन्ति वै भागवतस्य राजं-

स्तत: परां शान्तिमुपैति साक्षात्॥

(XI. ii.43)

“In the votary of God, O king, who worships uninterruptedly as aforesaid the feet of śrī Hari (the immortal Lord), devotion, a distaste for the enjoyments of the world and direct knowledge of God appear (simultaneously); and through them he directly attains the state of Supreme Peace.”

On the subject of meditation the Lord says in the Gītā—

प्रशान्तात्मा विगतभीर्ब्रह्मचारिव्रते स्थित:।

मन: संयम्य मच्चित्तो युक्त आसीत मत्पर:॥

युञ्जन्नेवं सदात्मानं योगी नियतमानस:।

शान्तिं निर्वाणपरमां मत्संस्थामधिगच्छति॥

(VI. 14-15)

“Firm in the vow of complete chastity and fearless, keeping himself perfectly calm and with his mind held in restraint and fixed on Me, the vigilant yogī should sit absorbed in Me. Thus constantly applying his mind to Me, the yogī of disciplined mind attains the everlasting peace, consisting of Supreme Bliss, which abides in Me.”

Further on in Discourse XVIII the Lord declares that Peace is attained by taking refuge in the Lord—

तमेव शरणं गच्छ सर्वभावेन भारत।

तत्प्रसादात् परां शान्तिं स्थानं प्राप्स्यसि शाश्वतम्॥

“Seek Him alone as shelter with all your being, O Arjuna ! By His grace (alone) you will attain supreme peace and the eternal state.”

In this way Supreme Peace has been declared as attainable through all the three Paths of Action, Knowledge and Devotion and all the three Paths are superior in everyway in their own place.

In the Gītā disinterestedness has been declared as superior to all in the following verse—

श्रेयो हि ज्ञानमभ्यासाज्ज्ञानाद् ध्यानं विशिष्यते।

ध्यानात्कर्मफलत्यागस्त्यागाच्छान्तिरनन्तरम्॥

(XII.12)

“Knowledge is better than practice (without discernment), meditation on God is superior to knowledge and renunciation of the reward of actions is superior (even) to meditation; for peace immediately follows from renunciation.”

The idea is that that discipline alone is the foremost, which is characterized by perfect disinterestedness and while glorifying it the Lord also says—

अनाश्रित: कर्मफलं कार्यं कर्म करोति य:।

स संन्यासी च योगी च न निरग्निर्न चाक्रिय:॥

(VI. 1)

“He who does his duty without expecting the fruit of actions is a Saṁnyāsī (Sāṅkhyayogī) and a Yogī (a Karmayogī) both. He is no Saṁnyāsī (renouncer) who has merely renounced the sacred fire; even so he is no yogī who has merely given up all activity.”

The purport is that he who has renounced the sacred fire is no Saṁnyāsī if he is not actuated by a disinterested spirit and even so he who has given up all activity is no Yogī if he is not actuated by a disinterested spirit.

A striver treading the Path of Devotion has been declared as superior to all in the following verse—

योगिनामपि सर्वेषां मद्गतेनान्तरात्मना।

श्रद्धावान् भजते यो मां स मे युक्ततमो मत:॥

(VI. 47)

“Of all yogīs, again, he who devoutly worships Me with his mind focussed on Me is considered by Me to be the yogī par excellence.”

Establishing the superiority of Knowledge the Lord likewise says—

श्रेयान्द्रव्यमयाद्यज्ञाज्ज्ञानयज्ञ: परंतप।

सर्वं कर्माखिलं पार्थ ज्ञाने परिसमाप्यते॥

(IV. 33)

“Sacrifice through Knowledge is superior to sacrifice performed with material things, O scorcher of foes ! For all actions without exception culminate in Knowledge, O son of Kuntī !”

In this way all these three courses of discipline have been declared as superior, easy to follow, speedily conducive to blessedness, capable of eradicating sin and bestowing true wisdom and helpful in realizing God, who is no other than, Supreme Peace. Hence that course of discipline alone is superior to all other courses of discipline for which the striver has developed reverence, faith, a liking and love. Realizing this and falling back on that very course of discipline he should diligently pursue it so that Supreme Peace may come to him.

 

How to Realize God in an Instant?

मूकं करोति वाचालं पङ्गु लङ्घयते गिरिम्।

यत्कृपा तमहं वन्दे परमानन्दमाधवम्॥

“I bow to Lord Viṣṇu (the Spouse of Goddess Lakṣmī), who is Supreme Bliss personified, and whose Grace makes a dumb person eloquent and enables a cripple to cross a mountain.”

A certain gentleman has asked me to specify the ‘moment’ in which one may realize God at once. In reply to this question I would submit by way of illustration that a house which has been fitted with electric wiring and bulbs etc., and supplied with electric connection is lighted the moment we press the button, and the darkness prevailing there also disperses that very moment. Similarly when a spiritual aspirant becomes ‘qualified’ and fully equipped in everyway, the knowledge about God dawns on him in an instant and as soon as the enlightenment comes ignorance disappears and God-realization takes place. To take another familiar illustration, a man who is labouring under a misconception in regard to the points of the compass mistakes the east for the west and vice versa. But whenever the misconception is cleared it is cleared in a moment and a correct notion of the quarters follows that very moment. Similarly when the enlightenment about God comes, all false notions disappear even like the misconception about the points of the compass, and the true nature of God is realized. To take a third illustration, suppose a man who is asleep at night is dreaming. Meanwhile, due to some reason he wakes up. Now as soon as he wakes up the entire dream-world melts away in a moment—it altogether ceases to be. Similarly, when we wake up in the Supreme Spirit, i.e., once we get established in identity with the divine essence and our eyes of wisdom open, this world of matter entirely vanishes. Even as a dream vanishes as soon as we wake up, this phenomenal world disappears and God-realization takes place.

The misconception about the quarters is cleared automatically in due course or it gets resolved when one returns to one’s birth-place. And the dreaming man automatically wakes up the moment he comes to realize his dream to be a dream; or he can wake up even on being awakened by another. Similarly the enlightenment which automatically dawns on a man as a result of his incessantly viewing the world as a dream consequent upon a profound study of the scriptures is what has been referred to above as waking up of one’s own accord (Gītā IV. 38). And the enlightenment that comes to a man on his resorting for guidance to an exalted soul is equivalent to waking up on being awakened by another (Gītā IV. 34-35). Now reverting to the illustration of a man labouring under a misconception about the quarters, we have already seen how correct vision returns to his bewildered eyes as soon as he goes back to his native place. Similarly God-realization comes to a man the moment he reaches his birth-place in the form of getting established in identity with the divine essence. The divine essence is our birth-place; that is our essential and primordial nature. Hence the moment we are established in the divine essence the illusion of the world vanishes. Just as the misconception regarding the points of the compass, which made the east appear as the west and vice versa, gets cleared up and correct vision returns, even so the delusion which was responsible for the appearance of this world in God is also resolved. Or just as the misconception regarding the quarters gets automatically cleared, even so the illusion of this world is automatically lifted in the case of certain individuals as a result of their constantly viewing the world as a dream. And when the mind is perfectly tranquil and perspicacious and feels an aversion for the pleasures of sense at the same time, enlightenment comes of its own accord. If under such circumstances one receives instruction from a saint in the truth relating to God, true enlightenment about God is definitely assured (Gītā IV. 34-35). And at the last moment of one’s life the very contemplation on God instantly leads to God-realization. The Lord says in the Gītā—

अन्तकाले च मामेव स्मरन् मुक्त्वा कलेवरम्।

य: प्रयाति स मद्भावं याति नास्त्यत्र संशय:॥

(VIII. 5)

“He who departs from the body, thinking of Me alone even at the time of death, attains My state, there is no doubt about it.”

Similarly the Lord says in II. 72 of the Gītā—

एषा ब्राह्मी स्थिति: पार्थ नैनां प्राप्य विमुह्यति।

स्थित्वास्यामन्तकालेऽपि ब्रह्मनिर्वाणमृच्छति॥

“Such is the state of one who has realized God. O Arjuna (son of Kuntī); having reached this state, he overcomes delusion. And established in this state even at the last moment (of one’s life), he attains Brahmic bliss.”

Such is the glory of attaining this state even at the last moment of one’s life. Similarly an aspirant gains a lot if he gives up the ghost at a time when he is established in the quality of Sattva. In Gītā XIV. 18 it is stated that “those who abide in Sattvaguṇa went their way upwards (ऊर्ध्वं गच्छन्ति सत्त्वस्था:).” Although this means that he alone who constantly abides in the quality of Sattva ascends to the higher regions, it may be pointed out that a man attains a good destiny if he is established in Sattvaguṇa even at the last moment of his life or even if he gives up the ghost at a time when the quality of Sattva is predominant in him. The Lord says in the Gītā—

यदा सत्त्वे प्रवृद्धे तु प्रलयं याति देहभृत्।

तदोत्तमविदां लोकानमलान् प्रतिपद्यते॥

(XIV. 14)

“When a man dies during the preponderance of Sattva, he obtains the stainless ethereal worlds (heaven etc.) attained by men of noble deeds.”

The Lord evidently means thereby that such a man ascends to the higher regions and, proceeding further, reaches God—the supreme abode of God. This topic has been discussed at length in the commentary on Gītā VIII. 24 in GītāTattva Number II (No. 1 of Vol. XIII) of the “Kalyana-Kalpataru”. This is what they call Krama-Mukti (Gradual Liberation). Just as it is a special feature of the last moment of one’s life that even a man of Rājasika and Tāmasika disposition who departs from this world meditating on the Lord or with a clear conception of the truth relating to God is able to realize Him, even so the truth relating to God is easily realized at a time when Sattvaguṇa prevails. It is a very propitious time—as important and convenient as the last moment of one’s life. One should strive for meditation with particular care at such a time; for even with a little effort during this period one can achieve great results. But how are we to understand whether the preponderance of Sattvaguṇa has already commenced. The Lord tells us a criterion. He says in the Gītā—

सर्वद्वारेषु देहेऽस्मिन् प्रकाश उपजायते।

ज्ञानं यदा तदा विद्याद् विवृद्धं सत्त्वमित्युत॥

(XIV. 11)

“When light and discernment dawn in this body, as well as in the mind and senses, then one should know that Sattva is predominant.”

If a man meditates on God or endeavours to know the truth relating to God during the predominance of Sattvaguṇa, he succeeds very quickly. At such a time God-realization is possible through Divine Grace even in a moment. During such a period man should spend his time solely in Self-realization and meditation with a mind full of dispassion, or in the company of exalted souls and, after hearing their teachings, in striving according to the same—in establishing himself in the same. By doing so it is not impossible to realize the Truth even in an instant. This is a most remarkable phenomenon. Just as one attains the highest state by giving up the ghost while meditating or contemplating on God at the last moment of one’s life, a similar thing happens during the predominance of Sattvaguṇa as well.
