Therefore, when the body, mind, senses and intellect are all alert, all are illumined as it were both within and without and there is an abundance of consciousness, when there are no tribulations and there is a feeling of tranquillity and Sāttvika joy, these should be taken to indicate the preponderance of Sattvaguṇa. In such a state even a little effort for meditation on God may yield remarkable results.

We cited above the illustration of electric light and saw how on a house being fitted with electric wires etc., and supplied with the electric current from the power-station you get light as soon as you press the button. Even so, as a result of continued Sādhanā, when a man gets fully equipped and qualified and his mind is linked with God, he instantly realizes Him. Just as the house is illumined and darkness dispelled the moment you press the button, a little instruction brings enlightenment and God-realization to the seeker in a moment when the latter is fully equipped and qualified and ripe for such realization. As a result of meditation on God, a study of good books, deep thought and listening to the talks of noble souls and through grace of God one can have spontaneous awakening in the heart and realize God in a moment. To become qualified or fit is what is meant by getting equipped. This eligibility or fitness is acquired only when the mind gets purified. Hence allowance must be made for the time which is taken by the mind to get purified. The mind gets purified only when it is rid of its impurity and distraction and the veil of ignorance removed by following the disciplines of Bhakti (Devotion), Karma (Disinterested Action) and JÛāna (Self-knowledge). With the elimination of those three evils the truth relating to God flashes on the mind and God-realization takes place in a trice, even as a room is lighted as soon as you press the button. We are told in the following verse of śrīmadBhagavadgītā how the practice of JÛānayoga (the discipline of Self-knowledge) brings about God-realization—

भक्त्या मामभिजानाति यावान् यश्चास्मि तत्त्वत:।

ततो मां तत्त्वतो ज्ञात्वा विशते तदनन्तरम्॥

(XVIII. 55)

“Though that supreme devotion (in the shape of true knowledge concerning God) he comes to know Me in reality, what and how great I am; and thereby knowing Me in essence he forthwith enters into My being.”

This represents the state of equipment. The verse immediately preceding it gives us the distinctive marks of this state—

ब्रह्मभूत: प्रसन्नात्मा न शोचति न कांक्षति।

सम: सर्वेषु भूतेषु मद्भक्तिं लभते पराम्॥

(XVIII. 54)

“Established in identity with Brahma (who is Truth, Consciousness and Bliss solidified) and cheerful of mind, the Sāṅkhyayogī no longer grieves nor craves for anything. In other words, he is completely rid of all worries, grief and desires and sees unity in all beings. He then attains Supreme Devotion to Me (the culmination of JÛāna).”

It may be asked here—What is meant by seeing unity in all beings ? The Lord says in the Gītā—

सर्वभूतस्थमात्मानं सर्वभूतानि चात्मनि।

ईक्षते योगयुक्तात्मा सर्वत्र समदर्शन:॥

(VI. 29)

“The Yogī who is united in identity with the All-pervading infinite consciousness and sees unity everywhere beholds the Self present in all beings (as water in ice), and all beings existing in the Self as a mere projection of his thought. That is to say, even as a man who has just awoken from a dream sees the dream-world in his own self as a projection of his thought, the Yogī referred to above beholds all beings in his own self, which is of the nature of All-pervading infinite consciousness. In other words, he beholds himself in all beings in the real sense.”

This is what is meant by seeing unity everywhere. From this perception of unity follows what they call the highest consummation of JÛānayoga, which has also been designated as supreme devotion. Through this supreme devotion a man is able to know God in reality. He grasps the truth relating to all the aspects of God, viz., God with form and without form, God with attributes and without attributes and God both manifest and unmanifest. He then attains oneness with God. But before he does so he must fully equip himself for this consummation. As a preparation for this he must mould himself according to the three foregoing verses, which delineate the steps leading to the highest consummation of JÛānayoga and run as under—

बुद्‍ध्या विशुद्धया युक्तो धृत्यात्मानं नियम्य च।

शब्दादीन्विषयांस्त्यक्त्वा रागद्वेषौ व्युदस्य च॥

विविक्तसेवी लघ्वाशी यतवाक्कायमानस:।

ध्यानयोगपरो नित्यं वैराग्यं समुपाश्रित:॥

अहंकारं बलं दर्पं कामं क्रोधं परिग्रहम्।

विमुच्य निर्मम: शान्तो ब्रह्मभूयाय कल्पते॥

(XVIII. 51—53)

“Endowed with an intellect absolutely untarnished (wholly free from impurity in the form of sin) and taking a light, Sāttvika and regulated diet, living in a lonely and undefiled place, having altogether rejected sound and other objects of sense and subdued the mind, speech and body by restraining the mind and senses through firmness of a Sāttvika type, taking a resolute stand on dispassion, after having completely got rid of attraction and aversion, and remaining ever devoted to the Yoga of meditation, having given up egotism, violence, arrogance, lust, anger and luxuries, devoid of the feeling of meum and tranquil at heart—such a man becomes qualified for oneness with Brahma (who is Truth, Consciousness and Bliss).”

When a man is established in meditation, all the morbidities of his heart get eradicated and sense-objects of various kinds are also withheld from him. The impurity of his heart having already been wiped out, distraction also now disappears. In this way when he is completely rid of all evils, he becomes fit for realizing his oneness with Brahma. He is now established in identity with the Absolute and he who is established in oneness with the Absolute is called ‘Brahmabhūta.’ The state which follows as a sequel to the state of a Brahmabhūta has already been delineated above. The attainment of supreme devotion or the highest consummation of JÛānayoga directly follows the state of Brahmabhūta. This culmination of knowledge eradicates ignorance. It is this eradication of ignorance which constitutes the tearing of the veil that conceals the true nature of things. In this way God-realization takes place as soon as the three evils of impurity, distraction and ignorance are eradicated. That is why God-realization itself has been declared to be the fruit of the highest consummation of JÛānayoga. In this way when the aspirant becomes fit for God-realization by gradually equipping himself for the same, he realizes God in a trice and, after realizing Him, gets merged in Him.

As for the man who has awoken from a dream he repeatedly recalls the world of his dream and feels that in the dreamstate he looked upon the body as his very self and the world outside him as an objective reality; but on waking he realizes that his self-identification with the body as well as the perception of the dream-world were both figments. An enlightened soul, however, no longer recalls the world even as a figment. When the world itself has ceased to exist, what will he think of in terms of ‘I’ and “this”? Subsequent to God-realization the world altogether ceases to be. While the man who has awoken from a dream look-upon the dream-world as a figment, in the eye of the man who has awoken from the dream of the world, the world no longer exists even as a dream. For, while the man who has awoken from a dream continues to have the same mind and reason which he had during the dream, in the case of the man who has realized Brahma the mind and reason are left behind with the body. For Brahma is beyond the reach of mind, reason and body. Then who is to view the world even as a dream and how? Nevertheless it is said that to an enlightened soul the world is like a dream. Here it may be asked—When the world does not exist at all, why do they speak of it as analogous to a dream? Our reply to this is that, as a matter of fact to theman who has realized Brahma the world does not appear even as a dream; for in his eye the world does not exist at all. The world neither appears to him nor exists for him. It has absolutely no existence. For him there is nothing other than himself and he, too, has existed from eternity. The world, of course, does have a body of his, and the body has a mind and reason—an internal sense. In this internal sense either, really speaking, there is no trace of this world nor of the body and the internal sense referred to above; it is filled with the presence of God. Nevertheless it is said of that enlightened soul that for him the world including the mind is analogous to a dream. Who can tell the glory of such an exalted soul? The realization that ‘God exists’ is itself a proof of His existence. The entire body of scriptures is nothing but the realization of such exalted souls. There can be no greater testimony than the realization of those exalted souls, which is absolutely immediate.

Now let us make an effort to understand something about God. There are two aspects of God—(i) the absolute formless Divinity, who is Truth, Consciousness and Bliss solidified and (ii) God with form and attributes. God with attributes, again, is either with or without form. He who pervades the whole creation in His saguṇa yet formless aspect is called by the names of God and the Supreme Spirit. As God with form and attributes He eternally abides in His Divine Abode and from time to time descends into the world of matter of His own free will. In the Satyayuga He appeared in the form of śrī Viṣṇu, in the age of Tretā in the person of śrī Rāma and in the Dwāpara age as śrī Kr̥ṣṇa.

The neo-Vedantist, subscribing as he does to the doctrine ‘Ajātavāda’, recognizes no other entity than Brahma. His belief in the one reality called Brahma is perfectly logical. But his views regarding the saguṇa yet formless Divinity—whom we call by the name of God, who is the Creator of the universe, the Protector, Knower, Witness and Seer of all and who appears in various divine manifestations (Avatāras)—are not quite sound to my mind. He speaks of God (ºśwara) as a product of Māyā. He tells us that “When the cosmos is no longer there, the saguṇa aspects of God both with and without form also cease to exist, and that the one attributeless and formless Brahma alone really exists for ever.” But, really speaking, the saguṇa aspects of God both with and without form never cease to be even when the world is no more. The world is a display of Māyā—a product of matter. The saguṇa aspects of God both with and without form constitute the very essence of God and are spiritual in character. Of course, when the Lord descends into the world of matter, He certainly screens Himself with a veil of Māyā; that is why His true character remains hidden to the ignorant. This is what the Lord says in the following verse of śrīmad Bhagavadgītā—

नाहं प्रकाश: सर्वस्य योगमायासमावृत:।

मूढोऽयं नाभिजानाति लोको मामजमव्ययम्॥

(VII. 25)

“Veiled by My Yogamāyā (divine potency), I am not manifest to all. Hence these ignorant folk do not recognize Me as the unborn and imperishable Supreme Spirit (i.e., take Me to be subject to birth and death).”

Surely the Lord removes this curtain of His Yogamāyā before His reverent and loving devotees, who are thus able to know—perceive Him in His true character. It is only before the unqualified and the ignorant that He screens Himself with a veil.

Here it should be borne in mind that Lords śrī Rāma and śrī Kr̥ṣṇa possess a form similar to the human body; that is why They are believed to follow the ways of human beings. Lord Viṣṇu, on the other hand, is endowed with a form resembling that of gods; the stuff of which His Body is constituted is more or less akin to the celestial stuff. That is why Their visible forms severally resemble human and celestial bodies; but in essence They are divine and spiritual, and in no way Mayic in character. Really speaking, it is the absolute formless Brahma that appears as the Saguṇa Divinity with and without form. The neo-Vedantist does not accept this view. He is ignorant of the truth and secret pointed out above. It is the divine and immaterial virtues of the Lord that are reflected on the quality of Sattva in this world of matter. The noble qualities forming part of the divine endowment spoken of in the Gītā are Sāttvika, though products of Māyā. The virtues appearing in Sattvaguṇna are all reflections of a ray of the divine virtues. Just as a reflection of the lunar orb is seen in a mirror, the divine excellences appear in the form of ideal virtues in a pure and sinless mind. Yet they are material and not spiritual.
The virtues of the Lord, however, are purely divine and immaterial.

 

Culture of Supreme Bliss

“Halloo, what has brought you so far away from your home ?” asked the friend who lived in the Punjab, extending a hearty welcome to the newcomer. It was no longer difficult for him to understand that there must have been some special reason for his coming all the way from Rajputana.

“Famine is stalking our land. There is a great scarcity of foodgrains and people are dying of starvation. Pangs of hunger have driven me to your place.” The newcomer, who was a Vaiśya hailing from Rajputana, uttered the plain truth. Heaps of grain lying in his friend’s compound gladdened his heart.

“You did well in coming over to this place. Treat my house as your own and make yourself comfortable here,” replied the friend in great endearment.

“I find heaps of grain lying strewn here, whereas on our side it is visible only to the fortunate few. There is a scramble going on there for every grain of cereals as between crows and kites. The sight of tons of grain stored in your house has brought life to me.” The Vaiśya made himself more explicit.

“By the grace of God there is no dearth of foodgrains in these parts. This is no matter for wonder either; anyone who comes here will not lack foodgrains. You being a friend of mine, everything belonging to me is yours. It was very wise of you to have come over here,” the friend observed. He was a clever and seasoned farmer.

“Everything belonging to you is mine, I have no doubt about it; but I should like to know wherefrom you got all this stock of foodgrains,” the wondering Vaiśya inquired.

“We are engaged in agriculture all the year round. This is the reward of our year-long toil,” replied the farmer trying to satisfy his Vaiśya friend. “You, too, will have heaps of grain if you take to agriculture.”

“This is a capital idea. I shall also devote myself to the pursuit of agriculture; but I have no experience of this avocation. I have got a thousand rupees with me. Can I make a beginning with this amount ?” inquired the Vaiśya.

“One thousand rupees is not a paltry sum. You can make a very good beginning in agriculture with this amount. My unstinted co-operation will also be available to you as a matter of course,” replied the farmer in a sympathetic tone.

“I for my part have no idea of this work. Do as you deem fit,” observed the Vaiśya, handing over the whole amount in his possession to the farmer.

****

“Look here, the whole stock of seeds has been reduced to dust! Every grain of wheat is split and has gone to the dogs,” complained the Vaiśya in great despondency. He had not interfered with any plan of his Punjabi friend, who had purchased seeds etc., out of the money advanced by him and got the fields ploughed. The seeds had been sown. But the ignorant Vaiśya was feeling uneasy to see all this. A couple of days had hardly elapsed when he visited the field and unearthed the seeds in order to see what had happened to them. He found that good many of them had sprouted; this led him to suspect that his whole money had been wasted.
In great sorrow he addressed the above words to his friend.

“Sprouts have begun to shoot in your field. You need not harbour any anxiety. The seeds are full of promise. You have no idea of these things.” With these words the farmer consoled his Vaiśya friend.

“I expect no gain even after having spent money and labour. I for my part feel much worried.” The Vaiśya clearly revealed the agony of his heart. “This is what happens in the beginning. You should be free from worry. Your crop is very promising.” Such was the loving assurance of the farmer.

****

“Grass and grass alone is visible everywhere in my field. I have suffered a great loss. The whole of my investment has been thrown away.” The Vaiśya made the same complaint to the farmer after some days. He had mistaken the wheat plants, which had grown span high, for grass.

The bewildered farmer visited the field himself, but he was much pleased to see the waving crop.

“Oh! your field has overshadowed all adjoining fields.” The farmer corrected his despairing friend.
“You should take it for granted that a large heap of wheat is about to accumulate on your land. But you must take care that the plants are not allowed to wither. Water is the life of these tender plants. You should make speedy arrangements for its supply. Promptly dig a well for watering them. At the same time enclose the field with a fence of thorns lest stray animals should eat down the plants. You will have to guard the field carefully.’’

“I shall literally follow every instruction of yours,” assured the Vaiśya. And he did the same. He dug a well and watered the plants abundantly. By the grace of God the clouds also came to his aid and sent occasional showers. The plants began to grow.

****

“Weed out every single blade of grass that has grown between the plants. They obstruct the growth of the plants.” Repairing to the field, one day, the farmer issued the above instruction to the Vaiśya in a loving tone.

“Not a single blade of grass will be allowed to remain in the field,” returned the Vaiśya in a reassuring vein.

****

“Grains of wheat have made their appearance in the ears of the plants; but they are all unripe,” the Vaiśya complained to his friend wiping out the sweat off his brow. He came running from the field and had gone out of breath. In obedience to the instruction of his farmer friend he had left no blade of grass on his field. He had taken immense pains. The plants had also borne fruit. But after such a long time he found that the seeds were all unripe. Having no knowledge of agriculture, the poor fellow had lost his nerve. He thought that along with his money his hard labour too was proving infructuous. He also placed before the farmer two or three ears of corn that he had brought with him.

“The cereals of your crop are very mature. They will ripen shortly. It won’t take much time. You need not worry. Nor should you brood over the matter. You will have a mountain of wheat and fodder by your side,” assured the Vaiśya in cheering words. “But at this juncture birds make their appearance, and singing melodiously eat up the whole crop; hence it will have to be scrupulously guarded. Unless protected from them the entire crop will be destroyed by these birds.”

****

“I am grateful to you. My joy knows no bounds. I have a large heap of wheat by my side.” The Vaiśya expressed his gratitude to his friend in cheerful accents.

“All this is due to God’s grace and your own industry. Turning a deaf ear to the warbling of birds, you kept yourself solely engaged in scaring them away. You pursued your avocation in right earnest.” The farmer gave the whole credit to his Vaiśya friend.

****

The Vaiśya bent his head in gratitude. Joy was beaming on his countenance.

****

The above story has been narrated by way of a parable. In the spiritual realm the famine-stricken Vaiśya should be taken to represent the seeker striving for the attainment of true happiness. The former’s craving for foodgrains occasioned by pangs of hunger should be taken to represent the worldly trials and tribulations. The farmer friend of the Vaiśya, living in the Punjab, should be understood to symbolize an exalted soul. The Vaiśya’s calling on his friend in the Punjab from Rajputana should be taken to symbolize the seeker’s call on a saint at the latter’s residence from his own home. The Vaiśya’s carrying his tale of suffering to his friend stands for the seeker’s confiding his woes to the man of wisdom. The Vaiśya’s handing over all his money to his friend stands for the aspirant’s dedicating the rest of his life at the feet of the saint. Investing money according to the friend’s advice illustrates the Sādhakas spending his time to the best advantage according to the bidding of the man of wisdom. The farmer’s procuring land and seeds for his friend illustrates the holy man’s exhortation to the aspirant to make the best use of his time. Forgoing one’s bodily comforts in the interest of agriculture typifies the renunciation of worldly happiness in this life for the attainment of supreme bliss. The sowing of seeds in the soil typifies the treasuring in one’s heart of the secret formula of spiritual discipline imparted by an exalted soul. The mental agony and despair which overtook the Vaiśya due to his ignorance on the sprouting of seeds in the field in course of his pursuit of agriculture should be taken to represent the dark night of the soul and the suffering caused by the same in the course of one’s Sādhanā. The Vaisya’s mistaking the tiny plants of wheat for weeds represents the tendency on the part of an aspirant during the period of his spiritual discipline to mistake his Sādhanā, in spite of the progress made by him in that field, as fruitless labour rather than a spiritual discipline because of the considerable strain involved therein. The enclosure of thorns should be taken to symbolize control of the mind and senses. Guarding the crop against birds stands for refraining from wasting one’s spiritual energy by associating with self-interested men of the world. Digging a well and watering the field should be taken to symbolize the muttering of the Divine Name, dwelling on the divine form of the Lord along with His virtues and glory and study of sacred books. Timely showers through the grace of God should be construed as typifying automatic practice of Japa and meditation. Destruction of weeds in the parable stands for the elimination of immoral traits and evil designs from the mind. The fructifying of the wheat plants should be understood as typifying steadiness of meditation on God, and the warbling of birds visiting the field for feeding on the cereals should be taken to represent the tributes of praise offered to the aspirant by selfish men. Driving away birds for the protection of the crop should be construed to symbolize a contempt for praise and glory and ignoring those bestowing the same; and the ripening of the cereals as well as reaping and gathering them should be understood to typify the cessation of all sorrows and the attainment of God, the embodiment of supreme bliss.

 

Value Your Sādhana More Than the End Itself

Whatever means there are for attaining God-realization should be valued more than God-realization itself. By holding one’s Sādhana in high esteem one’s progress in Sādhana is accelerated. Generally we undervalue our Sādhana. That is why Sādhana remains slack and success continues to be remote. The great sage PataÛjali has declared that Sādhana alone to be of a high order which is carried on with esteem. He says—

तत्र स्थितौ यत्नोऽभ्यास:।

(Yoga-Darśana I. 13)

“Of these two (Abhyāsa and Vairāgya) an effort made in the direction of steadying the mind is (known as) Abhyāsa.”

स तु दीर्घकालनैरन्तर्यसत्कारासेवितो दृढभूमि:।

(Ibid., I. 14)

“The aforesaid (Abhyāsa), however, gets firmly rooted (only) when it is continued for a long time uninterruptedly with esteem.”

Lord śrī Kr̥ṣṇa, too, declares in the Gītā as the best of all, His struggling devotee carrying on his spiritual discipline with reverence. He says—

मय्यावेश्य मनो ये मां नित्यायुक्ता उपासते।

श्रद्धया परयोपेतास्ते मे युक्ततमा मता:॥

(XII. 2)

“I consider them to be the best yogīs, who endowed with supreme faith, and ever united through meditation with Me, worship Me with the mind centred on Me.”

Where there is supreme reverence esteem is already there as a matter of course.

Therefore, respect should be shown in particular to the manifold Sādhanas or courses of discipline delineated in the Vedas, Smr̥ti texts, Itihāsas and Purāṇas and which have been enumerated in the Gītā too under the names of JÛānayoga1 (the Path of Knowledge), Bhakti-Yoga2 (the Path of Devotion), Karma-Yoga3 (the Path of Action) and Dhyāna-Yoga4 (the Path of Meditation) as well as under the names of so many YajÛas or sacrificial performances5 such as Brahma-YajÛa¤, Saṁyama-YajÛa, Dravya-YajÛa, Tapo-YajÛa, Yoga-YajÛa and YajÛa in the form of Prāṇāyāma.

1. तद्‍बुद्‍ध्यस्तदात्मानस्तन्निष्ठास्तत्परायणा:।

गच्छन्त्यपुनरावृत्तिं ज्ञाननिर्धूतकल्मषा:॥

(Gītā V. 17)

“Those whose mind and intellect are wholly merged in Him, who remain constantly established in identity with Him, and have finally become one with Him, their sins being wiped out by wisdom, reach the state whence there is no return.”

2. भक्त्या त्वनन्यया शक्य अहमेवंविधोऽर्जुन।

ज्ञातुं द्रष्टुं च तत्त्वेन प्रवेष्टुं च परंतप॥

मत्कर्मकृन्मत्परमो मद्भक्त: सङ्गवर्जित:।

निर्वैर: सर्वभूतेषु य: स मामेति पाण्डव॥

(Ibid., XI. 54-55)

“Through single-minded Devotion, however, I can be seen in this form (with four arms), nay, known in essence and even entered into, O valiant Arjuna. Arjuna, he who performs all his duties for My sake, depends on Me, is devoted to Me, has no attachment, and is free from malice towards all beings, reaches Me.”

3. योगस्थ: कुरु कर्माणि सङ्गं त्यक्त्वा धनंजय।

सिद्‍ध्यसिद्‍ध्यो: समो भूत्वा समत्वं योग उच्यते॥

(Ibid., II. 48)

“Arjuna, perform your duties established in Yoga, renouncing attachment, and even-empered in success and failure, evenness of temper is called Yoga.”

4. ध्यानेनात्मनि पश्यन्ति केचिदात्मानमात्मना।

(Ibid., XIII. 24)

“Some by meditation behold the Supreme Spirit in the heart with the help of their refined and sharp intellect.”

5. Vide IV. 24, 27—29.

Many strivers complain that in spite of their going through a course of discipline they fail to achieve success. What they say is true; but their failure to achieve success is due to their not pursuing their Sādhana in a reverent mood and in a disinterested spirit. Most of the strivers keep their eyes fixed on the fruit of their Sādhana. That is why and further due to lack of faith the Sādhana is not reverently pursued. It is therefore that the result does not appear to be what they expect. It is therefore that there is no zeal in their Sādhana and a sense of frustration overtakes them resulting in slackness. Hence it behoves man to do his duty reverently and never direct his eyes towards its reward.

The Lord says in the Gītā—

कर्मण्येवाधिकारस्ते मा फलेषु कदाचन।

मा कर्मफलहेतुर्भूर्मा ते सङ्गोऽस्त्वकर्मणि॥

(II. 47)

“Your right is to work only, but never to the fruit. Do not be instrumental in making your actions bear fruit, nor let your attachment be to inaction.”

In order to bring out the unmistakable character of disinterested service we reproduce a parable below.* There lived in a certain land a saintly king, who was a supreme devotee and one who had realized the truth. He was celebrated all round. There was one Rāmū, a Kahāra (a bearer of palanquins) by caste. He was seized with a desire to see the king and with this end in view he thought of a device and sought his friend, Gopāla Kahāra, who had been in the service of the Peśakāra of the Dewan of the aforesaid King. Rāmū, as soon as he saw Gopāla, fell at the latter’s feet and said, “You are blessed indeed !” Gopāla protested at this unusual behaviour of Rāmū and said, “why should you fall at my feet ? We are after all friends.” “Don’t you know, brother?” rejoined Rāmū. “That the ruler of this land is a highly enlightened and noble soul and a supreme devotee ? You have the good luck of being in the service of the Peśakāra of his Dewan. As such you are worthy of adoration to me. Please secure an employment for me too in this house.” Thereupon Gopāla broached the matter to the Peśakāra. The latter was in need of an additional servant and Rāmū was accordingly taken into service. At the end of a month the Peśakāra handed over to Gopāla a consolidated sum of twenty rupees by way of remuneration for both at the rate of Rupees ten per month, then prevailing. Gopāla took his monthly emolument. Rāmū, however, did not accept his own and told Gopāla that he had not sought that service for the sake of any emolument. The golden opportunity, he added, which he had thereby secured of serving the Peśakāra was itself a high emolument for him. At the close of the next month when the Peśakāra offered the salary of the two servants, Gopāla declined to accept it on the plea that Rāmū had not accepted his salary even for the preceding month, so that an extra amount of ten rupees had been lying in deposit with him, which might accordingly be debited to his (Gopāla’s) account.

* The story has already appeared with some modifications here and there in one of my articles published in the Kalyana-Kalpataru.

Thereupon the Peśakāra summoned Rāmū in his presence and insisted on the latter accepting his rightful dues. Rāmū, however, flatly declined and respectfully said, “I am not serving you, my master, for the sake of a few coins. Your service itself is a gain more valuable to me than salary. You must be knowing that His Majesty is an enlightened noble soul, a supreme devotee and saint; you are the Peśakāra of his Dewan. The golden opportunity of beholding and serving you has been secured by me through great luck. What remuneration greater than this could there be for me?” “Tell me then”, the Peśakāra said, “with what end in view are you slaving to me without any consideration?” “I do your work with this object” replied Rāmū, “that through service continually rendered to you I may be enabled some day to see face to face the Dewan himself, who has the good fortune of being His Majesty’s Prime Minister.” The Peśakāra said, “I can show you the Dewan even tomorrow.” “It is so kind of you,” Rāmū gratefully replied. “Get tea and water for me in the Dewan’s office at 8 A.M. tomorrow” added the Peśakāra. Rāmū did as he was told.

Going to the Dewan’s office Rāmū stood silently gazing at the Dewan like one spellbound. The Dewan inquired who he was and what for he had come. The Peśakāra told his boss that since he had come without taking his tea, the man, who was his personal servant, had brought tea for him. With the leave of the Dewan the Peśakāra took his tea aside. Rāmū set about scrubbing the cup and other utensils and this having been done, he dusted the shoes of the Dewan and swept the dust wherever he found it. While doing all this, he kept his gaze constantly fixed on the Dewan. At this the Dewan remarked that the fellow was very useful in that, he did everything unasked for, and inquired as to how much he was paid. The Peśakāra told the Dewan that the man did not accept any remuneration even though pressed much. The Dewan thereupon summoned Rāmū in his presence and inquired of him what his object was in doing service in that way without any consideration. “My object,” replied Rāmū, “is to secure your blessed sight. I am indeed very lucky in having seen you today.” The Dewan asked him what gain he sought from his sight. “His Majesty”, replied Rāmū, “is a most saintly and enlightened devotee of the Lord and an exalted soul and you are his Dewan. One gets purified even by your sight.” “If that is so,” said the Dewan, “you had better accept service in my household.” To this Rāmū replied that he would deem his greatest fortune to be allowed to serve him. The Dewan now turned towards the Peśakāra and expressed his desire to employ Rāmū in his own household. The Peśakāra offered to give him a servant even better than Rāmū. The Dewan, however, insisted on his taking Rāmū and kept him in his personal service.

When Rāmū had been in the Dewan’s service for many days, the latter pressed him to send some money to his own people. Rāmū, however, unequivocally declined and said, “Through your grace, my master, there is no dearth of money at my home. I have already submitted to you that it is not for the sake of money that I have sought service with you. Your service alone is the highest gain to me in that you are His Majesty’s own Prime Minister while His Majesty is a man of highest wisdom, a supreme devotee and a saintly personage.” The Dewan importunately pressed him once more to send something for his (the Dewan’s) gratification. Rāmū, however, categorically declined. The Dewan then asked him what for he served him. Rāmū told him that he served the Dewan in order that he might thereby be enabled some day to behold His Majesty. The Dewan undertook to show him His Majesty the very next day and asked him to be present at the Court of His Majesty at 1 p.m. with a tumbler of water for him. “Very well!” said Rāmū and did as he was told. On tomorrow he appeared in the Court of His Majesty punctually with a tumbler of water and gazed on His Majesty with unwinking eyes like one bewitched. His Majesty’s eyes fell on him. He inquired of the Dewan as to who the man was and what had brought him. The Dewan told His Majesty that in a hurry he had come without taking water after his meal and that the man, who was his personal servant, had accordingly brought water for him to drink. With His Majesty’s permission the Dewan took some water. Rāmū stayed on. He was blessed with an unusually comely appearance and the sight of His Majesty sent him into raptures. His countenance was beaming with joy. His Majesty continued to watch his movements. He felt unaccountably drawn towards the boy. Rāmū cleaned the utensils and set about dusting and arranging things quietly. Like bees hovering about a lotus, his eyes, remained riveted on His Majesty’s countenance along. His Majesty now turned to his Prime Minister and said, “This boy looks very smart. He picks up work without being asked for and does everything with ideal neatness and orderliness. He is intelligent too. By the way, what do you pay him ?” The Dewan told His Majesty that the fellow did not accept any remuneration even on being pressingly urged. His Majesty was greatly amazed to hear this. He called Rāmū by his side and asked him with what object he was serving the Dewan so whole-heartedly without any consideration. Rāmū replied that His Majesty’s sight was his only motive in serving the Dewan and through his blessed sight alone he felt accomplished of all purpose. “If that is so,” His Majesty remarked. “You had better stay with us.” Rāmū was overjoyed to hear this and submitted, “What higher gain could there be for me? My life itself will have realized its end if I secure the good fortune of serving the soles of Your Majesty’s blessed feet.” His Majesty then turned towards the Dewan and expressed his desire to keep Rāmū in his own personal service. Rāmū was a great favourite with the Dewan, who was naturally reluctant to part with him. He, therefore mildly protested and offered to give another good servant to His Majesty. The latter, however, expressed once more his strong inclination to take Rāmū in his own service. The Dewan was left no choice but to yield. Rāmū succeeded, as a matter of course, in securing a place in His Majesty’s own royal household.

Rāmū commenced his career as a personal servant of His Majesty with great joy, reverence, efficiency, affection and zeal. He won the pleasure of His Majesty through his service. He began to feel that even a worthy son could not render such services, much less a servant. Pleased with him, His Majesty offered him a very large sum of money to requisite his services and even pressed him to send a decent amount to his home. Rāmū, however, meekly declined saying, “Through God’s grace, my lord, there is everything in my house. I do not serve the soles of Your Majesty’s feet for the sake of abundant riches. The highest gain in the form of Your Majesty’s service is itself a great recompense for me. Your Majesty is a saint and a man of wisdom, a supreme devotee and an exalted soul. A man could be redeemed by your very sight, touch and talk with you. What wonder, then, that one should attain blessedness through service rendered to Your Majesty ! I stand accomplished of all purpose through your very sight. Over and above this Your Majesty has now been pleased to bestow on me the good fortune of personally serving you. What more remains to be attained by me? The object of my life has been accomplished, my lord!” “Ask of me,” the king still persisted, “anything you will for my pleasure.” “Even if he asks for my kingdom,” he thought, “I won’t mind it and will treat him as my son.” Rāmū then submitted, “If Your Majesty insists on giving me something, be pleased to assure me that neither will Your Majesty ever part me from your company nor will Your Majesty part company with me.” The King feelingly said, “Amen! Amen !!”

The following night when His Majesty was about to retire to his gynaeceum Rāmū too followed him. On being questioned Rāmū reminded the king of the boon so kindly granted to him by His Majesty and pressed his claim to remain wherever the king stayed. Entering the gynaeceum the king told Her Majesty that he had brought her a worthy son. The queen felt highly pleased because she had no issue. Since then she began to look upon Rāmū as her own son. The boy, too, viewed Their Majesties as his parents and set himself heart and soul to their service.

One day, His Majesty told Rāmū of his intention to retire to the woods and live in solitude, and offered to install Rāmū on his throne. The latter, however, declined and reminded His Majesty of the assurance given to him that His Majesty would neither part him from his company nor part company with him, and notified the king of his resolve to accompany His Majesty in case he proceeded to the woods. The king felt a bit concerned at this reply and said, “Who will look after the kingdom if both of us retire to the woods ?” Rāmū, who was entirely free from greed and was self-denial personified, suggested that the reins of government might as well be handed over to the Dewan. Seeing his disinterested spirit the king decided to stick to the throne, but added at the same time, “I have a great mind to appoint you as my regent (Yuvarāja).” Rāmū, however, insisted on His Majesty sticking to the throne and begged to be excused for his own disinclination to accept the office of Yuvarāja, adding that he was whole-heartedly prepared to do whatever else he would be called upon to do.

His Majesty then told Rāmū that he had to sign a number of official papers everyday in token of his approval and that it was a tiresome business for him. He would, therefore, feel much relieved if Rāmū did that for him. Rāmū had to agree to this proposal for the pleasure of His Majesty. The latter proclaimed in his court that Rāmū’s signature would thenceforward be considered to be his own signature.

Later on, one day, the king told Rāmū that he had to attend the court daily, but his body had grown weak and hence asked Rāmū to do that work also. Pressed by the king, Rāmū took up that duty as well. He attended the court and, remaining there for some time attended to the work of administration and signed all papers in token of his approval. Though he declined to accept the office of Yuvarāja, he did all the work of His Majesty under his orders.

He would be present in the court even before the arrival of the Dewan. One day, while the Dewan was entering the court, Rāmū fell at his feet. The Dewan protested and said that Rāmū, who at that time was the virtual ruler of the kingdom, should not behave like that. Rāmū replied that he was no other than his menial who scrubbed his unclean utensils. The Dewan, however, interrupted him saying that he was at that moment the regent of the kingdom and should not refer to himself in such derogatory terms. Rāmū, however, attributed all that to the Dewan’s grace and repeated that he had been the Dewan’s menial and would continue to be so. With these words he clasped the Dewan’s hand and led him to the royal throne. The Dewan, however, struggled and said that the throne was meant for him and not for the Dewan, who was a servant of the crown. So saying he bowed to the throne and halted where he was without proceeding further.

On another day still later Rāmū behaved with the Peśakāra in a similar way when he saw the latter from a distance. The Peśakāra felt very uncomfortable at this unexpected turn and wondered what had led the crown prince to do so. Rāmū, however, told the Peśakāra that he was the same man who scrubbed his unclean utensils and still continued to be his menial. The Peśkāra reminded Rāmū that by God’s grace he had since risen to the position of crown prince and should not utter such humiliating words. Rāmū, however, still repeated that all that was due to the goodwill of the Peśakāra, whose servant he had been and still continued to be. So saying Rāmū took the Peśakāra too by the hand and conducted him to the royal throne. The Peśakāra demurred and expressed his inability to occupy the throne of His Majesty, which he said Rāmū alone was entitled to occupy and not the Peśakāra. Rāmū, however, continued even then to pull the Peśakāra respectfully to the throne. The Dewan, who happened to pass by, intervened and pleaded with Rāmū saying, “How can the poor Peśakāra dare place his foot on a seat which even myself could not dream of occupying?” The Peśakāra did not move further.

One day, the Peśakāra’s personal servant, Gopāla, the quondam friend of Rāmū, happened to meet Rāmū, who at once ran and fell at his feet. Gopāla felt very uncomfortable and protested arguing that he was an abject menial. Rāmū, however, pleaded that he owed his all to the goodwill of Gopāla alone, whose friend and younger brother he had been and still was. With these words Rāmū struggled to take Gopāla to the royal throne, but how could the latter dream of accepting this honour. He halted where he was.

The story narrated in the preceding number is a sort of parable. It is intended to illustrate a spiritual truth. The king in the said parable, who has been depicted as an enlightened and exalted soul and a supreme devotee of God, should be understood as representing God, Himself. The Dewan should be taken for a God-realized soul capable of leading others to God-realization. The Peśakāra should be taken to represent an advanced striver and the Peśakāra’s servant as a servant of a striving soul. The royal court should be understood as representing the divine realm of the Lord and Rāmū Kahāra should be taken to representan advanced striverin the guise of an humble servant full of disinterested love. The gradual promotion of Rāmū Kahāra to a higher and higher status should be taken to illustrate the progress made by a striver in the field of spiritual discipline; and his ascension to the office of Regent as the attainment of the highest goal.

The moral we should draw from this parable is that, even as Rāmū spurned a large sum of money that would have proved helpful in maintaining his earthly family, and did not make much even of the office of Regent earned as a reward of his services, but preferred to it the service of the Peśakāra, those of us who may be treading the path of devotion to God should make much even of the service of a servant of servants of the Lord, spurning the greatest of worldly temptations. Just as Rāmū held the Dewan in high esteem, we, too, should show great respect to men who are supremely devoted to the Lord, and more so to the Lord Himself. And even as Rāmū held His Majesty in the highest esteem, we should show the greatest reverence to the Lord in a disinterested spirit and be most submissive to Him. Just as Rāmū Kahāra, even when exalted to the position of Regent, continued to show great respect to the Dewan and his Peśakāra, nay, he maintained the same attitude of friendliness towards even the latter’s servant (Gopāla), we should likewise never forget our indebtedness to those who may have helped us in our spiritual endeavour and through whose good offices we may have been enabled to attain the highest goal—nay, we should treasure them in our heart as an object of highest veneration and ever respect them; for exalted souls always treat others with great regard. The Lord Himself, though deserving of the highest honour, never seeks honour Himself but bestows it on others. In the Mahābhārata, Anuśāsana-Parva, CXL. 93 we read the following about the Lord—

‘अमानी मानदो मान्य:’

“Though deserving of honour (Himself), Lord Viṣṇu is free from self-esteem and bestows honour on others.”

We too on this ground should bestow honour on others and never seek it ourselves.

From the parable of Rāmū Kahāra, narrated here, we should learn as its most valuable part the lesson or ideal service rendered in a disinterested spirit, and follow it in our actual life.

This is what goes by the name of doing one’s duty without reference to its reward. Higher than this is the plane where spiritual discipline is esteemed more than the end itself. In our sacred books devotion to a husband has been recognized as the best means of attaining liberation for women, and service to parents and other elders as a royal road to liberation for men.

On the subject of devotion to a husband our sisters would do well to read the narrative of a devoted wife named Sudhā, forming part of the story of Narottama Brāhmaṇa, told in Discourse 47 of Padma-Purāṇa, Sr̥ṣṭi-Khaṇḍa, as well as the story of another devoted wife, Sukalā, partner of Kr̥kala, Vaiśya by caste, narrated in Discourses 41 to 58 of Padma-Purāṇa, Bhūmi-Khaṇḍa.

Anent the service of one’s parents and preceptor Lord Manu says—

त एव हि त्रयो लोकास्त एव त्रय आश्रमा:।

त एव हि त्रयो वेदास्त एवोक्तास्त्रयोऽग्नय:॥

त्रिष्वप्रमाद्यन्नेतेषु त्रीँल्लोकान् विजयेद् गृही।

(Manusmr̥ti, II. 230 and first half of 232)

“One’s parents and preceptor alone constitute the three worlds (heaven, earth and the space intervening them); they alone represent the three śramas (stages in life). They alone are the three Vedas (¡Rk, Sāma and Yajus) and they alone have been declared to be the three fires (known by the names of Dakṣiṇa, havanīya and Gārhapatya). Taking care not to neglect these three, a householder bids fair to conquer the three worlds.”

त्रिष्वेतेष्वितिकृत्यं हि पुरुषस्य समाप्यते।

एष धर्म: पर: साक्षादुपधर्मोऽन्य उच्यते॥

(Manusmr̥ti. II. 237)

“A man’s obligation ends with the service of these three. This latter actually represents the foremost duty; every other duty is spoken of as subordinate.”

We have been hearing that in South India there lived one Puṇḍalīka, who was a devotee of his parents. The moment he realized the value and glory of serving one’s parents he started serving his parents as no other than God. Later on he moved to Pandharpur and took up his abode there along with his parents. Rising early in the morning and after easing his nature and finishing his bath he turned to the service of his parents. Helping them to ease their nature and take their bath he used to cook food for them and fed them, and took his meals only after they had been fed. He then scrubbed the unclean utensils including the plates etc., in which they had been fed, gave them decent clothes to cover their bodies with and provided them with excellent beds to sleep on. He further kneaded their feet, gave them water to drink, fanned them and ministered to their comfort in every other way according to the changing conditions of weather. In this way he served his parents.

Pleased with his service to the parents, the Lord Himself came to see him one day and stood behind him saying, “Puṇḍalīka! here am I to see you. I am the Lord Himself.” Without turning towards Him, Puṇḍalīka told the Lord by mute signals that his parents were lying asleep with their head resting each on one of his own thighs and asked Him to keep mum lest their sleep should be disturbed. He further assured the Lord that he would meet Him when his parents had woken, and pushed a brick at his back for the Lord to sit on and wait. The Lord expressed wonder at this strange behaviour of Puṇḍalīka, who did not find time to see the Lord even though he had come all the way to meet him. Puṇḍalīka replied that he had never invited the Lord, who ought to have called at an hour convenient to him. It was no fault of his, he added, that the Lord had come at an odd hour. The Lord then told Puṇḍalīka that it was the latter’s devotion to his parents that had attracted Him to that place. Puṇḍalīka wanted to know if devotion to one’s parents was potent enough to compel the Lord to call on him without being invited. On the Lord confirming this fact, Puṇḍalīka said, “If that is so, how could You expect me to give up such valuable devotion to my parents and meet You ? You must, therefore, wait in order to be able to see me.” Pleased at this straightforward reply of Puṇḍalīka, the Lord came face to face with Puṇḍalīka and said, “Since you have given priority to service of your parents even over Me, I am much pleased with this behaviour of yours. Ask of Me any boon you will.” Puṇḍalīka only prayed that he should continue to cherish unwavering reverence and love for service to his parents. The Lord granted his prayer with the word “Amen!”

Just as Puṇḍalīka, a devotee of his parents, gave priority to his Sādhana in the form of service to his parents even over his goal in the shape of God-realization, we, too, should value spiritual disciplines such as JÛānayoga, Bhaktiyoga and Karmayoga more than God-realization itself. The idea is that whichever of the manifold courses of discipline we pursue, we ought to do so with utmost reverence and love and in a meek and disinterested spirit secretly without interruption as a matter of duty, never turning our eyes towards its fruit. If, however, there is something wanting in our Sādhana, we must of course be on our guard every moment.

If, for instance, we are treading on the path of knowledge, we should judge our Sādhana according to the standard set up at some length in Gītā XVIII, 50 to 55. If our Sādhana lacks in any detail mentioned there, special effort should be made to make up that deficiency without reference of course to the fruit of such endeavour in the shape of God-realization.

A striver on the path of Devotion too should likewise review his Sādhana. Detailing the discipline of Bhakti (devotion) in Discourses XII and XV of the Gītā, the Lord delineates in Discourse XVI the distinguishing marks of the Divine Endowment (Daivī Sampad). Hence a striver on the path of Bhakti needs must be endowed with the marks of Daivi Sampad. In Gītā IX. 13-14 the Lord says of particular—

महात्मानस्तु मां पार्थ दैवीं प्रकृतिमाश्रिता:।

भजन्त्यनन्यमनसो ज्ञात्वा भूतादिमव्ययम्॥

सततं कीर्तयन्तो मां यतन्तश्च दृढव्रता:।

नमस्यन्तश्च मां भक्त्या नित्ययुक्ता उपासते॥

“On the other hand, Arjuna, great souls who have embraced the divine nature, knowing Me as the (perennial) source of all lives and the imperishable eternal, worship Me constantly with none else in mind. Constantly chanting My names and glories and striving for My realization, and bowing (again and again) to Me, those devotees of firm resolve, ever united with Me (through meditation), worship Me (with single-minded devotion).”

Therefore, the striver treading the path of devotion should take note of the marks of Daivī Sampad enumerated in Gītā XVI.1 to 3 and try to develop such marks as may be lacking in him and persevere in his Sādhana without reference to its fruit.

For him who is practising Karmayoga emphasis has been laid on action; of course his actions must be performed in a disinterested spirit. If this is lacking in any respect, the deficiency ought to be made up, but no wistful eye should be cast towards the fruit. Bringing home to one’s mind what has been taught in verses 40 to 71 of Discourse II and in the whole of Discourse III as well as in the synopsis of all this given in my gloss on the Gītā contained in Gītā-Tattva Number—I, while showing the connecting link between verses 29 and 30 of Discourse III, one should diligently pursue the discipline of Karmayoga. The same remarks apply mutatis mutandis to other Yogas too.

If a striver pursuing any of the aforesaid disciplines does not directly seek its reward but feels sanguine in his heart that the Lord Himself will confer its reward on him or that its fruit will come to him as a matter of course, it will be seen that such an expectant attitude, too, implies a latent craving for the fruit since the motive is already there. Hence the best thing is not to have any expectation at all. The striver should pursue his Sādhana only as a matter of duty.

The example of Prahlāda is a very sublime model for strivers on the path of devotion to emulate in the matter of disinterestedness. He remained deeply and solely absorbed in devotion to the Lord from his very childhood. One day, his father Hiraṇyakaśipu called him by his side and lovingly spoke to him as follows—“Prahlāda, my darling, reproduce to me that which is liked by you the most of what you have so far learnt from your teachers.” To this Prahlāda made the following answer—

श्रवणं कीर्तनं विष्णो: स्मरणं पादसेवनम्।

अर्चनं वन्दनं दास्यं सख्यमात्मनिवेदनम्॥

इति पुंसार्पिता विष्णौ भक्तिश्चेन्नवलक्षणा।

क्रियते भगवत्यद्धा तन्मयेऽधीतमुत्तमम्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata VII. V. 23-24)

“(1) To hear the names, praises and stories of Lord Viṣṇu and (2) chant them, (3) to remember Him (as well as His names and stories), (4) to do service to Him, (5) to offer worship and (6) salutation to Him, (7) to dedicate one’s actions to Him (8) to cultivate friendship with Him and (9) to offer one’s own body as well as one’s dependants and belongings to Him—if devotion marked by these nine features is practised by a man as something already consecrated direct to Lord Viṣṇu, I reckon that (such devotion) to be the highest (form of) learning.”

Hiraṇyakaśipu flew into a rage to hear this reply of Prahlāda and exclaimed, “Take him out of the palace, O demons, and dispose him of by any means—for a veritable foe has appeared in my house in the garb of a son.” Saying so he had Prahlāda trampled by large elephants in rut, bitten by venomous serpents, hurled from mountain-tops, given poison, starved, cast into the sea, exposed to tempestuous winds, crushed under the weight of rocks and cast as fuel into a blasting fire and subjected to other such atrocious cruelties. But the demon did not succeed in killing Prahlāda by any of these devices. Prahlāda’s mind was absorbed in the thought of the Supreme Spirit, the transcendent Brahma, the Soul of the universe, that is beyond the ken of speech and mind, and the substratum of all energies. Hence all attempts on his life proved unfructuous. Although Hiraṇyakaśipu tried numerous expedients to kill Prahlāda (the details of which are given in śrīmad Bhāgavata VII. v. and Viṣṇu-Purāṇa I. xvii-xviii), Prahlāda on no occasion implored the Lord either to save him or for any other object. When the Lord appeared in the form of a Man-Lion and despatched Hiraṇyakaśipu, He turned to Prahlāda and said, “Prahlāda, I am highly pleased with you. Ask of Me any boon of your choice.” Even though enticed with such tempting offers, the devotee Prahlāda, an exclusive lover of the Lord, sought no boon. On the other hand, he deemed it an obstacle in the path of devotion to solicit a boon and said—

यस्त आशिष आशास्ते न स भृत्य: स वै वणिक्।

(śrīmad Bhāgavata VII. x. 4)

“He who seeks boons of You is no servant of Yours, he is only a barterer.”

“I am as a matter of fact Your disinterested devotee,” continued Prahlāda, “while You are my Master having no end to achieve. Unlike the interests of a king and his servant in this world, our interest (in each other) is not governed by any other consideration. (Hence) if You would bestow on me blessings sought by me, O Chief of the bestowers of boons. I ask of You this boon alone that no more cravings may (ever) sprout in my mind.”

यदि रासीश मे कामान् वरांस्त्वं वरदर्षभ।

कामानां हृद्यसंरोहं भवतस्तु वृणे वरम्॥

(śrīmad Bhāgavata VII. x. 7)

“For, with the very appearance of desire,” he went on, “the senses of perception as well as the organs of action, the mind, vital energy, the body, righteousness, firmness, understanding, modesty, affluence, glory, memory and truthfulness disappear. When (on the other hand) a man gets rid of cravings imbedded in his heart, then alone he becomes qualified for attaining the divine state.”

How sublime is the disinterested spirit of Prahlāda! Following the example of Prahlāda, who practised disinterested devotion to the Lord, we, too, should cultivate a highly disinterested spirit, whatever course of spiritual discipline we may be following.

The progress in spiritual discipline gets steadily accelerated very soon in the case of a striver who pursues such a discipline in a disinterested spirit as a matter of duty without reference to its fruit, and values it more than the end itself, and speedily attains God-realization.

 

End Page
